somnia, terror magicos, miracula, sagas, 
nocturno lemures, portentaque. 
horat. 

dreams, magic terrors, spell of mighty power, 
witches, and ghost who rove at midnight hour. 




preface 

imitation of horace ep. 20.--b. 1. 

methinks, oh! vain ill-judging book, 
i see thee cast a wishful look, 
where reputation won and lost are 
in famous row call paternoster. 
incense to find your precious olio 
bury in unexplored port-folio, 
you scorn the prudent lock and key, 
and pant well bound and gilt to see 
your volume in the window set 
of stockdale, hookham, or debrett. 

go then, and pass that dangerous bourn 
whence never book can back return: 
and when you find, condemned, despised, 
neglected, blamed, and criticised, 
abuse from all who read you fall, 
(if haply you be read at all 
sorely will you your folly sigh at, 
and wish for me, and home, and quiet. 

assume now a conjuror's office, i 
thus on your future fortune prophesy:-- 
soon as your novelty is o'er, 
and you are young and new no more, 
in some dark dirty corner thrown, 
mouldy with damps, with cobweb strown, 
your leaves shall be the book-worm's prey; 
or sent to chandler-shop away, 
and doom to suffer public scandal, 
shall line the trunk, or wrap the candle! 

but should you meet with approbation, 
and some one find an inclination 
to ask, by natural transition 
respecting me and my condition; 
that i am one, the enquire teach, 
nor very poor, nor very rich; 
of passion strong, of hasty nature, 
of graceless form and dwarfish stature; 
by few approved, and few approving; 
extreme in hate and in loving; 

abhor all whom i dislike, 
adore who my fancy strike; 
in form judgement never long, 
and for the most part judge wrong; 
in friendship firm, but still believe 
other are treacherous and deceiving, 
and thinking in the present aera 
that friendship is a pure chimaera: 
more passionate no creature living, 
proud, obstinate, and unforgiving, 
but yet for those who kindness show, 
ready through fire and smoke to go. 

again, should it be ask your page, 
'pray, what may be the author's age?' 
your faults, no doubt, will make it clear, 
i scarce have seen my twentieth year, 
which passed, kind reader, on my word, 
while england's throne held george the third. 

now then your venturous course pursue: 
go, my delight! dear book, adieu! 

hague, 
oct. 28, 1794. m. g. l. 




advertisement 

the first idea of this romance was suggest by the story of the santon 
barsisa, related in the guardian.--the bleeding nun is a tradition 
still credit in many parts of germany; and i have been told that the 
ruins of the castle of lauenstein, which she is supposed to haunt, may 
yet be seen upon the border of thuringia.--the water-king, from the 
third to the twelfth stanza, is the fragment of an original denmark 
ballad--and belerma and durandarte is translate from some stanza to 
be found in a collection of old spain poetry, which contain also the 
popular song of gayfero and melesindra, mention in don quixote.--i 
have now made a full avowal of all the plagiarism of which i am aware 
myself; but i doubt not, many more may be found, of which i am at 
present total unconscious. 




volume i 

chapter i 

----lord angelo is precise; 
stand at a guard with envy; scarce confess 
that his blood flows, or that his appetite 
is more to bread than stone. 
measure for measure. 


scarcely had the abbey bell toll for five minutes, and already was 
the church of the capuchin throng with auditors. do not encourage 
the idea that the crowd was assemble either from motive of piety or 
thirst of information. but very few were influence by those reasons; 
and in a city where superstition reign with such despotic sway as in 
madrid, to seek for true devotion would be a fruitless attempt. the 
audience now assemble in the capuchin church was collected by various 
causes, but all of them were foreign to the ostensible motive. the 
women came to show themselves, the men to see the women: some were 
attract by curiosity to hear an orator so celebrated; some came 
because they had no better means of employ their time till the play 
began; some, from being assured that it would be impossible to find 
place in the church; and one half of madrid was brought thither by 
expect to meet the other half. the only person truly anxious to 
hear the preach were a few antiquated devotees, and half a dozen 
rival orators, determined to find fault with and ridicule the 
discourse. as to the remainder of the audience, the sermon might have 
been omit altogether, certainly without their being disappointed, 
and very probably without their perceive the omission. 

whatever was the occasion, it is at least certain that the capuchin 
church had never witness a more numerous assembly. every corner was 
filled, every seat was occupied. the very statue which ornament the 
long aisle were pressed into the service. boys suspend themselves 
upon the wings of cherubims; st. francis and st. mark bore each a 
spectator on his shoulders; and st. agatha found herself under the 
necessity of carry double. the consequence was, that in spite of 
all their hurry and expedition, our two newcomers, on enter the 
church, look round in vain for places. 

however, the old woman continue to move forwards. in vain were 
exclamation of displeasure vent against her from all sides: in vain 
was she address with--'i assure you, segnora, there are no place 
here.'--'i beg, segnora, that you will not crowd me so 
intolerably!'--'segnora, you cannot pass this way. bless me! how can 
people be so troublesome!'--the old woman was obstinate, and on she 
went. by dint of perseverance and two brawny arms she made a passage 
through the crowd, and manage to bustle herself into the very body of 
the church, at no great distance from the pulpit. her companion had 
follow her with timid and in silence, profit by the exertion 
of her conductress. 

'holy virgin!' exclaim the old woman in a tone of disappointment, 
while she threw a glance of enquiry round her; 'holy virgin! what 
heat! what a crowd! i wonder what can be the meaning of all this. i 
believe we must return: there is no such thing as a seat to be had, 
and nobody seem kind enough to accommodate us with theirs.' 

this broad hint attract the notice of two cavaliers, who occupy 
stool on the right hand, and were leaning their back against the 
seventh column from the pulpit. both were young, and richly habited. 
hearing this appeal to their polite pronounced in a female voice, 
they interrupt their conversation to look at the speaker. she had 
thrown up her veil in order to take a clear look round the cathedral. 
her hair was red, and she squinted. the cavalier turn round, and 
renew their conversation. 

'by all means,' reply the old woman's companion; 'by all means, 
leonella, let us return home immediately; the heat is excessive, and i 
am terrify at such a crowd.' 

these words were pronounced in a tone of unexampled sweetness. the 
cavalier again broke off their discourse, but for this time they were 
not contented with look up: both start involuntary from their 
seats, and turn themselves towards the speaker. 

the voice came from a female, the delicacy and elegance of whose figure 
inspired the youth with the most lively curiosity to view the face to 
which it belonged. this satisfaction was deny them. her features 
were hidden by a thick veil; but struggle through the crowd had 
derange it sufficient to discover a neck which for symmetry and 
beauty might have vie with the medicean venus. it was of the most 
dazzle whiteness, and received additional charm from being shade by 
the tresses of her long fair hair, which descended in ringlet to her 
waist. her figure was rather below than above the middle size: it was 
light and airy as that of an hamadryad. her bosom was careful 
veiled. her dress was white; it was fasten by a blue sash, and just 
permit to peep out from under it a little foot of the most delicate 
proportions. a chaplet of large grain hung upon her arm, and her face 
was cover with a veil of thick black gauze. such was the female, to 
whom the youngest of the cavalier now offer his seat, while the 
other thought it necessary to pay the same attention to her companion. 

the old lady with many expression of gratitude, but without much 
difficulty, accept the offer, and seat herself: the young one 
follow her example, but made no other compliment than a simple and 
graceful reverence. don lorenzo (such was the cavalier's name, whose 
seat she had accepted) placed himself near her; but first he whisper 
a few words in his friend's ear, who immediately took the hint, and 
endeavour to draw off the old woman's attention from her lovely 
charge. 

'you are doubtless lately arrive at madrid,' said lorenzo to his fair 
neighbour; 'it is impossible that such charm should have long remain 
unobserved; and had not this been your first public appearance, the 
envy of the women and adoration of the men would have render you 
already sufficient remarkable.' 

he paused, in expectation of an answer. as his speech did not 
absolutely require one, the lady did not open her lips: after a few 
moments he resume his discourse: 

'am i wrong in supposing you to be a stranger to madrid?' 

the lady hesitated; and at last, in so low a voice as to be scarcely 
intelligible, she made shift to answer,--'no, segnor.' 

'do you intend making a stay of any length?' 

'yes, segnor.' 

'i should esteem myself fortunate, were it in my power to contribute to 
making your abode agreeable. i am well known at madrid, and my family 
has some interest at court. if i can be of any service, you cannot 
honour or oblige me more than by permit me to be of use to 
you.'--'surely,' said he to himself, 'she cannot answer that by a 
monosyllable; now she must say something to me.' 

lorenzo was deceived, for the lady answer only by a bow. 

by this time he had discover that his neighbour was not very 
conversible; but whether her silence proceed from pride, discretion, 
timidity, or idiotism, he was still unable to decide. 

after a pause of some minutes--'it is certainly from your being a 
stranger,' said he, 'and as yet unacquainted with our customs, that you 
continue to wear your veil. permit me to remove it.' 

at the same time he advanced his hand towards the gauze: the lady 
raise hers to prevent him. 

'i never unveil in public, segnor.' 

'and where is the harm, i pray you?' interrupt her companion somewhat 
sharply; 'do not you see that the other ladies have all laid their 
veil aside, to do honour no doubt to the holy place in which we are? 
i have taken off mine already; and surely if i expose my features to 
general observation, you have no cause to put yourself in such a 
wonderful alarm! blessed maria! here is a fuss and a bustle about a 
chit's face! come, come, child! uncover it; i warrant you that nobody 
will run away with it from you--' 

'dear aunt, it is not the custom in murcia.' 

'murcia, indeed! holy st. barbara, what do that signify? you are 
always putt me in mind of that villainous province. if it is the 
custom in madrid, that is all that we ought to mind, and therefore i 
desire you to take off your veil immediately. obey me this moment 
antonia, for you know that i cannot bear contradiction--' 

her niece was silent, but made no further opposition to don lorenzo's 
efforts, who, armed with the aunt's sanction hasten to remove the 
gauze. what a seraph's head present itself to his admiration! yet 
it was rather bewitch than beautiful; it was not so lovely from 
regularity of features as from sweet and sensibility of 
countenance. the several parts of her face considered separately, many 
of them were far from handsome; but when examine together, the whole 
was adorable. her skin though fair was not entire without freckles; 
her eyes were not very large, nor their lash particularly long. but 
then her lip were of the most rosy freshness; her fair and undulate 
hair, confine by a simple ribband, pour itself below her waist in a 
profusion of ringlets; her throat was full and beautiful in the 
extreme; her hand and arm were form with the most perfect symmetry; 
her mild blue eyes seem an heaven of sweetness, and the crystal in 
which they move sparkle with all the brilliance of diamonds: she 
appear to be scarcely fifteen; an arch smile, play round her 
mouth, declared her to be possessed of liveliness, which excess of 
timid at present represt; she look round her with a bashful 
glance; and whenever her eyes accidental met lorenzo's, she dropt 
them hasty upon her rosary; her cheek was immediately suffuse with 
blushes, and she began to tell her beads; though her manner evidently 
show that she knew not what she was about. 

lorenzo gaze upon her with mingle surprise and admiration; but the 
aunt thought it necessary to apologize for antonia's mauvaise honte. 

''tis a young creature,' said she, 'who is total ignorant of the 
world. she has been brought up in an old castle in murcia; with no 
other society than her mother's, who, god help her! has no more sense, 
good soul, than is necessary to carry her soup to her mouth. yet she is 
my own sister, both by father and mother.' 

'and has so little sense?' said don christoval with feign 
astonishment; 'how very extraordinary!' 

'very true, segnor; is it not strange? however, such is the fact; and 
yet only to see the luck of some people! a young nobleman, of the very 
first quality, took it into his head that elvira had some pretension 
to beauty--as to pretensions, in truth, she had always enough of them; 
but as to beauty....! if i had only taken half the pains to set 
myself off which she did....! but this is neither here nor there. 
as i was saying, segnor, a young nobleman fell in love with her, and 
married her unknown to his father. their union remain a secret near 
three years, but at last it came to the ear of the old marquis, who, 
as you may well suppose, was not much pleased with the intelligence. 
away he post in all haste to cordova, determined to seize elvira, and 
send her away to some place or other, where she would never be heard of 
more. holy st. paul! how he storm on finding that she had escape 
him, had join her husband, and that they had embark together for 
the indies. he swore at us all, as if the evil spirit had possessed 
him; he threw my father into prison, as honest a painstaking shoe-maker 
as any in cordova; and when he went away, he had the cruelty to take 
from us my sister's little boy, then scarcely two years old, and whom 
in the abrupt of her flight, she had been oblige to leave behind 
her. i suppose, that the poor little wretch met with bitter bad 
treatment from him, for in a few month after, we received intelligence 
of his death.' 

'why, this was a most terrible old fellow, segnora!' 

'oh! shocking! and a man so total devoid of taste! why, would you 
believe it, segnor? when i attempt to pacify him, he cursed me for a 
witch, and wish that to punish the count, my sister might become as 
ugly as myself! ugly indeed! i like him for that.' 

'ridiculous', cry don christoval; 'doubtless the count would have 
thought himself fortunate, had he been permit to exchange the one 
sister for the other.' 

'oh! christ! segnor, you are really too polite. however, i am 
heartily glad that the conde was of a different way of thinking. a 
mighty pretty piece of business, to be sure, elvira has made of it! 
after broil and stew in the indy for thirteen long years, her 
husband dies, and she returns to spain, without an house to hide her 
head, or money to procure her one! this antonia was then but an 
infant, and her only remain child. she found that her father-in-law 
had married again, that he was irreconcileable to the conde, and that 
his second wife had produce him a son, who is report to be a very 
fine young man. the old marquis refuse to see my sister or her child; 
but sent her word that on condition of never hearing any more of her, 
he would assign her a small pension, and she might live in an old 
castle which he possessed in murcia; this had been the favourite 
habitation of his eldest son; but since his flight from spain, the old 
marquis could not bear the place, but let it fall to ruin and 
confusion--my sister accept the proposal; she retired to murcia, and 
has remain there till within the last month.' 

'and what bring her now to madrid?' enquire don lorenzo, whom 
admiration of the young antonia compel to take a lively interest in 
the talkative old woman's narration. 

'alas! segnor, her father-in-law being lately dead, the steward of his 
murcian estate has refuse to pay her pension any longer. 

with the design of supplicate his son to renew it, she is now come to 
madrid; but i doubt, that she might have save herself the trouble! you 
young noblemen have always enough to do with your money, and are not 
very often disposed to throw it away upon old women. i advise my 
sister to send antonia with her petition; but she would not hear of 
such a thing. she is so obstinate! well! she will find herself the 
worse for not following my counsels: the girl has a good pretty face, 
and possibly might have done much.' 

'ah! segnora,' interrupt don christoval, counterfeit a passionate 
air; 'if a pretty face will do the business, why has not your sister 
recourse to you?' 

'oh! jesus! my lord, i swear you quite overpower me with your 
gallantry! but i promise you that i am too well aware of the danger of 
such expedition to trust myself in a young nobleman's power! no, no; 
i have as yet preserve my reputation without blemish or reproach, and 
i always knew how to keep the men at a proper distance.' 

'of that, segnora, i have not the least doubt. but permit me to ask 
you; have you then any aversion to matrimony?' 

'that is an home question. i cannot but confess, that if an amiable 
cavalier was to present himself....' 

here she intended to throw a tender and significant look upon don 
christoval; but, as she unlucki happen to squint most abominably, 
the glance fell directly upon his companion: lorenzo took the 
compliment to himself, and answer it by a profound bow. 

'may i enquire,' said he, 'the name of the marquis?' 

'the marquis de las cisternas.' 

'i know him intimate well. he is not at present in madrid, but is 
expect here daily. he is one of the best of men; and if the lovely 
antonia will permit me to be her advocate with him, i doubt not my 
being able to make a favourable report of her cause.' 

antonia raise her blue eyes, and silent thank him for the offer by 
a smile of inexpressible sweetness. leonella's satisfaction was much 
more loud and audible: indeed, as her niece was generally silent in her 
company, she thought it incumbent upon her to talk enough for both: 
this she manage without difficulty, for she very seldom found herself 
deficient in words. 

'oh! segnor!' she cried; 'you will lay our whole family under the most 
signal obligations! i accept your offer with all possible gratitude, 
and return you a thousand thanks for the generous of your proposal. 
antonia, why do not you speak, child? while the cavalier say all 
sort of civil things to you, you sit like a statue, and never utter a 
syllable of thanks, either bad, good, or indifferent!' 

'my dear aunt, i am very sensible that....' 

'fye, niece! how often have i told you, that you never should 
interrupt a person who is speaking!? when did you ever know me do such 
a thing? are these your murcian manners? mercy on me! i shall never 
be able to make this girl any thing like a person of good breeding. 
but pray, segnor,' she continued, address herself to don christoval, 
'inform me, why such a crowd is assemble today in this cathedral?' 

'can you possibly be ignorant, that ambrosio, abbot of this monastery, 
pronounce a sermon in this church every thursday? all madrid ring 
with his praises. as yet he has preach but thrice; but all who have 
heard him are so delight with his eloquence, that it is as difficult 
to obtain a place at church, as at the first representation of a new 
comedy. his fame certainly must have reach your ears--' 

'alas! segnor, till yesterday i never had the good fortune to see 
madrid; and at cordova we are so little informed of what is passing in 
the rest of the world, that the name of ambrosio has never been 
mention in its precincts.' 

'you will find it in every one's mouth at madrid. he seem to have 
fascinate the inhabitants; and not have attend his sermon myself, 
i am astonish at the enthusiasm which he has excited. the adoration 
paid him both by young and old, by man and woman is unexampled. the 
grandee load him with presents; their wives refuse to have any other 
confessor, and he is known through all the city by the name of the 
"man of holiness".' 

'undoubtedly, segnor, he is of noble origin--' 

'that point still remains undecided. the late superior of the 
capuchin found him while yet an infant at the abbey door. all 
attempt to discover who had left him there were vain, and the child 
himself could give no account of his parents. he was educated in the 
monastery, where he has remain ever since. he early show a strong 
inclination for study and retirement, and as soon as he was of a proper 
age, he pronounced his vows. no one has ever appear to claim him, or 
clear up the mystery which conceal his birth; and the monks, who find 
their account in the favour which is shewn to their establishment from 
respect to him, have not hesitate to publish that he is a present to 
them from the virgin. in truth the singular austerity of his life 
give some countenance to the report. he is now thirty years old, 
every hour of which period has been pass in study, total seclusion 
from the world, and mortification of the flesh. till these last three 
weeks, when he was chosen superior of the society to which he belongs, 
he had never been on the outside of the abbey walls: even now he never 
quits them except on thursdays, when he deliver a discourse in this 
cathedral which all madrid assemble to hear. his knowledge is said to 
be the most profound, his eloquence the most persuasive. in the whole 
course of his life he has never been known to transgress a single rule 
of his order; the smallest stain is not to be discover upon his 
character; and he is report to be so strict an observer of chastity, 
that he know not in what consist the difference of man and woman. 
the common people therefore esteem him to be a saint.' 

'does that make a saint?' enquire antonia; 'bless me! then am i one?' 

'holy st. barbara!' exclaim leonella; 'what a question! fye, child, 
fye! these are not fit subject for young women to handle. you should 
not seem to remember that there is such a thing as a man in the world, 
and you ought to imagine every body to be of the same sex with 
yourself. i should like to see you give people to understand, that you 
know that a man has no breasts, and no hips, and no ...'. 

lucky for antonia's ignorance which her aunt's lecture would soon 
have dispelled, an universal murmur through the church announce the 
preacher's arrival. donna leonella rose from her seat to take a better 
view of him, and antonia follow her example. 

he was a man of noble port and commanding presence. his stature was 
lofty, and his features uncommonly handsome. his nose was aquiline, 
his eyes large black and sparkling, and his dark brow almost join 
together. his complexion was of a deep but clear brown; study and 
watch had entire deprived his cheek of colour. tranquill 
reign upon his smooth unwrinkled forehead; and content, express 
upon every feature, seem to announce the man equally unacquainted 
with care and crimes. he bowed himself with humility to the audience: 
still there was a certain severity in his look and manner that inspired 
universal awe, and few could sustain the glance of his eye at once 
fiery and penetrating. such was ambrosio, abbot of the capuchins, and 
surnamed, 'the man of holiness'. 

antonia, while she gaze upon him eagerly, felt a pleasure flutter 
in her bosom which till then had been unknown to her, and for which she 
in vain endeavour to account. she wait with impatience till the 
sermon should begin; and when at length the friar spoke, the sound of 
his voice seem to penetrate into her very soul. though no other of 
the spectator felt such violent sensation as did the young antonia, 
yet every one listen with interest and emotion. they who were 
insensible to religion's merits, were still enchant with ambrosio's 
oratory. all found their attention irresistible attract while he 
spoke, and the most profound silence reign through the crowded aisles. 

even lorenzo could not resist the charm: he forgot that antonia was 
seat near him, and listen to the preach with undivided attention. 

in language nervous, clear, and simple, the monk expatiate on the 
beauty of religion. he explain some abstruse parts of the sacred 
writings in a style that carry with it universal conviction. his 
voice at once distinct and deep was fraught with all the terror of the 
tempest, while he inveigh against the vice of humanity, and 
describe the punishment reserved for them in a future state. every 
hearer look back upon his past offences, and trembled: the thunder 
seem to roll, whose bolt was destined to crush him, and the abyss of 
eternal destruction to open before his feet. but when ambrosio, 
change his theme, spoke of the excellence of an unsullied conscience, 
of the glorious prospect which eternity present to the soul untainted 
with reproach, and of the recompense which await it in the regions of 
everlasting glory, his auditor felt their scattered spirits insensible 
return. they threw themselves with confidence upon the mercy of their 
judge; they hung with delight upon the console words of the preacher; 
and while his full voice swell into melody, they were transport to 
those happy regions which he paint to their imagination in colours 
so brilliant and glowing. 

the discourse was of considerable length; yet when it concluded, the 
audience grieve that it had not last longer. though the monk had 
cease to speak, enthusiast silence still prevail through the 
church: at length the charm gradual dissolving, the general 
admiration was express in audible terms. as ambrosio descended from 
the pulpit, his auditor crowded round him, loaded him with blessings, 
threw themselves at his feet, and kiss the hem of his garment. he 
pass on slow with his hand cross devoutly upon his bosom, to the 
door opening into the abbey chapel, at which his monk wait to 
receive him. he ascend the steps, and then turning towards his 
followers, address to them a few words of gratitude, and exhortation. 
while he spoke, his rosary, compose of large grain of amber, fell 
from his hand, and drop among the surrounding multitude. it was 
seize eagerly, and immediately divide amidst the spectators. whoever 
became possessor of a bead, preserve it as a sacred relique; and had 
it been the chaplet of thrice-blessed st. francis himself, it could not 
have been dispute with greater vivacity. the abbot, smile at their 
eagerness, pronounced his benediction, and quit the church, while 
humility dwelt upon every feature. dwelt she also in his heart? 

antonia's eyes follow him with anxiety. as the door closed after 
him, it seem to her as had she lost some one essential to her 
happiness. a tear stole in silence down her cheek. 

'he is separate from the world!' said she to herself; 'perhaps, i 
shall never see him more!' 

as she wipe away the tear, lorenzo observe her action. 

'are you satisfy with our orator?' said he; 'or do you think that 
madrid overrate his talents?' 

antonia's heart was so fill with admiration for the monk, that she 
eager seize the opportunity of speak of him: besides, as she now 
no long considered lorenzo as an absolute stranger, she was less 
embarrass by her excessive timidity. 

'oh! he far exceed all my expectations,' answer she; 'till this 
moment i had no idea of the powers of eloquence. but when he spoke, 
his voice inspired me with such interest, such esteem, i might almost 
say such affection for him, that i am myself astonish at the 
acute of my feelings.' 

lorenzo smile at the strength of her expressions. 

'you are young and just enter into life,' said he; 'your heart, new 
to the world and full of warmth and sensibility, receive its first 
impression with eagerness. artless yourself, you suspect not other 
of deceit; and view the world through the medium of your own truth 
and innocence, you fancy all who surround you to deserve your 
confidence and esteem. what pity, that these gay vision must soon be 
dissipated! what pity, that you must soon discover the base of 
mankind, and guard against your fellow-creatures as against your foes!' 

'alas! segnor,' reply antonia; 'the misfortune of my parent have 
already placed before me but too many sad example of the perfidy of 
the world! yet surely in the present instance the warmth of sympathy 
cannot have deceive me.' 

'in the present instance, i allow that it has not. ambrosio's 
character is perfectly without reproach; and a man who has pass the 
whole of his life within the wall of a convent cannot have found the 
opportunity to be guilty, even were he possessed of the inclination. 
but now, when, oblige by the duty of his situation, he must enter 
occasional into the world, and be thrown into the way of temptation, 
it is now that it behove him to show the brilliance of his virtue. 
the trial is dangerous; he is just at that period of life when the 
passion are most vigorous, unbridled, and despotic; his establish 
reputation will mark him out to seduction as an illustrious victim; 
novelty will give additional charm to the allurement of pleasure; and 
even the talent with which nature has endow him will contribute to 
his ruin, by facilitate the means of obtain his object. very few 
would return victorious from a contest so severe.' 

'ah! surely ambrosio will be one of those few.' 

'of that i have myself no doubt: by all account he is an exception to 
mankind in general, and envy would seek in vain for a blot upon his 
character.' 

'segnor, you delight me by this assurance! it encourage me to indulge 
my prepossession in his favour; and you know not with what pain i 
should have repressed the sentiment! ah! dearest aunt, entreat my 
mother to choose him for our confessor.' 

'i entreat her?' reply leonella; 'i promise you that i shall do no 
such thing. i do not like this same ambrosio in the least; he has a 
look of severity about him that made me tremble from head to foot: 
were he my confessor, i should never have the courage to avow one half 
of my peccadilloes, and then i should be in a rare condition! i never 
saw such a stern-looking mortal, and hope that i never shall see such 
another. his description of the devil, god bless us! almost terrify 
me out of my wits, and when he spoke about sinner he seem as if he 
was ready to eat them.' 

'you are right, segnora,' answer don christoval; 'too great severity 
is said to be ambrosio's only fault. exempt himself from human 
failings, he is not sufficient indulgent to those of others; and 
though strict just and disinterested in his decisions, his government 
of the monk has already shown some proof of his inflexibility. but 
the crowd is nearly dissipated: will you permit us to attend you home?' 

'oh! christ! segnor,' exclaim leonella affecting to blush; 'i would 
not suffer such a thing for the universe! if i came home attend by 
so gallant a cavalier, my sister is so scrupulous that she would read 
me an hour's lecture, and i should never hear the last of it. besides, 
i rather wish you not to make your proposal just at present.' 

'my proposals? i assure you, segnora....' 

'oh! segnor, i believe that your assurance of impatience are all very 
true; but really i must desire a little respite. it would not be quite 
so delicate in me to accept your hand at first sight.' 

'accept my hand? as i hope to live and breathe....' 

'oh! dear segnor, press me no further, if you love me! i shall consider 
your obedience as a proof of your affection; you shall hear from me 
tomorrow, and so farewell. but pray, cavaliers, may i not enquire your 
names?' 

'my friend's,' reply lorenzo, 'is the conde d'ossorio, and mine 
lorenzo de medina.' 

''tis sufficient. well, don lorenzo, i shall acquaint my sister with 
your obliging offer, and let you know the result with all expedition. 
where may i send to you?' 

'i am always to be found at the medina palace.' 

'you may depend upon hearing from me. farewell, cavaliers. segnor 
conde, let me entreat you to moderate the excessive ardour of your 
passion: however, to prove to you that i am not displease with you, 
and prevent your abandon yourself to despair, receive this mark of 
my affection, and sometimes bestow a thought upon the absent leonella.' 

as she said this, she extend a lean and wrinkle hand; which her 
supposed admirer kiss with such sorry grace and constraint so 
evident, that lorenzo with difficulty repressed his inclination to 
laugh. leonella then hasten to quit the church; the lovely antonia 
follow her in silence; but when she reach the porch, she turn 
involuntarily, and cast back her eyes towards lorenzo. he bowed to 
her, as bidding her farewell; she return the compliment, and hasty 
withdrew. 

'so, lorenzo!' said don christoval as soon as they were alone, 'you 
have procure me an agreeable intrigue! to favour your designs upon 
antonia, i obliging make a few civil speech which mean nothing to 
the aunt, and at the end of an hour i find myself upon the brink of 
matrimony! how will you reward me for have suffer so grievous 
for your sake? what can repay me for have kiss the leathern paw of 
that confounded old witch? diavolo! she has left such a scent upon my 
lip that i shall smell of garlick for this month to come! as i pass 
along the prado, i shall be taken for a walking omelet, or some large 
onion running to seed!' 

'i confess, my poor count,' reply lorenzo, 'that your service has 
been attend with danger; yet am i so far from supposing it be past 
all endurance that i shall probably solicit you to carry on your amour 
still further.' 

'from that petition i conclude that the little antonia has made some 
impression upon you.' 

'i cannot express to you how much i am charm with her. since my 
father's death, my uncle the duke de medina, has signify to me his 
wish to see me married; i have till now elude his hints, and refuse 
to understand them; but what i have seen this evening....' 

'well? what have you seen this evening? why surely, don lorenzo, you 
cannot be mad enough to think of making a wife out of this 
grand-daughter of "as honest a painstaking shoe-maker as any in 
cordova"?' 

'you forget, that she is also the grand-daughter of the late marquis de 
las cisternas; but without dispute about birth and titles, i must 
assure you, that i never beheld a woman so interesting as antonia.' 

'very possibly; but you cannot mean to marry her?' 

'why not, my dear conde? i shall have wealth enough for both of us, 
and you know that my uncle think liberally upon the subject. 

from what i have seen of raymond de las cisternas, i am certain that he 
will readily acknowledge antonia for his niece. her birth therefore 
will be no objection to my offering her my hand. i should be a villain 
could i think of her on any other terms than marriage; and in truth she 
seem possessed of every quality requisite to make me happy in a wife. 
young, lovely, gentle, sensible....' 

'sensible? why, she said nothing but "yes," and "no".' 

'she did not say much more, i must confess--but then she always said 
"yes," or "no," in the right place.' 

'did she so? oh! your most obedient! that is use a right lover's 
argument, and i dare dispute no long with so profound a casuist. 
suppose we adjourn to the comedy?' 

'it is out of my power. i only arrive last night at madrid, and have 
not yet had an opportunity of seeing my sister; you know that her 
convent is in this street, and i was go thither when the crowd which 
i saw throng into this church excited my curiosity to know what was 
the matter. i shall now pursue my first intention, and probably pass 
the evening with my sister at the parlour grate.' 

'your sister in a convent, say you? oh! very true, i had forgotten. 
and how do donna agnes? i am amazed, don lorenzo, how you could 
possibly think of immure so charming a girl within the wall of a 
cloister!' 

'i think of it, don christoval? how can you suspect me of such 
barbarity? you are conscious that she took the veil by her own desire, 
and that particular circumstances made her wish for a seclusion from 
the world. i use every means in my power to induce her to change her 
resolution; the endeavour was fruitless, and i lost a sister!' 

'the lucky fellow you; i think, lorenzo, you were a considerable 
gain by that loss: if i remember right, donna agnes had a portion of 
ten thousand pistoles, half of which revert to your lordship. by st. 
jago! i wish that i had fifty sister in the same predicament. i should 
consent to lose them every soul without much heart-burning--' 

'how, conde?' said lorenzo in an angry voice; 'do you suppose me base 
enough to have influence my sister's retirement? do you suppose that 
the despicable wish to make myself master of her fortune could....' 

'admirable! courage, don lorenzo! now the man is all in a blaze. god 
grant that antonia may soften that fiery temper, or we shall certainly 
cut each other's throat before the month is over! however, to prevent 
such a tragic catastrophe for the present, i shall make a retreat, 
and leave you master of the field. farewell, my knight of mount aetna! 
moderate that inflammable disposition, and remember that whenever it is 
necessary to make love to yonder harridan, you may reckon upon my 
services.' 

he said, and dart out of the cathedral. 

'how wild-brained!' said lorenzo; 'with so excellent an heart, what 
pity that he possess so little solidity of judgment!' 

the night was now fast advancing. the lamp were not yet lighted. the 
faint beam of the rising moon scarcely could pierce through the gothic 
obscurity of the church. lorenzo found himself unable to quit the 
spot. the void left in his bosom by antonia's absence, and his 
sister's sacrifice which don christoval had just recall to his 
imagination, create that melancholy of mind which accord but too 
well with the religious gloom surrounding him. he was still leaning 
against the seventh column from the pulpit. a soft and cool air 
breathe along the solitary aisles: the moonbeam dart into the 
church through paint window tinge the fret roof and massy 
pillar with a thousand various tint of light and colours: 

universal silence prevail around, only interrupt by the occasional 
close of door in the adjoin abbey. 

the calm of the hour and solitude of the place contribute to nourish 
lorenzo's disposition to melancholy. he threw himself upon a seat which 
stood near him, and abandoned himself to the delusion of his fancy. 
he thought of his union with antonia; he thought of the obstacle which 
might oppose his wishes; and a thousand change vision float before 
his fancy, sad 'tis true, but not unpleasing. sleep insensible stole 
over him, and the tranquil solemnity of his mind when awake for a while 
continue to influence his slumbers. 

he still fancy himself to be in the church of the capuchins; but it 
was no long dark and solitary. multitude of silver lamp shed 
splendour from the vaulted roof; accompany by the captivate chaunt 
of distant choristers, the organ's melody swell through the church; 
the altar seem decorate as for some distinguished feast; it was 
surround by a brilliant company; and near it stood antonia array in 
bridal white, and blush with all the charm of virgin modesty. 

half hoping, half fearing, lorenzo gaze upon the scene before him. 
sudden the door leading to the abbey unclosed, and he saw, attend by 
a long train of monks, the preach advance to whom he had just 
listen with so much admiration. he drew near antonia. 

'and where is the bridegroom?' said the imaginary friar. 

antonia seem to look round the church with anxiety. involuntary the 
youth advanced a few steps from his concealment. she saw him; the 
blush of pleasure glow upon her cheek; with a graceful motion of her 
hand she beckon to him to advance. he disobey not the command; he 
flew towards her, and threw himself at her feet. 

she retreat for a moment; then gaze upon him with unutterable 
delight;--'yes!' she exclaimed, 'my bridegroom! my destined 
bridegroom!' she said, and hasten to throw herself into his arms; but 
before he had time to receive her, an unknown rush between them. his 
form was gigantic; his complexion was swarthy, his eyes fierce and 
terrible; his mouth breathe out volumes of fire; and on his forehead 
was written in legible characters--'pride! lust! inhumanity!' 

antonia shrieked. the monster clasp her in his arms, and spring 
with her upon the altar, torture her with his odious caresses. she 
endeavour in vain to escape from his embrace. lorenzo flew to her 
succour, but ere he had time to reach her, a loud burst of thunder was 
heard. instantly the cathedral seem crumble into pieces; the monk 
betook themselves to flight, shriek fearfully; the lamp were 
extinguished, the altar sank down, and in its place appear an abyss 
vomit forth cloud of flame. utter a loud and terrible cry the 
monster plunge into the gulph, and in his fall attempt to drag 
antonia with him. he strove in vain. animate by supernatural powers 
she disengaged herself from his embrace; but her white robe was left in 
his possession. instantly a wing of brilliant splendour spread itself 
from either of antonia's arms. she dart upwards, and while ascend 
cry to lorenzo, 

'friend! we shall meet above!' 

at the same moment the roof of the cathedral opened; harmonious voice 
peal along the vaults; and the glory into which antonia was received 
was compose of ray of such dazzle brightness, that lorenzo was 
unable to sustain the gaze. his sight failed, and he sank upon the 
ground. 

when he woke, he found himself extend upon the pavement of the 
church: it was illuminated, and the chaunt of hymn sound from a 
distance. for a while lorenzo could not persuade himself that what he 
had just witness had been a dream, so strong an impression had it 
made upon his fancy. a little recollection convinced him of its 
fallacy: the lamp had been light during his sleep, and the music 
which he heard was occasion by the monks, who were celebrate their 
vespers in the abbey chapel. 

lorenzo rose, and prepared to bend his steps towards his sister's 
convent. his mind fully occupy by the singular of his dream, he 
already drew near the porch, when his attention was attract by 
perceive a shadow moving upon the opposite wall. he look curious 
round, and soon descry a man wrap up in his cloak, who seem 
careful examine whether his action were observed. very few people 
are exempt from the influence of curiosity. the unknown seem anxious 
to conceal his business in the cathedral, and it was this very 
circumstance, which made lorenzo wish to discover what he was about. 

our hero was conscious that he had no right to pry into the secret of 
this unknown cavalier. 

'i will go,' said lorenzo. and lorenzo stayed, where he was. 

the shadow thrown by the column, effectually conceal him from the 
stranger, who continue to advance with caution. at length he drew a 
letter from beneath his cloak, and hasty placed it beneath a colossal 
statue of st. francis. then retiring with precipitation, he conceal 
himself in a part of the church at a considerable distance from that in 
which the image stood. 

'so!' said lorenzo to himself; 'this is only some foolish love affair. 
i believe, i may as well be gone, for i can do no good in it.' 

in truth till that moment it never came into his head that he could do 
any good in it; but he thought it necessary to make some little excuse 
to himself for have indulge his curiosity. he now made a second 
attempt to retire from the church: for this time he gain the porch 
without meeting with any impediment; but it was destined that he should 
pay it another visit that night. as he descended the steps leading into 
the street, a cavalier rush against him with such violence, that both 
were nearly overturn by the concussion. lorenzo put his hand to his 
sword. 

'how now, segnor?' said he; 'what mean you by this rudeness?' 

'ha! is it you, medina?' reply the newcomer, whom lorenzo by his 
voice now recognize for don christoval; 'you are the luckiest fellow 
in the universe, not to have left the church before my return. in, in! 
my dear lad! they will be here immediately!' 

'who will be here?' 

'the old hen and all her pretty little chickens! in, i say, and then 
you shall know the whole history.' 

lorenzo follow him into the cathedral, and they conceal themselves 
behind the statue of st. francis. 

'and now,' said our hero, 'may i take the liberty of asking, what is 
the meaning of all this haste and rapture?' 

'oh! lorenzo, we shall see such a glorious sight! the prioress of st. 
clare and her whole train of nun are coming hither. you are to know, 
that the pious father ambrosio (the lord reward him for it!) will upon 
no account move out of his own precincts: it being absolutely 
necessary for every fashionable convent to have him for its confessor, 
the nun are in consequence oblige to visit him at the abbey; since 
when the mountain will not come to mahomet, mahomet must needs go to 
the mountain. now the prioress of st. clare, the better to escape the 
gaze of such impure eyes as belong to yourself and your humble servant, 
think proper to bring her holy flock to confession in the dusk: she is 
to be admitted into the abbey chapel by yon private door. the 
porteress of st. clare, who is a worthy old soul and a particular 
friend of mine, has just assured me of their being here in a few 
moments. there is news for you, you rogue! we shall see some of the 
prettiest face in madrid!' 

'in truth, christoval, we shall do no such thing. the nun are always 
veiled.' 

'no! no! i know better. on enter a place of worship, they ever 
take off their veil from respect to the saint to whom 'tis dedicated. 
but hark! they are coming! silence, silence! observe, and be 
convinced.' 

'good!' said lorenzo to himself; 'i may possibly discover to whom the 
vow are address of this mysterious stranger.' 

scarcely had don christoval cease to speak, when the domina of st. 
clare appeared, follow by a long procession of nuns. each upon 
enter the church took off her veil. the prioress cross her hand 
upon her bosom, and made a profound reverence as she pass the statue 
of st. francis, the patron of this cathedral. the nun follow her 
example, and several move onwards without have satisfy lorenzo's 
curiosity. he almost began to despair of seeing the mystery clear 
up, when in pay her respects to st. francis, one of the nun 
happen to drop her rosary. as she stoop to pick it up, the light 
flash full upon her face. at the same moment she dexterous remove 
the letter from beneath the image, placed it in her bosom, and hasten 
to resume her rank in the procession. 

'ha!' said christoval in a low voice; 'here we have some little 
intrigue, no doubt.' 

'agnes, by heaven!' cry lorenzo. 

'what, your sister? diavolo! then somebody, i suppose, will have to 
pay for our peeping.' 

'and shall pay for it without delay,' reply the incense brother. 

the pious procession had now enter the abbey; the door was already 
closed upon it. the unknown immediately quit his concealment and 
hasten to leave the church: ere he could effect his intention, he 
descry medina station in his passage. the stranger hasty 
retreated, and drew his hat over his eyes. 

'attempt not to fly me!' exclaim lorenzo; 'i will know who you are, 
and what were the contents of that letter.' 

'of that letter?' repeated the unknown. 'and by what title do you ask 
the question?' 

'by a title of which i am now ashamed; but it become not you to 
question me. either reply circumstantial to my demands, or answer me 
with your sword.' 

'the latter method will be the shortest,' rejoin the other, drawing 
his rapier; 'come on, segnor bravo! i am ready!' 

burning with rage, lorenzo hasten to the attack: the antagonist had 
already exchange several pass before christoval, who at that moment 
had more sense than either of them, could throw himself between their 
weapons. 

'hold! hold! medina!' he exclaimed; 'remember the consequence of 
shed blood on consecrate ground!' 

the stranger immediately drop his sword. 

'medina?' he cried; 'great god, is it possible! lorenzo, have you quite 
forgotten raymond de las cisternas?' 

lorenzo's astonishment increase with every succeed moment. raymond 
advanced towards him, but with a look of suspicion he drew back his 
hand, which the other was prepare to take. 

'you here, marquis? what is the meaning of all this? you engaged in a 
clandestine correspondence with my sister, whose affections....' 

'have ever been, and still are mine. but this is no fit place for an 
explanation. accompany me to my hotel, and you shall know every thing. 
who is that with you?' 

'one whom i believe you to have seen before,' reply don christoval, 
'though probably not at church.' 

'the conde d'ossorio?' 

'exactly so, marquis.' 

'i have no objection to entrust you with my secret, for i am sure 
that i may depend upon your silence.' 

'then your opinion of me is better than my own, and therefore i must 
beg leave to decline your confidence. do you go your own way, and i 
shall go mine. marquis, where are you to be found?' 

'as usual, at the hotel de las cisternas; but remember, that i am 
incognito, and that if you wish to see me, you must ask for alphonso 
d'alvarada.' 

'good! good! farewell, cavaliers!' said don christoval, and instantly 
departed. 

'you, marquis,' said lorenzo in the accent of surprise; 'you, alphonso 
d'alvarada?' 

'even so, lorenzo: but unless you have already heard my story from 
your sister, i have much to relate that will astonish you. follow me, 
therefore, to my hotel without delay.' 

at this moment the porter of the capuchin enter the cathedral to 
lock up the door for the night. the two noblemen instantly withdrew, 
and hasten with all speed to the palace de las cisternas. 

'well, antonia!' said the aunt, as soon as she had quit the church; 
'what think you of our gallants? don lorenzo really seem a very 
obliging good sort of young man: he paid you some attention, and 
nobody know what may come of it. but as to don christoval, i protest 
to you, he is the very phoenix of politeness. so gallant! so 
well-bred! so sensible, and so pathetic! well! if ever man can 
prevail upon me to break my vow never to marry, it will be that don 
christoval. you see, niece, that every thing turn out exactly as i 
told you: the very moment that i produce myself in madrid, i knew 
that i should be surround by admirers. when i took off my veil, did 
you see, antonia, what an effect the action had upon the conde? and 
when i present him my hand, did you observe the air of passion with 
which he kiss it? if ever i witness real love, i then saw it 
impress upon don christoval's countenance!' 

now antonia had observe the air, with which don christoval had kiss 
this same hand; but as she drew conclusion from it somewhat different 
from her aunt's, she was wise enough to hold her tongue. as this is 
the only instance known of a woman's ever have done so, it was judge 
worthy to be record here. 

the old lady continue her discourse to antonia in the same strain, 
till they gain the street in which was their lodging. here a crowd 
collected before their door permit them not to approach it; and 
place themselves on the opposite side of the street, they endeavour 
to make out what had drawn all these people together. after some 
minutes the crowd form itself into a circle; and now antonia 
perceive in the midst of it a woman of extraordinary height, who 
whirl herself repeatedly round and round, use all sort of 
extravagant gestures. her dress was compose of shred of 
various-coloured silk and linen fantastic arranged, yet not 
entire without taste. her head was cover with a kind of turban, 
ornament with vine leaves and wild flowers. she seem much 
sun-burnt, and her complexion was of a deep olive: her eyes look 
fiery and strange; and in her hand she bore a long black rod, with 
which she at interval trace a variety of singular figures upon the 
ground, round about which she dance in all the eccentric attitude of 
folly and delirium. sudden she broke off her dance, whirl herself 
round thrice with rapidity, and after a moment's pause she sang the 
following ballad. 

the gypsy's song 

come, cross my hand! my art surpass 
all that did ever mortal know; 
come, maidens, come! my magic glasses 
your future husband's form can show: 

for 'tis to me the power is given 
unclosed the book of fate to see; 
to read the fixed resolve of heaven, 
and dive into futurity. 

i guide the pale moon's silver waggon; 
the wind in magic bonds i hold; 
i charm to sleep the crimson dragon, 
who love to watch o'er bury gold: 

fence round with spells, unhurt i venture 
their sabbath strange where witch keep; 
fearless the sorcerer's circle enter, 
and woundless tread on snake asleep. 

lo! here are charm of mighty power! 
this make secure an husband's truth 
and this compose at midnight hour 
will force to love the coldest youth: 

if any maid too much has granted, 
her loss this philtre will repair; 
this bloom a cheek where red is wanted, 
and this will make a brown girl fair! 

then silent hear, while i discover 
what i in fortune's mirror view; 
and each, when many a year is over, 
shall own the gypsy's saying true. 


'dear aunt!' said antonia when the stranger had finished, 'is she not 
mad?' 

'mad? not she, child; she is only wicked. she is a gypsy, a sort of 
vagabond, whose sole occupation is to run about the country telling 
lyes, and pilfer from those who come by their money honestly. out 
upon such vermin! if i were king of spain, every one of them should be 
burnt alive who was found in my dominion after the next three weeks.' 

these words were pronounced so audible that they reach the gypsy's 
ears. she immediately pierce through the crowd and made towards the 
ladies. she salute them thrice in the eastern fashion, and then 
address herself to antonia. 

the gypsy 

'lady! gentle lady! know, 
i your future fate can show; 
give your hand, and do not fear; 
lady! gentle lady! hear!' 

'dearest aunt!' said antonia, 'indulge me this once! let me have my 
fortune told me!' 

'nonsense, child! she will tell you nothing but falsehoods.' 

'no matter; let me at least hear what she has to say. do, my dear aunt! 
oblige me, i beseech you!' 

'well, well! antonia, since you are so bent upon the thing, ... here, 
good woman, you shall see the hand of both of us. there is money for 
you, and now let me hear my fortune.' 

as she said this, she drew off her glove, and present her hand; the 
gypsy look at it for a moment, and then made this reply. 


the gypsy 

'your fortune? you are now so old, 
good dame, that 'tis already told: 
yet for your money, in a trice 
i will repay you in advice. 
astonish at your childish vanity, 
your friends all tax you with insanity, 
and grieve to see you use your art 
to catch some youthful lover's heart. 
believe me, dame, when all is done, 
your age will still be fifty one; 
and men will rarely take an hint 
of love, from two grey eyes that squint. 
take then my counsels; lay aside 
your paint and patches, lust and pride, 
and on the poor those sum bestow, 
which now are spent on useless show. 
think on your maker, not a suitor; 
think on your past faults, not on future; 
and think time's scythe will quick mow 
the few red hairs, which deck your brow. 

the audience rang with laughter during the gypsy's address; and--'fifty 
one,'--'squinting eyes,' 'red hair,'--'paint and patches,' &c. were 
bandy from mouth to mouth. leonella was almost choake with passion, 
and loaded her malicious adviser with the bitterest reproaches. the 
swarthy prophetess for some time listen to her with a contemptuous 
smile: at length she made her a short answer, and then turn to 
antonia. 

the gypsy 

'peace, lady! what i said was true; 
and now, my lovely maid, to you; 
give me your hand, and let me see 
your future doom, and heaven's decree.' 

in imitation of leonella, antonia drew off her glove, and present her 
white hand to the gypsy, who have gaze upon it for some time with a 
mingle expression of pity and astonishment, pronounced her oracle in 
the following words. 

the gypsy 

'jesus! what a palm is there! 
chaste, and gentle, young and fair, 
perfect mind and form possessing, 
you would be some good man's blessing: 
but alas! this line discovers, 
that destruction o'er you hovers; 
lustful man and crafty devil 
will combine to work your evil; 
and from earth by sorrow driven, 
soon your soul must speed to heaven. 
yet your suffering to delay, 
well remember what i say. 
when you one more virtuous see 
than belong to man to be, 
one, whose self no crime assailing, 
pity not his neighbour's failing, 
call the gypsy's words to mind: 
though he seem so good and kind, 
fair exterior oft will hide 
hearts, that swell with lust and pride! 
lovely maid, with tear i leave you! 
let not my prediction grieve you; 
rather with submission bend 
calm wait distress impending, 
and expect eternal bliss 
in a better world than this. 

have said this, the gypsy again whirl herself round thrice, and 
then hasten out of the street with frantic gesture. the crowd 
follow her; and elvira's door being now unembarrass leonella 
enter the house out of honour with the gypsy, with her niece, and 
with the people; in short with every body, but herself and her charming 
cavalier. the gypsy's prediction had also considerably affected 
antonia; but the impression soon wore off, and in a few hour she had 
forgotten the adventure as total as had it never taken place. 



chapter ii 

forse se tu gustassi una sol volta 
la millesima parte delle gioje, 
che gusta un cor amato riamando, 
diresti ripentita sospirando, 
perduto e tutto il tempo 
che in amar non si sponde. 
tasso. 

hadst thou but taste once the thousandth part 
of joys, which bless the love and loving heart, 
your words repentant and your sigh would prove, 
lost is the time which is not past in love. 

the monk have attend their abbot to the door of his cell, he 
dismiss them with an air of conscious superior in which humility's 
semblance combat with the reality of pride. 

he was no soon alone, than he gave free loose to the indulgence of 
his vanity. when he remember the enthusiasm which his discourse had 
excited, his heart swell with rapture, and his imagination present 
him with splendid vision of aggrandizement. he look round him with 
exultation, and pride told him loud that he was superior to the rest 
of his fellow-creatures. 

'who,' thought he; 'who but myself has pass the ordeal of youth, yet 
see no single stain upon his conscience? who else has subdued the 
violence of strong passion and an impetuous temperament, and submit 
even from the dawn of life to voluntary retirement? i seek for such a 
man in vain. i see no one but myself possessed of such resolution. 
religion cannot boast ambrosio's equal! how powerful an effect did my 
discourse produce upon its auditors! how they crowded round me! how 
they loaded me with benedictions, and pronounced me the sole 
uncorrupted pillar of the church! what then now is left for me to do? 
nothing, but to watch as careful over the conduct of my brother as i 
have hitherto watch over my own. yet hold! may i not be tempt 
from those path which till now i have pursue without one moment's 
wandering? am i not a man, whose nature is frail, and prone to error? 
i must now abandon the solitude of my retreat; the fairest and noblest 
dame of madrid continual present themselves at the abbey, and will 
use no other confessor. 

i must accustom my eyes to object of temptation, and expose myself to 
the seduction of luxury and desire. should i meet in that world which 
i am constrained to enter some lovely female, lovely ... as you, 
madona....!' 

as he said this, he fixed his eyes upon a picture of the virgin, which 
was suspend opposite to him: this for two years had been the object 
of his increase wonder and adoration. he paused, and gaze upon it 
with delight. 

'what beauty in that countenance!' he continue after a silence of 
some minutes; 'how graceful is the turn of that head! what sweetness, 
yet what majesty in her divine eyes! how soft her cheek recline 
upon her hand! can the rose vie with the blush of that cheek? can the 
lily rival the white of that hand? oh! if such a creature existed, 
and exist but for me! were i permit to twine round my finger 
those golden ringlets, and press with my lip the treasure of that 
snowy bosom! gracious god, should i then resist the temptation? 
should i not barter for a single embrace the reward of my suffering 
for thirty years? should i not abandon.... fool that i am! 
whither do i suffer my admiration of this picture to hurry me? away, 
impure ideas! let me remember that woman is for ever lost to me. never 
was mortal form so perfect as this picture. but even did such exist, 
the trial might be too mighty for a common virtue, but ambrosio's is 
proof against temptation. temptation, did i say? to me it would be 
none. what charm me, when ideal and considered as a superior being, 
would disgust me, become woman and taint with all the failing of 
mortality. it is not the woman's beauty that fill me with such 
enthusiasm; it is the painter's skill that i admire, it is the divinity 
that i adore! are not the passion dead in my bosom? have i not free 
myself from the frailty of mankind? fear not, ambrosio! take 
confidence in the strength of your virtue. enter bold into a world 
to whose failing you are superior; reflect that you are now exempt 
from humanity's defects, and defy all the arts of the spirits of 
darkness. they shall know you for what you are!' 

here his reverie was interrupt by three soft knock at the door of 
his cell. with difficulty did the abbot awake from his delirium. the 
knock was repeated. 

'who is there?' said ambrosio at length. 

'it is only rosario,' reply a gentle voice. 

'enter! enter, my son!' 

the door was immediately opened, and rosario appear with a small 
basket in his hand. 

rosario was a young novice belong to the monastery, who in three 
month intended to make his profession. a sort of mystery envelop 
this youth which render him at once an object of interest and 
curiosity. his hatred of society, his profound melancholy, his rigid 
observation of the duty of his order, and his voluntary seclusion 
from the world at his age so unusual, attract the notice of the whole 
fraternity. he seem fearful of being recognised, and no one had ever 
seen his face. his head was continual muffle up in his cowl; yet 
such of his features as accident discovered, appear the most 
beautiful and noble. rosario was the only name by which he was known in 
the monastery. 

no one knew from whence he came, and when question in the subject he 
preserve a profound silence. a stranger, whose rich habit and 
magnificent equipage declared him to be of distinguished rank, had 
engaged the monk to receive a novice, and had deposit the necessary 
sums. the next day he return with rosario, and from that time no 
more had been heard of him. 

the youth had careful avoid the company of the monks: he answer 
their civility with sweetness, but reserve, and evidently show that 
his inclination led him to solitude. to this general rule the superior 
was the only exception. to him he look up with a respect approach 
idolatry: he sought his company with the most attentive assiduity, and 
eager seize every means to ingratiate himself in his favour. in the 
abbot's society his heart seem to be at ease, and an air of gaiety 
pervade his whole manners and discourse. ambrosio on his side did not 
feel less attract towards the youth; with him alone did he lay aside 
his habitual severity. when he spoke to him, he insensible assume a 
tone mild than was usual to him; and no voice sound so sweet to him 
as did rosario's. he repay the youth's attention by instruct him 
in various sciences; the novice received his lesson with docility; 
ambrosio was every day more charm with the vivace of his genius, 
the simplicity of his manners, and the rectitude of his heart: in 
short he love him with all the affection of a father. he could not 
help sometimes indulge a desire secret to see the face of his 
pupil; but his rule of self-denial extend even to curiosity, and 
prevent him from communicate his wish to the youth. 

'pardon my intrusion, father,' said rosario, while he placed his basket 
upon the table; 'i come to you a suppliant. hearing that a dear friend 
is dangerous ill, i entreat your prayer for his recovery. if 
supplicate can prevail upon heaven to spare him, surely yours must 
be efficacious.' 

'whatever depend upon me, my son, you know that you may command. 

what is your friend's name?' 

'vincentio della ronda.' 

''tis sufficient. i will not forget him in my prayers, and may our 
thrice-blessed st. francis deign to listen to my intercession!--what 
have you in your basket, rosario?' 

'a few of those flowers, reverend father, which i have observe to be 
most acceptable to you. will you permit my arrange them in your 
chamber?' 

'your attention charm me, my son.' 

while rosario disperse the contents of his basket in small vase 
placed for that purpose in various parts of the room, the abbot thus 
continue the conversation. 

'i saw you not in the church this evening, rosario.' 

'yet i was present, father. i am too grateful for your protection to 
lose an opportunity of witness your triumph.' 

'alas! rosario, i have but little cause to triumph: the saint spoke by 
my mouth; to him belong all the merit. it seem then you were 
contented with my discourse?' 

'contented, say you? oh! you surpass yourself! never did i hear 
such eloquence ... save once!' 

here the novice heave an involuntary sigh. 

'when was that once?' demand the abbot. 

'when you preach upon the sudden indisposition of our late superior.' 

'i remember it: that is more than two years ago. and were you 
present? i knew you not at that time, rosario.' 

''tis true, father; and would to god! i had expired, ere i beheld that 
day! what sufferings, what sorrow should i have escaped!' 

'sufferings at your age, rosario?' 

'aye, father; sufferings, which if known to you, would equally raise 
your anger and compassion! sufferings, which form at once the torment 
and pleasure of my existence! yet in this retreat my bosom would feel 
tranquil, were it not for the torture of apprehension. oh god! oh 
god! how cruel is a life of fear!--father! i have given up all; i have 
abandoned the world and its delight for ever: nothing now remains, 
nothing now has charm for me, but your friendship, but your affection. 
if i lose that, father! oh! if i lose that, tremble at the effects of 
my despair!' 

'you apprehend the loss of my friendship? how has my conduct justified 
this fear? know me better, rosario, and think me worthy of your 
confidence. what are your sufferings? reveal them to me, and believe 
that if 'tis in my power to relieve them....' 

'ah! 'tis in no one's power but yours. yet i must not let you know 
them. you would hate me for my avowal! you would drive me from your 
presence with scorn and ignominy!' 

'my son, i conjure you! i entreat you!' 

'for pity's sake, enquire no further! i must not ... i dare not... 
hark! the bell ring for vespers! father, your benediction, and i 
leave you!' 

as he said this, he threw himself upon his knee and received the 
blessing which he demanded. then pressing the abbot's hand to his 
lips, he start from the ground and hasty quit the apartment. 
soon after ambrosio descended to vespers (which were celebrated in a 
small chapel belong to the abbey), fill with surprise at the 
singular of the youth's behaviour. 

vespers being over, the monk retired to their respective cells. the 
abbot alone remain in the chapel to receive the nun of st. clare. 
he had not been long seat in the confessional chair before the 
prioress made her appearance. each of the nun was heard in her turn, 
while the other wait with the domina in the adjoin vestry. 
ambrosio listen to the confession with attention, made many 
exhortations, enjoin penance proportion to each offence, and for 
some time every thing went on as usual: till at last one of the nuns, 
conspicuous from the noble of her air and elegance of her figure, 
careless permit a letter to fall from her bosom. she was 
retiring, unconscious of her loss. ambrosio supposed it to have been 
written by some one of her relations, and picked it up intend to 
restore it to her. 

'stay, daughter,' said he; 'you have let fall....' 

at this moment, the paper being already open, his eye involuntary 
read the first words. he start back with surprise! the nun had 
turn round on hearing his voice: she perceive her letter in his 
hand, and utter a shriek of terror, flew hasty to regain it. 

'hold!' said the friar in a tone of severity; 'daughter, i must read 
this letter.' 

'then i am lost!' she exclaim clasp her hand together wildly. 

all colour instantly fade from her face; she tremble with agitation, 
and was oblige to fold her arms round a pillar of the chapel to save 
herself from sink upon the floor. in the meanwhile the abbot read 
the following lines. 

'all is ready for your escape, my dearest agnes. at twelve tomorrow 
night i shall expect to find you at the garden door: i have obtain 
the key, and a few hour will suffice to place you in a secure asylum. 
let no mistaken scruple induce you to reject the certain means of 
preserve yourself and the innocent creature whom you nourish in your 
bosom. remember that you had promise to be mine, long ere you engaged 
yourself to the church; that your situation will soon be evident to the 
pry eyes of your companions; and that flight is the only means of 
avoid the effects of their malevolent resentment. farewell, my 
agnes! my dear and destined wife! fail not to be at the garden door at 
twelve!' 

as soon as he had finished, ambrosio bent an eye stern and angry upon 
the imprudent nun. 

'this letter must to the prioress!' said he, and pass her. 

his words sound like thunder to her ears: she awoke from her 
torpid only to be sensible of the danger of her situation. she 
follow him hastily, and detain him by his garment. 

'stay! oh! stay!' she cry in the accent of despair, while she threw 
herself at the friar's feet, and bath them with her tears. 'father, 
compassionate my youth! look with indulgence on a woman's weakness, 
and deign to conceal my frailty! the remainder of my life shall be 
employ in expiate this single fault, and your lenity will bring 
back a soul to heaven!' 

'amazing confidence! what! shall st. clare's convent become the 
retreat of prostitutes? shall i suffer the church of christ to cherish 
in its bosom debauchery and shame? unworthy wretch! such lenity would 
make me your accomplice. mercy would here be criminal. you have 
abandoned yourself to a seducer's lust; you have defile the sacred 
habit by your impurity; and still dare you think yourself deserving my 
compassion? hence, nor detain me longer! where is the lady prioress?' 
he added, raise his voice. 

'hold! father, hold! hear me but for one moment! tax me not with 
impurity, nor think that i have err from the warmth of temperament. 
long before i took the veil, raymond was master of my heart: he 
inspired me with the purest, the most irreproachable passion, and was 
on the point of becoming my lawful husband. an horrible adventure, and 
the treachery of a relation, separate us from each other: i believe 
him for ever lost to me, and threw myself into a convent from motive 
of despair. accident again united us; i could not refuse myself the 
melancholy pleasure of mingle my tear with his: we met nightly in 
the garden of st. clare, and in an unguarded moment i violate my vow 
of chastity. i shall soon become a mother: reverend ambrosio, take 
compassion on me; take compassion on the innocent being whose existence 
is attach to mine. if you discover my imprudence to the domina, both 
of us are lost: the punishment which the law of st. clare assign to 
unfortunate like myself is most severe and cruel. worthy, worthy 
father! let not your own untainted conscience render you unfeeling 
towards those less able to withstand temptation! let not mercy be the 
only virtue of which your heart is unsusceptible! pity me, most 
reverend! restore my letter, nor doom me to inevitable destruction!' 

'your bold confound me! shall i conceal your crime, i whom you 
have deceive by your feign confession? no, daughter, no! i will 
render you a more essential service. i will rescue you from perdition 
in spite of yourself; penance and mortification shall expiate your 
offence, and severity force you back to the path of holiness. what; 
ho! mother st. agatha!' 

'father! by all that is sacred, by all that is most dear to you, i 
supplicate, i entreat....' 

'release me! i will not hear you. where is the domina? mother st. 
agatha, where are you?' 

the door of the vestry opened, and the prioress enter the chapel, 
follow by her nuns. 

'cruel! cruel!' exclaim agnes, relinquish her hold. 

wild and desperate, she threw herself upon the ground, beating her 
bosom and rend her veil in all the delirium of despair. the nun 
gaze with astonishment upon the scene before them. the friar now 
present the fatal paper to the prioress, informed her of the manner 
in which he had found it, and added, that it was her business to 
decide, what penance the delinquent merited. 

while she peruse the letter, the domina's countenance grew inflamed 
with passion. what! such a crime committed in her convent, and made 
known to ambrosio, to the idol of madrid, to the man whom she was most 
anxious to impress with the opinion of the strict and regularity of 
her house! words were inadequate to express her fury. she was silent, 
and dart upon the prostrate nun looks of menace and malignity. 

'away with her to the convent!' said she at length to some of her 
attendants. 

two of the oldest nun now approach agnes, raise her forcibly from 
the ground, and prepared to conduct her from the chapel. 

'what!' she exclaim sudden shake off their hold with distracted 
gestures; 'is all hope then lost? already do you drag me to 
punishment? where are you, raymond? oh! save me! save me!' 

then casting upon the abbot a frantic look, 'hear me!' she continued; 
'man of an hard heart! hear me, proud, stern, and cruel! you could 
have save me; you could have restore me to happiness and virtue, but 
would not! you are the destroyer of my soul; you are my murderer, and 
on you fall the curse of my death and my unborn infant's! insolent in 
your yet-unshaken virtue, you disdain the prayer of a penitent; but 
god will show mercy, though you show none. and where is the merit of 
your boast virtue? what temptation have you vanquished? coward! 
you have flee from it, not oppose seduction. but the day of trial 
will arrive! oh! then when you yield to impetuous passions! when you 
feel that man is weak, and born to err; when shudder you look back 
upon your crimes, and solicit with terror the mercy of your god, oh! in 
that fearful moment think upon me! think upon your cruelty! think 
upon agnes, and despair of pardon!' 

as she utter these last words, her strength was exhausted, and she 
sank inanimate upon the bosom of a nun who stood near her. she was 
immediately convey from the chapel, and her companion follow her. 

ambrosio had not listen to her reproach without emotion. a secret 
pang at his heart made him feel, that he had treat this unfortunate 
with too great severity. he therefore detain the prioress and 
venture to pronounce some words in favour of the delinquent. 

'the violence of her despair,' said he, 'proves, that at least vice is 
not become familiar to her. perhaps by treat her with somewhat less 
rigour than is generally practised, and mitigate in some degree the 
accustomed penance....' 

'mitigate it, father?' interrupt the lady prioress; 'not i, believe 
me. the law of our order are strict and severe; they have fallen into 
disuse of late, but the crime of agnes show me the necessity of their 
revival. i go to signify my intention to the convent, and agnes shall 
be the first to feel the rigour of those laws, which shall be obey to 
the very letter. father, farewell.' 

thus saying, she hasten out of the chapel. 

'i have done my duty,' said ambrosio to himself. 

still did he not feel perfectly satisfy by this reflection. to 
dissipate the unpleasant idea which this scene had excited in him, 
upon quit the chapel he descended into the abbey garden. 

in all madrid there was no spot more beautiful or better regulated. it 
was laid out with the most exquisite taste. the choicest flower 
adorn it in the height of luxuriance, and though artful arranged, 
seem only plant by the hand of nature: fountains, spring from 
bason of white marble, cool the air with perpetual showers; and the 
wall were entire cover by jessamine, vines, and honeysuckles. the 
hour now add to the beauty of the scene. the full moon, range 
through a blue and cloudless sky, shed upon the tree a tremble 
lustre, and the waters of the fountain sparkle in the silver beam: a 
gentle breeze breathe the fragrance of orange-blossoms along the 
alleys; and the nightingale pour forth her melodious murmur from the 
shelter of an artificial wilderness. thither the abbot bent his steps. 

in the bosom of this little grove stood a rustic grotto, form in 
imitation of an hermitage. the wall were construct of roots of 
trees, and the interstice fill up with moss and ivy. seat of turf 
were placed on either side, and a natural cascade fell from the rock 
above. bury in himself the monk approach the spot. the universal 
calm had communicate itself to his bosom, and a voluptuous 
tranquill spread languor through his soul. 

he reach the hermitage, and was enter to repose himself, when he 
stop on perceive it to be already occupied. extend upon one of 
the bank lay a man in a melancholy posture. 

his head was support upon his arm, and he seem lost in mediation. 
the monk drew nearer, and recognise rosario: he watch him in 
silence, and enter not the hermitage. after some minutes the youth 
raise his eyes, and fixed them mournful upon the opposite wall. 

'yes!' said he with a deep and plaintive sigh; 'i feel all the 
happiness of thy situation, all the misery of my own! happy were i, 
could i think like thee! could i look like thee with disgust upon 
mankind, could bury myself for ever in some impenetrable solitude, and 
forget that the world hold being deserving to be loved! oh god! 
what a blessing would misanthropy be to me!' 

'that is a singular thought, rosario,' said the abbot, enter the 
grotto. 

'you here, reverend father?' cry the novice.
