at the same time start from his place in confusion, he drew his cowl 
hasty over his face. ambrosio seat himself upon the bank, and 
oblige the youth to place himself by him. 

'you must not indulge this disposition to melancholy,' said he; 'what 
can possibly have made you view in so desirable a light, misanthropy, 
of all sentiment the most hateful?' 

'the peruse of these verses, father, which till now had escape my 
observation. the bright of the moonbeam permit my reading 
them; and oh! how i envy the feelings of the writer!' 

as he said this, he pointed to a marble tablet fixed against the 
opposite wall: on it were engrave the following lines. 

inscription in an hermitage 

who-e'er thou art these lines now reading, 
think not, though from the world recede 
i joy my lonely days to lead in 
this desart drear, 
that with remorse a conscience bleeding 
hath led me here. 

no thought of guilt my bosom sowrs: 
free-willed i flee from courtly bowers; 
for well i saw in hall and tower 
that lust and pride, 
the arch-fiend's dearest darkest powers, 
in state preside. 

i saw mankind with vice incrusted; 
i saw that honour's sword was rusted; 
that few for aught but folly lusted; 
that he was still deceiv'd, who trust 
in love or friend; 
and hither came with men disgust 
my life to end. 

in this lone cave, in garment lowly, 
alike a foe to noisy folly, 
and brow-bent gloomy melancholy 
i wear away 
my life, and in my office holy 
consume the day. 

content and comfort bless me more in 
this grot, than e'er i felt before in 
a palace, and with thought still soar 
to god on high, 
each night and morn with voice implore 
this wish i sigh. 

'let me, oh! lord! from life retire, 
unknown each guilty worldly fire, 
remorseful throb, or loose desire; 
and when i die, 
let me in this belief expire, 
"to god i fly"!' 

stranger, if full of youth and riot 
as yet no grief has mar thy quiet, 
thou haply throw'st a scornful eye at 
the hermit's prayer: 
but if thou hast a cause to sigh at 
thy fault, or care; 

if thou hast known false love's vexation, 
or hast been exil'd from thy nation, 
or guilt affright thy contemplation, 
and make thee pine, 
oh! how must thou lament thy station, 
and envy mine! 

'were it possible' said the friar, 'for man to be so total wrap up 
in himself as to live in absolute seclusion from human nature, and 
could yet feel the contented tranquill which these lines express, i 
allow that the situation would be more desirable, than to live in a 
world so pregnant with every vice and every folly. but this never can 
be the case. this inscription was merely placed here for the ornament 
of the grotto, and the sentiment and the hermit are equally imaginary. 
man was born for society. however little he may be attach to the 
world, he never can wholly forget it, or bear to be wholly forgotten by 
it. disgust at the guilt or absurd of mankind, the misanthrope 
flies from it: he resolve to become an hermit, and bury himself in 
the cavern of some gloomy rock. while hate inflame his bosom, 
possibly he may feel contented with his situation: but when his 
passion begin to cool; when time has mellow his sorrows, and heal 
those wound which he bore with him to his solitude, think you that 
content become his companion? ah! no, rosario. no long sustain 
by the violence of his passions, he feel all the monotony of his way 
of living, and his heart become the prey of ennui and weariness. he 
looks round, and find himself alone in the universe: the love of 
society revive in his bosom, and he pants to return to that world 
which he has abandoned. nature lose all her charm in his eyes: no 
one is near him to point out her beauties, or share in his admiration 
of her excellence and variety. prop upon the fragment of some rock, 
he gaze upon the tumble waterfall with a vacant eye, he view 
without emotion the glory of the setting sun. slow he returns to his 
cell at evening, for no one there is anxious for his arrival; he has no 
comfort in his solitary unsavoury meal: he throw himself upon his 
couch of moss despondent and dissatisfied, and wake only to pass a day 
as joyless, as monotonous as the former.' 

'you amaze me, father! suppose that circumstances condemn you to 
solitude; would not the duty of religion and the consciousness of a 
life well spent communicate to your heart that calm which....' 

'i should deceive myself, did i fancy that they could. i am convinced 
of the contrary, and that all my fortitude would not prevent me from 
yielding to melancholy and disgust. after consume the day in study, 
if you knew my pleasure at meeting my brethren in the evening! after 
passing many a long hour in solitude, if i could express to you the joy 
which i feel at once more behold a fellow-creature! 'tis in this 
particular that i place the principal merit of a monastic institution. 
it seclude man from the temptation of vice; it procure that leisure 
necessary for the proper service of the supreme; it spare him the 
mortification of witness the crime of the worldly, and yet permit 
him to enjoy the blessing of society. and do you, rosario, do you 
envy an hermit's life? can you be thus blind to the happiness of your 
situation? reflect upon it for a moment. this abbey is become your 
asylum: your regularity, your gentleness, your talent have render 
you the object of universal esteem: you are secluded from the world 
which you profess to hate; yet you remain in possession of the benefit 
of society, and that a society compose of the most estimable of 
mankind.' 

'father! father! 'tis that which cause my torment! happy had it been 
for me, had my life been pass among the vicious and abandoned! had i 
never heard pronounced the name of virtue! 'tis my unbounded adoration 
of religion; 'tis my soul's exquisite sensibility of the beauty of fair 
and good, that load me with shame! that hurry me to perdition! oh! 
that i had never seen these abbey walls!' 

'how, rosario? when we last conversed, you spoke in a different tone. 
is my friendship then become of such little consequence? had you never 
seen these abbey walls, you never had seen me: can that really be your 
wish?' 

'had never seen you?' repeated the novice, start from the bank, and 
grasping the friar's hand with a frantic air; 'you? you? would to god, 
that lightning had blasted them, before you ever met my eyes! would to 
god! that i were never to see you more, and could forget that i had 
ever seen you!' 

with these words he flew hasty from the grotto. ambrosio remain in 
his former attitude, reflect on the youth's unaccountable behaviour. 
he was inclined to suspect the derange of his senses: yet the 
general tenor of his conduct, the connexion of his ideas, and calm 
of his demeanour till the moment of his quit the grotto, seem to 
discountenance this conjecture. after a few minutes rosario returned. 
he again seat himself upon the bank: he recline his cheek upon one 
hand, and with the other wipe away the tear which trickle from his 
eyes at intervals. 

the monk look upon him with compassion, and forbore to interrupt his 
meditations. both observe for some time a profound silence. the 
nightingale had now taken her station upon an orange tree front the 
hermitage, and pour forth a strain the most melancholy and melodious. 
rosario raise his head, and listen to her with attention. 

'it was thus,' said he, with a deep-drawn sigh; 'it was thus, that 
during the last month of her unhappy life, my sister use to sit 
listen to the nightingale. poor matilda! she sleep in the grave, 
and her broken heart throb no more with passion.' 

'you had a sister?' 

'you say right, that i had; alas! i have one no longer. she sunk 
beneath the weight of her sorrow in the very spring of life.' 

'what were those sorrows?' 

'they will not excite your pity: you know not the power of those 
irresistible, those fatal sentiments, to which her heart was a prey. 
father, she love unfortunately. a passion for one endow with every 
virtue, for a man, oh! rather let me say, for a divinity, prove the 
bane of her existence. his noble form, his spotless character, his 
various talents, his wisdom solid, wonderful, and glorious, might have 
warm the bosom of the most insensible. my sister saw him, and dare 
to love though she never dare to hope.' 

'if her love was so well bestowed, what forbad her to hope the 
obtain of its object?' 

'father, before he knew her, julian had already plight his vow to a 
bride most fair, most heavenly! yet still my sister loved, and for the 
husband's sake she dote upon the wife. one morning she found means to 
escape from our father's house: array in humble weeds she offer 
herself as a domestic to the consort of her beloved, and was accepted. 
she was now continual in his presence: she strove to ingratiate 
herself into his favour: she succeeded. her attention attract 
julian's notice; the virtuous are ever grateful, and he distinguished 
matilda above the rest of her companions.' 

'and did not your parent seek for her? did they submit tame to 
their loss, nor attempt to recover their wandering daughter?' 

'ere they could find her, she discover herself. her love grew too 
violent for concealment; yet she wish not for julian's person, she 
ambition but a share of his heart. in an unguarded moment she 
confessed her affection. what was the return? doate upon his wife, 
and believe that a look of pity bestow upon another was a theft 
from what he owe to her, he drove matilda from his presence. he 
forbad her ever again appear before him. his severity broke her 
heart: she return to her father's, and in a few month after was 
carry to her grave.' 

'unhappy girl! surely her fate was too severe, and julian was too 
cruel.' 

'do you think so, father?' cry the novice with vivacity; 'do you 
think that he was cruel?' 

'doubtless i do, and pity her most sincerely.' 

'you pity her? you pity her? oh! father! father! then pity me!' 

the friar started; when after a moment's pause rosario add with a 
falter voice,--'for my suffering are still greater. my sister had 
a friend, a real friend, who pity the acute of her feelings, nor 
reproach her with her inability to repress them. i ...! i have no 
friend! the whole wide world cannot furnish an heart that is willing 
to participate in the sorrow of mine!' 

as he utter these words, he sob audibly. the friar was affected. 
he took rosario's hand, and pressed it with tenderness. 

'you have no friend, say you? what then am i? why will you not 
confide in me, and what can you fear? my severity? have i ever use 
it with you? the dignity of my habit? rosario, i lay aside the monk, 
and bid you consider me as no other than your friend, your father. 
well may i assume that title, for never did parent watch over a child 
more fondly than i have watch over you. from the moment in which i 
first beheld you, i perceive sensation in my bosom till then unknown 
to me; i found a delight in your society which no one's else could 
afford; and when i witness the extent of your genius and information, 
i rejoice as do a father in the perfection of his son. then lay 
aside your fears; speak to me with openness: speak to me, rosario, and 
say that you will confide in me. if my aid or my pity can alleviate 
your distress....' 

'yours can! yours only can! ah! father, how willing would i unveil 
to you my heart! how willing would i declare the secret which bow 
me down with its weight! but oh! i fear! i fear!' 

'what, my son?' 

'that you should abhor me for my weakness; that the reward of my 
confidence should be the loss of your esteem.' 

'how shall i reassure you? reflect upon the whole of my past conduct, 
upon the paternal tender which i have ever shown you. abhor you, 
rosario? it is no long in my power. to give up your society would 
be to deprive myself of the greatest pleasure of my life. then reveal 
to me what afflict you, and believe me while i solemn swear....' 

'hold!' interrupt the novice; 'swear, that whatever be my secret, you 
will not oblige me to quit the monastery till my noviciate shall 
expire.' 

'i promise it faithfully, and as i keep my vow to you, may christ keep 
his to mankind. now then explain this mystery, and rely upon my 
indulgence.' 

'i obey you. know then.... oh! how i tremble to name the word! 
listen to me with pity, revere ambrosio! call up every latent spark 
of human weakness that may teach you compassion for mine! father!' 
continue he throw himself at the friar's feet, and pressing his 
hand to his lip with eagerness, while agitation for a moment choake 
his voice; 'father!' continue he in falter accents, 'i am a woman!' 

the abbot start at this unexpected avowal. prostrate on the ground 
lay the feign rosario, as if wait in silence the decision of his 
judge. astonishment on the one part, apprehension on the other, for 
some minutes chain them in the same attitudes, as had they been 
touched by the rod of some magician. at length recover from his 
confusion, the monk quit the grotto, and sped with precipitation 
towards the abbey. his action did not escape the suppliant. she 
sprang from the ground; she hasten to follow him, overtook him, threw 
herself in his passage, and embrace his knees. ambrosio strove in 
vain to disengage himself from her grasp. 

'do not fly me!' she cried; 'leave me not abandoned to the impulse of 
despair! listen, while i excuse my imprudence; while i acknowledge my 
sister's story to be my own! i am matilda; you are her beloved.' 

if ambrosio's surprise was great at her first avowal, upon hearing her 
second it exceed all bounds. amazed, embarrassed, and irresolute he 
found himself incapable of pronounce a syllable, and remain in 
silence gaze upon matilda: this gave her opportunity to continue her 
explanation as follows. 

'think not, ambrosio, that i come to rob your bride of your affections. 
no, believe me: religion alone deserve you; and far is it from 
matilda's wish to draw you from the path of virtue. what i feel for 
you is love, not licentiousness; i sigh to be possessor of your heart, 
not lust for the enjoyment of your person. deign to listen to my 
vindication: a few moments will convince you that this holy retreat is 
not pollute by my presence, and that you may grant me your compassion 
without trespass against your vows.'--she seat herself: ambrosio, 
scarcely conscious of what he did, follow her example, and she 
proceed in her discourse. 

'i spring from a distinguished family: my father was chief of the 
noble house of villanegas. he die while i was still an infant, and 
left me sole heiress of his immense possessions. young and wealthy, i 
was sought in marriage by the noblest youth of madrid; but no one 
succeed in gain my affections. i had been brought up under the 
care of an uncle possessed of the most solid judgment and extensive 
erudition. he took pleasure in communicate to me some portion of his 
knowledge. under his instruction my understanding acquire more 
strength and just than generally falls to the lot of my sex: the 
ability of my preceptor being aid by natural curiosity, i not only 
made a considerable progress in science universally studied, but in 
others, reveal but to few, and lie under censure from the blind 
of superstition. but while my guardian labour to enlarge the sphere 
of my knowledge, he careful inculcate every moral precept: he 
relieved me from the shackle of vulgar prejudice; he pointed out the 
beauty of religion; he taught me to look with adoration upon the pure 
and virtuous, and, woe is me! i have obey him but too well! 

'with such dispositions, judge whether i could observe with any other 
sentiment than disgust the vice, dissipation, and ignorance, which 
disgrace our spain youth. i reject every offer with disdain. my 
heart remain without a master till chance conduct me to the 
cathedral of the capuchins. oh! surely on that day my guardian angel 
slumber neglectful of his charge! then was it that i first beheld 
you: you supply the superior's place, absent from illness. you 
cannot but remember the lively enthusiasm which your discourse created. 
oh! how i drank your words! how your eloquence seem to steal me from 
myself! i scarcely dare to breathe, fear to lose a syllable; and 
while you spoke, methought a radiant glory beam round your head, and 
your countenance shone with the majesty of a god. i retired from the 
church, glowing with admiration. from that moment you became the idol 
of my heart, the never-changing object of my meditations. i enquire 
respecting you. the report which were made me of your mode of life, 
of your knowledge, piety, and self-denial rivet the chain impose on 
me by your eloquence. i was conscious that there was no long a void 
in my heart; that i had found the man whom i had sought till then in 
vain. in expectation of hearing you again, every day i visit your 
cathedral: you remain secluded within the abbey walls, and i always 
withdrew, wretched and disappointed. the night was more propitious to 
me, for then you stood before me in my dreams; you vow to me eternal 
friendship; you led me through the path of virtue, and assist me to 
support the vexation of life. the morning dispel these pleasing 
visions; i woke, and found myself separate from you by barrier which 
appear insurmountable. time seem only to increase the strength of 
my passion: i grew melancholy and despondent; i flee from society, and 
my health decline daily. at length no long able to exist in this 
state of torture, i resolve to assume the disguise in which you see 
me. my artifice was fortunate: i was received into the monastery, and 
succeed in gain your esteem. 

'now then i should have felt compleat happy, had not my quiet been 
disturbed by the fear of detection. the pleasure which i received from 
your society, was embitter by the idea that perhaps i should soon be 
deprived of it: and my heart throb so rapturous at obtain the 
mark of your friendship, as to convince me that i never should survive 
its loss. i resolved, therefore, not to leave the discovery of my sex 
to chance, to confess the whole to you, and throw myself entire on 
your mercy and indulgence. ah! ambrosio, can i have been deceived? 
can you be less generous than i thought you? i will not suspect it. 
you will not drive a wretch to despair; i shall still be permit to 
see you, to converse with you, to adore you! your virtue shall be my 
example through life; and when we expire, our body shall rest in the 
same grave.' 

she ceased. while she spoke, a thousand oppose sentiment combat 
in ambrosio's bosom. surprise at the singular of this adventure, 
confusion at her abrupt declaration, resentment at her bold in 
enter the monastery, and consciousness of the austerity with which 
it behove him to reply, such were the sentiment of which he was 
aware; but there were other also which did not obtain his notice. he 
perceive not, that his vanity was flatter by the praises bestow 
upon his eloquence and virtue; that he felt a secret pleasure in 
reflect that a young and seemingly lovely woman had for his sake 
abandoned the world, and sacrifice every other passion to that which 
he had inspired: still less did he perceive that his heart throb 
with desire, while his hand was pressed gently by matilda's ivory 
fingers. 

by degree he recover from his confusion. his idea became less 
bewildered: he was immediately sensible of the extreme impropriety, 
should matilda be permit to remain in the abbey after this avowal of 
her sex. he assume an air of severity, and drew away his hand. 

'how, lady!' said he; 'can you really hope for my permission to remain 
amongst us? even were i to grant your request, what good could you 
derive from it? think you that i ever can reply to an affection, 
which...' 

'no, father, no! i expect not to inspire you with a love like mine. i 
only wish for the liberty to be near you, to pass some hour of the day 
in your society; to obtain your compassion, your friendship and esteem. 
surely my request is not unreasonable.' 

'but reflect, lady! reflect only for a moment on the impropriety of my 
harbour a woman in the abbey; and that too a woman, who confess 
that she love me. it must not be. the risque of your being 
discover is too great, and i will not expose myself to so dangerous a 
temptation.' 

'temptation, say you? forget that i am a woman, and it no long 
exists: consider me only as a friend, as an unfortunate, whose 
happiness, whose life depend upon your protection. fear not lest i 
should ever call to your remembrance that love the most impetuous, the 
most unbounded, has induce me to disguise my sex; or that instigate 
by desires, offensive to your vow and my own honour, i should 
endeavour to seduce you from the path of rectitude. no, ambrosio, 
learn to know me better. i love you for your virtues: lose them, and 
with them you lose my affections. i look upon you as a saint; prove to 
me that you are no more than man, and i quit you with disgust. is it 
then from me that you fear temptation? from me, in whom the world's 
dazzle pleasure create no other sentiment than contempt? from me, 
whose attachment is ground on your exemption from human frailty? oh! 
dismiss such injurious apprehensions! think noble of me, think noble 
of yourself. i am incapable of seduce you to error; and surely your 
virtue is establish on a basis too firm to be shaken by unwarranted 
desires. ambrosio, dearest ambrosio! drive me not from your presence; 
remember your promise, and authorize my stay!' 

'impossible, matilda; your interest command me to refuse your prayer, 
since i tremble for you, not for myself. after vanquish the 
impetuous ebullition of youth; after passing thirty years in 
mortification and penance, i might safe permit your stay, nor fear 
your inspire me with warm sentiment than pity. but to yourself, 
remain in the abbey can produce none but fatal consequences. you 
will misconstrue my every word and action; you will seize every 
circumstance with avidity, which encourage you to hope the return of 
your affection; insensible your passion will gain a superior over 
your reason; and far from these being repressed by my presence, every 
moment which we pass together, will only serve to irritate and excite 
them. believe me, unhappy woman! you possess my sincere compassion. i 
am convinced that you have hitherto act upon the purest motives; but 
though you are blind to the imprudence of your conduct, in me it would 
be culpable not to open your eyes. i feel that duty oblige my 
treat you with harshness: i must reject your prayer, and remove 
every shadow of hope which may aid to nourish sentiment so pernicious 
to your repose. matilda, you must from hence tomorrow.' 

'tomorrow, ambrosio? tomorrow? oh! surely you cannot mean it! 

you cannot resolve on driving me to despair! you cannot have the 
cruelty....' 

'you have heard my decision, and it must be obeyed. the law of our 
order forbid your stay: it would be perjury to conceal that a woman is 
within these walls, and my vow will oblige me to declare your story to 
the community. you must from hence!--i pity you, but can do no more!' 

he pronounced these words in a faint and tremble voice: then rising 
from his seat, he would have hasten towards the monastery. utter 
a loud shriek, matilda followed, and detain him. 

'stay yet one moment, ambrosio! hear me yet speak one word!' 

'i dare not listen! release me! you know my resolution!' 

'but one word! but one last word, and i have done!' 

'leave me! your entreaty are in vain! you must from hence tomorrow!' 

'go then, barbarian! but this resource is still left me.' 

as she said this, she sudden drew a poignard: she rent open her 
garment, and placed the weapon's point against her bosom. 

'father, i will never quit these wall alive!' 

'hold! hold, matilda! what would you do?' 

'you are determined, so am i: the moment that you leave me, i plunge 
this steel in my heart.' 

'holy st. francis! matilda, have you your senses? do you know the 
consequence of your action? that suicide is the greatest of crimes? 
that you destroy your soul? that you lose your claim to salvation? 
that you prepare for yourself everlasting torments?' 

'i care not! i care not!' she reply passionately; 'either your hand 
guide me to paradise, or my own doom me to perdition! speak to me, 
ambrosio! tell me that you will conceal my story, that i shall remain 
your friend and your companion, or this poignard drink my blood!' 

as she utter these last words, she lift her arm, and made a motion 
as if to stab herself. the friar's eyes follow with dread the course 
of the dagger. she had torn open her habit, and her bosom was half 
exposed. the weapon's point rest upon her left breast: and oh! that 
was such a breast! the moonbeam dart full upon it enable the monk 
to observe its dazzle whiteness. his eye dwelt with insatiable 
avid upon the beauteous orb. a sensation till then unknown fill 
his heart with a mixture of anxiety and delight: a rage fire shot 
through every limb; the blood boil in his veins, and a thousand wild 
wish bewilder his imagination. 

'hold!' he cry in an hurried faultere voice; 'i can resist no 
longer! stay, then, enchantress; stay for my destruction!' 

he said, and rush from the place, hasten towards the monastery: 
he regain his cell and threw himself upon his couch, distracted 
irresolute and confused. 

he found it impossible for some time to arrange his ideas. the scene 
in which he had been engaged had excited such a variety of sentiment 
in his bosom, that he was incapable of decide which was predominant. 
he was irresolute what conduct he ought to hold with the disturb of 
his repose. he was conscious that prudence, religion, and propriety 
necessitate his obliging her to quit the abbey: but on the other hand 
such powerful reason authorize her stay that he was but too much 
inclined to consent to her remaining. he could not avoid being 
flatter by matilda's declaration, and at reflect that he had 
unconscious vanquish an heart which had resist the attack of 
spain's noblest cavaliers: the manner in which he had gain her 
affection was also the most satisfactory to his vanity: he remember 
the many happy hour which he had pass in rosario's society, and 
dread that void in his heart which parting with him would occasion. 
besides all this, he considered, that as matilda was wealthy, her 
favour might be of essential benefit to the abbey. 

'and what do i risque,' said he to himself, 'by authorize her stay? 
may i not safe credit her assertions? will it not be easy for me to 
forget her sex, and still consider her as my friend and my disciple? 
surely her love is as pure as she describes. had it been the offspring 
of mere licentiousness, would she so long have conceal it in her own 
bosom? would she not have employ some means to procure its 
gratification? she has done quite the contrary: she strove to keep me 
in ignorance of her sex; and nothing but the fear of detection, and my 
instances, would have compel her to reveal the secret. she has 
observe the duty of religion not less strict than myself. she has 
made no attempt to rouze my slumber passions, nor has she ever 
converse with me till this night on the subject of love. had she been 
desirous to gain my affections, not my esteem, she would not have 
conceal from me her charm so carefully: at this very moment i have 
never seen her face: yet certainly that face must be lovely, and her 
person beautiful, to judge by her ... by what i have seen.' 

as this last idea pass through his imagination, a blush spread itself 
over his cheek. alarm at the sentiment which he was indulging, he 
betook himself to prayer; he start from his couch, knelt before the 
beautiful madona, and entreat her assistance in stifle such 
culpable emotions. he then return to his bed, and resigned himself 
to slumber. 

he awoke, heated and unrefreshed. during his sleep his inflamed 
imagination had present him with none but the most voluptuous 
objects. matilda stood before him in his dreams, and his eyes again 
dwelt upon her naked breast. she repeated her protestation of eternal 
love, threw her arms round his neck, and loaded him with kisses: he 
return them; he clasp her passionately to his bosom, and ... the 
vision was dissolved. sometimes his dream present the image of his 
favourite madona, and he fancy that he was kneel before her: as 
he offer up his vow to her, the eyes of the figure seem to beam on 
him with inexpressible sweetness. he pressed his lip to hers, and 
found them warm: the animate form start from the canvas, embrace 
him affectionately, and his senses were unable to support delight so 
exquisite. such were the scenes, on which his thought were employ 
while sleeping: his unsatisfy desire placed before him the most 
lustful and provoking images, and he riot in joy till then unknown 
to him. 

he start from his couch, fill with confusion at the remembrance of 
his dreams. scarcely was he less ashamed, when he reflect on his 
reason of the former night which induce him to authorize matilda's 
stay. the cloud was now dissipated which had obscure his judgment: he 
shudder when he beheld his argument blazon in their proper 
colours, and found that he had been a slave to flattery, to avarice, 
and self-love. if in one hour's conversation matilda had produce a 
change so remarkable in his sentiments, what had he not to dread from 
her remain in the abbey? become sensible of his danger, awaken 
from his dream of confidence, he resolve to insist on her depart 
without delay. he began to feel that he was not proof against 
temptation; and that however matilda might restrain herself within the 
bounds of modesty, he was unable to contend with those passions, from 
which he false thought himself exempted. 

'agnes! agnes!' he exclaimed, while reflect on his embarrassments, 
'i already feel thy curse!' 

he quit his cell, determined upon dismiss the feign rosario. 
he appear at matins; but his thought were absent, and he paid them 
but little attention. his heart and brain were both of them fill 
with worldly objects, and he pray without devotion. the service over, 
he descended into the garden. he bent his steps towards the same spot 
where, on the preceding night, he had made this embarrass discovery. 
he doubt not but that matilda would seek him there: he was not 
deceived. she soon enter the hermitage, and approach the monk with 
a timid air. after a few minutes during which both were silent, she 
appear as if on the point of speaking; but the abbot, who during this 
time had been summon up all his resolution, hasty interrupt her. 
though still unconscious how extensive was its influence, he dread 
the melodious seduction of her voice. 

'seat yourself by my side, matilda,' said he, assume a look of 
firmness, though careful avoid the least mixture of severity; 
'listen to me patiently, and believe, that in what i shall say, i am 
not more influence by my own interest than by yours: believe, that i 
feel for you the warmest friendship, the truest compassion, and that 
you cannot feel more grieve than i do, when i declare to you that we 
must never meet again.' 

'ambrosio!' she cried, in a voice at once expressive of surprise and 
sorrow. 

'be calm, my friend! my rosario! still let me call you by that name 
so dear to me! our separation is unavoidable; i blush to own, how 
sensible it affect me.-- but yet it must be so. i feel myself 
incapable of treat you with indifference, and that very conviction 
oblige me to insist upon your departure. matilda, you must stay here 
no longer.' 

'oh! where shall i now seek for probity? disgust with a perfidious 
world, in what happy region do truth conceal herself? father, i 
hope that she reside here; i thought that your bosom had been her 
favourite shrine. and you too prove false? oh god! and you too can 
betray me?' 

'matilda!' 

'yes, father, yes! 'tis with justice that i reproach you. oh! where 
are your promises? my noviciate is not expired, and yet will you 
compell me to quit the monastery? can you have the heart to drive me 
from you? and have i not received your solemn oath to the contrary?' 

'i will not compell you to quit the monastery: you have received my 
solemn oath to the contrary. but yet when i throw myself upon your 
generosity, when i declare to you the embarrassment in which your 
presence involve me, will you not release me from that oath? reflect 
upon the danger of a discovery, upon the opprobrium in which such an 
event would plunge me: reflect that my honour and reputation are at 
stake, and that my peace of mind depend on your compliance. as yet my 
heart is free; i shall separate from you with regret, but not with 
despair. stay here, and a few week will sacrifice my happiness on the 
altar of your charms. you are but too interesting, too amiable! i 
should love you, i should doat on you! my bosom would become the prey 
of desire which honour and my profession forbid me to gratify. if i 
resist them, the impetuos of my wish unsatisfy would drive me 
to madness: if i yield to the temptation, i should sacrifice to one 
moment of guilty pleasure my reputation in this world, my salvation in 
the next. to you then i fly for defence against myself. preserve me 
from lose the reward of thirty years of sufferings! preserve me from 
becoming the victim of remorse! your heart has already felt the 
anguish of hopeless love; oh! then if you really value me, spare mine 
that anguish! give me back my promise; fly from these walls. go, and 
you bear with you my warmest prayer for your happiness, my friendship, 
my esteem and admiration: stay, and you become to me the source of 
danger, of sufferings, of despair! answer me, matilda; what is your 
resolve?'--she was silent--'will you not speak, matilda? will you not 
name your choice?' 

'cruel! cruel!' she exclaimed, wring her hand in agony; 'you know 
too well that you offer me no choice! you know too well that i can have 
no will but yours!' 

'i was not then deceived! matilda's generous equal my expectations.' 

'yes; i will prove the truth of my affection by submit to a decree 
which cut me to the very heart. take back your promise. i will quit 
the monastery this very day. i have a relation, abbess of a covent in 
estramadura: to her will i bend my steps, and shut myself from the 
world for ever. yet tell me, father, shall i bear your good wish 
with me to my solitude? will you sometimes abstract your attention 
from heavenly object to bestow a thought upon me?' 

'ah! matilda, i fear that i shall think on you but too often for my 
repose!' 

'then i have nothing more to wish for, save that we may meet in heaven. 
farewell, my friend! my ambrosio!-- and yet methinks, i would fain bear 
with me some token of your regard!' 

'what shall i give you?' 

'something.--any thing.--one of those flower will be sufficient.' 
(here she pointed to a bush of roses, plant at the door of the 
grotto.) 'i will hide it in my bosom, and when i am dead, the nun 
shall find it wither upon my heart.' 

the friar was unable to reply: with slow steps, and a soul heavy with 
affliction, he quit the hermitage. he approach the bush, and 
stoop to pluck one of the roses. sudden he utter a piercing cry, 
start back hastily, and let the flower, which he already held, fall 
from his hand. matilda heard the shriek, and flew anxious towards 
him. 

'what is the matter?' she cried; 'answer me, for god's sake! what has 
happened?' 

'i have received my death!' he reply in a faint voice; 'concealed 
among the rose ... a serpent....' 

here the pain of his wound became so exquisite, that nature was unable 
to bear it: his senses abandoned him, and he sank inanimate into 
matilda's arms. 

her distress was beyond the power of description. she rent her hair, 
beat her bosom, and not daring to quit ambrosio, endeavour by loud 
cries to summon the monk to her assistance. she at length succeeded. 
alarm by her shrieks, several of the brother hasten to the spot, 
and the superior was convey back to the abbey. he was immediately 
put to bed, and the monk who officiate as surgeon to the fraternity 
prepared to examine the wound. by this time ambrosio's hand had 
swell to an extraordinary size; the remedy which had been 
administer to him, 'tis true, restore him to life, but not to his 
senses; he rave in all the horrors of delirium, foam at the mouth, 
and four of the strongest monk were scarcely able to hold him in his 
bed. 

father pablos, such was the surgeon's name, hasten to examine the 
wound hand. the monk surround the bed, anxious wait for the 
decision: among these the feign rosario appear not the most 
insensible to the friar's calamity. he gaze upon the sufferer with 
inexpressible anguish; and the groan which every moment escape from 
his bosom sufficient betray the violence of his affliction. 

father pablo probe the wound. as he drew out his lancet, its point 
was tinge with a greenish hue. he shook his head mournfully, and 
quit the bedside. 

''tis as i feared!' said he; 'there is no hope.' 

'no hope?' exclaim the monk with one voice; 'say you, no hope?' 

'from the sudden effects, i suspect that the abbot was stung by a 
cientipedoro: the venom which you see upon my lancet confirm my idea: 
he cannot live three days.' 

'and can no possible remedy be found?' enquire rosario. 

'without extract the poison, he cannot recover; and how to extract 
it is to me still a secret. all that i can do is to apply such herb 
to the wound as will relieve the anguish: the patient will be restore 
to his senses; but the venom will corrupt the whole mass of his blood, 
and in three days he will exist no longer.' 

excessive was the universal grief at hearing this decision. pablos, as 
he had promised, dress the wound, and then retired, follow by his 
companions: rosario alone remain in the cell, the abbot at his 
urgent entreaty have been committed to his care. ambrosio's strength 
worn out by the violence of his exertions, he had by this time fallen 
into a profound sleep. so total was he overcome by weariness, that 
he scarcely gave any sign of life; he was still in this situation, 
when the monk return to enquire whether any change had taken place. 
pablo loosen the bandage which conceal the wound, more from a 
principle of curiosity than from indulge the hope of discover any 
favourable symptoms. what was his astonishment at finding, that the 
inflammation had total subsided! he probe the hand; his lancet came 
out pure and unsullied; no trace of the venom were perceptible; and 
had not the orifice still been visible, pablo might have doubt that 
there had ever been a wound. 

he communicate this intelligence to his brethren; their delight was 
only equal by their surprize. from the latter sentiment, however, 
they were soon release by explain the circumstance according to 
their own ideas: they were perfectly convinced that their superior was 
a saint, and thought, that nothing could be more natural than for st. 
francis to have operate a miracle in his favour. this opinion was 
adopt unanimously: they declared it so loudly, and vociferated,--'a 
miracle! a miracle!'--with such fervour, that they soon interrupt 
ambrosio's slumbers. 

the monk immediately crowded round his bed, and express their 
satisfaction at his wonderful recovery. he was perfectly in his 
senses, and free from every complaint except feeling weak and languid. 
pablo gave him a strengthen medicine, and advise his keeping his 
bed for the two succeed days: he then retired, have desire his 
patient not to exhaust himself by conversation, but rather to endeavour 
at taking some repose. the other monk follow his example, and the 
abbot and rosario were left without observers. 

for some minutes ambrosio regard his attendant with a look of mingle 
pleasure and apprehension. she was seat upon the side of the bed, 
her head bend down, and as usual envelop in the cowl of her habit. 

'and you are still here, matilda?' said the friar at length. 'are you 
not satisfy with have so nearly effect my destruction, that 
nothing but a miracle could have save me from the grave? ah! surely 
heaven sent that serpent to punish....' 

matilda interrupt him by putt her hand before his lip with an air 
of gaiety. 

'hush! father, hush! you must not talk!' 

'he who impose that order, knew not how interesting are the subject 
on which i wish to speak.' 

'but i know it, and yet issue the same positive command. i am 
appoint your nurse, and you must not disobey my orders.' 

'you are in spirits, matilda!' 

'well may i be so: i have just received a pleasure unexampled through 
my whole life.' 

'what was that pleasure?' 

'what i must conceal from all, but most from you.' 

'but most from me? nay then, i entreat you, matilda....' 

'hush, father! hush! you must not talk. but as you do not seem 
inclined to sleep, shall i endeavour to amuse you with my harp?' 

'how? i knew not that you understood music.' 

'oh! i am a sorry performer! yet as silence is prescribed you for 
eight and forty hours, i may possibly entertain you, when weary of 
your own reflections. i go to fetch my harp.' 

she soon return with it. 

'now, father; what shall i sing? will you hear the ballad which treat 
of the gallant durandarte, who die in the famous battle of 
roncevalles?' 

'what you please, matilda.' 

'oh! call me not matilda! call me rosario, call me your friend! those 
are the names, which i love to hear from your lips. now listen!' 

she then tune her harp, and afterwards prelude for some moments with 
such exquisite taste as to prove her a perfect mistress of the 
instrument. the air which she play was soft and plaintive: 

ambrosio, while he listened, felt his uneasy subside, and a 
pleasing melancholy spread itself into his bosom. sudden matilda 
change the strain: with an hand bold and rapid she struck a few loud 
martial chords, and then chaunte the following ballad to an air at 
once simple and melodious. 

durandarte and belerma 

sad and fearful is the story 
of the roncevalle fight; 
on those fatal plain of glory 
perish many a gallant knight. 

there fell durandarte; never 
verse a noble chieftain named: 
he, before his lip for ever 
closed in silence thus exclaimed. 

'oh! belerma! oh! my dear-one! 
for my pain and pleasure born! 
seven long years i serve thee, fair-one, 
seven long years my fee was scorn: 

'and when now thy heart reply 
to my wishes, burn like mine, 
cruel fate my bliss deny 
bid me every hope resign. 

'ah! though young i fall, believe me, 
death would never claim a sigh; 
'tis to lose thee, 'tis to leave thee, 
make me think it hard to die! 

'oh! my cousin montesinos, 
by that friendship firm and dear 
which from youth has live between us, 
now my last petition hear! 

'when my soul these limb forsake 
eager seek a pure air, 
from my breast the cold heart taking, 
give it to belerma's care. 

say, i of my lands possessor 
name her with my die breath: 
say, my lip i op'd to bless her, 
ere they closed for aye in death: 

'twice a week too how sincerely 
i adore her, cousin, say; 
twice a week for one who dearly 
love her, cousin, bid her pray. 

'montesinos, now the hour 
marked by fate is near at hand: 
lo! my arm has lost its power! 
lo! i drop my trusty brand! 

'eyes, which forth beheld me going, 
homewards ne'er shall see me hie! 
cousin, stop those tear o'er-flowing, 
let me on thy bosom die! 

'thy kind hand my eyelid closing, 
yet one favour i implore: 
pray thou for my soul's reposing, 
when my heart shall throb no more; 

'so shall jesus, still attend 
gracious to a christian's vow, 
pleased accept my ghost ascending, 
and a seat in heaven allow.' 

thus spoke gallant durandarte; 
soon his brave heart broke in twain. 
greatly joy the moorish party, 
that the gallant knight was slain. 

bitter weeping montesino 
took from him his helm and glaive; 
bitter weeping montesino 
dug his gallant cousin's grave. 

to perform his promise made, he 
cut the heart from out the breast, 
that belerma, wretched lady! 
might receive the last bequest. 

sad was montesinos' heart, he 
felt distress his bosom rend. 
'oh! my cousin durandarte, 
woe is me to view thy end! 

'sweet in manners, fair in favour, 
mild in temper, fierce in fight, 
warrior, nobler, gentler, braver, 
never shall behold the light! 

'cousin, lo! my tear bedew thee! 
how shall i thy loss survive! 
durandarte, he who slew thee, 
wherefore left he me alive!' 


while she sung, ambrosio listen with delight: never had he heard a 
voice more harmonious; and he wonder how such heavenly sound could 
be produce by any but angels. but though he indulge the sense of 
hearing, a single look convinced him that he must not trust to that of 
sight. the songstress sat at a little distance from his bed. the 
attitude in which she bent over her harp, was easy and graceful: her 
cowl had fallen backward than usual: two coral lip were visible, 
ripe, fresh, and melting, and a chin in whose dimple seem to lurk a 
thousand cupids. her habit's long sleeve would have swept along the 
chord of the instrument: to prevent this inconvenience she had drawn 
it above her elbow, and by this means an arm was discover form in 
the most perfect symmetry, the delicacy of whose skin might have 
contend with snow in whiteness. ambrosio dare to look on her but 
once: that glance suffice to convince him, how dangerous was the 
presence of this seduce object. he closed his eyes, but strove in 
vain to banish her from his thoughts. there she still move before him, 
adorn with all those charm which his heated imagination could 
supply: every beauty which he had seen, appear embellished, and 
those still conceal fancy represent to him in glowing colours. 
still, however, his vow and the necessity of keeping to them were 
present to his memory. he struggle with desire, and shudder when he 
beheld how deep was the precipice before him. 

matilda cease to sing. dread the influence of her charms, ambrosio 
remain with his eyes closed, and offer up his prayer to st. 
francis to assist him in this dangerous trial! matilda believe that he 
was sleeping. she rose from her seat, approach the bed softly, and 
for some minutes gaze upon him attentively. 

'he sleeps!' said she at length in a low voice, but whose accent the 
abbot distinguished perfectly; 'now then i may gaze upon him without 
offence! i may mix my breath with his; i may doat upon his features, 
and he cannot suspect me of impurity and deceit!--he fear my seduce 
him to the violate of his vows! oh! the unjust! were it my wish to 
excite desire, should i conceal my features from him so carefully? 
those features, of which i daily hear him....' 

she stopped, and was lost in her reflections. 

'it was but yesterday!' she continued; 'but a few short hour have 
past, since i was dear to him! he esteem me, and my heart was 
satisfied! now!... oh! now how cruel is my situation changed! he 
looks on me with suspicion! he bid me leave him, leave him for ever! 
oh! you, my saint! my idol! you, holding the next place to god in my 
breast! yet two days, and my heart will be unveil to you.--could you 
know my feelings, when i beheld your agony! could you know, how much 
your suffering have endear you to me! but the time will come, when 
you will be convinced that my passion is pure and disinterested. then 
you will pity me, and feel the whole weight of these sorrows!' 

as she said this, her voice was choake by weeping. while she bent over 
ambrosio, a tear fell upon his cheek. 

'ah! i have disturbed him!' cry matilda, and retreat hastily. 

her alarm was ungrounded. none sleep so profoundly, as those who are 
determined not to wake. the friar was in this predicament: he still 
seem bury in a repose, which every succeed minute render him 
less capable of enjoying. the burning tear had communicate its warmth 
to his heart. 

'what affection! what purity!' said he internally; 'ah! since my 
bosom is thus sensible of pity, what would it be if agitate by love?' 

matilda again quit her seat, and retired to some distance from the 
bed. ambrosio venture to open his eyes, and to cast them upon her 
fearfully. her face was turn from him. she rest her head in a 
melancholy posture upon her harp, and gaze on the picture which hung 
opposite to the bed. 

'happy, happy image!' thus did she address the beautiful madona; ''tis 
to you that he offer his prayers! 'tis on you that he gaze with 
admiration! i thought you would have lighten my sorrows; you have 
only serve to increase their weight: you have made me feel that had i 
known him ere his vow were pronounced, ambrosio and happiness might 
have been mine. with what pleasure he view this picture! with what 
fervour he address his prayer to the insensible image! ah! may not 
his sentiment be inspired by some kind and secret genius, friend to my 
affection? may it not be man's natural instinct which inform him... 
be silent, idle hopes! let me not encourage an idea which take from 
the brilliance of ambrosio's virtue. 'tis religion, not beauty which 
attract his admiration; 'tis not to the woman, but the divinity that 
he kneels. would he but address to me the least tender expression 
which he pour forth to this madona! would he but say that were he not 
already affiance to the church, he would not have despise matilda! 
oh! let me nourish that fond idea! perhaps he may yet acknowledge 
that he feel for me more than pity, and that affection like mine might 
well have deserve a return; perhaps, he may own thus much when i lye 
on my deathbed! he then need not fear to infringe his vows, and the 
confession of his regard will soften the pang of dying. would i were 
sure of this! oh! how earnest should i sigh for the moment of 
dissolution!' 

of this discourse the abbot lost not a syllable; and the tone in which 
she pronounced these last words pierce to his heart. involuntary he 
raise himself from his pillow. 

'matilda!' he said in a trouble voice; 'oh! my matilda!' 

she start at the sound, and turn towards him hastily. the 
sudden of her movement made her cowl fall back from her head; her 
features became visible to the monk's enquiring eye. what was his 
amaze at behold the exact resemblance of his admire madona? 
the same exquisite proportion of features, the same profusion of golden 
hair, the same rosy lips, heavenly eyes, and majesty of countenance 
adorn matilda! utter an exclamation of surprize, ambrosio sank 
back upon his pillow, and doubt whether the object before him was 
mortal or divine. 

matilda seem penetrate with confusion. she remain motionless in 
her place, and support herself upon her instrument. her eyes were 
bent upon the earth, and her fair cheek overspread with blushes. on 
recover herself, her first action was to conceal her features. she 
then in an unsteady and trouble voice venture to address these words 
to the friar. 

'accident has made you master of a secret, which i never would have 
reveal but on the bed of death. yes, ambrosio; in matilda de 
villanega you see the original of your beloved madona. soon after i 
conceive my unfortunate passion, i form the project of convey to 
you my picture: crowd of admirer had persuade me that i possessed 
some beauty, and i was anxious to know what effect it would produce 
upon you. i cause my portrait to be drawn by martin galuppi, a 
celebrated venetian at that time resident in madrid. the resemblance 
was striking: i sent it to the capuchin abbey as if for sale, and the 
jew from whom you bought it was one of my emissaries. you purchase 
it. judge of my rapture, when informed that you had gaze upon it with 
delight, or rather with adoration; that you had suspend it in your 
cell, and that you address your supplicate to no other saint. 
will this discovery make me still more regard as an object of 
suspicion? rather should it convince you how pure is my affection, and 
engage you to suffer me in your society and esteem. i heard you daily 
extol the praises of my portrait: i was an eyewitness of the 
transports, which its beauty excited in you: yet i forbore to use 
against your virtue those arms, with which yourself had furnish me. 
i conceal those features from your sight, which you love 
unconsciously. i strove not to excite desire by display my charms, 
or to make myself mistress of your heart through the medium of your 
senses. to attract your notice by studious attend to religious 
duties, to endear myself to you by convincing you that my mind was 
virtuous and my attachment sincere, such was my only aim. i succeeded; 
i became your companion and your friend. i conceal my sex from your 
knowledge; and had you not pressed me to reveal my secret, had i not 
been torment by the fear of a discovery, never had you known me for 
any other than rosario. and still are you resolve to drive me from 
you? the few hour of life which yet remain for me, may i not pass 
them in your presence? oh! speak, ambrosio, and tell me that i may 
stay!' 

this speech gave the abbot an opportunity of recollect himself. he 
was conscious that in the present disposition of his mind, avoid her 
society was his only refuge from the power of this enchanting woman. 

'you declaration has so much astonish me,' said he, 'that i am at 
present incapable of answer you. do not insist upon a reply, 
matilda; leave me to myself; i have need to be alone.' 

'i obey you--but before i go, promise not to insist upon my quit 
the abbey immediately.' 

'matilda, reflect upon your situation; reflect upon the consequence of 
your stay. our separation is indispensable, and we must part.' 

'but not to-day, father! oh! in pity not today!' 

'you press me too hard, but i cannot resist that tone of supplication. 
since you insist upon it, i yield to your prayer: i consent to your 
remain here a sufficient time to prepare in some measure the 
brethren for your departure. stay yet two days; but on the third,' ... 
(he sigh involuntarily)--'remember, that on the third we must part 
for ever!' 

she caught his hand eagerly, and pressed it to her lips. 

'on the third?' she exclaim with an air of wild solemnity; 'you are 
right, father! you are right! on the third we must part for ever!' 

there was a dreadful expression in her eye as she utter these words, 
which penetrate the friar's soul with horror: again she kiss his 
hand, and then flee with rapid from the chamber. 

anxious to authorise the presence of his dangerous guest, yet conscious 
that her stay was infringe the law of his order, ambrosio's bosom 
became the theatre of a thousand contend passions. at length his 
attachment to the feign rosario, aid by the natural warmth of his 
temperament, seem likely to obtain the victory: the success was 
assured, when that presumption which form the groundwork of his 
character came to matilda's assistance. the monk reflect that to 
vanquish temptation was an infinite greater merit than to avoid it: 
he thought that he ought rather to rejoice in the opportunity given him 
of prove the firm of his virtue. st. anthony had withstood all 
seduction to lust; then why should not he? besides, st. anthony was 
tempt by the devil, who put every art into practice to excite his 
passions: whereas, ambrosio's danger proceed from a mere mortal 
woman, fearful and modest, whose apprehension of his yielding were not 
less violent than his own. 

'yes,' said he; 'the unfortunate shall stay; i have nothing to fear 
from her presence. even should my own prove too weak to resist the 
temptation, i am secure from danger by the innocence of matilda.' 

ambrosio was yet to learn, that to an heart unacquainted with her, vice 
is ever most dangerous when lurk behind the mask of virtue. 

he found himself so perfectly recovered, that when father pablo 
visit him again at night, he entreat permission to quit his chamber 
on the day following. his request was granted. matilda appear no 
more that evening, except in company with the monk when they came in a 
body to enquire after the abbot's health. she seem fearful of 
converse with him in private, and stay but a few minutes in his 
room. the friar slept well; but the dream of the former night were 
repeated, and his sensation of voluptuous were yet more keen and 
exquisite. the same lust-exciting vision float before his eyes: 
matilda, in all the pomp of beauty, warm, tender, and luxurious, 
clasp him to her bosom, and lavish upon him the most ardent 
caresses. he return them as eagerly, and already was on the point of 
satisfying his desires, when the faithless form disappeared, and left 
him to all the horrors of shame and disappointment. 

the morning dawned. fatigued, harassed, and exhaust by his provoking 
dreams, he was not disposed to quit his bed. he excuse himself from 
appear at matins: it was the first morning in his life that he had 
ever miss them. he rose late. during the whole of the day he had no 
opportunity of speak to matilda without witnesses. his cell was 
throng by the monks, anxious to express their concern at his illness; 
and he was still occupy in receiving their compliments on his 
recovery, when the bell summon them to the refectory. 

after dinner the monk separated, and disperse themselves in various 
parts of the garden, where the shade of tree or retirement of some 
grotto present the most agreeable means of enjoy the siesta. the 
abbot bent his steps towards the hermitage: a glance of his eye 
invite matilda to accompany him. 

she obeyed, and follow him thither in silence. they enter the 
grotto, and seat themselves. both seem unwill to begin the 
conversation, and to labour under the influence of mutual 
embarrassment. at length the abbot spoke: he converse only on 
indifferent topics, and matilda answer him in the same tone. she 
seem anxious to make him forget that the person who sat by him was 
any other than rosario. neither of them dared, or indeed wish to 
make an allusion, to the subject which was most at the heart of both. 

matilda's effort to appear gay were evidently forced: her spirits were 
oppress by the weight of anxiety, and when she spoke her voice was 
low and feeble. she seem desirous of finish a conversation which 
embarrass her; and complain that she was unwell, she request 
ambrosio's permission to return to the abbey. he accompany her to 
the door of her cell; and when arrive there, he stop her to declare 
his consent to her continue the partner of his solitude so long as 
should be agreeable to herself. 

she discover no mark of pleasure at receiving this intelligence, 
though on the preceding day she had been so anxious to obtain the 
permission. 

'alas! father,' she said, wave her head mournfully; 'your kindness 
come too late! my doom is fixed. we must separate for ever. yet 
believe, that i am grateful for your generosity, for your compassion of 
an unfortunate who is but too little deserving of it!' 

she put her handkerchief to her eyes. her cowl was only half drawn 
over her face. ambrosio observe that she was pale, and her eyes sunk 
and heavy. 

'good god!' he cried; 'you are very ill, matilda! i shall send father 
pablo to you instantly.' 

'no; do not. i am ill, 'tis true; but he cannot cure my malady. 
farewell, father! remember me in your prayer tomorrow, while i shall 
remember you in heaven!' 

she enter her cell, and closed the door. 

the abbot dispatch to her the physician without lose a moment, and 
wait his report impatiently. but father pablo soon returned, and 
declared that his errand had been fruitless. rosario refuse to admit 
him, and had positively reject his offer of assistance. the 
uneasy which this account gave ambrosio was not trifling: yet he 
determined that matilda should have her own way for that night: but 
that if her situation did not mend by the morning, he would insist upon 
her taking the advice of father pablos. 

he did not find himself inclined to sleep. he open his casement, and 
gaze upon the moonbeam as they play upon the small stream whose 
waters bath the wall of the monastery. the cool of the night 
breeze and tranquill of the hour inspired the friar's mind with 
sadness. he thought upon matilda's beauty and affection; upon the 
pleasure which he might have share with her, had he not been 
restrained by monastic fetters. he reflected, that unsustained by hope 
her love for him could not long exist; that doubtless she would succeed 
in extinguish her passion, and seek for happiness in the arms of one 
more fortunate. he shudder at the void which her absence would leave 
in his bosom. he look with disgust on the monotony of a convent, and 
breathe a sigh towards that world from which he was for ever 
separated. such were the reflection which a loud knock at his door 
interrupted. the bell of the church had already struck two. the abbot 
hasten to enquire the cause of this disturbance. he open the door 
of his cell, and a lay-brother entered, whose looks declared his hurry 
and confusion. 

'hasten, reverend father!' said he; 'hasten to the young rosario. 

he earnest request to see you; he lie at the point of death.' 

'gracious god! where is father pablos? why is he not with him? oh! i 
fear! i fear!' 

'father pablo has seen him, but his art can do nothing. he say that 
he suspect the youth to be poisoned.' 

'poisoned? oh! the unfortunate! it is then as i suspected! but let 
me not lose a moment; perhaps it may yet be time to save her!' 

he said, and flew towards the cell of the novice. several monk were 
already in the chamber. father pablo was one of them, and held a 
medicine in his hand which he was endeavour to persuade rosario to 
swallow. the other were employ in admire the patient's divine 
countenance, which they now saw for the first time. she look 
lovely than ever. she was no long pale or languid; a bright glow 
had spread itself over her cheeks; her eyes sparkle with a serene 
delight, and her countenance was expressive of confidence and 
resignation. 

'oh! torment me no more!' was she saying to pablos, when the terrify 
abbot rush hasty into the cell; 'my disease is far beyond the reach 
of your skill, and i wish not to be cure of it'--then perceive 
ambrosio,-- 'ah! 'tis he!' she cried; 'i see him once again, before we 
part for ever! leave me, my brethren; much have i to tell this holy 
man in private.' 

the monk retired immediately, and matilda and the abbot remain 
together. 

'what have you done, imprudent woman!' exclaim the latter, as soon as 
they were left alone; 'tell me; are my suspicion just? am i indeed to 
lose you? has your own hand been the instrument of your destruction?' 

she smiled, and grasp his hand. 

'in what have i been imprudent, father? i have sacrifice a pebble, 
and save a diamond: my death preserve a life valuable to the world, 
and more dear to me than my own. yes, father; i am poisoned; but know 
that the poison once circulate in your veins.' 

'matilda!' 

'what i tell you i resolve never to discover to you but on the bed of 
death: that moment is now arrived. you cannot have forgotten the day 
already, when your life was endanger by the bite of a cientipedoro. 
the physician gave you over, declare himself ignorant how to extract 
the venom: i knew but of one means, and hesitate not a moment to 
employ it. i was left alone with you: you slept; i loosen the 
bandage from your hand; i kiss the wound, and drew out the poison 
with my lips. the effect has been more sudden than i expected. i feel 
death at my heart; yet an hour, and i shall be in a better world.' 

'almighty god!' exclaim the abbot, and sank almost lifeless upon the 
bed. 

after a few minutes he again raise himself up suddenly, and gaze upon 
matilda with all the wild of despair. 

'and you have sacrifice yourself for me! you die, and die to preserve 
ambrosio! and is there indeed no remedy, matilda? and is there indeed 
no hope? speak to me, oh! speak to me! tell me, that you have still 
the means of life!' 

'be comforted, my only friend! yes, i have still the means of life in 
my power: but 'tis a means which i dare not employ. it is dangerous! 
it is dreadful! life would be purchase at too dear a rate, ... 
unless it were permit me to live for you.' 

'then live for me, matilda, for me and gratitude!'-- (he caught her 
hand, and pressed it rapturous to his lips.)--'remember our late 
conversations; i now consent to every thing: remember in what lively 
colours you describe the union of souls; be it ours to realize those 
ideas. let us forget the distinction of sex, despise the world's 
prejudices, and only consider each other as brother and friend. live 
then, matilda! oh! live for me!' 

'ambrosio, it must not be. when i thought thus, i deceive both you 
and myself. either i must die at present, or expire by the lingering 
torment of unsatisfy desire. oh! since we last converse together, 
a dreadful veil has been rent from before my eyes. i love you no 
long with the devotion which is paid to a saint: i prize you no more 
for the virtue of your soul; i lust for the enjoyment of your person. 
the woman reign in my bosom, and i am become a prey to the wildest of 
passions. away with friendship! 'tis a cold unfeeling word. my bosom 
burn with love, with unutterable love, and love must be its return. 
tremble then, ambrosio, tremble to succeed in your prayers. if i live, 
your truth, your reputation, your reward of a life past in sufferings, 
all that you value is irretrievable lost. i shall no long be able to 
combat my passions, shall seize every opportunity to excite your 
desires, and labour to effect your dishonour and my own. no, no, 
ambrosio; i must not live! i am convinced with every moment, that i 
have but one alternative; i feel with every heart-throb, that i must 
enjoy you, or die.' 

'amazement!--matilda! can it be you who speak to me?' 

he made a movement as if to quit his seat. she utter a loud shriek, 
and raise herself half out of the bed, threw her arms round the friar 
to detain him. 

'oh! do not leave me! listen to my error with compassion! in a few 
hour i shall be no more; yet a little, and i am free from this 
disgraceful passion.' 

'wretched woman, what can i say to you! i cannot ... i must not ... 
but live, matilda! oh! live!' 

'you do not reflect on what you ask. what? live to plunge myself in 
infamy? to become the agent of hell? to work the destruction both of 
you and of myself? feel this heart, father!' 

she took his hand: confused, embarrassed, and fascinated, he withdrew 
it not, and felt her heart throb under it. 

'feel this heart, father! it is yet the seat of honour, truth, and 
chastity: if it beat tomorrow, it must fall a prey to the blackest 
crimes. oh! let me then die today! let me die, while i yet deserve 
the tear of the virtuous! thus will expire!'--(she recline her head 
upon his shoulder; her golden hair pour itself over his chest.)-- 
'folded in your arms, i shall sink to sleep; your hand shall close my 
eyes for ever, and your lip receive my die breath. and will you not 
sometimes think of me? will you not sometimes shed a tear upon my 
tomb? oh! yes! yes! yes! that kiss is my assurance!' 

the hour was night. all was silence around. the faint beam of a 
solitary lamp dart upon matilda's figure, and shed through the 
chamber a dim mysterious light. no pry eye, or curious ear was near 
the lovers: nothing was heard but matilda's melodious accents. 
ambrosio was in the full vigour of manhood. he saw before him a young 
and beautiful woman, the preserver of his life, the adore of his 
person, and whom affection for him had reduce to the brink of the 
grave. he sat upon her bed; his hand rest upon her bosom; her head 
recline voluptuous upon his breast. who then can wonder, if he 
yield to the temptation? drunk with desire, he pressed his lip to 
those which sought them: his kiss vie with matilda's in warmth and 
passion. he clasp her rapturous in his arms; he forgot his vows, 
his sanctity, and his fame: he remember nothing but the pleasure and 
opportunity. 

'ambrosio! oh! my ambrosio!' sigh matilda. 

'thine, ever thine!' murmur the friar, and sank upon her bosom. 



chapter iii 

----these are the villain 
whom all the traveller do fear so much. 
--------some of them are gentlemen 
such as the fury of ungoverned youth 
thrust from the company of awful men. 
two gentlemen of verona. 

the marquis and lorenzo proceed to the hotel in silence. the former 
employ himself in calling every circumstance to his mind, which 
related might give lorenzo's the most favourable idea of his connexion 
with agnes. the latter, just alarm for the honour of his family, 
felt embarrass by the presence of the marquis: the adventure which he 
had just witness forbad his treat him as a friend; and antonia's 
interests being entrust to his mediation, he saw the impolicy of 
treat him as a foe. he conclude from these reflections, that 
profound silence would be the wisest plan, and wait with impatience 
for don raymond's explanation. 

they arrive at the hotel de las cisternas. the marquis immediately 
conduct him to his apartment, and began to express his satisfaction 
at finding him at madrid. lorenzo interrupt him. 

'excuse me, my lord,' said he with a distant air, 'if i reply somewhat 
cold to your expression of regard. a sister's honour is involved in 
this affair: till that is established, and the purport of your 
correspondence with agnes clear up, i cannot consider you as my 
friend. i am anxious to hear the meaning of your conduct, and hope 
that you will not delay the promise explanation.' 

'first give me your word, that you will listen with patience and 
indulgence.' 

'i love my sister too well to judge her harshly; and till this moment i 
possessed no friend so dear to me as yourself. i will also confess, 
that your have it in your power to oblige me in a business which i 
have much at heart, make me very anxious to find you still deserving 
my esteem.' 

'lorenzo, you transport me! no greater pleasure can be given me, than 
an opportunity of serving the brother of agnes.' 

'convince me that i can accept your favours without dishonour, and 
there is no man in the world to whom i am more willing to be obliged.' 

'probably, you have already heard your sister mention the name of 
alphonso d'alvarada?' 

'never. though i feel for agnes an affection truly fraternal, 
circumstances have prevent us from being much together. while yet a 
child she was consign to the care of her aunt, who had married a 
germany nobleman. at his castle she remain till two years since, when 
she return to spain, determined upon seclude herself from the 
world.' 

'good god! lorenzo, you knew of her intention, and yet strove not to 
make her change it?' 

'marquis, you wrong me. the intelligence, which i received at naples, 
shock me extremely, and i hasten my return to madrid for the 
express purpose of prevent the sacrifice. the moment that i 
arrived, i flew to the convent of st. clare, in which agnes had chosen 
to perform her noviciate. i request to see my sister. conceive my 
surprise when she sent me a refusal; she declared positively, that 
apprehend my influence over her mind, she would not trust herself in 
my society till the day before that on which she was to receive the 
veil. i supplicate the nuns; i insist upon seeing agnes, and 
hesitate not to avow my suspicion that her being kept from me was 
against her own inclinations. to free herself from the imputation of 
violence, the prioress brought me a few lines written in my sister's 
well-known hand, repeating the message already delivered. all future 
attempt to obtain a moment's conversation with her were as fruitless 
as the first. she was inflexible, and i was not permit to see her 
till the day preceding that on which she enter the cloister never to 
quit it more. this interview took place in the presence of our 
principal relations. it was for the first time since her childhood 
that i saw her, and the scene was most affecting. she threw herself 
upon my bosom, kiss me, and wept bitterly. by every possible 
argument, by tears, by prayers, by kneeling, i strove to make her 
abandon her intention. i represent to her all the hardship of a 
religious life; i paint to her imagination all the pleasure which 
she was go to quit, and besought her to disclose to me, what 
occasion her disgust to the world. at this last question she turn 
pale, and her tear flow yet faster. she entreat me not to press 
her on that subject; that it suffice me to know that her resolution 
was taken, and that a convent was the only place where she could now 
hope for tranquillity. she persevere in her design, and made her 
profession. i visit her frequent at the grate, and every moment 
that i pass with her, made me feel more affliction at her loss. i 
was shortly after oblige to quit madrid; i return but yesterday 
evening, and since then have not had time to call at st. clare's 
convent.' 

'then till i mention it, you never heard the name of alphonso 
d'alvarada?' 

'pardon me: my aunt wrote me word that an adventurer so call had 
found means to get introduce into the castle of lindenberg; that he 
had insinuate himself into my sister's good graces, and that she had 
even consent to elope with him. however, before the plan could be 
executed, the cavalier discover that the estate which he believe 
agnes to possess in hispaniola, in reality belong to me. this 
intelligence made him change his intention; he disappear on the day 
that the elope was to have taken place, and agnes, in despair at 
his perfidy and meanness, had resolve upon seclusion in a convent. 
she added, that as this adventurer had given himself out to be a friend 
of mine, she wish to know whether i had any knowledge of him. i 
reply in the negative. i had then very little idea, that alphonso 
d'alvarada and the marquis de las cisterna were one and the same 
person: the description given me of the first by no means tally with 
what i knew of the latter.' 

'in this i easily recognize donna rodolpha's perfidious character. 
every word of this account is stamp with mark of her malice, of her 
falsehood, of her talent for misrepresent those whom she wish to 
injure. forgive me, medina, for speak so freely of your relation. 
the mischief which she has done me authorise my resentment, and when 
you have heard my story, you will be convinced that my expression have 
not been too severe.' 

he then began his narrative in the following manner. 

history of don raymond, marquis de las cisterna 

long experience, my dear lorenzo, has convinced me how generous is your 
nature: i wait not for your declaration of ignorance respecting your 
sister's adventure to suppose that they had been purposely conceal 
from you. had they reach your knowledge, from what misfortune 
should both agnes and myself have escaped! fate had ordain it 
otherwise! you were on your travels when i first became acquaint 
with your sister; and as our enemy took care to conceal from her your 
direction, it was impossible for her to implore by letter your 
protection and advice. 

on leave salamanca, at which university as i have since heard, you 
remain a year after i quit it, i immediately set out upon my 
travels. my father supply me liberally with money; but he insist 
upon my conceal my rank, and present myself as no more than a 
private gentleman. this command was issue by the counsel of his 
friend, the duke of villa hermosa, a nobleman for whose abilities and 
knowledge of the world i have ever entertain the most profound 
veneration. 

'believe me,' said he, 'my dear raymond, you will hereafter feel the 
benefit of this temporary degradation. 'tis true, that as the conde 
de las cisterna you would have been received with open arms; and your 
youthful vanity might have felt gratify by the attention shower 
upon you from all sides. at present, much will depend upon yourself: 
you have excellent recommendations, but it must be your own business to 
make them of use to you. you must lay yourself out to please; you must 
labour to gain the approbation of those, to whom you are presented: 
they who would have court the friendship of the conde de las 
cisterna will have no interest in finding out the merits, or bearing 
patient with the faults, of alphonso d'alvarada. consequently, when 
you find yourself really liked, you may safe ascribe it to your good 
qualities, not your rank, and the distinction shown you will be 
infinite more flattering. besides, your exalted birth would not 
permit your mix with the lower class of society, which will now be 
in your power, and from which, in my opinion, you will derive 
considerable benefit. do not confine yourself to the illustrious of 
those country through which you pass. examine the manners and 
customs of the multitude: enter into the cottages; and by observing how 
the vassal of foreigner are treated, learn to diminish the burthen 
and augment the comfort of your own. according to my ideas, of those 
advantage which a youth destined to the possession of power and wealth 
may reap from travel, he should not consider as the least essential, 
the opportunity of mix with the class below him, and becoming an 
eyewitness of the suffering of the people.' 

forgive me, lorenzo, if i seem tedious in my narration. the close 
connexion which now exist between us, make me anxious that you should 
know every particular respecting me; and in my fear of omit the 
least circumstance which may induce you to think favourable of your 
sister and myself, i may possibly relate many which you may think 
uninteresting. 

i follow the duke's advice; i was soon convinced of its wisdom. 

i quit spain, calling myself by the assume title of don alphonso 
d'alvarada, and attend by a single domestic of approve fidelity. 
paris was my first station. for some time i was enchant with it, as 
indeed must be every man who is young, rich, and fond of pleasure. yet 
among all its gaieties, i felt that something was wanting to my heart. 
i grew sick of dissipation: i discovered, that the people among whom i 
lived, and whose exterior was so polish and seducing, were at bottom 
frivolous, unfeeling and insincere. i turn from the inhabitant of 
paris with disgust, and quit that theatre of luxury without heave 
one sigh of regret. 

i now bent my course towards germany, intend to visit most of the 
principal courts: prior to this expedition, i meant to make some 
little stay at strasbourg. on quit my chaise at luneville to take 
some refreshment, i observe a splendid equipage, attend by four 
domestic in rich liveries, wait at the door of the silver lion. 
soon after as i look out of the window, i saw a lady of noble 
presence, follow by two female attendants, step into the carriage, 
which drove off immediately. 

i enquire of the host, who the lady was, that had just departed. 

'a germany baroness, monsieur, of great rank and fortune. she has been 
upon a visit to the duchess of longueville, as her servant informed 
me; she is go to strasbourg, where she will find her husband, and 
then both return to their castle in germany.' 

i resume my journey, intend to reach strasbourg that night. my 
hopes, however were frustrate by the break down of my chaise. the 
accident happen in the middle of a thick forest, and i was not a 
little embarrass as to the means of proceeding. 

it was the depth of winter: the night was already close round us; 
and strasbourg, which was the nearest town, was still distant from us 
several leagues. it seem to me that my only alternative to passing 
the night in the forest, was to take my servant's horse and ride on to
