strasbourg, an undertaking at that season very far from agreeable. 
however, seeing no other resource, i was oblige to make up my mind to 
it. accordingly i communicate my design to the postillion, telling 
him that i would send people to assist him as soon as i reach 
strasbourg. i had not much confidence in his honesty; but stephano 
being well-armed, and the driver to all appearance considerably 
advanced in years, i believe i ran no danger of lose my baggage. 

luckily, as i then thought, an opportunity present itself of passing 
the night more agreeably than i expected. on mention my design of 
proceeding by myself to strasbourg, the postillion shook his head in 
disapprobation. 

'it is a long way,' said he; 'you will find it a difficult matter to 
arrive there without a guide. besides, monsieur seem unaccustomed to 
the season's severity, and 'tis possible that unable to sustain the 
excessive cold....' 

'what use is there to present me with all these objections?' said i, 
impatient interrupt him; 'i have no other resource: i run still 
greater risque of perishing with cold by passing the night in the 
forest.' 

'passing the night in the forest?' he replied; 'oh! by st. denis! we 
are not in quite so bad a plight as that come to yet. if i am not 
mistaken, we are scarcely five minutes walk from the cottage of my old 
friend, baptiste. he is a wood-cutter, and a very honest fellow. i 
doubt not but he will shelter you for the night with pleasure. in the 
meantime i can take the saddle-horse, ride to strasbourg, and be back 
with proper people to mend your carriage by break of day.' 

'and in the name of god,' said i, 'how could you leave me so long in 
suspense? why did you not tell me of this cottage sooner? what 
excessive stupidity!' 

'i thought that perhaps monsieur would not deign to accept....' 

'absurd! come, come! say no more, but conduct us without delay to the 
wood-man's cottage.' 

he obeyed, and we move onwards: the horse contrived with some 
difficulty to drag the shatter vehicle after us. my servant was 
become almost speechless, and i began to feel the effects of the cold 
myself, before we reach the wished-for cottage. it was a small but 
neat building: as we drew near it, i rejoice at observing through the 
window the blaze of a comfortable fire. our conductor knock at the 
door: it was some time before any one answered; the people within 
seem in doubt whether we should be admitted. 

'come! come, friend baptiste!' cry the driver with impatience; 'what 
are you about? are you asleep? or will you refuse a night's lodging 
to a gentleman, whose chaise has just broken down in the forest?' 

'ah! is it you, honest claude?' reply a man's voice from within; 
'wait a moment, and the door shall be opened.' 

soon after the bolt were drawn back. the door was unclosed, and a man 
present himself to us with a lamp in his hand. he gave the guide an 
hearty reception, and then address himself to me. 

'walk in, monsieur; walk in, and welcome! excuse me for not admit 
you at first: but there are so many rogue about this place, that 
saving your presence, i suspect you to be one.' 

thus saying, he usher me into the room, where i had observe the 
fire: i was immediately placed in an easy chair, which stood close to 
the hearth. a female, whom i supposed to be the wife of my host, rose 
from her seat upon my entrance, and received me with a slight and 
distant reverence. she made no answer to my compliment, but 
immediately re-seating herself, continue the work on which she had 
been employed. her husband's manners were as friendly as hers were 
harsh and repulsive. 

'i wish, i could lodge you more conveniently, monsieur,' said he; 'but 
we cannot boast of much spare room in this hovel. however, a chamber 
for yourself, and another for your servant, i think, we can make shift 
to supply. you must content yourself with sorry fare; but to what we 
have, believe me, you are heartily welcome.' ----then turning to his 
wife--'why, how you sit there, marguerite, with as much tranquill as 
if you had nothing better to do! stir about, dame! stir about! get 
some supper; look out some sheets; here, here; throw some log upon the 
fire, for the gentleman seem perish with cold.' 

the wife threw her work hasty upon the table, and proceed to 
execute his command with every mark of unwillingness. her countenance 
had displease me on the first moment of my examine it. yet upon the 
whole her features were handsome unquestionably; but her skin was 
sallow, and her person thin and meagre; a lour gloom over-spread her 
countenance; and it bore such visible mark of rancour and ill-will, as 
could not escape being notice by the most inattentive observer. her 
every look and action express discontent and impatience, and the 
answer which she gave baptiste, when he reproach her good-humouredly 
for her dissatisfy air, were tart, short, and cutting. in tine, i 
conceive at first sight equal disgust for her, and prepossession in 
favour of her husband, whose appearance was calculate to inspire 
esteem and confidence. his countenance was open, sincere, and 
friendly; his manners had all the peasant's honesty unaccompanied by 
his rudeness; his cheek were broad, full, and ruddy; and in the 
solidity of his person he seem to offer an ample apology for the 
lean of his wife's. from the wrinkle on his brow i judge him to 
be turn of sixty; but he bore his years well, and seem still hearty 
and strong: the wife could not be more than thirty, but in spirits and 
vivace she was infinite old than the husband. 

however, in spite of her unwillingness, marguerite began to prepare the 
supper, while the wood-man converse gaily on different subjects. the 
postillion, who had been furnish with a bottle of spirits, was now 
ready to set out for strasbourg, and enquired, whether i had any 
further commands. 

'for strasbourg?' interrupt baptiste; 'you are not go thither 
tonight?' 

'i beg your pardon: if i do not fetch workmen to mend the chaise, how 
is monsieur to proceed tomorrow?' 

'that is true, as you say; i had forgotten the chaise. well, but 
claude; you may at least eat your supper here? that can make you lose 
very little time, and monsieur looks too kind-hearted to send you out 
with an empty stomach on such a bitter cold night as this is.' 

to this i readily assented, telling the postillion that my reach 
strasbourg the next day an hour or two later would be perfectly 
immaterial. he thank me, and then leave the cottage with stephano, 
put up his horse in the wood-man's stable. baptiste follow them to 
the door, and look out with anxiety. 

''tis a sharp biting wind!' said he; 'i wonder, what detain my boys so 
long! monsieur, i shall show you two of the finest lads, that ever 
stept in shoe of leather. the eldest is three and twenty, the second a 
year younger: their equal for sense, courage, and activity, are not 
to be found within fifty mile of strasbourg. would they were back 
again! i begin to feel uneasy about them.' 

marguerite was at this time employ in lay the cloth. 

'and are you equally anxious for the return of your sons?' said i to 
her. 

'not i!' she reply peevishly; 'they are no children of mine.' 

'come! come, marguerite!' said the husband; 'do not be out of humour 
with the gentleman for ask a simple question. had you not look so 
cross, he would never have thought you old enough to have a son of 
three and twenty: but you see how many years ill-temper add to 
you!--excuse my wife's rudeness, monsieur. a little thing put her 
out, and she is somewhat displease at your not thinking her to be 
under thirty. that is the truth, is it not, marguerite? you know, 
monsieur, that age is always a ticklish subject with a woman. come! 
come! marguerite, clear up a little. if you have not son as old, you 
will some twenty years hence, and i hope, that we shall live to see 
them just such lad as jacque and robert.' 

marguerite clasp her hand together passionately. 

'god forbid!' said she; 'god forbid! if i thought it, i would strangle 
them with my own hands!' 

she quit the room hastily, and went up stairs. 

i could not help express to the wood-man how much i pity him for 
being chain for life to a partner of such ill-humour. 

'ah! lord! monsieur, every one has his share of grievances, and 
marguerite has fallen to mine. besides, after all she is only cross, 
and not malicious. the worst is, that her affection for two children 
by a former husband make her play the step-mother with my two sons. 
she cannot bear the sight of them, and by her good-will they would 
never set a foot within my door. but on this point i always stand 
firm, and never will consent to abandon the poor lad to the world's 
mercy, as she has often solicit me to do. in every thing else i let 
her have her own way; and truly she manage a family rarely, that i 
must say for her.' 

we were converse in this manner, when our discourse was interrupt 
by a loud halloo, which rang through the forest. 

'my sons, i hope!' exclaim the wood-man, and ran to open the door. 

the halloo was repeated: we now distinguished the trample of horses, 
and soon after a carriage, attend by several cavalier stop at the 
cottage door. one of the horsemen enquire how far they were still 
from strasbourg. as he address himself to me, i answer in the 
number of mile which claude had told me; upon which a volley of curse 
was vent against the driver for have lost their way. the person 
in the coach were now informed of the distance of strasbourg, and also 
that the horse were so fatigue as to be incapable of proceeding 
further. a lady, who appear to be the principal, express much 
chagrin at this intelligence; but as there was no remedy, one of the 
attendant ask the wood-man, whether he could furnish them with 
lodging for the night. 

he seem much embarrassed, and reply in the negative; add that a 
spain gentleman and his servant were already in possession of the 
only spare apartments in his house. on hearing this, the gallantry of 
my nation would not permit me to retain those accommodations, of which 
a female was in want. i instantly signify to the wood-man, that i 
transfer my right to the lady; he made some objections; but i 
overrule them, and hasten to the carriage, open the door, and 
assist the lady to descend. i immediately recognize her for the 
same person whom i had seen at the inn at luneville. i took an 
opportunity of ask one of her attendants, what was her name? 

'the baroness lindenberg,' was the answer. 

i could not but remark how different a reception our host had given 
these newcomer and myself. his reluctance to admit them was visibly 
express on his countenance, and he prevail on himself with 
difficulty to tell the lady that she was welcome. i conduct her into 
the house, and placed her in the armed-chair, which i had just quitted. 
she thank me very graciously; and made a thousand apology for 
putt me to an inconvenience. sudden the wood-man's countenance 
clear up. 

'at last i have arrange it!' said he, interrupt her excuses; 'i can 
lodge you and your suite, madam, and you will not be under the 
necessity of making this gentleman suffer for his politeness. 

we have two spare chambers, one for the lady, the other, monsieur, for 
you: my wife shall give up hers to the two waiting-women; as for the 
men-servants, they must content themselves with passing the night in a 
large barn, which stand at a few yard distance from the house. there 
they shall have a blazing fire, and as good a supper as we can make 
shift to give them.' 

after several expression of gratitude on the lady's part, and 
opposition on mine to marguerite's give up her bed, this arrangement 
was agree to. as the room was small, the baroness immediately 
dismiss her male domestics: baptiste was on the point of conduct 
them to the barn which he had mention when two young men appear at 
the door of the cottage. 

'hell and furies!' exclaim the first start back; 'robert, the 
house is fill with strangers!' 

'ha! there are my sons!' cry our host. 'why, jacques! robert! 
whither are you running, boys? there is room enough still for you.' 

upon this assurance the youth returned. the father present them to 
the baroness and myself: after which he withdrew with our domestics, 
while at the request of the two waiting-women, marguerite conduct 
them to the room design for their mistress. 

the two new-comers were tall, stout, well-made young men, 
hard-featured, and very much sun-burnt. they paid their compliments to 
us in few words, and acknowledge claude, who now enter the room, as 
an old acquaintance. they then threw aside their cloak in which they 
were wrap up, took off a leathern belt to which a large cutlass was 
suspended, and each drawing a brace of pistol from his girdle laid 
them upon a shelf. 

'you travel well-armed,' said i. 

'true, monsieur;' reply robert. 'we left strasbourg late this 
evening, and 'tis necessary to take precaution at passing through this 
forest after dark. it do not bear a good repute, i promise you.' 

'how?' said the baroness; 'are there robber hereabout?' 

'so it is said, madame; for my own part, i have travelled through the 
wood at all hours, and never met with one of them.' 

here marguerite returned. her stepson drew her to the other end of 
the room, and whisper her for some minutes. by the looks which they 
cast towards us at intervals, i conjecture them to be enquiring our 
business in the cottage. 

in the meanwhile the baroness express her apprehensions, that her 
husband would be suffering much anxiety upon her account. she had 
intended to send on one of her servant to inform the baron of her 
delay; but the account which the young men gave of the forest render 
this plan impracticable. claude relieved her from her embarrassment. 
he informed her that he was under the necessity of reach strasbourg 
that night, and that would she trust him with a letter, she might 
depend upon its being safe delivered. 

'and how come it,' said i, 'that you are under no apprehension of 
meeting these robbers?' 

'alas! monsieur, a poor man with a large family must not lose certain 
profit because 'tis attend with a little danger, and perhaps my lord 
the baron may give me a trifle for my pains. besides, i have nothing to 
lose except my life, and that will not be worth the robber taking.' 

i thought his argument bad, and advise his wait till the morning; 
but as the baroness did not second me, i was oblige to give up the 
point. the baroness lindenberg, as i found afterwards, had long been 
accustomed to sacrifice the interests of other to her own, and her 
wish to send claude to strasbourg blind her to the danger of the 
undertaking. accordingly, it was resolve that he should set out 
without delay. the baroness wrote her letter to her husband, and i 
sent a few lines to my banker, apprise him that i should not be at 
strasbourg till the next day. claude took our letters, and left the 
cottage. 

the lady declared herself much fatigue by her journey: besides have 
come from some distance, the driver had contrived to lose their way in 
the forest. she now address herself to marguerite, desire to be 
shown to her chamber, and permit to take half an hour's repose. one 
of the waiting-women was immediately summoned; she appear with a 
light, and the baroness follow her up stairs. the cloth was 
spread in the chamber where i was, and marguerite soon gave me to 
understand that i was in her way. her hint were too broad to be 
easily mistaken; i therefore desire one of the young men to conduct me 
to the chamber where i was to sleep, and where i could remain till 
supper was ready. 

'which chamber is it, mother?' said robert. 

'the one with green hangings,' she replied; 'i have just been at the 
trouble of get it ready, and have put fresh sheet upon the bed; if 
the gentleman choose to lollop and lounge upon it, he may make it 
again himself for me.' 

'you are out of humour, mother, but that is no novelty. have the 
goodness to follow me, monsieur.' 

he open the door, and advanced towards a narrow staircase. 

'you have got no light!' said marguerite; 'is it your own neck or the 
gentleman's that you have a mind to break?' 

she cross by me, and put a candle into robert's hand, have received 
which, he began to ascend the staircase. jacque was employ in 
lay the cloth, and his back was turn towards me. 

marguerite seize the moment, when we were unobserved. she caught my 
hand, and pressed it strongly. 

'look at the sheets!' said she as she pass me, and immediately 
resume her former occupation. 

startle by the abrupt of her action, i remain as if petrified. 
robert's voice, desire me to follow him, recall me to myself. i 
ascend the staircase. my conductor usher me into a chamber, where 
an excellent wood-fire was blazing upon the hearth. he placed the 
light upon the table, enquire whether i had any further commands, and 
on my reply in the negative, he left me to myself. you may be 
certain that the moment when i found myself alone was that on which i 
comply with marguerite's injunction. i took the candle, hasty 
approach the bed, and turn down the coverture. what was my 
astonishment, my horror, at finding the sheet crimson with blood! 

at that moment a thousand confuse idea pass before my imagination. 
the robber who infest the wood, marguerite's exclamation respecting 
her children, the arms and appearance of the two young men, and the 
various anecdote which i had heard related, respecting the secret 
correspondence which frequent exist between banditti and 
postillions, all these circumstances flash upon my mind, and inspired 
me with doubt and apprehension. i ruminate on the most probable means 
of ascertain the truth of my conjectures. sudden i was aware of 
someone below pace hasty backwards and forwards. every thing now 
appear to me an object of suspicion. with precaution i drew near the 
window, which, as the room had been long shut up, was left open in 
spite of the cold. i venture to look out. the beam of the moon 
permit me to distinguish a man, whom i had no difficulty to 
recognize for my host. i watch his movements. 

he walk swiftly, then stopped, and seem to listen: he stamp upon 
the ground, and beat his stomach with his arms as if to guard himself 
from the inclement of the season. at the least noise, if a voice was 
heard in the lower part of the house, if a bat flit past him, or the 
wind rattle amidst the leafless boughs, he started, and look round 
with anxiety. 

'plague take him!' said he at length with impatience; 'what can he be 
about!' 

he spoke in a low voice; but as he was just below my window, i had no 
difficulty to distinguish his words. 

i now heard the steps of one approaching. baptiste went towards the 
sound; he join a man, whom his low stature and the horn suspend 
from his neck, declared to be no other than my faithful claude, whom i 
had supposed to be already on his way to strasbourg. expect their 
discourse to throw some light upon my situation, i hasten to put 
myself in a condition to hear it with safety. for this purpose i 
extinguish the candle, which stood upon a table near the bed: the 
flame of the fire was not strong enough to betray me, and i immediately 
resume my place at the window. 

the object of my curiosity had station themselves directly under it. 
i suppose that during my momentary absence the wood-man had been 
blame claude for tardiness, since when i return to the window, the 
latter was endeavour to excuse his fault. 

'however,' add he, 'my diligence at present shall make up for my past 
delay.' 

'on that condition,' answer baptiste, 'i shall readily forgive you. 
but in truth as you share equally with us in our prizes, your own 
interest will make you use all possible diligence. 'twould be a shame 
to let such a noble booty escape us! you say, that this spaniard is 
rich?' 

'his servant boast at the inn, that the effects in his chaise were 
worth above two thousand pistoles.' 

oh! how i cursed stephano's imprudent vanity! 

'and i have been told,' continue the postillion, 'that this baroness 
carry about her a casket of jewel of immense value.' 

'may be so, but i had rather she had stay away. the spaniard was a 
secure prey. the boys and myself could easily have master him and 
his servant, and then the two thousand pistol would have been share 
between us four. now we must let in the band for a share, and perhaps 
the whole covey may escape us. should our friends have betaken 
themselves to their different post before you reach the cavern, all 
will be lost. the lady's attendant are too numerous for us to 
overpower them: unless our associate arrive in time, we must needs 
let these traveller set out tomorrow without damage or hurt.' 

''tis plaguy unlucky that my comrade who drove the coach should be 
those unacquainted with our confederacy! but never fear, friend 
baptiste. an hour will bring me to the cavern; it is now but ten 
o'clock, and by twelve you may expect the arrival of the band. by the 
bye, take care of your wife: you know how strong is her repugnance to 
our mode of life, and she may find means to give information to the 
lady's servant of our design.' 

'oh! i am secure of her silence; she is too much afraid of me, and fond 
of her children, to dare to betray my secret. besides, jacque and 
robert keep a strict eye over her, and she is not permit to set a 
foot out of the cottage. the servant are safe lodge in the barn; i 
shall endeavour to keep all quiet till the arrival of our friends. 
were i assured of your finding them, the stranger should be dispatch 
this instant; but as it is possible for you to miss the banditti, i am 
fearful of being summon to produce them by their domestic in the 
morning.' 

'and suppose either of the traveller should discover your design?' 

'then we must poignard those in our power, and take our chance about 
master the rest. however, to avoid running such a risque, hasten to 
the cavern: the banditti never leave it before eleven, and if you use 
diligence, you may reach it in time to stop them.' 

'tell robert that i have taken his horse: my own has broken his 
bridle, and escape into the wood. what is the watch-word?' 

'the reward of courage.' 

''tis sufficient. i hasten to the cavern.' 

'and i to rejoin my guests, lest my absence should create suspicion. 
farewell, and be diligent.' 

these worthy associate now separated: the one bent his course towards 
the stable, while the other return to the house. 

you may judge, what must have been my feelings during this 
conversation, of which i lost not a single syllable. i dare not trust 
myself to my reflections, nor did any means present itself to escape 
the danger which threaten me. resistance, i knew to be vain; i was 
unarmed, and a single man against three: however, i resolve at least 
to sell my life as dearly as i could. dread lest baptiste should 
perceive my absence, and suspect me to have overheard the message with 
which claude was dispatched, i hasty relighte my candle and quit 
the chamber. on descending, i found the table spread for six persons. 
the baroness sat by the fireside: marguerite was employ in dressing a 
sallad, and her step-sons were whisper together at the further end 
of the room. baptiste have the round of the garden to make, ere he 
could reach the cottage door, was not yet arrived. i seat myself 
quiet opposite to the baroness. 

a glance upon marguerite told her that her hint had not been thrown 
away upon me. how different did she now appear to me! what before 
seem gloom and sullenness, i now found to be disgust at her 
associates, and compassion for my danger. i look up to her as to my 
only resource; yet knowing her to be watch by her husband with a 
suspicious eye, i could place but little reliance on the exertion of 
her good-will. 

in spite of all my endeavour to conceal it, my agitation was but too 
visibly express upon my countenance. i was pale, and both my words 
and action were disorder and embarrassed. the young men observe 
this, and enquire the cause. i attribute it to excess of fatigue, 
and the violent effect produce on me by the severity of the season. 
whether they believe me or not, i will not pretend to say: they at 
least cease to embarrass me with their questions. i strove to divert 
my attention from the peril which surround me, by converse on 
different subject with the baroness. i talk of germany, declare 
my intention of visiting it immediately: god knows, that i little 
thought at that moment of ever seeing it! she reply to me with great 
ease and politeness, professed that the pleasure of making my 
acquaintance ample compensate for the delay in her journey, and gave 
me a pressing invitation to make some stay at the castle of lindenberg. 
as she spoke thus, the youth exchange a malicious smile, which 
declared that she would be fortunate if she ever reach that castle 
herself. this action did not escape me; but i conceal the emotion 
which it excited in my breast. i continue to converse with the lady; 
but my discourse was so frequent incoherent, that as she has since 
informed me, she began to doubt whether i was in my right senses. the 
fact was, that while my conversation turn upon one subject, my 
thought were entire occupy by another. i meditate upon the means 
of quit the cottage, finding my way to the barn, and give the 
domestic information of our host's designs. i was soon convinced, how 
impracticable was the attempt. jacque and robert watch my every 
movement with an attentive eye, and i was oblige to abandon the idea. 
all my hope now rest upon claude's not finding the banditti: in 
that case, according to what i had overheard, we should be permit to 
depart unhurt. 

i shudder involuntary as baptiste enter the room. he made many 
apology for his long absence, but 'he had been detain by affair 
impossible to be delayed.' he then entreat permission for his family 
to sup at the same table with us, without which, respect would not 
authorize his taking such a liberty. oh! how in my heart i cursed the 
hypocrite! how i loathe his presence, who was on the point of 
deprive me of an existence, at that time infinite dear! i had 
every reason to be satisfy with life; i had youth, wealth, rank, and 
education; and the fairest prospects present themselves before me. i 
saw those prospects on the point of close in the most horrible 
manner: yet was i oblige to dissimulate, and to receive with a 
semblance of gratitude the false civility of him who held the dagger 
to my bosom. 

the permission which our host demanded, was easily obtained. we seat 
ourselves at the table. the baroness and myself occupy one side: 
the son were opposite to us with their back to the door. baptiste 
took his seat by the baroness at the upper end, and the place next to 
him was left for his wife. she soon enter the room, and placed 
before us a plain but comfortable peasant's repast. our host thought 
it necessary to apologize for the poorness of the supper: 'he had not 
been apprize of our coming; he could only offer us such fare as had 
been intended for his own family:' 

'but,' add he, 'should any accident detain my noble guest long 
than they at present intend, i hope to give them a better treatment.' 

the villain! i well knew the accident to which he alluded; i shudder 
at the treatment which he taught us to expect! 

my companion in danger seem entire to have got rid of her chagrin 
at being delayed. she laughed, and converse with the family with 
infinite gaiety. i strove but in vain to follow her example. my 
spirits were evidently forced, and the constraint which i put upon 
myself escape not baptiste's observation. 

'come, come, monsieur, cheer up!' said he; 'you seem not quite 
recover from your fatigue. to raise your spirits, what say you to a 
glass of excellent old wine which was left me by my father? god rest 
his soul, he is in a better world! i seldom produce this wine; but as 
i am not honour with such guest every day, this is an occasion which 
deserve a bottle.' 

he then gave his wife a key, and instruct her where to find the wine 
of which he spoke. she seem by no means pleased with the commission; 
she took the key with an embarrass air, and hesitate to quit the 
table. 

'did you hear me?' said baptiste in an angry tone. 

marguerite dart upon him a look of mingle anger and fear, and left 
the chamber. his eyes follow her suspiciously, till she had closed 
the door. 

she soon return with a bottle seal with yellow wax. she placed it 
upon the table, and gave the key back to her husband. i suspect that 
this liquor was not present to us without design, and i watch 
marguerite's movements with inquietude. she was employ in rinse 
some small horn goblets. as she placed them before baptiste, she saw 
that my eye was fixed upon her; and at the moment when she thought 
herself unobserved by the banditti, she motion to me with her head 
not to taste the liquor, she then resume her place. 

in the mean while our host had drawn the cork, and filling two of the 
goblets, offer them to the lady and myself. she at first made some 
objections, but the instance of baptiste were so urgent, that she was 
oblige to comply. fear to excite suspicion, i hesitate not to 
take the goblet present to me. by its smell and colour i guess it 
to be champagne; but some grain of powder floating upon the top 
convinced me that it was not unadulterated. however, i dare not to 
express my repugnance to drink it; i lift it to my lips, and 
seem to be swallow it: sudden start from my chair, i made 
the best of my way towards a vase of water at some distance, in which 
marguerite had been rinse the goblets. i pretended to spit out the 
wine with disgust, and took an opportunity unperceived of empty the 
liquor into the vase. 

the banditti seem alarm at my action. jacque half rose from his 
chair, put his hand into his bosom, and i discover the haft of a 
dagger. i return to my seat with tranquillity, and affected not to 
have observe their confusion. 

'you have not suit my taste, honest friend,' said i, address 
myself to baptiste. 'i never can drink champagne without its produce 
a violent illness. i swallow a few mouthful ere i was aware of its 
quality, and fear that i shall suffer for my imprudence.' 

baptiste and jacque exchange looks of distrust. 

'perhaps,' said robert, 'the smell may be disagreeable to you.' 

he quit his chair, and remove the goblet. i observed, that he 
examined, whether it was nearly empty. 

'he must have drank sufficient,' said he to his brother in a low voice, 
while he reseat himself. 

marguerite look apprehensive, that i had taste the liquor: a glance 
from my eye reassure her. 

i wait with anxiety for the effects which the beverage would produce 
upon the lady. i doubt not but the grain which i had observe were 
poisonous, and lament that it had been impossible for me to warn her 
of the danger. but a few minutes had elapse before i perceive her 
eyes grow heavy; her head sank upon her shoulder, and she fell into a 
deep sleep. i affected not to attend to this circumstance, and 
continue my conversation with baptiste, with all the outward gaiety in 
my power to assume. but he no long answer me without constraint. 
he eye me with distrust and astonishment, and i saw that the banditti 
were frequent whisper among themselves. my situation became every 
moment more painful; i sustain the character of confidence with a 
worse grace than ever. equally afraid of the arrival of their 
accomplice and of their suspect my knowledge of their designs, i 
knew not how to dissipate the distrust which the banditti evidently 
entertain for me. in this new dilemma the friendly marguerite again 
assist me. she pass behind the chair of her stepsons, stop for 
a moment opposite to me, closed her eyes, and recline her head upon 
her shoulder. this hint immediately dispel my incertitude. it told 
me, that i ought to imitate the baroness, and pretend that the liquor 
had taken its full effect upon me. i did so, and in a few minutes 
seem perfectly overcome with slumber. 

'so!' cry baptiste, as i fell back in my chair; 'at last he sleeps! 
i began to think that he had scent our design, and that we should 
have been forced to dispatch him at all events.' 

'and why not dispatch him at all events?' enquire the ferocious 
jacques. 'why leave him the possibility of betray our secret? 
marguerite, give me one of my pistols: a single touch of the trigger 
will finish him at once.' 

'and supposing,' rejoin the father, 'supposing that our friends 
should not arrive tonight, a pretty figure we should make when the 
servant enquire for him in the morning! no, no, jacques; we must wait 
for our associates. if they join us, we are strong enough to dispatch 
the domestic as well as their masters, and the booty is our own; if 
claude do not find the troop, we must take patience, and suffer the 
prey to slip through our fingers. ah! boys, boys, had you arrive but 
five minutes sooner, the spaniard would have been done for, and two 
thousand pistol our own. but you are always out of the way when you 
are most wanted. 

you are the most unlucky rogues!' 

'well, well, father!' answer jacques; 'had you been of my mind, all 
would have been over by this time. you, robert, claude, and myself, why 
the stranger were but double the number, and i warrant you we might 
have master them. however, claude is gone; 'tis too late to think of 
it now. we must wait patient for the arrival of the gang; and if the 
traveller escape us tonight, we must take care to waylay them 
tomorrow.' 

'true! true!' said baptiste; 'marguerite, have you given the 
sleeping-draught to the waiting-women?' 

she reply in the affirmative. 

'all then is safe. come, come, boys; whatever falls out, we have no 
reason to complain of this adventure. we run no danger, may gain much, 
and can lose nothing.' 

at this moment i heard a trample of horses. oh! how dreadful was the 
sound to my ears. a cold sweat flow down my forehead, and i felt all 
the terror of impending death. i was by no means reassure by hearing 
the compassionate marguerite exclaim in the accent of despair, 

'almighty god! they are lost!' 

lucky the wood-man and his son were too much occupy by the arrival 
of their associate to attend to me, or the violence of my agitation 
would have convinced them that my sleep was feigned. 

'open! open!' exclaim several voice on the outside of the cottage. 

'yes! yes!' cry baptiste joyfully; 'they are our friends sure 
enough! now then our booty is certain. away! lads, away! lead them 
to the barn; you know what is to be done there.' 

robert hasten to open the door of the cottage. 

'but first,' said jacques, taking up his arms; 'first let me dispatch 
these sleepers.' 

'no, no, no!' reply his father; 'go you to the barn, where your 
presence is wanted. leave me to take care of these and the women 
above.' 

jacque obeyed, and follow his brother. they seem to converse with 
the new-comers for a few minutes: after which i heard the robber 
dismount, and as i conjectured, bend their course towards the barn. 

'so! that is wise done!' mutter baptiste; 'they have quit their 
horses, that they may fall upon the stranger by surprise. good! good! 
and now to business.' 

i heard him approach a small cupboard which was fixed up in a distant 
part of the room, and unlock it. at this moment i felt myself shaken 
gently. 

'now! now!' whisper marguerite. 

i open my eyes. baptiste stood with his back towards me. no one 
else was in the room save marguerite and the sleep lady. the villain 
had taken a dagger from the cupboard and seem examine whether it 
was sufficient sharp. i had neglect to furnish myself with arms; 
but i perceive this to be my only chance of escaping, and resolve not 
to lose the opportunity. i sprang from my seat, dart sudden upon 
baptiste, and clasp my hand round his throat, pressed it so 
forcibly as to prevent his utter a single cry. you may remember 
that i was remarkable at salamanca for the power of my arm: it now 
render me an essential service. surprised, terrified, and 
breathless, the villain was by no means an equal antagonist. i threw 
him upon the ground; i grasp him still tighter; and while i fixed him 
without motion upon the floor, marguerite, wrest the dagger from his 
hand, plunge it repeatedly in his heart till he expired. 

no soon was this horrible but necessary act perpetrate than 
marguerite call on me to follow her. 

'flight is our only refuge!' said she; 'quick! quick! away!' 

i hesitate not to obey her: but unwill to leave the baroness a 
victim to the vengeance of the robbers, i raise her in my arms still 
sleeping, and hasten after marguerite. the horse of the banditti 
were fasten near the door: my conductress sprang upon one of them. 
i follow her example, placed the baroness before me, and spur on 
my horse. our only hope was to reach strasbourg, which was much near 
than the perfidious claude had assured me. marguerite was well 
acquaint with the road, and gallop on before me. we were oblige 
to pass by the barn, where the robber were slaughter our domestics. 
the door was open: we distinguished the shriek of the die and 
imprecation of the murderers! what i felt at that moment language is 
unable to describe! 

jacque heard the trample of our horse as we rush by the barn. he 
flew to the door with a burning torch in his hand, and easily 
recognise the fugitives. 

'betrayed! betrayed!' he shout to his companions. 

instantly they left their bloody work, and hasten to regain their 
horses. we heard no more. i bury my spur in the side of my 
courser, and marguerite goad on hers with the poignard, which had 
already render us such good service. we flew like lightning, and 
gain the open plains. already was strasbourg's steeple in sight, 
when we heard the robber pursue us. marguerite look back, and 
distinguished our follower descend a small hill at no great 
distance. it was in vain that we urge on our horses; the noise 
approach near with every moment. 

'we are lost!' she exclaimed; 'the villain gain upon us!' 

'on! on!' reply i; 'i hear the trample of horse coming from the 
town.' 

we redouble our exertions, and were soon aware of a numerous band of 
cavaliers, who came towards us at full speed. they were on the point 
of passing us. 

'stay! stay!' shriek marguerite; 'save us! for god's sake, save us!' 

the foremost, who seem to act as guide, immediately rein in his 
steed. 

''tis she! 'tis she!' exclaim he, spring upon the ground; 'stop, 
my lord, stop! they are safe! 'tis my mother!' 

at the same moment marguerite threw herself from her horse, clasp him 
in her arms, and cover him with kisses. the other cavalier stop 
at the exclamation. 

'the baroness lindenberg?' cry another of the stranger eagerly; 
'where is she? is she not with you?' 

he stop on behold her lie senseless in my arms. hasty he 
caught her from me. the profound sleep in which she was plunge made 
him at first tremble for her life; but the beating of her heart soon 
reassure him. 

'god be thanked!' said he; 'she has escape unhurt.' 

i interrupt his joy by point out the brigands, who continue to 
approach. no soon had i mention them than the greatest part of the 
company, which appear to be chiefly compose of soldiers, hasten 
forward to meet them. the villain stay not to receive their attack: 
perceive their danger they turn the head of their horses, and flee 
into the wood, whither they were follow by our preservers. in the 
mean while the stranger, whom i guess to be the baron lindenberg, 
after thank me for my care of his lady, propose our return with 
all speed to the town. the baroness, on whom the effects of the opiate 
had not cease to operate, was placed before him; marguerite and her 
son remount their horses; the baron's domestic followed, and we soon 
arrive at the inn, where he had taken his apartments. 

this was at the austria eagle, where my banker, whom before my 
quit paris i had apprise of my intention to visit strasbourg, had 
prepared lodgings for me. i rejoice at this circumstance. it gave me 
an opportunity of cultivate the baron's acquaintance, which i foresaw 
would be of use to me in germany. immediately upon our arrival the 
lady was convey to bed; a physician was sent for, who prescribed a 
medicine likely to counteract the effects of the sleepy potion, and 
after it had been pour down her throat, she was committed to the care 
of the hostess. the baron then address himself to me, and entreat 
me to recount the particulars of this adventure. i comply with his 
request instantaneously; for in pain respecting stephano's fate, whom i 
had been compel to abandon to the cruelty of the banditti, i found 
it impossible for me to repose, till i had some news of him. i 
received but too soon the intelligence, that my trusty servant had 
perished. the soldier who had pursue the brigand return while i 
was employ in relate my adventure to the baron. by their account i 
found that the robber had been overtaken: guilt and true courage are 
incompatible; they had thrown themselves at the feet of their pursuers, 
had surrender themselves without striking a blow, had discover 
their secret retreat, made known their signal by which the rest of the 
gang might be seized, and in short had betray ever mark of cowardice 
and baseness. by this means the whole of the band, consist of near 
sixty persons, had been made prisoners, bound, and conduct to 
strasbourg. some of the soldier hasten to the cottage, one of the 
banditti serving them as guide. their first visit was to the fatal 
barn, where they were fortunate enough to find two of the baron's 
servant still alive, though desperate wounded. the rest had expire 
beneath the sword of the robbers, and of these my unhappy stephano was 
one. 

alarm at our escape, the robber in their haste to overtake us, had 
neglect to visit the cottage. in consequence, the soldier found the 
two waiting-women unhurt, and bury in the same death-like slumber 
which had overpower their mistress. there was nobody else found in 
the cottage, except a child not above four years old, which the 
soldier brought away with them. we were busy ourselves with 
conjecture respecting the birth of this little unfortunate, when 
marguerite rush into the room with the baby in her arms. she fell at 
the feet of the officer who was making us this report, and blessed him 
a thousand times for the preservation of her child. 

when the first burst of maternal tender was over, i besought her to 
declare, by what means she had been united to a man whose principles 
seem so total discordant with her own. she bent her eyes 
downwards, and wipe a few tear from her cheek. 

'gentlemen,' said she after a silence of some minutes, 'i would request 
a favour of you: you have a right to know on whom you confer an 
obligation. i will not therefore stifle a confession which cover me 
with shame; but permit me to comprise it in as few words as possible. 

'i was born in strasbourg of respectable parents; their name i must at 
present conceal: my father still lives, and deserve not to be 
involved in my infamy; if you grant my request, you shall be informed 
of my family name. a villain made himself master of my affections, and 
to follow him i quit my father's house. yet though my passion 
overpower my virtue, i sank not into that degeneracy of vice, but too 
commonly the lot of women who make the first false step. i love my 
seducer; dearly love him! i was true to his bed; this baby, and the 
youth who warn you, my lord baron, of your lady's danger, are the 
pledge of our affection. even at this moment i lament his loss, 
though 'tis to him that i owe all the misery of my existence. 

'he was of noble birth, but he had squander away his paternal 
inheritance. his relations considered him as a disgrace to their name, 
and utterly discard him. his excesses drew upon him the indignation 
of the police. he was oblige to fly from strasbourg, and saw no other 
resource from beggary than an union with the banditti who infest the 
neighbouring forest, and whose troop was chiefly compose of young men 
of family in the same predicament with himself. i was determined not 
to forsake him. i follow him to the cavern of the brigands, and 
share with him the misery inseparable from a life of pillage. but 
though i was aware that our existence was support by plunder, i knew 
not all the horrible circumstances attach to my lover's profession. 
these he conceal from me with the utmost care; he was conscious that 
my sentiment were not sufficient deprave to look without horror 
upon assassination: he supposed, and with justice, that i should fly 
with detest from the embrace of a murderer. eight years of 
possession had not abate his love for me; and he cautious remove 
from my knowledge every circumstance, which might lead me to suspect 
the crime in which he but too often participated. he succeed 
perfectly: it was not till after my seducer's death, that i discover 
his hand to have been stain with the blood of innocence. 

'one fatal night he was brought back to the cavern cover with wounds: 
he received them in attack an english traveller, whom his companion 
immediately sacrifice to their resentment. he had only time to 
entreat my pardon for all the sorrow which he had cause me: he 
pressed my hand to his lips, and expired. my grief was inexpressible. 
as soon as its violence abated, i resolve to return to strasbourg, to 
throw myself with my two children at my father's feet, and implore his 
forgiveness, though i little hope to obtain it. what was my 
consternation when informed that no one entrust with the secret of 
their retreat was ever permit to quit the troop of the banditti; 
that i must give up all hope of ever rejoin society, and consent 
instantly to accept one of their band for my husband! my prayer 
and remonstrance were vain. they cast lot to decide to whose 
possession i should fall; i became the property of the infamous 
baptiste. a robber, who had once been a monk, pronounced over us a 
burlesque rather than a religious ceremony: i and my children were 
deliver into the hand of my new husband, and he convey us 
immediately to his home. 

'he assured me that he had long entertain for me the most ardent 
regard; but that friendship for my deceased lover had oblige him to 
stifle his desires. he endeavour to reconcile me to my fate, and for 
some time treat me with respect and gentleness: at length finding 
that my aversion rather increase than diminished, he obtain those 
favours by violence, which i persist to refuse him. no resource 
remain for me but to bear my sorrow with patience; i was conscious 
that i deserve them but too well. flight was forbidden: my children 
were in the power of baptiste, and he had sworn that if i attempt to 
escape, their lives should pay for it. i had had too many 
opportunity of witness the barbarous of his nature to doubt his 
fulfill his oath to the very letter. sad experience had convinced 
me of the horrors of my situation: my first lover had careful 
conceal them from me; baptiste rather rejoice in opening my eyes to 
the cruelty of his profession, and strove to familiarise me with 
blood and slaughter. 

'my nature was licentious and warm, but not cruel: my conduct had been 
imprudent, but my heart was not unprincipled. judge then what i must 
have felt at being a continual witness of crime the most horrible and 
revolting! judge how i must have grieve at being united to a man who 
received the unsuspect guest with an air of open and 
hospitality, at the very moment that he meditate his destruction. 
chagrin and discontent prey upon my constitution: the few charm 
bestow on me by nature wither away, and the dejection of my 
countenance denote the suffering of my heart. i was tempt a 
thousand times to put an end to my existence; but the remembrance of my 
children held my hand. i tremble to leave my dear boys in my tyrant's 
power, and tremble yet more for their virtue than their lives. the 
second was still too young to benefit by my instructions; but in the 
heart of my eldest i labour unceasing to plant those principles, 
which might enable him to avoid the crime of his parents. he listen 
to me with docility, or rather with eagerness. even at his early age, 
he show that he was not calculate for the society of villains; and 
the only comfort which i enjoy among my sorrows, was to witness the 
dawn virtue of my theodore. 

'such was my situation, when the perfidy of don alphonso's postillion 
conduct him to the cottage. his youth, air, and manners interested 
me most forcibly in his behalf. the absence of my husband's son gave 
me an opportunity which i had long wish to find, and i resolve to 
risque every thing to preserve the stranger. the vigilance of baptiste 
prevent me from warning don alphonso of his danger: i knew that my 
betray the secret would be immediately punish with death; and 
however embitter was my life by calamities, i want courage to 
sacrifice it for the sake of preserve that of another person. my only 
hope rest upon procure succour from strasbourg: at this i resolve 
to try; and should an opportunity offer of warning don alphonso of his 
danger unobserved, i was determined to seize it with avidity. by 
baptiste's orders i went upstairs to make the stranger's bed: i spread 
upon it sheet in which a traveller had been murder but a few nights 
before, and which still were stain with blood. i hope that these 
mark would not escape the vigilance of our guest, and that he would 
collect from them the designs of my perfidious husband. neither was 
this the only step which i took to preserve the stranger. theodore was 
confine to his bed by illness. i stole into his room unobserved by my 
tyrant, communicate to him my project, and he enter into it with 
eagerness. he rose in spite of his malady, and dress himself with 
all speed. i fasten one of the sheet round his arms, and lower 
him from the window. he flew to the stable, took claude's horse, and 
hasten to strasbourg. had he been accost by the banditti, he was 
to have declared himself sent upon a message by baptiste, but 
fortunately he reach the town without meeting any obstacle. 
immediately upon his arrival at strasbourg, he entreat assistance 
from the magistrature: his story pass from mouth to mouth, and at 
length came to the knowledge of my lord the baron. anxious for the 
safety of his lady, whom he knew would be upon the road that evening, 
it struck him that she might have fallen into the power of the robbers. 
he accompany theodore who guide the soldier towards the cottage, 
and arrive just in time to save us from fall once more into the 
hand of our enemies.' 

here i interrupt marguerite to enquire why the sleepy potion had been 
present to me. she said that baptiste supposed me to have arms about 
me, and wish to incapacitate me from making resistance: it was a 
precaution which he always took, since as the traveller had no hope 
of escaping, despair would have incite them to sell their lives dearly. 

the baron then desire marguerite to inform him, what were her present 
plans. i join him in declare my readiness to show my gratitude to 
her for the preservation of my life. 

'disgusted with a world,' she replied, 'in which i have met with 
nothing but misfortunes, my only wish is to retire into a convent. but 
first i must provide for my children. i find that my mother is no 
more, probably driven to an untimely grave by my desertion! my father 
is still living; he is not an hard man; perhaps, gentlemen, in spite of 
my ingratitude and imprudence, your intercession may induce him to 
forgive me, and to take charge of his unfortunate grand-sons. if you 
obtain this boon for me, you will repay my service a thousand-fold!' 

both the baron and myself assured marguerite, that we would spare no 
pains to obtain her pardon: and that even should her father be 
inflexible, she need be under no apprehension respecting the fate of 
her children. i engaged myself to provide for theodore, and the baron 
promise to take the youngest under his protection. 

the grateful mother thank us with tear for what she call 
generosity, but which in fact was no more than a proper sense of our 
obligation to her. she then left the room to put her little boy to 
bed, whom fatigue and sleep had compleat overpowered. 

the baroness, on recover and being informed from what danger i had 
rescue her, set no bounds to the expression of her gratitude. she 
was join so warm by her husband in pressing me to accompany them to 
their castle in bavaria, that i found it impossible to resist their 
entreaties. during a week which we pass at strasbourg, the interests 
of marguerite were not forgotten: in our application to her father we 
succeed as ample as we could wish. the good old man had lost his 
wife: he had no children but this unfortunate daughter, of whom he had 
received no news for almost fourteen years. he was surround by 
distant relations, who wait with impatience for his decease in order 
to get possession of his money. when therefore marguerite appear 
again so unexpectedly, he considered her as a gift from heaven: he 
received her and her children with open arms, and insist upon their 
establish themselves in his house without delay. the disappointed 
cousin were oblige to give place. the old man would not hear of his 
daughter's retiring into a convent: he said that she was too necessary 
to his happiness, and she was easily persuade to relinquish her 
design. but no persuasion could induce theodore to give up the plan 
which i had at first marked out for him. he had attach himself to me 
most sincerely during my stay at strasbourg; and when i was on the 
point of leave it, he besought me with tear to take him into my 
service: he set forth all his little talent in the most favourable 
colours, and try to convince me that i should find him of infinite 
use to me upon the road. i was unwill to charge myself with a lad 
but scarcely turn of thirteen, whom i knew could only be a burthen to 
me: however, i could not resist the entreaty of this affectionate 
youth, who in fact possessed a thousand estimable qualities. with some 
difficulty he persuade his relations to let him follow me, and that 
permission once obtained, he was dub with the title of my page. 
have pass a week at strasbourg, theodore and myself set out for 
bavaria in company with the baron and his lady. these latter as well 
as myself had forced marguerite to accept several presents of value, 
both for herself, and her youngest son: on leave her, i promise his 
mother faithfully that i would restore theodore to her within the year. 

i have related this adventure at length, lorenzo, that you might 
understand the means by which 'the adventurer, alphonso d'alvarada got 
introduce into the castle of lindenberg.' judge from this specimen 
how much faith should be given to your aunt's assertions!

macbeth. 

continuation of the history of don raymond. 

my journey was uncommonly agreeable: i found the baron a man of some 
sense, but little knowledge of the world. he had past a great part of 
his life without stirring beyond the precincts of his own domains, and 
consequently his manners were far from being the most polished: but he 
was hearty, good-humoured, and friendly. his attention to me was all 
that i could wish, and i had every reason to be satisfy with his 
behaviour. his ruling passion was hunting, which he had brought himself 
to consider as a serious occupation; and when talk over some 
remarkable chace, he treat the subject with as much gravity as it had 
been a battle on which the fate of two kingdom was depending. i 
happen to be a tolerable sportsman: soon after my arrival at 
lindenberg i gave some proof of my dexterity. the baron immediately 
marked me down for a man of genius, and vow to me an eternal 
friendship. 

that friendship was become to me by no means indifferent. at the 
castle of lindenberg i beheld for the first time your sister, the 
lovely agnes. for me whose heart was unoccupied, and who grieve at 
the void, to see her and to love her were the same. i found in agnes 
all that was requisite to secure my affection. she was then scarcely 
sixteen; her person light and elegant was already formed; she possessed 
several talent in perfection, particularly those of music and drawing: 
her character was gay, open, and good-humoured; and the graceful 
simplicity of her dress and manners form an advantageous contrast to 
the art and studied coquetry of the parisian dames, whom i had just 
quitted. from the moment that i beheld her, i felt the most lively 
interest in her fate. i made many enquiry respecting her of the 
baroness. 

'she is my niece,' reply that lady; 'you are still ignorant, don 
alphonso, that i am your countrywoman. i am sister to the duke of 
medina celi: agnes is the daughter of my second brother, don gaston: 
she has been destined to the convent from her cradle, and will soon 
make her profession at madrid.' 

(here lorenzo interrupt the marquis by an exclamation of surprise. 

'intended for the convent from her cradle?' said he; 'by heaven, this 
is the first word that i ever heard of such a design!' 

'i believe it, my dear lorenzo,' answer don raymond; 'but you must 
listen to me with patience. you will not be less surprised, when i 
relate some particulars of your family still unknown to you, and which 
i have learnt from the mouth of agnes herself.' 

he then resume his narrative as follows.) 

you cannot but be aware that your parent were unfortunately slave to 
the grossest superstition: when this foible was call into play, 
their every other sentiment, their every other passion yield to its 
irresistible strength. while she was big with agnes, your mother was 
seize by a dangerous illness, and given over by her physicians. in 
this situation, donna inesilla vowed, that if she recover from her 
malady, the child then living in her bosom if a girl should be 
dedicated to st. clare, if a boy to st. benedict. her prayer were 
heard; she got rid of her complaint; agnes enter the world alive, and 
was immediately destined to the service of st. clare. 

don gaston readily chime in with his lady's wishes: but knowing the 
sentiment of the duke, his brother, respecting a monastic life, it was 
determined that your sister's destination should be careful conceal 
from him. the better to guard the secret, it was resolve that agnes 
should accompany her aunt, donna rodolpha into germany, whither that 
lady was on the point of following her new-married husband, baron 
lindenberg. on her arrival at that estate, the young agnes was put 
into a convent, situated but a few mile from the castle. the nun to 
whom her education was confide perform their charge with exactitude: 
they made her a perfect mistress of many talents, and strove to infuse 
into her mind a taste for the retirement and tranquil pleasure of a 
convent. but a secret instinct made the young recluse sensible that 
she was not born for solitude: in all the freedom of youth and gaiety, 
she scruple not to treat as ridiculous many ceremony which the nun 
regard with awe; and she was never more happy than when her lively 
imagination inspired her with some scheme to plague the stiff lady 
abbess, or the ugly ill-tempered old porteress. she look with 
disgust upon the prospect before her: however no alternative was 
offer to her, and she submit to the decree of her parents, though 
not without secret repining. 

that repugnance she had not art enough to conceal long: don gaston was 
informed of it. alarmed, lorenzo, lest your affection for her should 
oppose itself to his projects, and lest you should positively object to 
your sister's misery, he resolve to keep the whole affair from your 
knowledge as well as the duke's, till the sacrifice should be 
consummated. the season of her taking the veil was fixed for the time 
when you should be upon your travels: in the meanwhile no hint was 
drop of donna inesilla's fatal vow. your sister was never permit 
to know your direction. all your letters were read before she received 
them, and those parts effaced, which were likely to nourish her 
inclination for the world: her answer were dictate either by her 
aunt, or by dame cunegonda, her governess. these particulars i learnt 
partly from agnes, partly from the baroness herself. 

i immediately determined upon rescue this lovely girl from a fate so 
contrary to her inclinations, and ill-suited to her merit. i 
endeavour to ingratiate myself into her favour: i boast of my 
friendship and intimacy with you. she listen to me with avidity; she 
seem to devour my words while i spoke in your praise, and her eyes 
thank me for my affection to her brother. my constant and unremitt 
attention at length gain me her heart, and with difficulty i oblige 
her to confess that she love me. when however, i propose her 
quit the castle of lindenberg, she reject the idea in positive 
terms. 

'be generous, alphonso,' she said; 'you possess my heart, but use not 
the gift ignobly. employ not your ascendancy over me in persuade me 
to take a step, at which i should hereafter have to blush. i am young 
and deserted: my brother, my only friend, is separate from me, and my 
other relations act with me as my enemies. take pity on my unprotected 
situation. instead of seduce me to an action which would cover me 
with shame, strive rather to gain the affection of those who govern 
me. the baron esteem you. my aunt, to other ever harsh proud and 
contemptuous, remember that you rescue her from the hand of 
murderers, and wear with you alone the appearance of kindness and 
benignity. try then your influence over my guardians. if they consent 
to our union my hand is yours: from your account of my brother, i 
cannot doubt your obtain his approbation: and when they find the 
impossible of execute their design, i trust that my parent will 
excuse my disobedience, and expiate by some other sacrifice my mother's 
fatal vow.' 

from the first moment that i beheld agnes, i had endeavour to 
conciliate the favour of her relations. authorise by the confession 
of her regard, i redouble my exertions. my principal battery was 
direct against the baroness; it was easy to discover that her word 
was law in the castle: her husband paid her the most absolute 
submission, and considered her as a superior being. she was about 
forty: in her youth she had been a beauty; but her charm had been 
upon that large scale which can but ill sustain the shock of years: 
however she still possessed some remains of them. her understanding 
was strong and excellent when not obscure by prejudice, which 
unlucki was but seldom the case. her passion were violent: she 
spare no pains to gratify them, and pursue with unremitting vengeance 
those who oppose themselves to her wishes. the warmest of friends, 
the most inveterate of enemies, such was the baroness lindenberg. 

i labour incessant to please her: unlucki i succeed but too 
well. she seem gratify by my attention, and treat me with a 
distinction accord by her to no one else. one of my daily 
occupation was reading to her for several hours: those hour i should 
much rather have past with agnes; but as i was conscious that 
complaisance for her aunt would advance our union, i submit with a 
good grace to the penance impose upon me. donna rodolpha's library 
was principally compose of old spain romances: these were her 
favourite studies, and once a day one of these unmerciful volumes was 
put regularly into my hands. i read the wearisome adventure of 
'perceforest,' 'tirante the white,' 'palmerin of england,' and 'the 
knight of the sun,' till the book was on the point of fall from my 
hand through ennui. however, the increase pleasure which the 
baroness seem to take in my society, encourage me to persevere; and 
latterly she show for me a partiality so marked, that agnes advise 
me to seize the first opportunity of declare our mutual passion to 
her aunt. 

one evening, i was alone with donna rodolpha in her own apartment. as 
our reading generally treat of love, agnes was never permit to 
assist at them. i was just congratulate myself on have finished 
'the love of tristan and the queen iseult----' 

'ah! the unfortunates!' cry the baroness; 'how say you, segnor? do 
you think it possible for man to feel an attachment so disinterested 
and sincere?' 

'i cannot doubt it,' reply i; 'my own heart furnish me with the 
certainty. ah! donna rodolpha, might i but hope for your approbation 
of my love! might i but confess the name of my mistress without 
incur your resentment!' 

she interrupt me. 

'suppose, i were to spare you that confession? suppose i were to 
acknowledge that the object of your desire is not unknown to me? 
suppose i were to say that she returns your affection, and lament not 
less sincerely than yourself the unhappy vow which separate her from 
you?' 

'ah! donna rodolpha!' i exclaimed, throw myself upon my knee before 
her, and pressing her hand to my lips, 'you have discover my secret! 
what is your decision? must i despair, or may i reckon upon your 
favour?' 

she withdrew not the hand which i held; but she turn from me, and 
cover her face with the other. 

'how can i refuse it you?' she replied; 'ah! don alphonso, i have long 
perceive to whom your attention were directed, but till now i 
perceive not the impression which they made upon my heart. 

at length i can no long hide my weakness either from myself or from 
you. i yield to the violence of my passion, and own that i adore you! 
for three long month i stifle my desires; but grown strong by 
resistance, i submit to their impetuosity. pride, fear, and honour, 
respect for myself, and my engagement to the baron, all are 
vanquished. i sacrifice them to my love for you, and it still seem to 
me that i pay too mean a price for your possession.' 

she pause for an answer.--judge, my lorenzo, what must have been my 
confusion at this discovery. i at once saw all the magnitude of this 
obstacle, which i had raise myself to my happiness. the baroness had 
placed those attention to her own account, which i had merely paid her 
for the sake of agnes: and the strength of her expressions, the looks 
which accompany them, and my knowledge of her revengeful disposition 
made me tremble for myself and my beloved. i was silent for some 
minutes. i knew not how to reply to her declaration: i could only 
resolve to clear up the mistake without delay, and for the present to 
conceal from her knowledge the name of my mistress. no soon had she 
avowed her passion than the transport which before were evident in my 
features gave place to consternation and constraint. i drop her 
hand, and rose from my knees. the change in my countenance did not 
escape her observation. 

'what means this silence?' said she in a tremble voice; 'where is 
that joy which you led me to expect?' 

'forgive me, segnora,' i answered, 'if what necessity forces from me 
should seem harsh and ungrateful: to encourage you in an error, which, 
however it may flatter myself, must prove to you the source of 
disappointment, would make me appear criminal in every eye. honour 
oblige me to inform you that you have mistaken for the solicitude of 
love what was only the attention of friendship. the latter sentiment 
is that which i wish to excite in your bosom: to entertain a warmer, 
respect for you forbid me, and gratitude for the baron's generous 
treatment. perhaps these reason would not be sufficient to shield me 
from your attractions, were it not that my affection are already 
bestow upon another. you have charms, segnora, which might captivate 
the most insensible; no heart unoccupy could resist them. happy is 
it for me that mine is no long in my possession; or i should have to 
reproach myself for ever with have violate the law of hospitality. 
recollect yourself, noble lady; recollect what is owe by you to 
honour, by me to the baron, and replace by esteem and friendship those 
sentiment which i never can return.' 

the baroness turn pale at this unexpected and positive declaration: 
she doubt whether she slept or woke. at length recover from her 
surprise, consternation gave place to rage, and the blood rush back 
into her cheek with violence. 

'villain!' she cried; 'monster of deceit! thus is the avowal of my 
love received? is it thus that.... but no, no! it cannot, it 
shall not be! alphonso, behold me at your feet! be witness of my 
despair! look with pity on a woman who love you with sincere 
affection! she who possess your heart, how has she merit such a 
treasure? what sacrifice has she made to you? 

what raise her above rodolpha?' 

i endeavour to lift her from her knees. 

'for god's sake, segnora, restrain these transports: they disgrace 
yourself and me. your exclamation may be heard, and your secret 
divulge to your attendants. i see that my presence only irritate 
you: permit me to retire.' 

i prepared to quit the apartment: the baroness caught me sudden by 
the arm. 

'and who is this happy rival?' said she in a menace tone; 'i will 
know her name, and when i know it.... ! she is someone in my power; 
you entreat my favour, my protection! let me but find her, let me 
but know who dare to rob me of your heart, and she shall suffer every 
torment which jealousy and disappointment can inflict! who is she? 
answer me this moment. hope not to conceal her from my vengeance! 
spy shall be set over you; every step, every look shall be watched; 
your eyes will discover my rival; i shall know her, and when she is 
found, tremble, alphonso for her and for yourself!' 

as she utter these last words her fury mount to such a pitch as to 
stop her powers of respiration. she panted, groaned, and at length 
faint away. as she was fall i caught her in my arms, and placed 
her upon a sopha. then hasten to the door, i summon her women to 
her assistance; i committed her to their care, and seize the 
opportunity of escaping. 

agitate and confuse beyond expression i bent my steps towards the 
garden. the benignity with which the baroness had listen to me at 
first raise my hope to the highest pitch: i imagine her to have 
perceive my attachment for her niece, and to approve of it. extreme 
was my disappointment at understanding the true purport of her

parent of agnes, aid by her aunt's unfortunate passion, seem to 
oppose such obstacle to our union as were almost insurmountable. 

as i past by a low parlour, whose window look into the garden,

were scattered round her. i entered, still undetermined whether i 
should acquaint her with the declaration of the baroness. 

'oh! is it only you?' said she, raise her head; 'you are no stranger, 
and i shall continue my occupation without ceremony. take a chair, and 
seat yourself by me.' 

i obeyed, and placed myself near the table. unconscious what i was 
doing, and total occupy by the scene which had just passed, i took

subject struck me from its singularity. it represent the great hall 
of the castle of lindenberg. a door conduct to a narrow staircase 
stood half open. in the foreground appear a groupe of figures, 
placed in the most grotesque attitudes; terror was express upon every 
countenance. 

here was one upon his knee with his eyes cast up to heaven, and 
pray most devoutly; there another was creep away upon all fours. 
some hid their face in their cloak or the lap of their companions; 
some had conceal themselves beneath a table, on which the remnant of 
a feast were visible; while other with gape mouth and eyes 
wide-stretched pointed to a figure, supposed to have create this

clothe in the habit of some religious order. her face was veiled; on 
her arm hung a chaplet of beads; her dress was in several place 
stain with the blood which trickle from a wound upon her bosom. in 
one hand she held a lamp, in the other a large knife, and she seem

'what do this mean, agnes?' said i; 'is this some invention of your 
own?' 

she cast her eye upon the drawing. 

'oh! no,' she replied; ''tis the invention of much wise head than 
mine. but can you possibly have live at lindenberg for three whole 
month without hearing of the bleeding nun?' 

'you are the first, who ever mention the name to me. pray, who may 
the lady be?' 

'that is more than i can pretend to tell you. all my knowledge of her 
history come from an old tradition in this family, which has been 
hand down from father to son, and is firm credit throughout the 
baron's domains. nay, the baron believe it himself; and as for my 
aunt who has a natural turn for the marvellous, she would soon doubt 
the veracity of the bible, than of the bleeding nun. shall i tell you

i answer that she would oblige me much by relate it: she resume 
her drawing, and then proceed as follow in a tone of burlesque

'it is surprising that in all the chronicle of past times, this 
remarkable personage is never once mentioned. fain would i recount to 
you her life; but unlucki till after her death she was never known to 
have existed. then first did she think it necessary to make some noise 
in the world, and with that intention she made bold to seize upon the 
castle of lindenberg. have a good taste, she took up her abode in the 
best room of the house: and once establish there, she began to amuse

to ascertain. according to the tradition, this entertainment commence 
about a century ago. it was accompany with shrieking, howling, 
groaning, swearing, and many other agreeable noise of the same kind. 
but though one particular room was more especially honour with her 
visits, she did not entire confine herself to it. she occasional 
venture into the old galleries, pace up and down the spacious halls, 
or sometimes stopping at the door of the chambers, she wept and wail 
there to the universal terror of the inhabitants. in these nocturnal 
excursion she was seen by different people, who all describe her 
appearance as you behold it here, trace by the hand of her unworthy 
historian.' 

the singular of this account insensible engaged my attention. 

'did she never speak to those who met her?' said i. 

'not she. the specimen indeed, which she gave nightly of her talent 
for conversation, were by no means inviting. sometimes the castle rung 
with oath and execrations: a moment after she repeated her 
paternoster: now she howl out the most horrible blasphemies, and then 
chaunte de profundis, as orderly as if still in the choir. in short

cursed, whether she was impious or devout, she always contrived to 
terrify her auditor out of their senses. the castle became scarcely

well-behaved. she walk about in silence, and never made her

fifth of may of every fifth year, as soon as the clock strike one, the

gate of the castle open, out of respect to the apparition: not that 
this is thought by any means necessary, since she could easily whip 
through the keyhole if she chose it; but merely out of politeness, and 
to prevent her from making her exit in a way so derogatory to the 
dignity of her ghost-ship.' 

'and whither do she go on quit the castle?' 

'to heaven, i hope; but if she does, the place certainly is not to her 
taste, for she always returns after an hour's absence. the lady then 
retire to her chamber, and is quiet for another five years.' 

'and you believe this, agnes?' 

'how can you ask such a question? no, no, alphonso! i have too much 
reason to lament superstition's influence to be its victim myself. 
however i must not avow my incredulity to the baroness: she entertain 
not a doubt of the truth of this history. as to dame cunegonda, my

bleeding nun, as the ghost is call in the castle: 'tis from her 
account that i drew this sketch, and you may be certain that cunegonda 
was not omitted. there she is! i shall never forget what a passion 
she was in, and how ugly she look while she scold me for have

here she pointed to a burlesque figure of an old woman in an attitude 
of terror. 

in spite of the melancholy which oppress me, i could not help smile 
at the playful imagination of agnes: she had perfectly preserve dame 
cunegonda's resemblance, but had so much exaggerate every fault, and 
render every feature so irresistible laughable, that i could easily

'the figure is admirable, my dear agnes! i knew not that you possessed 
such talent for the ridiculous.' 

'stay a moment,' she replied; 'i will show you a figure still more

she rose, and went to a cabinet at some little distance. unlock a 
drawer, she took out a small case, which she opened, and present to 
me. 

'do you know the resemblance?' said she smiling. 

it was her own. 

transport at the gift, i pressed the portrait to my lip with

rage on the part of donna rodolpha, embarrassment on mine, for some

after the positive declaration of its mistress, it was impossible for 
me to make a long stay at the castle of lindenberg. accordingly i the

at quit him the baron shook my hand affectionately, and assured me

'farewell, don alphonso!' said the baroness, and stretch out her hand

her husband was at the other end of the room, and out of hearing.

opportunity of concert our future plans. adieu. 
agnes. 

at peruse these lines my transport exceed all bounds; neither did 
i set any to the expression of gratitude which i heap upon theodore. 
in fact his address and attention merit my warmest praise. you will 
readily believe that i had not entrust him with my passion for agnes; 
but the arch youth had too much discernment not to discover my secret, 
and too much discretion not to conceal his knowledge of it. he 
observe in silence what was go on, nor strove to make himself an 
agent in the business till my interests require his interference. i 
equally admire his judgment, his penetration, his address, and his 
fidelity. this was not the first occasion in which i had found him of 
infinite use, and i was every day more convinced of his quick and 
capacity. during my short stay at strasbourg, he had applied himself 
diligent to learning the rudiments of spanish: he continue to study 
it, and with so much success that he spoke it with the same facility as 
his native language. he past the greatest part of his time in reading; 
he had acquire much information for his age; and united the advantage 
of a lively countenance and prepossessing figure to an excellent 
understanding and the very best of hearts. he is now fifteen; he is 
still in my service, and when you see him, i am sure that he will 
please you. but excuse this digression: i return to the subject which 
i quitted. 

i obey the instruction of agnes. i proceed to munich. there i 
left my chaise under the care of lucas, my france servant, and then 
return on horseback to a small village about four mile distant from 
the castle of lindenberg. upon arrive there a story was related to 
the host at whose inn i descended, which prevent his wonder at my 
making so long a stay in his house. the old man fortunately was 
credulous and incurious: he believe all i said, and sought to know no 
more than what i thought proper to tell him. nobody was with me but 
theodore; both were disguised, and as we kept ourselves close, we were 
not suspect to be other than what we seemed. in this manner the 
fortnight pass away. during that time i had the pleasing conviction 
that agnes was once more at liberty. she past through the village with 
dame cunegonda: she seem in health and spirits, and talk to her 
companion without any appearance of constraint.
