'who are those ladies?' said i to my host, as the carriage past. 

'baron lindenberg's niece with her governess,' he replied; 'she go 
regularly every friday to the convent of st. catharine, in which she 
was brought up, and which is situated about a mile from hence.' 

you may be certain that i wait with impatience for the ensue 
friday. i again beheld my lovely mistress. she cast her eyes upon me, 
as she pass the inn-door. a blush which overspread her cheek told me 
that in spite of my disguise i had been recognised. i bowed 
profoundly. she return the compliment by a slight inclination of the 
head as if made to one inferior, and look another way till the 
carriage was out of sight. 

the long-expected, long-wished for night arrived. it was calm, and the 
moon was at the full. as soon as the clock struck eleven i hasten to 
my appointment, determined not to be too late. theodore had provided a 
ladder; i ascend the garden wall without difficulty; the page 
follow me, and drew the ladder after us. i post myself in the west 
pavilion, and wait impatient for the approach of agnes. every 
breeze that whispered, every leaf that fell, i believe to be her 
footstep, and hasten to meet her. thus was i oblige to pass a full 
hour, every minute of which appear to me an age. the castle bell at 
length toll twelve, and scarcely could i believe the night to be no 
further advanced. another quarter of an hour elapsed, and i heard the 
light foot of my mistress approach the pavilion with precaution. i 
flew to receive her, and conduct her to a seat. i threw myself at 
her feet, and was express my joy at seeing her, when she thus 
interrupt me. 

'we have no time to lose, alphonso: the moments are precious, for 
though no more a prisoner, cunegonda watches my every step. an express 
is arrive from my father; i must depart immediately for madrid, and 
'tis with difficulty that i have obtain a week's delay. the 
superstition of my parents, support by the representations of my 
cruel aunt, leaves me no hope of soften them to compassion. in this 
dilemma i have resolve to commit myself to your honour: god grant 
that you may never give me cause to repent my resolution! flight is my 
only resource from the horrors of a convent, and my imprudence must be 
excuse by the urgent of the danger. now listen to the plan by which 
i hope to effect my escape. 

'we are now at the thirtieth of april. on the fifth day from this the 
visionary nun is expect to appear. in my last visit to the convent i 
provided myself with a dress proper for the character: a friend, whom 
i have left there and to whom i made no scruple to confide my secret, 
readily consent to supply me with a religious habit. provide a 
carriage, and be with it at a little distance from the great gate of 
the castle. as soon as the clock strike 'one,' i shall quit my 
chamber, drest in the same apparel as the ghost is supposed to wear. 
whoever meet me will be too much terrify to oppose my escape. i 
shall easily reach the door, and throw myself under your protection. 
thus far success is certain: but oh! alphonso, should you deceive me! 
should you despise my imprudence and reward it with ingratitude, the 
world will not hold a being more wretched than myself! i feel all the 
danger to which i shall be exposed. i feel that i am give you a 
right to treat me with levity: but i rely upon your love, upon your 
honour! the step which i am on the point of taking, will incense my 
relations against me: should you desert me, should you betray the 
trust repose in you, i shall have no friend to punish your insult, or 
support my cause. on yourself alone rest all my hope, and if your own 
heart do not plead in my behalf, i am undone for ever!' 

the tone in which she pronounced these words was so touching, that in 
spite of my joy at receiving her promise to follow me, i could not help 
being affected. i also repine in secret at not have taken the 
precaution to provide a carriage at the village, in which case i might 
have carry off agnes that very night. such an attempt was now 
impracticable: neither carriage or horse were to be procure near 
than munich, which was distant from lindenberg two good days journey. 
i was therefore oblige to chime in with her plan, which in truth 
seem well arranged: her disguise would secure her from being stop 
in quit the castle, and would enable her to step into the carriage 
at the very gate without difficulty or lose time. 

agnes recline her head mournful upon my shoulder, and by the light 
of the moon i saw tear flowing down her cheek. i strove to dissipate 
her melancholy, and encourage her to look forward to the prospect of 
happiness. i protest in the most solemn terms that her virtue and 
innocence would be safe in my keeping, and that till the church had 
made her my lawful wife, her honour should be held by me as sacred as a 
sister's. i told her that my first care should be to find you out, 
lorenzo, and reconcile you to our union; and i was continue to speak 
in the same strain, when a noise without alarm me. sudden the door 
of the pavilion was thrown open, and cunegonda stood before us. she 
had heard agnes steal out of her chamber, follow her into the garden, 
and perceive her enter the pavilion. favoured by the tree which 
shade it, and unperceived by theodore who wait at a little distance, 
she had approach in silence, and overheard our whole conversation. 

'admirable!' cry cunegonda in a voice shrill with passion, while 
agnes utter a loud shriek; 'by st. barbara, young lady, you have an 
excellent invention! you must personate the bleeding nun, truly? what 
impiety! what incredulity! marry, i have a good mind to let you 
pursue your plan: when the real ghost met you, i warrant, you would be 
in a pretty condition! don alphonso, you ought to be ashamed of 
yourself for seduce a young ignorant creature to leave her family and 
friends: however, for this time at least i shall mar your wicked 
designs. the noble lady shall be informed of the whole affair, and 
agnes must defer play the spectre till a better opportunity. 
farewell, segnor-- donna agnes, let me have the honour of conduct 
your ghost-ship back to your apartment.' 

she approach the sopha on which her tremble pupil was seated, took 
her by the hand, and prepared to lead her from the pavilion. 

i detain her, and strove by entreaties, soothing, promises, and 
flattery to win her to my party: but finding all that i could say of 
no avail, i abandoned the vain attempt. 

'your obstinacy must be its own punishment,' said i; 'but one resource 
remains to save agnes and myself, and i shall not hesitate to employ 
it.' 

terrify at this menace, she again endeavour to quit the pavilion; 
but i seize her by the wrist, and detain her forcibly. at the same 
moment theodore, who had follow her into the room, closed the door, 
and prevent her escape. i took the veil of agnes: i threw it round 
the duenna's head, who utter such piercing shriek that in spite of 
our distance from the castle, i dread their being heard. at length i 
succeed in gag her so compleat that she could not produce a 
single sound. theodore and myself with some difficulty next contrived 
to bind her hand and feet with our handkerchiefs; and i advise agnes 
to regain her chamber with all diligence. i promise that no harm 
should happen to cunegonda, bad her remember that on the fifth of may i 
should be in wait at the great gate of the castle, and took of her 
an affectionate farewell. tremble and uneasy she had scarce power 
enough to signify her consent to my plans, and flee back to her 
apartment in disorder and confusion. 

in the meanwhile theodore assist me in carry off my antiquated 
prize. she was hoist over the wall, placed before me upon my horse 
like a portmanteau, and i gallop away with her from the castle of 
lindenberg. the unlucky duenna never had made a more disagreeable 
journey in her life: she was jolt and shaken till she was become 
little more than an animate mummy; not to mention her fright when we 
wade through a small river through which it was necessary to pass in 
order to regain the village. before we reach the inn, i had already 
determined how to dispose of the troublesome cunegonda. we enter the 
street in which the inn stood, and while the page knocked, i wait at 
a little distance. the landlord open the door with a lamp in his 
hand. 

'give me the light!' said theodore; 'my master is coming.' 

he snatch the lamp hastily, and purposely let it fall upon the 
ground: the landlord return to the kitchen to re-light the lamp, 
leave the door open. i profit by the obscurity, sprang from my 
horse with cunegonda in my arms, dart up stairs, reach my chamber 
unperceived, and unlock the door of a spacious closet, stow her 
within it, and then turn the key. the landlord and theodore soon 
after appear with lights: the former express himself a little 
surprise at my return so late, but ask no impertinent questions. 
he soon quit the room, and left me to exult in the success of my 
undertaking. 

i immediately paid a visit to my prisoner. i strove to persuade her 
submit with patience to her temporary confinement. my attempt was 
unsuccessful. unable to speak or move, she express her fury by her 
looks, and except at meal i never dare to unbind her, or release her 
from the gag. at such times i stood over her with a drawn sword, and 
protested, that if she utter a single cry, i would plunge it in her 
bosom. as soon as she had done eating, the gag was replaced. i was 
conscious that this proceeding was cruel, and could only be justified 
by the urgent of circumstances: as to theodore, he had no scruple 
upon the subject. cunegonda's captivity entertain him beyond 
measure. during his abode in the castle, a continual warfare had been 
carry on between him and the duenna; and now that he found his enemy 
so absolutely in his power, he triumph without mercy. he seem to 
think of nothing but how to find out new means of plague her: 
sometimes he affected to pity her misfortune, then laugh at, abused, 
and mimicke her; he play her a thousand tricks, each more provoking 
than the other, and amuse himself by telling her that her elope 
must have occasion much surprise at the baron's. this was in fact 
the case. no one except agnes could imagine what was become of dame 
cunegonda: every hole and corner was search for her; the pond were 
dragged, and the wood underwent a thorough examination. still no dame 
cunegonda made her appearance. agnes kept the secret, and i kept the 
duenna: the baroness, therefore, remain in total ignorance 
respecting the old woman's fate, but suspect her to have perish by 
suicide. thus past away five days, during which i had prepared every 
thing necessary for my enterprise. on quit agnes, i had made it my 
first business to dispatch a peasant with a letter to luca at munich, 
order him to take care that a coach and four should arrive about ten 
o'clock on the fifth of may at the village of rosenwald. he obey my 
instruction punctually: the equipage arrive at the time appointed. 
as the period of her lady's elope drew nearer, cunegonda's rage 
increased. i verily believe that spight and passion would have kill 
her, had i not lucky discover her prepossession in favour of cherry 
brandy. with this favourite liquor she was plentiful supplied, and 
theodore always remain to guard her, the gag was occasional 
removed. the liquor seem to have a wonderful effect in soften the 
acrimony of her nature; and her confinement not admit of any other 
amusement, she got drunk regularly once a day just by way of passing 
the time. 

the fifth of may arrived, a period by me never to be forgotten! before 
the clock struck twelve, i betook myself to the scene of action. 
theodore follow me on horseback. i conceal the carriage in a 
spacious cavern of the hill, on whose brow the castle was situated: 
this cavern was of considerable depth, and among the peasant was known 
by the name of lindenberg hole. the night was calm and beautiful: the 
moonbeam fell upon the antient tower of the castle, and shed upon 
their summit a silver light. all was still around me: nothing was to 
be heard except the night breeze sigh among the leaves, the distant 
bark of village dogs, or the owl who had establish herself in a 
nook of the desert eastern turret. i heard her melancholy shriek, 
and look upwards. she sat upon the ride of a window, which i 
recognize to be that of the haunt room. this brought to my 
remembrance the story of the bleeding nun, and i sigh while i 
reflect on the influence of superstition and weakness of human 
reason. sudden i heard a faint chorus steal upon the silence of the 
night. 

'what can occasion that noise, theodore?' 

'a stranger of distinction,' reply he, 'passed through the village 
today in his way to the castle: he is report to be the father of 
donna agnes. doubtless, the baron has given an entertainment to 
celebrate his arrival.' 

the castle bell announce the hour of midnight: this was the usual 
signal for the family to retire to bed. soon after i perceive lights 
in the castle moving backwards and forward in different directions. i 
conjecture the company to be separating. i could hear the heavy door 
grate as they open with difficulty, and as they closed again the 
rotten case rattle in their frames. the chamber of agnes was on 
the other side of the castle. i tremble lest she should have fail 
in obtain the key of the haunt room: through this it was 
necessary for her to pass in order to reach the narrow staircase by 
which the ghost was supposed to descend into the great hall. agitate 
by this apprehension, i kept my eyes constant fixed upon the window, 
where i hope to perceive the friendly glare of a lamp borne by agnes. 
i now heard the massy gate unbarred. by the candle in his hand i 
distinguished old conrad, the porter. he set the portal door wide 
open, and retired. the lights in the castle gradual disappeared, and 
at length the whole building was wrapt in darkness. 

while i sat upon a broken ridge of the hill, the still of the scene 
inspired me with melancholy idea not altogether unpleasing. the 
castle which stood full in my sight, form an object equally awful and 
picturesque. its ponderous wall tinge by the moon with solemn 
brightness, its old and partly-ruined tower lift themselves into 
the cloud and seeming to frown on the plain around them, its lofty 
battlements oergrown with ivy, and fold gate expand in honour of 
the visionary inhabitant, made me sensible of a sad and reverential 
horror. yet did not these sensation occupy me so fully, as to prevent 
me from witness with impatience the slow progress of time. i 
approach the castle, and venture to walk round it. a few ray of 
light still glimmer in the chamber of agnes. i observe them with 
joy. i was still gaze upon them, when i perceive a figure draw near 
the window, and the curtain was careful closed to conceal the lamp 
which burn there. convinced by this observation that agnes had not 
abandoned our plan, i return with a light heart to my former station. 

the half-hour struck! the three-quarters struck! my bosom beat high 
with hope and expectation. at length the wished-for sound was heard. 
the bell toll 'one,' and the mansion echo with the noise loud and 
solemn. i look up to the case of the haunt chamber. scarcely 
had five minutes elapsed, when the expect light appeared. i was now 
close to the tower. the window was not so far from the ground but that 
i fancy i perceive a female figure with a lamp in her hand moving 
slow along the apartment. the light soon fade away, and all was 
again dark and gloomy. 

occasional gleam of bright dart from the staircase window as 
the lovely ghost past by them. i trace the light through the hall: 
it reach the portal, and at length i beheld agnes pass through the 
fold gates. she was habit exactly as she had describe the 
spectre. a chaplet of bead hung upon her arm; her head was envelop 
in a long white veil; her nun's dress was stain with blood, and she 
had taken care to provide herself with a lamp and dagger. she advanced 
towards the spot where i stood. i flew to meet her, and clasp her in 
my arms. 

'agnes!' said i while i pressed her to my bosom, 
agnes! agnes! thou art mine! 
agnes! agnes! i am thine! 
in my vein while blood shall roll, 
thou art mine! 
i am thine! 
thine my body! thine my soul! 

terrify and breathless she was unable to speak: she dropt her lamp 
and dagger, and sank upon my bosom in silence. i raise her in my 
arms, and convey her to the carriage. theodore remain behind in 
order to release dame cunegonda. i also charged him with a letter to 
the baroness explain the whole affair, and entreat her good 
offices in reconcile don gaston to my union with his daughter. i 
discover to her my real name: i prove to her that my birth and 
expectations justified my pretend to her niece, and assured her, 
though it was out of my power to return her love, that i would strive 
unceasing to obtain her esteem and friendship. 

i step into the carriage, where agnes was already seated. theodore 
closed the door, and the postillion drove away. at first i was 
delight with the rapid of our progress; but as soon as we were in 
no danger of pursuit, i call to the drivers, and bad them moderate 
their pace. they strove in vain to obey me. the horse refuse to 
answer the rein, and continue to rush on with astonish swiftness. 
the postillion redouble their effort to stop them, but by kick 
and plunge the beast soon release themselves from this restraint. 
utter a loud shriek, the driver were hurl upon the ground. 
immediately thick cloud obscure the sky: the wind howl around us, 
the lightning flashed, and the thunder roar tremendously. never did 
i behold so frightful a tempest! terrify by the jar of contend 
elements, the horse seem every moment to increase their speed. 
nothing could interrupt their career; they drag the carriage through 
hedge and ditches, dashed down the most dangerous precipices, and 
seem to vye in swift with the rapid of the winds. 

all this while my companion lay motionless in my arms. truly alarm 
by the magnitude of the danger, i was in vain attempt to recall her 
to her senses; when a loud crash announced, that a stop was put to our 
progress in the most disagreeable manner. the carriage was shatter to 
pieces. in fall i struck my temple against a flint. the pain of 
the wound, the violence of the shock, and apprehension for the safety 
of agnes combine to overpower me so compleatly, that my senses forsook 
me, and i lay without animation on the ground. 

i probably remain for some time in this situation, since when i 
open my eyes, it was broad daylight. several peasant were standing 
round me, and seem dispute whether my recovery was possible. i 
spoke germany tolerably well. as soon as i could utter an articulate 
sound, i enquire after agnes. what was my surprise and distress, when 
assured by the peasants, that nobody had been seen answer the 
description which i gave of her! they told me that in go to their 
daily labour they had been alarm by observing the fragment of my 
carriage, and by hearing the groan of an horse, the only one of the 
four which remain alive: the other three lay dead by my side. nobody 
was near me when they came up, and much time had been lost, before they 
succeed in recover me. uneasy beyond expression respecting the 
fate of my companion, i besought the peasant to disperse themselves in 
search of her: i describe her dress, and promise immense rewards to 
whoever brought me any intelligence. as for myself, it was impossible 
for me to join in the pursuit: i had broken two of my rib in the fall: 
my arm being dislocate hung useless by my side; and my left leg was 
shatter so terribly, that i never expect to recover its use. 

the peasant comply with my request: all left me except four, who 
made a litter of bough and prepared to convey me to the neighbouring 
town. i enquire its name. it prove to be ratisbon, and i could 
scarcely persuade myself that i had travelled to such a distance in a 
single night. i told the countrymen that at one o'clock that morning i 
had past through the village of rosenwald. they shook their head 
wistfully, and made sign to each other that i must certainly be 
delirious. i was convey to a decent inn and immediately put to bed. 
a physician was sent for, who set my arm with success. he then 
examine my other hurts, and told me that i need be under no 
apprehension of the consequence of any of them; but ordered me to keep 
myself quiet, and be prepared for a tedious and painful cure. i 
answer him that if he hope to keep me quiet, he must first endeavour 
to procure me some news of a lady who had quit rosenwald in my 
company the night before, and had been with me at the moment when the 
coach broke down. he smiled, and only reply by advise me to make 
myself easy, for that all proper care should be taken of me. as he 
quit me, the hostess met him at the door of the room. 

'the gentleman is not quite in his right senses;' i heard him say to 
her in a low voice; ''tis the natural consequence of his fall, but that 
will soon be over.' 

one after another the peasant return to the inn, and informed me 
that no trace had been discover of my unfortunate mistress. 

uneasy now became despair. i entreat them to renew their search 
in the most urgent terms, double the promise which i had already 
made them. my wild and frantic manner confirmed the bye-standers in 
the idea of my being delirious. no sign of the lady have appeared, 
they believe her to be a creature fabricate by my over-heated brain, 
and paid no attention to my entreaties. however, the hostess assured 
me that a fresh enquiry should be made, but i found afterwards that her 
promise was only given to quiet me. no further steps were taken in the 
business. 

though my baggage was left at munich under the care of my france 
servant, have prepared myself for a long journey, my purse was ample 
furnished: besides my equipage prove me to be of distinction, and in 
consequence all possible attention was paid me at the inn. the day 
pass away: still no news arrive of agnes. the anxiety of fear now 
gave place to despondency. i cease to rave about her and was plunge 
in the depth of melancholy reflections. perceive me to be silent and 
tranquil, my attendant believe my delirium to have abated, and that 
my malady had taken a favourable turn. according to the physician's 
order i swallow a compose medicine; and as soon as the night shut 
in, my attendant withdrew and left me to repose. 

that repose i woo in vain. the agitation of my bosom chase away 
sleep. restless in my mind, in spite of the fatigue of my body, i 
continue to toss about from side to side, till the clock in a 
neighbouring steeple struck 'one.' as i listen to the mournful 
hollow sound, and heard it die away in the wind, i felt a sudden 
chill spread itself over my body. i shudder without knowing 
wherefore; cold dew pour down my forehead, and my hair stood 
bristle with alarm. sudden i heard slow and heavy steps ascend 
the staircase. by an involuntary movement i start up in my bed, and 
drew back the curtain. a single rush-light which glimmer upon the 
hearth shed a faint gleam through the apartment, which was hung with 
tapestry. the door was thrown open with violence. a figure entered, 
and drew near my bed with solemn measured steps. with tremble 
apprehension i examine this midnight visitor. god almighty! it was 
the bleeding nun! it was my lost companion! her face was still 
veiled, but she no long held her lamp and dagger. she lift up her 
veil slowly. what a sight present itself to my startle eyes! i 
beheld before me an animate corse. her countenance was long and 
haggard; her cheek and lip were bloodless; the pale of death was 
spread over her features, and her eyeball fixed stedfast upon me 
were lustreless and hollow. 

i gaze upon the spectre with horror too great to be described. my 
blood was frozen in my veins. i would have call for aid, but the 
sound expire ere it could pass my lips. my nerves were bound up in 
impotence, and i remain in the same attitude inanimate as a statue. 

the visionary nun look upon me for some minutes in silence: there was 
something petrify in her regard. at length in a low sepulchral voice 
she pronounced the following words. 

"raymond! raymond! thou art mine! 
raymond! raymond! i am thine! 
in thy vein while blood shall roll, 
i am thine! 
thou art mine! 
mine thy body! mine thy soul!----" 

breathless with fear, i listen while she repeated my own expressions. 
the apparition seat herself opposite to me at the foot of the bed, 
and was silent. her eyes were fixed earnest upon mine: they seem 
endow with the property of the rattlesnake's, for i strove in vain to 
look off her. my eyes were fascinated, and i had not the power of 
withdraw them from the spectre's. 

in this attitude she remain for a whole long hour without speak or 
moving; nor was i able to do either. at length the clock struck two. 
the apparition rose from her seat, and approach the side of the bed. 
she grasp with her icy finger my hand which hung lifeless upon the 
coverture, and pressing her cold lip to mine, again repeated, 

"raymond! raymond! thou art mine! 
raymond! raymond! 
i am thine! &c.----" 

she then drop my hand, quit the chamber with slow steps, and the 
door closed after her. till that moment the faculty of my body had 
been all suspended; those of my mind had alone been waking. the charm 
now cease to operate: the blood which had been frozen in my vein 
rush back to my heart with violence: i utter a deep groan, and sank 
lifeless upon my pillow. 

the adjoin room was only separate from mine by a thin partition: 
it was occupy by the host and his wife: the former was rouze by my 
groan, and immediately hasten to my chamber: the hostess soon 
follow him. with some difficulty they succeed in restore me to 
my senses, and immediately sent for the physician, who arrive in all 
diligence. he declared my fever to be very much increased, and that if 
i continue to suffer such violent agitation, he would not take upon 
him to ensure my life. some medicine which he gave me in some degree 
tranquillize my spirits. i fell into a sort of slumber towards 
daybreak; but fearful dream prevent me from derive any benefit 
from my repose. agnes and the bleeding nun present themselves by 
turn to my fancy, and combine to harass and torment me. i awoke 
fatigue and unrefreshed. my fever seem rather augment than 
diminished; the agitation of my mind impede my fracture bone from 
knitting: i had frequent faint fits, and during the whole day the 
physician judge it expedient not to quit me for two hour together. 

the singular of my adventure made me determine to conceal it from 
every one, since i could not expect that a circumstance so strange 
should gain credit. i was very uneasy about agnes. i knew not what 
she would think at not finding me at the rendezvous, and dread her 
entertaining suspicion of my fidelity. however, i depend upon 
theodore's discretion, and trust that my letter to the baroness would 
convince her of the rectitude of my intentions. these consideration 
somewhat lighten my inquietude upon her account: but the impression 
left upon my mind by my nocturnal visitor grew strong with every 
succeed moment. the night drew near; i dread its arrival. yet i 
strove to persuade myself that the ghost would appear no more, and at 
all event i desire that a servant might sit up in my chamber. 

the fatigue of my body from not have slept on the former night, 
co-operating with the strong opiate administer to me in profusion, 
at length procure me that repose of which i was so much in need. i 
sank into a profound and tranquil slumber, and had already slept for 
some hours, when the neighbouring clock rouze me by striking 'one'. 
its sound brought with it to my memory all the horrors of the night 
before. the same cold shiver seize me. i start up in my bed, 
and perceive the servant fast asleep in an armed-chair near me. i 
call him by his name: he made no answer. i shook him forcibly by 
the arm, and strove in vain to wake him. he was perfectly insensible 
to my efforts. i now heard the heavy steps ascend the staircase; 
the door was thrown open, and again the bleeding nun stood before me. 
once more my limb were chain in second infancy. once more i heard 
those fatal words repeated, 

"raymond! raymond! thou art mine! 
raymond! raymond! i am thine! &c.----" 

the scene which had shock me so sensible on the former night, was 
again presented. the spectre again pressed her lip to mine, again 
touched me with her rot fingers, and as on her first appearance, 
quit the chamber as soon as the clock told 'two.' 

even night was this repeated. far from grow accustomed to the 
ghost, every succeed visit inspired me with greater horror. her idea 
pursue me continually, and i became the prey of habitual melancholy. 
the constant agitation of my mind naturally retarded the 
re-establishment of my health. several month elapse before i was 
able to quit my bed; and when at length i was move to a sopha, i was 
so faint, spiritless, and emaciated, that i could not cross the room 
without assistance. the looks of my attendant sufficient denote 
the little hope, which they entertain of my recovery. the profound 
sadness, which oppress me without remission made the physician 
consider me to be an hypochondriac. the cause of my distress i 
careful conceal in my own bosom, for i knew that no one could give 
me relief: the ghost was not even visible to any eye but mine. i had 
frequent cause attendant to sit up in my room: but the moment that 
the clock struck 'one,' irresistible slumber seize them, nor left them 
till the departure of the ghost. 

you may be surprize that during this time i made no enquiry after 
your sister. theodore, who with difficulty had discover my abode, 
had quiet my apprehension for her safety: at the same time he 
convinced me that all attempt to release her from captivity must be 
fruitless till i should be in a condition to return to spain. the 
particulars of her adventure which i shall now relate to you, were 
partly communicate to me by theodore, and partly by agnes herself. 

on the fatal night when her elope was to have taken place, accident 
had not permit her to quit her chamber at the appoint time. at 
length she venture into the haunt room, descended the staircase 
leading into the hall, found the gate open as she expected, and left 
the castle unobserved. what was her surprize at not finding me ready 
to receive her! she examine the cavern, range through every alley of 
the neighbouring wood, and pass two full hour in this fruitless 
enquiry. she could discover no trace either of me or of the carriage. 
alarm and disappointed, her only resource was to return to the castle 
before the baroness miss her: but here she found herself in a fresh 
embarrassment. the bell had already toll 'two:' the ghostly hour was 
past, and the careful porter had lock the fold gates. after much 
irresolute she venture to knock softly. lucky for her, conrad was 
still awake: he heard the noise and rose, murmur at being call up 
a second time. no soon had he open one of the doors, and beheld 
the supposed apparition wait there for admittance, than he utter a 
loud cry, and sank upon his knees. agnes profit by his terror. she 
glide by him, flew to her own apartment, and have thrown off her 
spectre's trappings, retired to bed endeavour in vain to account for 
my disappearing. 

in the mean while theodore have seen my carriage drive off with the 
false agnes, return joyful to the village. the next morning he 
release cunegonda from her confinement, and accompany her to the 
castle. there he found the baron, his lady, and don gaston, dispute 
together upon the porter's relation. all of them agree in believe 
the existence of spectres: but the latter contended, that for a ghost 
to knock for admittance was a proceeding till then unwitnessed, and 
total incompatible with the immaterial nature of a spirit. they were 
still discuss this subject when the page appear with cunegonda and 
clear up the mystery. on hearing his deposition, it was agree 
unanimous that the agnes whom theodore had seen step into my carriage 
must have been the bleeding nun, and that the ghost who had terrify 
conrad was no other than don gaston's daughter. 

the first surprize which this discovery occasion being over, the 
baroness resolve to make it of use in persuade her niece to take the 
veil. fear lest so advantageous an establishment for his daughter 
should induce don gaston to renounce his resolution, she suppress my 
letter, and continue to represent me as a needy unknown adventurer. a 
childish vanity had led me to conceal my real name even from my 
mistress; i wish to be love for myself, not for being the son and 
heir of the marquis de las cisternas. the consequence was that my rank 
was known to no one in the castle except the baroness, and she took 
good care to confine the knowledge to her own breast. don gaston 
have approve his sister's design, agnes was summon to appear 
before them. she was tax with have meditate an elopement, oblige 
to make a full confession, and was amaze at the gentle with which 
it was received: but what was her affliction, when informed that the 
failure of her project must be attribute to me! cunegonda, tutor by 
the baroness, told her that when i release her, i had desire her to 
inform her lady that our connexion was at an end, that the whole affair 
was occasion by a false report, and that it by no means suit my 
circumstances to marry a woman without fortune or expectations. 

to this account my sudden disappear gave but too great an air of 
probability. theodore, who could have contradict the story, by donna 
rodolpha's order was kept out of her sight: what prove a still 
greater confirmation of my being an impostor, was the arrival of a 
letter from yourself declare that you had no sort of acquaintance 
with alphonso d'alvarada. these seeming proof of my perfidy, aid by 
the artful insinuation of her aunt, by cunegonda's flattery, and her 
father's threat and anger, entire conquer your sister's repugnance 
to a convent. incense at my behaviour, and disgust with the world in 
general, she consent to receive the veil. she past another month at 
the castle of lindenberg, during which my non-appearance confirmed her 
in her resolution, and then accompany don gaston into spain. 
theodore was now set at liberty. he hasten to munich, where i had 
promise to let him hear from me; but finding from luca that i had 
never arrive there, he pursue his search with indefatigable 
perseverance, and at length succeed in rejoin me at ratisbon. 

so much was i altered, that scarcely could he recollect my features: 
the distress visible upon his sufficient testify how lively was the 
interest which he felt for me. the society of this amiable boy, whom i 
had always considered rather as a companion than a servant, was now my 
only comfort. his conversation was gay yet sensible, and his 
observations shrewd and entertaining: he had picked up much more 
knowledge than is usual at his age: but what render him most 
agreeable to me, was his have a delightful voice, and some skill in 
music. he had also acquire some taste in poetry, and even venture 
sometimes to write verse himself. he occasional compose little 
ballad in spanish, his composition were but indifferent, i must 
confess; yet they were pleasing to me from their novelty, and hearing 
him sing them to his guitar was the only amusement, which i was capable 
of receiving. theodore perceive well enough that something prey 
upon my mind; but as i conceal the cause of my grief even from him, 
respect would not permit him to pry into my secrets. 

one evening i was lie upon my sopha, plunge in reflection very far 
from agreeable: theodore amuse himself by observing from the window a 
battle between two postillions, who were quarrel in the inn-yard. 

'ha! ha!' cry he suddenly; 'yonder is the great mogul.' 

'who?' said i. 

'only a man who made me a strange speech at munich.' 

'what was the purport of it?' 

'now you put me in mind of it, segnor, it was a kind of message to you; 
but truly it was not worth delivering. i believe the fellow to be mad, 
for my part. when i came to munich in search of you, i found him 
living at 'the king of the romans,' and the host gave me an odd account 
of him. by his accent he is supposed to be a foreigner, but of what 
country nobody can tell. he seem to have no acquaintance in the 
town, spoke very seldom, and never was seen to smile. he had neither 
servant or baggage; but his purse seem well-furnished, and he did 
much good in the town. some supposed him to be an arabia astrologer, 
other to be a travel mountebank, and many declared that he was 
doctor faustus, whom the devil had sent back to germany. the landlord, 
however told me, that he had the best reason to believe him to be the 
great mogul incognito.' 

'but the strange speech, theodore.' 

'true, i had almost forgotten the speech: indeed for that matter, it 
would not have been a great loss if i had forgotten it altogether. you 
are to know, segnor, that while i was enquiring about you of the 
landlord, this stranger pass by. he stopped, and look at me 
earnestly. 'youth!' said he in a solemn voice, 'he whom you seek, has 
found that which he would fain lose. my hand alone can dry up the 
blood: bid your master wish for me when the clock strikes, 'one.' 

'how?' cry i, start from my sopha. (the words which theodore had 
repeated, seem to imply the stranger's knowledge of my secret) 'fly 
to him, my boy! entreat him to grant me one moment's conversation!' 

theodore was surprise at the vivace of my manner: however, he ask 
no questions, but hasten to obey me. i wait his return 
impatiently. but a short space of time had elapse when he again 
appear and usher the expect guest into my chamber. he was a man 
of majestic presence: his countenance was strong marked, and his 
eyes were large, black, and sparkling: yet there was a something in 
his look which, the moment that i saw him, inspired me with a secret 
awe, not to say horror. he was drest plainly, his hair was unpowdered, 
and a band of black velvet which encircle his forehead spread over his 
features an additional gloom. his countenance wore the mark of 
profound melancholy; his step was slow, and his manner grave, stately, 
and solemn. 

he salute me with politeness; and have reply to the usual 
compliments of introduction, he motion to theodore to quit the 
chamber. the page instantly withdrew. 

'i know your business,' said he, without give me time to speak. 

'i have the power of release you from your nightly visitor; but this 
cannot be done before sunday. on the hour when the sabbath morning 
breaks, spirits of dark have least influence over mortals. after 
saturday the nun shall visit you no more.' 

'may i not enquire,' said i, 'by what means you are in possession of a 
secret which i have careful conceal from the knowledge of everyone?' 

'how can i be ignorant of your distress, when their cause at this 
moment stand beside you?' 

i started. the stranger continued. 

'though to you only visible for one hour in the twenty-four, neither 
day or night do she ever quit you; nor will she ever quit you till 
you have grant her request.' 

'and what is that request?' 

'that she must herself explain: it lie not in my knowledge. wait with 
patience for the night of saturday: all shall be then clear up.' 

i dare not press him further. he soon after change the conversation 
and talk of various matters. he name people who had cease to exist 
for many centuries, and yet with whom he appear to have been 
personally acquainted. i could not mention a country however distant 
which he had not visited, nor could i sufficient admire the extent 
and variety of his information. i remark to him that have 
travelled, seen, and known so much, must have given him infinite 
pleasure. he shook his head mournfully. 

'no one,' he replied, 'is adequate to comprehend the misery of my 
lot! fate oblige me to be constant in movement: i am not permit 
to pass more than a fortnight in the same place. i have no friend in 
the world, and from the restless of my destiny i never can acquire 
one. fain would i lay down my miserable life, for i envy those who 
enjoy the quiet of the grave: but death elude me, and flies from my 
embrace. in vain do i throw myself in the way of danger. i plunge 
into the ocean; the wave throw me back with abhorrence upon the shore: 
i rush into fire; the flame recoil at my approach: i oppose myself to 
the fury of banditti; their sword become blunted, and break against my 
breast: the hungry tiger shudder at my approach, and the alligator 
flies from a monster more horrible than itself. god has set his seal 
upon me, and all his creature respect this fatal mark!' 

he put his hand to the velvet, which was bound round his forehead. 
there was in his eyes an expression of fury, despair, and malevolence, 
that struck horror to my very soul. an involuntary convulsion made me 
shudder. the stranger perceive it. 

'such is the curse impose on me,' he continued: 'i am doom to 
inspire all who look on me with terror and detestation. you already 
feel the influence of the charm, and with every succeed moment will 
feel it more. i will not add to your suffering by my presence. 
farewell till saturday. as soon as the clock strike twelve, expect me 
at your chamber door.' 

have said this he departed, leave me in astonishment at the 
mysterious turn of his manner and conversation. 

his assurance that i should soon be relieved from the apparition's 
visit produce a good effect upon my constitution. theodore, whom i 
rather treat as an adopt child than a domestic, was surprize at 
his return to observe the amendment in my looks. he congratulate me 
on this symptom of return health, and declared himself delight at 
my have received so much benefit from my conference with the great 
mogul. upon enquiry i found that the stranger had already past eight 
days in ratisbon: according to his own account, therefore, he was only 
to remain there six days longer. saturday was still at the distance of 
three. oh! with what impatience did i expect its arrival! in the 
interim, the bleeding nun continue her nocturnal visits; but hope 
soon to be release from them altogether, the effects which they 
produce on me became less violent than before. 

the wished-for night arrived. to avoid create suspicion i retired to 
bed at my usual hour: but as soon as my attendant had left me, i 
dress myself again, and prepared for the stranger's reception. he 
enter my room upon the turn of midnight. a small chest was in his 
hand, which he placed near the stove. he salute me without speaking; 
i return the compliment, observing an equal silence. he then open 
his chest. the first thing which he produce was a small wooden 
crucifix: he sank upon his knees, gaze upon it mournfully, and cast 
his eyes towards heaven. he seem to be pray devoutly. at length 
he bowed his head respectfully, kiss the crucifix thrice, and quit 
his kneel posture. he next drew from the chest a cover goblet: 
with the liquor which it contained, and which appear to be blood, he 
sprinkle the floor, and then dip in it one end of the crucifix, he 
describe a circle in the middle of the room. round about this he 
placed various reliques, sculls, thigh-bones &c; i observed, that he 
disposed them all in the form of crosses. lastly he took out a large 
bible, and beckon me to follow him into the circle. i obeyed. 

'be cautious not to utter a syllable!' whisper the stranger; 'step 
not out of the circle, and as you love yourself, dare not to look upon 
my face!' 

holding the crucifix in one hand, the bible in the other, he seem to 
read with profound attention. the clock struck 'one'! as usual i heard 
the spectre's steps upon the staircase: but i was not seize with the 
accustomed shivering. i wait her approach with confidence. she 
enter the room, drew near the circle, and stopped. the stranger 
mutter some words, to me unintelligible. then raise his head from 
the book, and extend the crucifix towards the ghost, he pronounced 
in a voice distinct and solemn, 

'beatrice! beatrice! beatrice!' 

'what wouldst thou?' reply the apparition in a hollow falter tone. 

'what disturb thy sleep? why dost thou afflict and torture this 
youth? how can rest be restore to thy unquiet spirit?' 

'i dare not tell!--i must not tell!--fain would i repose in my grave, 
but stern command force me to prolong my punishment!' 

'knowest thou this blood? knowest thou in whose vein it flowed? 

beatrice! beatrice! in his name i charge thee to answer me!' 

'i dare not disobey my taskers.' 

'darest thou disobey me?' 

he spoke in a commanding tone, and drew the sable band from his 
forehead. in spite of his injunction to the contrary, curiosity would 
not suffer me to keep my eyes off his face: i raise them, and beheld 
a burning cross impress upon his brow. for the horror with which this 
object inspired me i cannot account, but i never felt its equal! my 
senses left me for some moments; a mysterious dread overcame my 
courage, and had not the exorcise caught my hand, i should have fallen 
out of the circle. 

when i recover myself, i perceive that the burning cross had 
produce an effect no less violent upon the spectre. her countenance 
express reverence, and horror, and her visionary limb were shaken by 
fear. 

'yes!' she said at length; 'i tremble at that mark!--respect it!--i 
obey you! know then, that my bone lie still unburied: they rot in the 
obscurity of lindenberg hole. none but this youth has the right of 
consign them to the grave. his own lip have made over to me his 
body and his soul: never will i give back his promise, never shall he 
know a night devoid of terror, unless he engage to collect my 
moulder bones, and deposit them in the family vault of his 
andalusian castle. then let thirty masses be said for the repose of my 
spirit, and i trouble this world no more. now let me depart! those 
flame are scorching!' 

he let the hand drop slow which held the crucifix, and which till 
then he had pointed towards her. the apparition bowed her head, and 
her form melt into air. the exorcise led me out of the circle. he 
replace the bible &c. in the chest, and then address himself to me, 
who stood near him speechless from astonishment. 

'don raymond, you have heard the conditions on which repose is promise 
you. be it your business to fulfil them to the letter. for me nothing 
more remains than to clear up the dark still spread over the 
spectre's history, and inform you that when living, beatrice bore the 
name of las cisternas. she was the great aunt of your grandfather: in 
quality of your relation, her ashes demand respect from you, though the 
enormity of her crime must excite your abhorrence. the nature of 
those crime no one is more capable of explain to you than myself: 
i was personally acquaint with the holy man who proscribe her 
nocturnal riot in the castle of lindenberg, and i hold this narrative 
from his own lips. 

'beatrice de las cisterna took the veil at an early age, not by her 
own choice, but at the express command of her parents. she was then 
too young to regret the pleasure of which her profession deprived her: 
but no soon did her warm and voluptuous character begin to be 
develop than she abandoned herself freely to the impulse of her 
passions, and seize the first opportunity to procure their 
gratification. this opportunity was at length presented, after many 
obstacle which only add new force to her desires. she contrived to 
elope from the convent, and flee to germany with the baron lindenberg. 
she live at his castle several month as his avowed concubine: all 
bavaria was scandalize by her impudent and abandoned conduct. her 
feast vie in luxury with cleopatra's, and lindenberg became the 
theatre of the most unbridled debauchery. not satisfy with 
display the incontinence of a prostitute, she professed herself an 
atheist: she took every opportunity to scoff at her monastic vows, 
and loaded with ridicule the most sacred ceremony of religion. 

'possessed of a character so depraved, she did not long confine her 
affection to one object. soon after her arrival at the castle, the 
baron's younger brother attract her notice by his strong-marked 
features, gigantic stature, and herculean limbs. she was not of an 
humour to keep her inclination long unknown; but she found in otto von 
lindenberg her equal in depravity. he return her passion just 
sufficient to increase it; and when he had work it up to the 
desire pitch, he fixed the price of his love at his brother's murder. 
the wretch consent to this horrible agreement. a night was pitch 
upon for perpetrate the deed. otto, who reside on a small estate a 
few mile distant from the castle, promise that at one in the morning 
he would be wait for her at lindenberg hole; that he would bring 
with him a party of chosen friends, by whose aid he doubt not being 
able to make himself master of the castle; and that his next step 
should be the unite her hand to his. it was this last promise, which 
overrule every scruple of beatrice, since in spite of his affection 
for her, the baron had declared positively that he never would make her 
his wife. 

'the fatal night arrived. the baron slept in the arms of his 
perfidious mistress, when the castle-bell struck 'one.' immediately 
beatrice drew a dagger from underneath the pillow, and plunge it in 
her paramour's heart. the baron utter a single dreadful groan, and 
expired. the murderess quit her bed hastily, took a lamp in one 
hand, in the other the bloody dagger, and bent her course towards the 
cavern. the porter dare not to refuse opening the gate to one more 
dread in the castle than its master. beatrice reach lindenberg 
hole unopposed, where according to promise she found otto wait for 
her. he received and listen to her narrative with transport: but ere 
she had time to ask why he came unaccompanied, he convinced her that he 
wish for no witness to their interview. anxious to conceal his 
share in the murder, and to free himself from a woman, whose violent 
and atrocious character made him tremble with reason for his own 
safety, he had resolve on the destruction of his wretched agent. 
rush upon her suddenly, he wrest the dagger from her hand: he 
plunge it still reek with his brother's blood in her bosom, and put 
an end to her existence by repeated blows. 

'otto now succeed to the barony of lindenberg. the murder was 
attribute solely to the fugitive nun, and no one suspect him to have 
persuade her to the action. but though his crime was unpunished by 
man, god's justice permit him not to enjoy in peace his 
blood-stained honours. her bone lie still unbury in the cave, the 
restless soul of beatrice continue to inhabit the castle. drest in 
her religious habit in memory of her vow broken to heaven, furnish 
with the dagger which had drank the blood of her paramour, and holding 
the lamp which had guide her flying steps, every night did she stand 
before the bed of otto. the most dreadful confusion reign through the 
castle; the vaulted chambers resound with shriek and groans; and the 
spectre, as she range along the antique galleries, utter an 
incoherent mixture of prayer and blasphemies. otto was unable to 
withstand the shock which he felt at this fearful vision: its horror 
increase with every succeed appearance: his alarm at length became 
so insupportable that his heart burst, and one morning he was found in 
his bed total deprived of warmth and animation. his death did not 
put an end to the nocturnal riots. the bone of beatrice continue to 
lie unburied, and her ghost continue to haunt the castle. 

'the domain of lindenberg now fell to a distant relation. but 
terrify by the account given him of the bleeding nun (so was the 
spectre call by the multitude), the new baron call to his 
assistance a celebrated exorciser. this holy man succeed in obliging 
her to temporary repose; but though she discover to him her history, 
he was not permit to reveal it to others, or cause her skeleton to 
be remove to hallow ground. that office was reserved for you, and 
till your coming, her ghost was doom to wander about the castle and 
lament the crime which she had there committed. however, the exorcise 
oblige her to silence during his lifetime. so long as he existed, the 
haunt chamber was shut up, and the spectre was invisible. at his 
death which happen in five years after, she again appeared, but only 
once on every fifth year, on the same day and at the same hour when she 
plunge her knife in the heart of her sleep lover: she then visit 
the cavern which held her moulder skeleton, return to the castle 
as soon as the clock struck 'two,' and was seen no more till the next 
five years had elapsed. 

'she was doom to suffer during the space of a century. that period 
is past. nothing now remains but to consign to the grave the ashes of 
beatrice. i have been the means of release you from your visionary 
tormentor; and amidst all the sorrow which oppress me, to think that i 
have been of use to you, is some consolation. youth, farewell! may 
the ghost of your relation enjoy that rest in the tomb, which the 
almighty's vengeance has deny to me for ever!' 

here the stranger prepared to quit the apartment. 

'stay yet one moment!' said i; 'you have satisfy my curiosity with 
regard to the spectre, but you leave me in prey to yet greater 
respecting yourself. deign to inform me, to whom i am under such real 
obligations. you mention circumstances long past, and person long 
dead: you were personally acquaint with the exorciser, who by your 
own account has been deceased near a century. how am i to account for 
this? what means that burning cross upon your forehead, and why did 
the sight of it strike such horror to my soul?' 

on these points he for some time refuse to satisfy me. at length 
overcome by my entreaties, he consent to clear up the whole, on 
condition that i would defer his explanation till the next day. with 
this request i was oblige to comply, and he left me. in the morning 
my first care was to enquire after the mysterious stranger. conceive 
my disappointment when informed that he had already quit ratisbon. 
i dispatch messenger in pursuit of him but in vain. no trace of 
the fugitive were discovered. since that moment i never have heard any 
more of him, and 'tis most probable that i never shall.' 

(lorenzo here interrupt his friend's narrative. 

'how?' said he; 'you have never discover who he was, or even form a 
guess?' 

'pardon me,' reply the marquis; 'when i related this adventure to my 
uncle, the cardinal-duke, he told me that he had no doubt of this 
singular man's being the celebrated character known universally by the 
name of 'the wandering jew.' his not being permit to pass more than 
fourteen days on the same spot, the burning cross impress upon his 
forehead, the effect which it produce upon the beholders, and many 
other circumstances give this supposition the colour of truth. the 
cardinal is fully persuade of it; and for my own part i am inclined to 
adopt the only solution which offer itself to this riddle. i return 
to the narrative from which i have digressed.') 

from this period i recover my health so rapid as to astonish my 
physicians. the bleeding nun appear no more, and i was soon able to 
set out for lindenberg. the baron received me with open arms. i 
confide to him the sequel of my adventure; and he was not a little 
pleased to find that his mansion would be no long trouble with the 
phantom's quiennial visits. i was sorry to perceive that absence had 
not weaken donna rodolpha's imprudent passion. in a private 
conversation which i had with her during my short stay at the castle, 
she renew her attempt to persuade me to return her affection. 
regarding her as the primary cause of all my sufferings, i entertain 
for her no other sentiment than disgust. the skeleton of beatrice was 
found in the place which she had mentioned. this being all that i 
sought at lindenberg, i hasten to quit the baron's domains, equally 
anxious to perform the obsequies of the murder nun, and escape the 
importune of a woman whom i detested. i departed, follow by donna 
rodolpha's menace that my contempt should not be long unpunished. 

i now bent my course towards spain with all diligence. luca with my 
baggage had join me during my abode at lindenberg. i arrive in my 
native country without any accident, and immediately proceed to my 
father's castle in andalusia. the remains of beatrice were deposit 
in the family vault, all due ceremony performed, and the number of 
masses said which she had required. nothing now hinder me from 
employ all my endeavour to discover the retreat of agnes. the 
baroness had assured me that her niece had already taken the veil: 
this intelligence i suspect to have been forge by jealousy, and 
hope to find my mistress still at liberty to accept my hand. i 
enquire after her family; i found that before her daughter could reach 
madrid, donna inesilla was no more: you, my dear lorenzo, were said to 
be abroad, but where i could not discover: your father was in a 
distant province on a visit to the duke de medina, and as to agnes, no 
one could or would inform me what was become of her. theodore, 
according to promise, had return to strasbourg, where he found his 
grandfather dead, and marguerite in possession of his fortune. all her 
persuasion to remain with her were fruitless: he quit her a second 
time, and follow me to madrid. he exert himself to the utmost in 
forwarding my search: but our united endeavour were unattended by 
success. the retreat which conceal agnes remain an impenetrable 
mystery, and i began to abandon all hope of recover her. 

about eight month ago i was return to my hotel in a melancholy 
humour, have past the evening at the play-house. the night was dark, 
and i was unaccompanied. plunge in reflection which were far from 
being agreeable, i perceive not that three men had follow me from 
the theatre; till, on turning into an unfrequented street, they all 
attack me at the same time with the utmost fury. i sprang back a few 
paces, drew my sword, and threw my cloak over my left arm. the 
obscurity of the night was in my favour. for the most part the blow 
of the assassins, being aim at random, fail to touch me. i at 
length was fortunate enough to lay one of my adversary at my feet; 
but before this i had already received so many wounds, and was so 
warm pressed, that my destruction would have been inevitable, had not 
the clash of sword call a cavalier to my assistance. he ran 
towards me with his sword drawn: several domestic follow him with 
torches. his arrival made the combat equal: yet would not the bravo 
abandon their design till the servant were on the point of join us. 
they then flee away, and we lost them in the obscurity. 

the stranger now address himself to me with politeness, and enquire 
whether i was wounded. faint with the loss of blood, i could scarcely 
thank him for his seasonable aid, and entreat him to let some of his 
servant convey me to the hotel de las cisternas. i no soon 
mention the name than he profest himself an acquaintance of my 
father's, and declared that he would not permit my being transport to 
such a distance before my wound had been examined. he add that his 
house was hard by, and beg me to accompany him thither. his manner 
was so earnest, that i could not reject his offer, and leaning upon his 
arm, a few minutes brought me to the porch of a magnificent hotel. 

on enter the house, an old grey-headed domestic came to welcome my 
conductor: he enquire when the duke, his master, meant to quit the 
country, and was answer that he would remain there yet some months. 
my deliver then desire the family surgeon to be summon without 
delay. his orders were obeyed. i was seat upon a sopha in a noble 
apartment; and my wound being examined, they were declared to be very 
slight. the surgeon, however, advise me not to expose myself to the 
night air; and the stranger pressed me so earnest to take a bed in 
his house, that i consent to remain where i was for the present. 

being now left alone with my deliverer, i took the opportunity of 
thank him in more express terms, than i had done hitherto: but he 
beg me to be silent upon the subject. 

'i esteem myself happy,' said he, 'in have had it in my power to 
render you this little service; and i shall think myself eternal 
oblige to my daughter for detain me so late at the convent of st. 
clare. the high esteem in which i have ever held the marquis de las 
cisternas, though accident has not permit our being so intimate as i 
could wish, make me rejoice in the opportunity of making his son's 
acquaintance. i am certain that my brother in whose house you now are, 
will lament his not being at madrid to receive you himself: but in the 
duke's absence i am master of the family, and may assure you in his 
name, that every thing in the hotel de medina is perfectly at your 
disposal.' 

conceive my surprize, lorenzo, at discover in the person of my 
preserver don gaston de medina: it was only to be equal by my 
secret satisfaction at the assurance that agnes inhabit the convent 
of st. clare. this latter sensation was not a little weakened, when in 
answer to my seemingly indifferent question he told me that his 
daughter had really taken the veil. i suffer not my grief at this 
circumstance to take root in my mind: i flatter myself with the idea 
that my uncle's credit at the court of rome would remove this obstacle, 
and that without difficulty i should obtain for my mistress a 
dispensation from her vows. buoy up with this hope i calm the 
uneasy of my bosom; and i redouble my endeavour to appear 
grateful for the attention and pleased with the society of don gaston. 

a domestic now enter the room, and informed me that the bravo whom i 
had wound discover some sign of life. i desire that he might be 
carry to my father's hotel, and that as soon as he recover his 
voice, i would examine him respecting his reason for attempt my 
life. i was answer that he was already able to speak, though with 
difficulty: don gaston's curiosity made him press me to interrogate 
the assassin in his presence, but this curiosity i was by no means 
inclined to gratify. one reason was, that doubt from whence the 
blow came, i was unwill to place before don gaston's eyes the guilt 
of a sister: another was, that i fear to be recognize for alphonso 
d'alvarada, and precaution taken in consequence to keep me from the 
sight of agnes. to avow my passion for his daughter, and endeavour to 
make him enter into my schemes, what i knew of don gaston's character 
convinced me would be an imprudent step: and considering it to be 
essential that he should know me for no other than the conde de las 
cisternas, i was determined not to let him hear the bravo's confession. 
i insinuate to him, that as i suspect a lady to be concerned in the 
business, whose name might accidental escape from the assassin, it 
was necessary for me to examine the man in private. don gaston's 
delicacy would not permit his urge the point any longer, and in 
consequence the bravo was convey to my hotel. 

the next morning i took leave of my host, who was to return to the duke 
on the same day. my wound had been so trifling that, except being 
oblige to wear my arm in a sling for a short time, i felt no 
inconvenience from the night's adventure. the surgeon who examine the 
bravo's wound declared it to be mortal: he had just time to confess 
that he had been instigate to murder me by the revengeful donna 
rodolpha, and expire in a few minutes after. 

all my thought were now bent upon get to the speech of my lovely 
nun. theodore set himself to work, and for this time with better 
success. he attack the garden of st. clare so forcibly with bribe 
and promise that the old man was entire gain over to my interests; 
and it was settled that i should be introduce into the convent in the 
character of his assistant. the plan was put into execution without 
delay. disguise in a common habit, and a black patch covering one of 
my eyes, i was present to the lady prioress, who condescend to 
approve of the gardener's choice. i immediately enter upon my 
employment. botany have been a favourite study with me, i was by no 
means at a loss in my new station. for some days i continue to work 
in the convent garden without meeting the object of my disguise: on the 
fourth morning i was more successful. i heard the voice of agnes, and 
was speeding towards the sound, when the sight of the domina stop 
me. i drew back with caution, and conceal myself behind a thick 
clump of trees. 

the prioress advanced and seat herself with agnes on a bench at no 
great distance. i heard her in an angry tone blame her companion's 
continual melancholy: she told her that to weep the loss of any lover 
in her situation was a crime; but that to weep the loss of a faithless 
one was folly and absurd in the extreme. agnes reply in so low a 
voice that i could not distinguish her words, but i perceive that she 
use terms of gentle and submission. the conversation was 
interrupt by the arrival of a young pensioner who informed the domina 
that she was wait for in the parlour. the old lady rose, kiss the 
cheek of agnes, and retired. the newcomer remained. agnes spoke much 
to her in praise of somebody whom i could not make out, but her auditor 
seem highly delighted, and interested by the conversation. the nun 
show her several letters; the other peruse them with evident 
pleasure, obtain permission to copy them, and withdrew for that 
purpose to my great satisfaction. 

no soon was she out of sight, than i quit my concealment. fear 
to alarm my lovely mistress, i drew near her gently, intend to 
discover myself by degrees. but who for a moment can deceive the eyes 
of love? she raise her head at my approach, and recognise me in 
spite of my disguise at a single glance. she rose hasty from her 
seat with an exclamation of surprize, and attempt to retire; but i 
follow her, detain her, and entreat to be heard. persuade of my 
falsehood she refuse to listen to me, and ordered me positively to 
quit the garden. it was now my turn to refuse. i protest that 
however dangerous might be the consequences, i would not leave her till 
she had heard my justification. i assured her that she had been 
deceive by the artifice of her relations; that i could convince her 
beyond the power of doubt that my passion had been pure and 
disinterested; and i ask her what should induce me to seek her in the 
convent, were i influence by the selfish motive which my enemy had 
ascribe to me. 

my prayers, my arguments, and vow not to quit her, till she had 
promise to listen to me, united to her fear lest the nun should see 
me with her, to her natural curiosity, and to the affection which she 
still felt for me in spite of my supposed desertion, at length 
prevailed. she told me that to grant my request at that moment was 
impossible; but she engaged to be in the same spot at eleven that 
night, and to converse with me for the last time. have obtain this 
promise i release her hand, and she flee back with rapid towards 
the convent. 

i communicate my success to my ally, the old gardener: he pointed out 
an hiding place where i might shelter myself till night without fear of 
a discovery. thither i betook myself at the hour when i ought to have 
retired with my supposed master, and wait impatient for the 
appoint time. the chill of the night was in my favour, since it 
kept the other nun confine to their cells. agnes alone was 
insensible of the inclement of the air, and before eleven join me at 
the spot which had witness our former interview. secure from 
interruption, i related to her the true cause of my disappear on the 
fatal fifth of may. she was evidently much affected by my narrative: 
when it was concluded, she confessed the injustice of her suspicions, 
and blame herself for have taken the veil through despair at my 
ingratitude. 

'but now it is too late to repine!' she added; 'the die is thrown: i 
have pronounced my vows, and dedicated myself to the service of heaven. 
i am sensible, how ill i am calculate for a convent. my disgust at a 
monastic life increase daily: ennui and discontent are my constant 
companions; and i will not conceal from you that the passion which i 
formerly felt for one so near being my husband is not yet extinguish 
in my bosom. but we must part! insuperable barrier divide us from 
each other, and on this side the grave we must never meet again!' 

i now exert myself to prove that our union was not so impossible as 
she seem to think it. i vaunt to her the cardinal-duke of lerma's 
influence at the court of rome: i assured her that i should easily 
obtain a dispensation from her vows; and i doubt not but don gaston 
would coincide with my views, when informed of my real name and long 
attachment. agnes reply that since i encourage such an hope, i 
could know but little of her father. liberal and kind in every other 
respect, superstition form the only stain upon his character. upon 
this head he was inflexible; he sacrifice his dearest interests to his 
scruples, and would consider it an insult to suppose him capable of 
authorise his daughter to break her vow to heaven. 

'but suppose,' said i interrupt her; 'suppose that he should 
disapprove of our union; let him remain ignorant of my proceedings, 
till i have rescue you from the prison in which you are now confined. 
once my wife, you are free from his authority: i need from him no 
pecuniary assistance; and when he see his resentment to be unavailing, 
he will doubtless restore you to his favour. but let the worst happen; 
should don gaston be irreconcileable, my relations will vie with each 
other in making you forget his loss: and you will find in my father a 
substitute for the parent of whom i shall deprive you.' 

'don raymond,' reply agnes in a firm and resolute voice, 'i love my 
father: he has treat me harsh in this one instance; but i have 
received from him in every other so many proof of love that his 
affection is become necessary to my existence. were i to quit the 
convent, he never would forgive me; nor can i think that on his 
deathbed he would leave me his curse, without shudder at the very 
idea. besides, i am conscious myself, that my vow are binding: 
wilful did i contract my engagement with heaven; i cannot break it 
without a crime. then banish from your mind the idea of our being ever 
united. i am devoted to religion; and however i may grieve at our 
separation, i would oppose obstacle myself, to what i feel would 
render me guilty.' 

i strove to overrule these ill-grounded scruples: we were still 
dispute upon the subject, when the convent bell summon the nun to 
matins. agnes was oblige to attend them; but she left me not till i 
had compel her to promise that on the following night she would be 
at the same place at the same hour. these meeting continue for 
several week uninterrupted; and 'tis now, lorenzo, that i must implore 
your indulgence. reflect upon our situation, our youth, our long 
attachment: weigh all the circumstances which attend our 
assignations, and you will confess the temptation to have been 
irresistible; you will even pardon me when i acknowledge, that in an 
unguarded moment, the honour of agnes was sacrifice to my passion.' 

(lorenzo's eyes sparkle with fury: a deep crimson spread itself over 
his face. he start from his seat, and attempt to draw his sword. 
the marquis was aware of his movement, and caught his hand: he pressed 
it affectionately. 

'my friend! my brother! hear me to the conclusion! till then restrain 
your passion, and be at least convinced, that if what i have related is 
criminal, the blame must fall upon me, and not upon your sister.' 

lorenzo suffer himself to be prevail upon by don raymond's 
entreaties. he resume his place, and listen to the rest of the 
narrative with a gloomy and impatient countenance. the marquis thus 
continued.) 

'scarcely was the first burst of passion past when agnes, recover 
herself, start from my arms with horror. she call me infamous 
seducer, loaded me with the bitterest reproaches, and beat her bosom in 
all the wild of delirium. ashamed of my imprudence, i with 
difficulty found words to excuse myself. i endeavour to console her; 
i threw myself at her feet, and entreat her forgiveness. she forced 
her hand from me, which i had taken, and would have prest to my lips. 

'touch me not!' she cry with a violence which terrify me; 'monster 
of perfidy and ingratitude, how have i been deceive in you! i look 
upon you as my friend, my protector: i trust myself in your hand 
with confidence, and rely upon your honour, thought that mine ran no 
risque. and 'tis by you, whom i adored, that i am cover with infamy! 
'tis by you that i have been seduce into break my vow to god, that 
i am reduce to a level with the basest of my sex! shame upon you, 
villain, you shall never see me more!' 

she start from the bank on which she was seated. i endeavour to 
detain her; but she disengaged herself from me with violence, and took 
refuge in the convent. 

i retired, fill with confusion and inquietude. the next morning i 
fail not as usual to appear in the garden; but agnes was no where to 
be seen. at night i wait for her at the place where we generally 
met; i found no better success. several days and nights pass away in 
the same manner. at length i saw my offend mistress cross the walk 
on whose border i was working: she was accompany by the same young 
pensioner, on whose arm she seem from weakness oblige to support 
herself. she look upon me for a moment, but instantly turn her 
head away. i wait her return; but she pass on to the convent 
without pay any attention to me, or the penitent looks with which i 
implore her forgiveness. 

as soon as the nun were retired, the old garden join me with a 
sorrowful air. 

'segnor,' said he, 'it grieve me to say, that i can be no long of 
use to you. the lady whom you use to meet has just assured me that if 
i admitted you again into the garden, she would discover the whole 
business to the lady prioress. she bade me tell you also, that your 
presence was an insult, and that if you still possess the least respect 
for her, you will never attempt to see her more. excuse me then for 
inform you that i can favour your disguise no longer. should the 
prioress be acquaint with my conduct, she might not be contented with 
dismiss me her service: out of revenge she might accuse me of 
have profane the convent, and cause me to be thrown into the prison 
of the inquisition.' 

fruitless were my attempt to conquer his resolution. he deny me all 
future entrance into the garden, and agnes persevere in neither 
letting me see or hear from her. in about a fortnight after, a violent 
illness which had seize my father oblige me to set out for andalusia. 
i hasten thither, and as i imagined, found the marquis at the point 
of death. though on its first appearance his complaint was declared 
mortal, he linger out several months; during which my attendance upon 
him during his malady, and the occupation of settle his affair after 
his decease, permit not my quit andalusia. within these four 
days i return to madrid, and on arrive at my hotel, i there found 
this letter wait for me. 

(here the marquis unlock the drawer of a cabinet: he took out a 
fold paper, which he present to his auditor. lorenzo open it, 
and recognise his sister's hand. the contents were as follows. 

into what an abyss of misery have you plunge me! raymond, you force 
me to become as criminal as yourself. i had resolve never to see you 
more; if possible, to forget you; if not, only to remember you with 
hate. a being for whom i already feel a mother's tenderness, solicit 
me to pardon my seducer, and apply to his love for the means of 
preservation. raymond, your child lives in my bosom. i tremble at the 
vengeance of the prioress; i tremble much for myself, yet more for the 
innocent creature whose existence depend upon mine. both of us are 
lost, should my situation be discovered. advise me then what steps to 
take, but seek not to see me. the gardener, who undertake to deliver 
this, is dismissed, and we have nothing to hope from that quarter: the 
man engaged in his place is of incorruptible fidelity. the best means 
of convey to me your answer, is by conceal it under the great 
statue of st. francis, which stand in the capuchin cathedral. thither 
i go every thursday to confession, and shall easily have an opportunity 
of secure your letter. i hear that you are now absent from madrid; 
need i entreat you to write the very moment of your return? i will not 
think it. ah! raymond! mine is a cruel situation! deceive by my 
nearest relations, compel to embrace a profession the duty of 
which i am ill-calculated to perform, conscious of the sanctity of 
those duties, and seduce into violate them by one whom i least 
suspect of perfidy, i am now oblige by circumstances to chuse 
between death and perjury. woman's timidity, and maternal affection, 
permit me not to balance in the choice. i feel all the guilt into 
which i plunge myself, when i yield to the plan which you before 
propose to me. my poor father's death which has taken place since we 
met, has remove one obstacle. he sleep in his grave, and i no long 
dread his anger. but from the anger of god, oh! raymond! who shall 
shield me? who can protect me against my conscience, against myself? 
i dare not dwell upon these thoughts; they will drive me mad. i have 
taken my resolution: procure a dispensation from my vows; i am ready 
to fly with you. write to me, my husband! tell me, that absence has 
not abate your love, tell me that you will rescue from death your 
unborn child, and its unhappy mother. i live in all the agony of 
terror: every eye which is fixed upon me seem to read my secret and 
my shame. and you are the cause of those agonies! oh! when my heart 
first love you, how little did it suspect you of making it feel such 
pangs! 

agnes. 

have peruse the letter, lorenzo restore it in silence. the marquis 
replace it in the cabinet, and then proceeded.) 

'excessive was my joy at reading this intelligence so 
earnestly-desired, so little expected. my plan was soon arranged. 
when don gaston discover to me his daughter's retreat, i entertain 
no doubt of her readiness to quit the convent: i had, therefore, 
entrust the cardinal-duke of lerma with the whole affair, who 
immediately busy himself in obtain the necessary bull. 
fortunately i had afterwards neglect to stop his proceedings. not 
long since i received a letter from him, state that he expect daily 
to receive the order from the court of rome. upon this i would 
willing have relyed: but the cardinal wrote me word, that i must 
find some means of convey agnes out of the convent, unknown to the 
prioress. he doubt not but this latter would be much incense by 
lose a person of such high rank from her society, and consider the 
renunciation of agnes as an insult to her house. he represent her as
