a woman of a violent and revengeful character, capable of proceeding to 
the greatest extremities. it was therefore to be feared, lest by 
confine agnes in the convent she should frustrate my hopes, and 
render the pope's mandate unavailing. influence by this 
consideration, i resolve to carry off my mistress, and conceal her 
till the arrival of the expect bull in the cardinal-duke's estate. 
he approve of my design, and profest himself ready to give a shelter 
to the fugitive. i next cause the new garden of st. clare to be 
seize privately, and confine in my hotel. by this means i became 
master of the key to the garden door, and i had now nothing more to do 
than prepare agnes for the elopement. this was done by the letter, 
which you saw me deliver this evening. i told her in it, that i should 
be ready to receive her at twelve tomorrow night, that i had secure 
the key of the garden, and that she might depend upon a speedy release. 

you have now, lorenzo, heard the whole of my long narrative. i have 
nothing to say in my excuse, save that my intentions towards your 
sister have been ever the most honourable: that it has always been, 
and still is my design to make her my wife: and that i trust, when you 
consider these circumstances, our youth, and our attachment, you will 
not only forgive our momentary lapse from virtue, but will aid me in 
repair my fault to agnes, and secure a lawful title to her person 
and her heart.

o you! whom vanity's light bark convey 
on fame's mad voyage by the wind of praise, 
with what a shift gale your course you ply, 
for ever sunk too low, or borne too high! 
who pants for glory find but short repose, 
a breath revive him, and a breath o'er-throws. 
pope. 

here the marquis conclude his adventures. lorenzo, before he could 
determine on his reply, past some moments in reflection. at length he 
broke silence. 

'raymond,' said he taking his hand, 'strict honour would oblige me to 
wash off in your blood the stain thrown upon my family; but the 
circumstances of your case forbid me to consider you as an enemy. the 
temptation was too great to be resisted. 'tis the superstition of my 
relations which has occasion these misfortunes, and they are more the 
offender than yourself and agnes. what has past between you cannot be 
recalled, but may yet be repair by unite you to my sister. you 
have ever been, you still continue to be, my dearest and indeed my only 
friend. i feel for agnes the truest affection, and there is no one on 
whom i would bestow her more willing than on yourself. pursue then 
your design. i will accompany you tomorrow night, and conduct her 
myself to the house of the cardinal. my presence will be a sanction 
for her conduct, and prevent her incur blame by her flight from the 
convent.' 

the marquis thank him in terms by no means deficient in gratitude. 
lorenzo then informed him that he had nothing more to apprehend from 
donna rodolpha's enmity. five month had already elapse since, in an 
excess of passion, she broke a blood-vessel and expire in the course 
of a few hours. he then proceed to mention the interests of antonia. 
the marquis was much surprize at hearing of this new relation: his 
father had carry his hatred of elvira to the grave, and had never 
given the least hint that he knew what was become of his eldest son's 
widow. don raymond assured his friend that he was not mistaken in 
supposing him ready to acknowledge his sister-in-law and her amiable 
daughter. the preparation for the elope would not permit his 
visiting them the next day; but in the meanwhile he desire lorenzo to 
assure them of his friendship, and to supply elvira upon his account 
with any sum which she might want. this the youth promise to do, as 
soon as her abode should be known to him: he then took leave of his 
future brother, and return to the palace de medina. 

the day was already on the point of break when the marquis retired 
to his chamber. conscious that his narrative would take up some hours, 
and wish to secure himself from interrupt on return to the 
hotel, he ordered his attendant not to sit up for him. consequently, 
he was somewhat surprise on enter his antiroom, to find theodore 
establish there. the page sat near a table with a pen in his hand, 
and was so total occupy by his employment that he perceive not his 
lord's approach. the marquis stop to observe him. theodore wrote a 
few lines, then paused, and scratch out a part of the writing: then 
wrote again, smiled, and seem highly pleased with what he had been 
about. at last he threw down his pen, sprang from his chair, and 
clap his hand together joyfully. 

'there it is!' cry he aloud: 'now they are charming!' 

his transport were interrupt by a laugh from the marquis, who 
suspect the nature of his employment. 

'what is so charming, theodore?' 

the youth started, and look round. he blushed, ran to the table, 
seize the paper on which he had been writing, and conceal it in 
confusion. 

'oh! my lord, i knew not that you were so near me. can i be of use to 
you? luca is already gone to bed.' 

'i shall follow his example when i have given my opinion of your 
verses.' 

'my verses, my lord?' 

'nay, i am sure that you have been writing some, for nothing else could 
have kept you awake till this time of the morning. where are they, 
theodore? i shall like to see your composition.' 

theodore's cheek glow with still deep crimson: he long to show 
his poetry, but first chose to be pressed for it. 

'indeed, my lord, they are not worthy your attention.' 

'not these verses, which you just now declared to be so charming? 

come, come, let me see whether our opinion are the same. i promise 
that you shall find in me an indulgent critic.' 

the boy produce his paper with seeming reluctance; but the 
satisfaction which sparkle in his dark expressive eyes betray the 
vanity of his little bosom. the marquis smile while he observe the 
emotion of an heart as yet but little skilled in veil its 
sentiments. he seat himself upon a sopha: theodore, while hope and 
fear contend on his anxious countenance, wait with inquietude for 
his master's decision, while the marquis read the following lines. 

love and age 

the night was dark; the wind blew cold; 
anacreon, grown morose and old, 
sat by his fire, and fed the chearful flame: 
sudden the cottage-door expands, 
and lo! before him cupid stands, 
cast round a friendly glance, and greet him by his name. 

'what is it thou?' the startle sire 
in sullen tone exclaimed, while ire 
with crimson flushed his pale and wrinkle cheek: 
'wouldst thou again with amorous rage 
inflame my bosom? steel by age, 
vain boy, to pierce my breast thine arrow are too weak. 

'what seek you in this desart drear? 
no smile or sports inhabit here; 
ne'er did these val witness dalliance sweet: 
eternal winter bind the plains; 
age in my house despotic reigns, 
my garden boast no flower, my bosom boast no heat. 

'begone, and seek the blooming bower, 
where some ripe virgin court thy power, 
or bid provoking dream flit round her bed; 
on damon's amorous breast repose; 
wanton--on chloe's lip of rose, 
or make her blush cheek a pillow for thy head. 

'be such thy haunts; these regions cold 
avoid! nor think grown wise and old 
this hoary head again thy yoke shall bear: 
remember that my fairest years 
by thee were marked with sigh and tears, 
i think thy friendship false, and shun the guileful snare. 

'i have not yet forgot the pains 
i felt, while bound in julia's chains; 
the ardent flame with which my bosom burned; 
the nights i pass deprived of rest; 
the jealous pang which rack my breast; 
my disappointed hopes, and passion unreturned. 

'then fly, and curse mine eyes no more! 
fly from my peaceful cottage-door! 
no day, no hour, no moment shalt thou stay. 
i know thy falsehood, scorn thy arts, 
distrust thy smiles, and fear thy darts; 
traitor, begone, and seek some other to betray!' 

'does age, old man, your wit confound?' 
reply the offend god, and frowned; 
(his frown was sweet as is the virgin's smile!) 
'do you to me these words address? 
to me, who do not love you less, 
though you my friendship scorn, and pleasure past revile! 

'if one proud fair you chance to find, 
an hundred other nymph were kind, 
whose smile might well for julia's frown atone: 
but such is man! his partial hand 
unnumbered favours write on sand, 
but stamp one little fault on solid lasting stone. 

'ingrate! who led thee to the wave, 
at noon where lesbia love to lave? 
who name the bower alone where daphne lay? 
and who, when caelia shriek for aid, 
bad you with kiss hush the maid? 
what other was't than love, oh! false anacreon, say! 

'then you could call me--"gentle boy! 
"my only bliss! my source of joy!"-- 
then you could prize me dear than your soul! 
could kiss, and dance me on your knees; 
and swear, not wine itself would please, 
had not the lip of love first touched the flowing bowl! 

'must those sweet days return no more? 
must i for aye your loss deplore, 
banish your heart, and from your favour driven? 
ah! no; my fear that smile denies; 
that heave breast, those sparkle eyes 
declare me ever dear and all my fault forgiven. 

'again beloved, esteemed, carest, 
cupid shall in thine arms be prest, 
sport on thy knees, or on thy bosom sleep: 
my torch thine age-struck heart shall warm; 
my hand pale winter's rage disarm, 
and youth and spring shall here once more their revel keep.'-- 

a feather now of golden hue 
he smile from his pinion drew; 
this to the poet's hand the boy commits; 
and straight before anacreon's eyes 
the fairest dream of fancy rise, 
and round his favoured head wild inspiration flits. 

his bosom glow with amorous fire 
eager he grasp the magic lyre; 
swift o'er the tuneful chord his finger move: 
the feather pluck from cupid's wing 
sweep the too-long-neglected string, 
while soft anacreon sing the power and praise of love. 

soon as that name was heard, the wood 
shook off their snows; the melting flood 
broke their cold chains, and winter flee away. 
once more the earth was deckt with flowers; 
mild zephyr breathe through blooming bowers; 
high tower the glorious sun, and pour the blaze of day. 

attract by the harmonious sound, 
sylvan and faun the cot surround, 
and curious crowd the minstrel to behold: 
the wood-nymphs haste the spell to prove; 
eager they run; they list, they love, 
and while they hear the strain, forget the man is old. 

cupid, to nothing constant long, 
perch on the harp attend the song, 
or stifle with a kiss the dulcet notes: 
now on the poet's breast reposes, 
now twine his hoary locks with roses, 
or borne on wings of gold in wanton circle floats. 

then thus anacreon--'i no more 
at other shrine my vow will pour, 
since cupid deign my numbers to inspire: 
from phoebu or the blue-eyed maid 
now shall my verse request no aid, 
for love alone shall be the patron of my lyre. 

'in lofty strain, of early days, 
i spread the king's or hero's praise, 
and struck the martial chord with epic fire: 
but farewell, hero! farewell, king! 
your deed my lip no more shall sing, 
for love alone shall be the subject of my lyre. 


the marquis return the paper with a smile of encouragement. 

'your little poem please me much,' said he; 'however, you must not 
count my opinion for anything. i am no judge of verses, and for my own 
part, never compose more than six lines in my life: those six produce 
so unlucky an effect that i am fully resolve never to compose another. 
but i wander from my subject. i was go to say that you cannot 
employ your time worse than in making verses. an author, whether good 
or bad, or between both, is an animal whom everybody is privileged to 
attack; for though all are not able to write books, all conceive 
themselves able to judge them. a bad composition carry with it its 
own punishment, contempt and ridicule. a good one excite envy, and 
entail upon its author a thousand mortifications. he find himself 
assail by partial and ill-humoured criticism: one man find fault 
with the plan, another with the style, a third with the precept, which 
it strive to inculcate; and they who cannot succeed in finding fault 
with the book, employ themselves in stigmatize its author. they 
malicious rake out from obscurity every little circumstance which may 
throw ridicule upon his private character or conduct, and aim at 
wound the man, since they cannot hurt the writer. in short, to 
enter the lists of literature is wilful to expose yourself to the 
arrow of neglect, ridicule, envy, and disappointment. whether you 
write well or ill, be assured that you will not escape from blame; 
indeed this circumstance contain a young author's chief consolation: 
he remember that lope de vega and calderona had unjust and envious 
critics, and he modest conceive himself to be exactly in their 
predicament. but i am conscious that all these sage observations are 
thrown away upon you. authorship is a mania to conquer which no 
reason are sufficient strong; and you might as easily persuade me 
not to love, as i persuade you not to write. however, if you cannot 
help being occasional seize with a poetical paroxysm, take at least 
the precaution of communicate your verse to none but those whose 
partiality for you secure their approbation.' 

'then, my lord, you do not think these lines tolerable?' said theodore 
with an humble and dejected air. 

'you mistake my meaning. as i said before, they have pleased me much; 
but my regard for you make me partial, and other might judge them 
less favourably. i must still remark that even my prejudice in your 
favour do not blind me so much as to prevent my observing several 
faults. for instance, you make a terrible confusion of metaphors; you 
are too apt to make the strength of your lines consist more in the 
words than sense; some of the verse only seem introduce in order to 
rhyme with others; and most of the best idea are borrow from other 
poets, though possibly you are unconscious of the theft yourself. 
these fault may occasional be excuse in a work of length; but a 
short poem must be correct and perfect.' 

'all this is true, segnor; but you should consider that i only write 
for pleasure.' 

'your defect are the less excusable. their incorrect may be 
forgiven in those who work for money, who are oblige to compleat a 
given task in a given time, and are paid according to the bulk, not 
value of their productions. but in those whom no necessity forces to 
turn author, who merely write for fame, and have full leisure to polish 
their compositions, fault are impardonable, and merit the sharpest 
arrow of criticism.' 

the marquis rose from the sopha; the page look discourage and 
melancholy, and this did not escape his master's observation. 

'however' add he smiling, 'i think that these lines do you no 
discredit. your versification is tolerably easy, and your ear seem to 
be just. the peruse of your little poem upon the whole gave me much 
pleasure; and if it is not ask too great a favour, i shall be highly 
oblige to you for a copy.' 

the youth's countenance immediately clear up. he perceive not the 
smile, half approving, half ironical, which accompany the request, 
and he promise the copy with great readiness. the marquis withdrew to 
his chamber, much amuse by the instantaneous effect produce upon 
theodore's vanity by the conclusion of his criticism. he threw himself 
upon his couch; sleep soon stole over him, and his dream present him 
with the most flatter pictures of happiness with agnes. 

on reach the hotel de medina, lorenzo's first care was to enquire 
for letters. he found several wait for him; but that which he 
sought was not amongst them. leonella had found it impossible to write 
that evening. however, her impatience to secure don christoval's 
heart, on which she flatter herself with have made no slight 
impression, permit her not to pass another day without inform him 
where she was to be found. on her return from the capuchin church, she 
had related to her sister with exultation how attentive an handsome 
cavalier had been to her; as also how his companion had undertaken to 
plead antonia's cause with the marquis de las cisternas. elvira 
received this intelligence with sensation very different from those 
with which it was communicated. she blame her sister's imprudence in 
confiding her history to an absolute stranger, and express her fear 
lest this inconsiderate step should prejudice the marquis against her. 
the greatest of her apprehension she conceal in her own breast. she 
had observe with inquietude that at the mention of lorenzo, a deep 
blush spread itself over her daughter's cheek. the timid antonia dare 
not to pronounce his name: without knowing wherefore, she felt 
embarrass when he was made the subject of discourse, and endeavour 
to change the conversation to ambrosio. elvira perceive the emotion 
of this young bosom: in consequence, she insist upon leonella's 
break her promise to the cavaliers. a sigh, which on hearing this 
order escape from antonia, confirmed the wary mother in her resolution. 

through this resolution leonella was determined to break: she 
conceive it to be inspired by envy, and that her sister dread her 
being elevated above her. without impart her design to anyone, she 
took an opportunity of dispatch the following note to lorenzo; it 
was deliver to him as soon as he woke. 

'doubtless, segnor don lorenzo, you have frequent accused me of 
ingratitude and forgetfulness: but on the word of a virgin, it was out 
of my power to perform my promise yesterday. i know not in what words 
to inform you how strange a reception my sister gave your kind wish to 
visit her. she is an odd woman, with many good points about her; but 
her jealousy of me frequent make her conceive notions quite 
unaccountable. on hearing that your friend had paid some little 
attention to me, she immediately took the alarm: she blame my 
conduct, and has absolutely forbidden me to let you know our abode. my 
strong sense of gratitude for your kind offer of service, and ... 
shall i confess it? my desire to behold once more the too amiable don 
christoval, will not permit my obey her injunctions. i have 
therefore stolen a moment to inform you, that we lodge in the strada di 
san iago, four door from the palace d'albornos, and nearly opposite to 
the barber's miguel coello. enquire for donna elvira dalfa, since in 
compliance with her father-in-law's order, my sister continue to be 
call by her maiden name. at eight this evening you will be sure of 
finding us: but let not a word drop which may raise a suspicion of my 
have written this letter. should you see the conde d'ossorio, tell 
him ... i blush while i declare it ... 

tell him that his presence will be but too acceptable to the 
sympathetic leonella. 

the latter sentence were written in red ink, to express the blush of 
her cheek, while she committed an outrage upon her virgin modesty. 

lorenzo had no soon peruse this note than he set out in search of 
don christoval. not being able to find him in the course of the day, 
he proceed to donna elvira's alone, to leonella's infinite 
disappointment. the domestic by whom he sent up his name, have 
already declared his lady to be at home, she had no excuse for refuse 
his visit: yet she consent to receive it with much reluctance. that 
reluctance was increase by the change which his approach produce in 
antonia's countenance; nor was it by any means abate when the youth 
himself appeared. the symmetry of his person, animation of his 
features, and natural elegance of his manners and address, convinced 
elvira that such a guest must be dangerous for her daughter. she 
resolve to treat him with distant politeness, to decline his service 
with gratitude for the tender of them, and to make him feel, without 
offence, that his future visit would be far from acceptable. 

on his entrance he found elvira, who was indisposed, recline upon a 
sopha: antonia sat by her embroidery frame, and leonella, in a 
pastoral dress, held 'montemayor's diana.' in spite of her being the 
mother of antonia, lorenzo could not help expect to find in elvira 
leonella's true sister, and the daughter of 'as honest a painstaking 
shoe-maker, as any in cordova.' a single glance was sufficient to 
undeceive him. he beheld a woman whose features, though impair by 
time and sorrow, still bore the mark of distinguished beauty: a 
serious dignity reign upon her countenance, but was temper by a 
grace and sweet which render her truly enchanting. lorenzo 
fancy that she must have resemble her daughter in her youth, and 
readily excuse the imprudence of the late conde de las cisternas. she 
desire him to be seated, and immediately resume her place upon the 
sopha. 

antonia received him with a simple reverence, and continue her work: 
her cheek were suffuse with crimson, and she strove to conceal her 
emotion by leaning over her embroidery frame. her aunt also chose to 
play off her airs of modesty; she affected to blush and tremble, and 
wait with her eyes cast down to receive, as she expected, the 
compliments of don christoval. finding after some time that no sign of 
his approach was given, she venture to look round the room, and 
perceive with vexation that medina was unaccompanied. impatience 
would not permit her wait for an explanation: interrupt lorenzo, 
who was deliver raymond's message, she desire to know what was 
become of his friend. 

he, who thought it necessary to maintain himself in her good graces, 
strove to console her under her disappointment by commit a little 
violence upon truth. 

'ah! segnora,' he reply in a melancholy voice 'how grieve will he be 
at lose this opportunity of pay you his respects! a relation's 
illness has oblige him to quit madrid in haste: but on his return, he 
will doubtless seize the first moment with transport to throw himself 
at your feet!' 

as he said this, his eyes met those of elvira: she punish his 
falsehood sufficient by dart at him a look expressive of 
displeasure and reproach. neither did the deceit answer his intention. 
vex and disappointed leonella rose from her seat, and retired in 
dudgeon to her own apartment. 

lorenzo hasten to repair the fault, which had injure him in elvira's 
opinion. he related his conversation with the marquis respecting her: 
he assured her that raymond was prepared to acknowledge her for his 
brother's widow; and that till it was in his power to pay his 
compliments to her in person, lorenzo was commission to supply his 
place. this intelligence relieved elvira from an heavy weight of 
uneasiness: she had now found a protector for the fatherless antonia, 
for whose future fortune she had suffer the greatest apprehensions. 
she was not sparing of her thanks to him who had interfere so 
generous in her behalf; but still she gave him no invitation to 
repeat his visit. 

however, when upon rising to depart he request permission to enquire 
after her health occasionally, the polite earnest of his manner, 
gratitude for his services, and respect for his friend the marquis, 
would not admit of a refusal. she consent reluctantly to receive 
him: he promise not to abuse her goodness, and quit the house. 

antonia was now left alone with her mother: a temporary silence 
ensued. both wish to speak upon the same subject, but neither knew 
how to introduce it. the one felt a bashful which seal up her 
lips, and for which she could not account: the other fear to find 
her apprehension true, or to inspire her daughter with notions to 
which she might be still a stranger. at length elvira began the 
conversation. 

'that is a charming young man, antonia; i am much pleased with him. 
was he long near you yesterday in the cathedral?' 

'he quit me not for a moment while i staid in the church: he gave 
me his seat, and was very obliging and attentive.' 

'indeed? why then have you never mention his name to me? your aunt 
lanche out in praise of his friend, and you vaunt ambrosio's 
eloquence: but neither said a word of don lorenzo's person and 
accomplishments. had not leonella spoken of his readiness to undertake 
our cause, i should not have known him to be in existence.' 

she paused. antonia coloured, but was silent. 

'perhaps you judge him less favourable than i do. in my opinion his 
figure is pleasing, his conversation sensible, and manners engaging. 
still he may have struck you differently: you may think him 
disagreeable, and ...'. 

'disagreeable? oh! dear mother, how should i possibly think him so? i 
should be very ungrateful were i not sensible of his kindness 
yesterday, and very blind if his merit had escape me. his figure is 
so graceful, so noble! his manners so gentle, yet so manly! i never 
yet saw so many accomplishment united in one person, and i doubt 
whether madrid can produce his equal.' 

'why then were you so silent in praise of this phoenix of madrid? 

why was it conceal from me that his society had afford you 
pleasure?' 

'in truth, i know not: you ask me a question which i cannot resolve 
myself. i was on the point of mention him a thousand times: his 
name was constant upon my lips, but when i would have pronounced it, 
i want courage to execute my design. however, if i did not speak of 
him, it was not that i thought of him the less.' 

'that i believe; but shall i tell you why you want courage? it was 
because, accustomed to confide to me your most secret thoughts, you 
knew not how to conceal, yet fear to acknowledge, that your heart 
nourish a sentiment which you were conscious i should disapprove. 
come hither to me, my child.' 

antonia quit her embroidery frame, threw herself upon her knee by 
the sopha, and hid her face in her mother's lap. 

'fear not, my sweet girl! consider me equally as your friend and 
parent, and apprehend no reproof from me. i have read the emotion of 
your bosom; you are yet ill-skilled in conceal them, and they could 
not escape my attentive eye. this lorenzo is dangerous to your repose; 
he has already made an impression upon your heart. 'tis true that i 
perceive easily that your affection is returned; but what can be the 
consequence of this attachment? you are poor and friendless, my 
antonia; lorenzo is the heir of the duke of medina celi. even should 
himself mean honourably, his uncle never will consent to your union; 
nor without that uncle's consent, will i. by sad experience i know 
what sorrow she must endure, who marry into a family unwill to 
receive her. then struggle with your affection: whatever pains it may 
cost you, strive to conquer it. your heart is tender and susceptible: 
it has already received a strong impression; but when once convinced 
that you should not encourage such sentiments, i trust, that you have 
sufficient fortitude to drive them from your bosom.' 

antonia kiss her hand, and promise implicit obedience. elvira then 
continued. 

'to prevent your passion from grow stronger, it will be needful to 
prohibit lorenzo's visits. the service which he has render me 
permit not my forbidding them positively; but unless i judge too 
favourable of his character, he will discontinue them without taking 
offence, if i confess to him my reasons, and throw myself entire on 
his generosity. the next time that i see him, i will honestly avow to 
him the embarrassment which his presence occasions. how say you, my 
child? is not this measure necessary?' 

antonia subscribe to every thing without hesitation, though not 
without regret. her mother kiss her affectionately, and retired to 
bed. antonia follow her example, and vow so frequent never more 
to think of lorenzo, that till sleep closed her eyes she thought of 
nothing else. 

while this was passing at elvira's, lorenzo hasten to rejoin the 
marquis. every thing was ready for the second elope of agnes; and 
at twelve the two friends with a coach and four were at the garden wall 
of the convent. don raymond drew out his key, and unlock the door. 
they entered, and wait for some time in expectation of being join 
by agnes. at length the marquis grew impatient: beginning to fear 
that his second attempt would succeed no better than the first, he 
propose to reconnoitre the convent. the friends advanced towards it. 
every thing was still and dark. the prioress was anxious to keep the 
story a secret, fear lest the crime of one of its member should 
bring disgrace upon the whole community, or that the interposition of 
powerful relations should deprive her vengeance of its intended victim. 
she took care therefore to give the lover of agnes no cause to suppose 
that his design was discovered, and his mistress on the point of 
suffering the punishment of her fault. the same reason made her reject 
the idea of arrest the unknown seduce in the garden; such a 
proceeding would have create much disturbance, and the disgrace of her 
convent would have been noise about madrid. she contented herself 
with confine agnes closely; as to the lover, she left him at liberty 
to pursue his designs. what she had expect was the result. the 
marquis and lorenzo wait in vain till the break of day: they then 
retired without noise, alarm at the failure of their plan, and 
ignorant of the cause of its ill-success. 

the next morning lorenzo went to the convent, and request to see his 
sister. the prioress appear at the grate with a melancholy 
countenance: she informed him that for several days agnes had appear 
much agitated; that she had been prest by the nun in vain to reveal 
the cause, and apply to their tender for advice and consolation; 
that she had obstinate persist in conceal the cause of her 
distress; but that on thursday evening it had produce so violent an 
effect upon her constitution, that she had fallen ill, and was actually 
confine to her bed. lorenzo did not credit a syllable of this 
account: he insist upon seeing his sister; if she was unable to come 
to the grate, he desire to be admitted to her cell. the prioress 
cross herself! she was shock at the very idea of a man's profane 
eye pervade the interior of her holy mansion, and professed herself 
astonish that lorenzo could think of such a thing. she told him that 
his request could not be granted; but that if he return the next day, 
she hope that her beloved daughter would then be sufficient 
recover to join him at the parlour grate. 

with this answer lorenzo was oblige to retire, unsatisfy and 
tremble for his sister's safety. 

he return the next morning at an early hour. 'agnes was worse; the 
physician had pronounced her to be in imminent danger; she was ordered 
to remain quiet, and it was utterly impossible for her to receive her 
brother's visit.' lorenzo storm at this answer, but there was no 
resource. he raved, he entreated, he threatened: no means were left 
untry to obtain a sight of agnes. his endeavour were as fruitless 
as those of the day before, and he return in despair to the marquis. 
on his side, the latter had spare no pains to discover what had 
occasion his plot to fail: don christoval, to whom the affair was 
now entrusted, endeavour to worm out the secret from the old 
porteress of st. clare, with whom he had form an acquaintance; but 
she was too much upon her guard, and he gain from her no 
intelligence. the marquis was almost distracted, and lorenzo felt 
scarcely less inquietude. both were convinced that the purpose 
elope must have been discovered: they doubt not but the malady 
of agnes was a pretence, but they knew not by what means to rescue her 
from the hand of the prioress. 

regularly every day did lorenzo visit the convent: as regularly was he 
informed that his sister rather grew worse than better. certain that 
her indisposition was feigned, these account did not alarm him: but 
his ignorance of her fate, and of the motive which induce the 
prioress to keep her from him, excited the most serious uneasiness. he 
was still uncertain what steps he ought to take, when the marquis 
received a letter from the cardinal-duke of lerma. it inclose the 
pope's expect bull, order that agnes should be release from her 
vows, and restore to her relations. this essential paper decided at 
once the proceedings of her friends: they resolve that lorenzo should 
carry it to the domina without delay, and demand that his sister should 
be instantly given up to him. against this mandate illness could not 
be pleaded: it gave her brother the power of remove her instantly to 
the palace de medina, and he determined to use that power on the 
following day. 

his mind relieved from inquietude respecting his sister, and his 
spirits raise by the hope of soon restore her to freedom, he now had 
time to give a few moments to love and to antonia. at the same hour as 
on his former visit he repair to donna elvira's: she had given 
orders for his admission. as soon as he was announced, her daughter 
retired with leonella, and when he enter the chamber, he found the 
lady of the house alone. she received him with less distance than 
before, and desire him to place himself near her upon the sopha. she 
then without lose time open her business, as had been agree 
between herself and antonia. 

'you must not think me ungrateful, don lorenzo, or forgetful how 
essential are the service which you have render me with the marquis. 
i feel the weight of my obligations; nothing under the sun should 
induce my taking the step to which i am now compel but the interest 
of my child, of my beloved antonia. my health is declining; god only 
know how soon i may be summon before his throne. my daughter will 
be left without parents, and should she lose the protection of the 
cisterna family, without friends. 

she is young and artless, uninstructed in the world's perfidy, and with 
charm sufficient to render her an object of seduction. judge then, how 
i must tremble at the prospect before her! judge how anxious i must be 
to keep her from their society who may excite the yet dormant passion 
of her bosom. you are amiable, don lorenzo: antonia has a 
susceptible, a loving heart, and is grateful for the favours confer 
upon us by your interference with the marquis. your presence make me 
tremble: i fear lest it should inspire her with sentiment which may 
embitter the remainder of her life, or encourage her to cherish hope 
in her situation unjustifiable and futile. pardon me when i avow my 
terrors, and let my frank plead in my excuse. i cannot forbid you 
my house, for gratitude restrain me; i can only throw myself upon your 
generosity, and entreat you to spare the feelings of an anxious, of a 
doting mother. believe me when i assure you that i lament the 
necessity of reject your acquaintance; but there is no remedy, and 
antonia's interest oblige me to beg you to forbear your visits. by 
comply with my request, you will increase the esteem which i already 
feel for you, and of which everything convince me that you are truly 
deserving.' 

'your frank charm me,' reply lorenzo; 'you shall find that in 
your favourable opinion of me you were not deceived. yet i hope that 
the reasons, now in my power to allege, will persuade you to withdraw a 
request which i cannot obey without infinite reluctance. i love your 
daughter, love her most sincerely: i wish for no greater happiness 
than to inspire her with the same sentiments, and receive her hand at 
the altar as her husband. 'tis true, i am not rich myself; my father's 
death has left me but little in my own possession; but my expectations 
justify my pretend to the conde de las cisternas' daughter.' 

he was proceeding, but elvira interrupt him. 

'ah! don lorenzo, you forget in that pompous title the mean of my 
origin. you forget that i have now past fourteen years in spain, 
disavow by my husband's family, and existing upon a stipend barely 
sufficient for the support and education of my daughter. nay, i have 
even been neglect by most of my own relations, who out of envy affect 
to doubt the reality of my marriage. my allowance being discontinue 
at my father-in-law's death, i was reduce to the very brink of want. 
in this situation i was found by my sister, who amongst all her foible 
possess a warm, generous, and affectionate heart. she aid me with 
the little fortune which my father left her, persuade me to visit 
madrid, and has support my child and myself since our quit 
murcia. then consider not antonia as descended from the conde de la 
cisternas: consider her as a poor and unprotected orphan, as the 
grand-child of the tradesman torribio dalfa, as the needy pensioner of 
that tradesman's daughter. reflect upon the difference between such a 
situation, and that of the nephew and heir of the potent duke of 
medina. i believe your intentions to be honourable; but as there are 
no hope that your uncle will approve of the union, i foresee that the 
consequence of your attachment must be fatal to my child's repose.' 

'pardon me, segnora; you are misinform if you suppose the duke of 
medina to resemble the generality of men. his sentiment are liberal 
and disinterested: he love me well; and i have no reason to dread his 
forbidding the marriage when he perceive that my happiness depend 
upon antonia. but supposing him to refuse his sanction, what have i 
still to fear? my parent are no more; my little fortune is in my own 
possession: it will be sufficient to support antonia, and i shall 
exchange for her hand medina's dukedom without one sigh of regret.' 

'you are young and eager; it is natural for you to entertain such 
ideas. but experience has taught me to my cost that curse accompany 
an unequal alliance. i married the conde de las cisterna in 
opposition to the will of his relations; many an heart-pang has 
punish me for the imprudent step. whereever we bent our course, a 
father's execrate pursue gonzalvo. poverty overtook us, and no 
friend was near to relieve our wants. still our mutual affection 
existed, but alas! not without interruption. 

accustomed to wealth and ease, ill could my husband support the 
transition to distress and indigence. he look back with repine to 
the comfort which he once enjoyed. he regret the situation which 
for my sake he had quitted; and in moments when despair possessed his 
mind, has reproach me with have made him the companion of want and 
wretchedness! he has call me his bane! the source of his sorrows, 
the cause of his destruction! ah god! he little knew how much keen 
were my own heart's reproaches! he was ignorant that i suffer 
trebly, for myself, for my children, and for him! 'tis true that his 
anger seldom last long: his sincere affection for me soon revive in 
his heart; and then his repentance for the tear which he had made me 
shed torture me even more than his reproaches. he would throw himself 
on the ground, implore my forgiveness in the most frantic terms, and 
load himself with curse for being the murder of my repose. taught 
by experience that an union contract against the inclination of 
family on either side must be unfortunate, i will save my daughter 
from those misery which i have suffered. without your uncle's 
consent, while i live, she never shall be yours. undoubted he will 
disapprove of the union; his power is immense, and antonia shall not be 
expose to his anger and persecution.' 

'his persecution? how easily may that be avoided! let the worst 
happen, it is but quit spain. my wealth may easily be realised; 
the indian island will offer us a secure retreat; i have an estate, 
though not of value, in hispaniola: thither will we fly, and i shall 
consider it to be my native country, if it give me antonia's 
undisturbed possession.' 

'ah! youth, this is a fond romantic vision. gonzalvo thought the same. 
he fancy that he could leave spain without regret; but the moment of 
parting undeceive him. you know not yet what it is to quit your 
native land; to quit it, never to behold it more! 

you know not, what it is to exchange the scene where you have pass 
your infancy, for unknown realm and barbarous climates! to be 
forgotten, utterly eternal forgotten, by the companion of your 
youth! to see your dearest friends, the fondest object of your 
affection, perishing with disease incidental to indian atmospheres, 
and find yourself unable to procure for them necessary assistance! i 
have felt all this! my husband and two sweet babe found their grave 
in cuba: nothing would have save my young antonia but my sudden 
return to spain. ah! don lorenzo, could you conceive what i suffer 
during my absence! could you know how sorely i regret all that i 
left behind, and how dear to me was the very name of spain! i envy 
the wind which blew towards it: and when the spain sailor chaunte 
some well-known air as he past my window, tear fill my eyes while i 
thought upon my native land. gonzalvo too ... my husband ...'. 

elvira paused. her voice faltered, and she conceal her face with her 
handkerchief. after a short silence she rose from the sopha, and 
proceeded. 

'excuse my quit you for a few moments: the remembrance of what i 
have suffer has much agitate me, and i need to be alone. till i 
return peruse these lines. after my husband's death i found them among 
his papers; had i known soon that he entertain such sentiments, 
grief would have kill me. he wrote these verse on his voyage to 
cuba, when his mind was cloud by sorrow, and he forgot that he had a 
wife and children. 

what we are losing, ever seem to us the most precious: gonzalvo was 
quit spain for ever, and therefore was spain dear to his eyes 
than all else which the world contained. read them, don lorenzo; they 
will give you some idea of the feelings of a banish man!' 

elvira put a paper into lorenzo's hand, and retired from the chamber. 
the youth examine the contents, and found them to be as follows. 

the exile 

farewell, oh! native spain! farewell for ever! 
these banish eyes shall view thy coast no more; 
a mournful presage tell my heart, that never 
gonzalvo's steps again shall press thy shore. 

hush are the winds; while soft the vessel sailing 
with gentle motion plow the unruffled main, 
i feel my bosom's boast courage failing, 
and curse the wave which bear me far from spain. 

i see it yet! beneath yon blue clear heaven 
still do the spires, so well beloved, appear; 
from yonder craggy point the gale of even 
still waft my native accent to mine ear: 

prop on some moss-crowned rock, and gaily singing, 
there in the sun his nets the fisher dries; 
oft have i heard the plaintive ballad, bring 
scene of past joy before my sorrow eyes. 

ah! happy swain! he waits the accustomed hour, 
when twilight-gloom obscure the close sky; 
then gladly seek his love paternal bower, 
and shares the feast his native field supply: 

friendship and love, his cottage guests, receive him 
with honest welcome and with smile sincere; 
no threaten woe of present joy bereave him, 
no sigh his bosom owns, his cheek no tear. 

ah! happy swain! such bliss to me denying, 
fortune thy lot with envy bid me view; 
me, who from home and spain an exile flying, 
bid all i value, all i love, adieu. 

no more mine ear shall list the well-known ditty 
sung by some mountain-girl, who tend her goats, 
some village-swain implore amorous pity, 
or shepherd chaunt wild his rustic notes: 

no more my arms a parent's fond embraces, 
no more my heart domestic calm, must know; 
far from these joys, with sigh which memory traces, 
to sultry skies, and distant clime i go. 

where indian sun engender new diseases, 
where snake and tiger breed, i bend my way 
to brave the feverish thirst no art appeases, 
the yellow plague, and mad blaze of day: 

but not to feel slow pang consume my liver, 
to die by piece-meal in the bloom of age, 
my boil blood drank by insatiate fever, 
and brain delirious with the day-star's rage, 

can make me know such grief, as thus to sever 
with many a bitter sigh, dear land, from thee; 
to feel this heart must doat on thee for ever, 
and feel, that all thy joy are torn from me! 

ah me! how oft will fancy's spell in slumber 
recall my native country to my mind! 
how oft regret will bid me sadly number 
each lost delight and dear friend left behind! 

wild murcia's vales, and love romantic bowers, 
the river on whose bank a child i played, 
my castle's antient halls, its frown towers, 
each much-regretted wood, and well-known glade, 

dream of the land where all my wish centre, 
thy scenes, which i am doom no more to know, 
full oft shall memory trace, my soul's tormentor, 
and turn each pleasure past to present woe. 

but lo! the sun beneath the wave retires; 
night speed apace her empire to restore: 
cloud from my sight obscure the village-spires, 
now seen but faintly, and now seen no more. 

oh! breathe not, winds! still be the water's motion! 
sleep, sleep, my bark, in silence on the main! 
so when to-morrow's light shall gild the ocean, 
once more mine eyes shall see the coast of spain. 

vain is the wish! my last petition scorning, 
fresh blow the gale, and high the billow swell: 
far shall we be before the break of morning; 
oh! then for ever, native spain, farewell! 


lorenzo had scarcely time to read these lines, when elvira return to 
him: the give a free course to her tear had relieved her, and her 
spirits had regain their usual composure. 

'i have nothing more to say, my lord,' said she; 'you have heard my 
apprehensions, and my reason for beg you not to repeat your 
visits. i have thrown myself in full confidence upon your honour: i 
am certain that you will not prove my opinion of you to have been too 
favourable.' 

'but one question more, segnora, and i leave you. should the duke of 
medina approve my love, would my address be unacceptable to yourself 
and the fair antonia?' 

'i will be open with you, don lorenzo: there being little probability 
of such an union taking place, i fear that it is desire but too 
ardent by my daughter. you have made an impression upon her young 
heart, which give me the most serious alarm: to prevent that 
impression from grow stronger, i am oblige to decline your 
acquaintance. for me, you may be sure that i should rejoice at 
establish my child so advantageously. conscious that my 
constitution, impair by grief and illness, forbid me to expect a 
long continuance in this world, i tremble at the thought of leave her 
under the protection of a perfect stranger. the marquis de las 
cisterna is total unknown to me: 

he will marry; his lady may look upon antonia with an eye of 
displeasure, and deprive her of her only friend. should the duke, your 
uncle, give his consent, you need not doubt obtain mine, and my 
daughter's: but without his, hope not for ours. at all events, what 
ever steps you may take, what ever may be the duke's decision, till you 
know it let me beg your forbearing to strengthen by your presence 
antonia's prepossession. if the sanction of your relations authorise 
your address her as your wife, my door fly open to you: if that 
sanction is refused, be satisfy to possess my esteem and gratitude, 
but remember, that we must meet no more.' 

lorenzo promise reluctantly to conform to this decree: but he add 
that he hope soon to obtain that consent which would give him a claim 
to the renewal of their acquaintance. he then explain to her why the 
marquis had not call in person, and made no scruple of confiding to 
her his sister's history. he conclude by saying that he hope to set 
agnes at liberty the next day; and that as soon as don raymond's fear 
were quiet upon this subject, he would lose no time in assure donna 
elvira of his friendship and protection. 

the lady shook her head. 

'i tremble for your sister,' said she; 'i have heard many trait of the 
domina of st. clare's character, from a friend who was educated in the 
same convent with her. she report her to be haughty, inflexible, 
superstitious, and revengeful. i have since heard that she is 
infatuated with the idea of rendering her convent the most regular in 
madrid, and never forgave those whose imprudence threw upon it the 
slightest stain. though naturally violent and severe, when her 
interests require it, she well know how to assume an appearance of 
benignity. she leaves no means untry to persuade young women of rank 
to become member of her community: she is implacable when once 
incensed, and has too much intrepid to shrink at taking the most 
rigorous measure for punishing the offender. doubtless, she will 
consider your sister's quit the convent as a disgrace thrown upon 
it: she will use every artifice to avoid obey the mandate of his 
holiness, and i shudder to think that donna agnes is in the hand of 
this dangerous woman.' 

lorenzo now rose to take leave. elvira gave him her hand at parting, 
which he kiss respectfully; and telling her that he soon hope for 
the permission to salute that of antonia, he return to his hotel. 
the lady was perfectly satisfy with the conversation which had past 
between them. she look forward with satisfaction to the prospect of 
his becoming her son-in-law; but prudence bad her conceal from her 
daughter's knowledge the flatter hope which herself now venture to 
entertain. 

scarcely was it day, and already lorenzo was at the convent of st. 
clare, furnish with the necessary mandate. the nun were at matins. 
he wait impatient for the conclusion of the service, and at length 
the prioress appear at the parlour grate. agnes was demanded. the 
old lady replied, with a melancholy air, that the dear child's 
situation grew hourly more dangerous; that the physician despair of 
her life; but that they had declared the only chance for her recovery 
to consist in keeping her quiet, and not to permit those to approach 
her whose presence was likely to agitate her. not a word of all this 
was believe by lorenzo, any more than he credit the expression of 
grief and affection for agnes, with which this account was interlarded. 
to end the business, he put the pope's bull into the hand of the 
domina, and insist that, ill or in health, his sister should be 
deliver to him without delay. 

the prioress received the paper with an air of humility: but no soon 
had her eye glance over the contents, than her resentment baffle all 
the effort of hypocrisy. a deep crimson spread itself over her face, 
and she dart upon lorenzo looks of rage and menace. 

'this order is positive,' said she in a voice of anger, which she in 
vain strove to disguise; 'willingly would i obey it; but unfortunately 
it is out of my power.' 

lorenzo interrupt her by an exclamation of surprize. 

'i repeat it, segnor; to obey this order is total out of my power. 
from tender to a brother's feelings, i would have communicate the 
sad event to you by degrees, and have prepared you to hear it with 
fortitude. my measure are broken through: this order command me to 
deliver up to you the sister agnes without delay; i am therefore 
oblige to inform you without circumlocution, that on friday last, she 
expired.' 

lorenzo start back with horror, and turn pale. a moment's 
recollection convinced him that this assertion must be false, and it 
restore him to himself. 

'you deceive me!' said he passionately; 'but five minutes past since 
you assured me that though ill she was still alive. produce her this 
instant! see her i must and will, and every attempt to keep her from 
me will be unavailing.' 

'you forget yourself, segnor; you owe respect to my age as well as my 
profession. your sister is no more. if i at first conceal her 
death, it was from dread lest an event so unexpected should produce 
on you too violent an effect. in truth, i am but ill repaid for my 
attention. and what interest, i pray you, should i have in detain 
her? to know her wish of quit our society is a sufficient reason 
for me to wish her absence, and think her a disgrace to the sisterhood 
of st. clare: but she has forfeit my affection in a manner yet more 
culpable. her crime were great, and when you know the cause of her 
death, you will doubtless rejoice, don lorenzo, that such a wretch is 
no long in existence. she was taken ill on thursday last on 
return from confession in the capuchin chapel. her malady seem 
attend with strange circumstances; but she persist in conceal 
its cause: thanks to the virgin, we were too ignorant to suspect it! 
judge then what must have been our consternation, our horror, when she 
was deliver the next day of a stillborn child, whom she immediately 
follow to the grave. how, segnor? is it possible that your 
countenance express no surprize, no indignation? is it possible that 
your sister's infamy was known to you, and that still she possessed 
your affection? in that case, you have no need of my compassion. i 
can say nothing more, except repeat my inability of obey the orders 
of his holiness. agnes is no more, and to convince you that what i say 
is true, i swear by our blessed saviour, that three days have past 
since she was buried.' 

here she kiss a small crucifix which hung at her girdle. she then 
rose from her chair, and quit the parlour. as she withdrew, she 
cast upon lorenzo a scornful smile. 

'farewell, segnor,' said she; 'i know no remedy for this accident: i 
fear that even a second bull from the pope will not procure your 
sister's resurrection.' 

lorenzo also retired, penetrate with affliction: but don raymond's at 
the news of this event amount to madness. he would not be convinced 
that agnes was really dead, and continue to insist that the wall of 
st. clare still confine her. no argument could make him abandon his 
hope of regain her: every day some fresh scheme was invent for 
procure intelligence of her, and all of them were attend with the 
same success. 

on his part, medina gave up the idea of ever seeing his sister more: 
yet he believe that she had been taken off by unfair means. under 
this persuasion, he encourage don raymond's researches, determined, 
should he discover the least warrant for his suspicions, to take a 
severe vengeance upon the unfeeling prioress. the loss of his sister 
affected him sincerely; nor was it the least cause of his distress that 
propriety oblige him for some time to defer mention antonia to the 
duke. in the meanwhile his emissary constant surround elvira's 
door. he had intelligence of all the movements of his mistress: as she 
never fail every thursday to attend the sermon in the capuchin 
cathedral, he was secure of seeing her once a week, though in 
compliance with his promise, he careful shun her observation. 
thus two long month pass away. still no information was procure of 
agnes: all but the marquis credit her death; and now lorenzo 
determined to disclose his sentiment to his uncle. he had already 
dropt some hint of his intention to marry; they had been as favourable 
received as he could expect, and he harbour no doubt of the success 
of his application. 



chapter iii 

while in each other's arms entrance they lay, 
they blessed the night, and curst the coming day. 
lee. 

the burst of transport was past: ambrosio's lust was satisfied; 
pleasure fled, and shame usurp her seat in his bosom. confuse and 
terrify at his weakness, he drew himself from matilda's arms. his 
perjury present itself before him: he reflect on the scene which 
had just been acted, and tremble at the consequence of a discovery. 
he look forward with horror; his heart was despondent, and became the 
abode of satiety and disgust. he avoid the eyes of his partner in 
frailty; a melancholy silence prevailed, during which both seem 
busy with disagreeable reflections. 

matilda was the first to break it. she took his hand gently, and 
pressed it to her burning lips. 

'ambrosio!' she murmur in a soft and tremble voice. 

the abbot start at the sound. he turn his eyes upon matilda's: 
they were fill with tears; her cheek were cover with blushes, and 
her supplicate looks seem to solicit his compassion. 

'dangerous woman!' said he; 'into what an abyss of misery have you 
plunge me! should your sex be discovered, my honour, nay my life, 
must pay for the pleasure of a few moments. fool that i was, to trust 
myself to your seductions! what can now be done? how can my offence be 
expiated? what atone can purchase the pardon of my crime? 
wretched matilda, you have destroy my quiet for ever!' 

'to me these reproaches, ambrosio? to me, who have sacrifice for you 
the world's pleasures, the luxury of wealth, the delicacy of sex, my 
friends, my fortune, and my fame? what have you lost, which i 
preserved? have _i_ not share in your guilt? have you not share in 
my pleasure? guilt, did i say? in what consist ours, unless in the 
opinion of an ill-judging world? let that world be ignorant of them, 
and our joy become divine and blameless! unnatural were your vow of 
celibacy; man was not create for such a state; and were love a crime, 
god never would have made it so sweet, so irresistible! then banish 
those cloud from your brow, my ambrosio! indulge in those pleasure 
freely, without which life is a worthless gift: cease to reproach me 
with have taught you what is bliss, and feel equal transport with 
the woman who adore you!' 

as she spoke, her eyes were fill with a delicious languor. her bosom 
panted: she twin her arms voluptuous round him, drew him towards 
her, and glewe her lip to his. ambrosio again rage with desire: 
the die was thrown: his vow were already broken; he had already 
committed the crime, and why should he refrain from enjoy its 
reward? he clasp her to his breast with redouble ardour. no long 
repressed by the sense of shame, he gave a loose to his intemperate 
appetites. while the fair wanton put every invention of lust in 
practice, every refinement in the art of pleasure which might heighten 
the bliss of her possession, and render her lover's transport still 
more exquisite, ambrosio riot in delight till then unknown to him: 
swift flee the night, and the morning blush to behold him still 
clasp in the embrace of matilda. 

intoxicate with pleasure, the monk rose from the syren's luxurious 
couch. he no long reflect with shame upon his incontinence, or 
dread the vengeance of offend heaven. his only fear was lest death 
should rob him of enjoyments, for which his long fast had only given a 
keen edge to his appetite. matilda was still under the influence of 
poison, and the voluptuous monk tremble less for his preserver's life 
than his concubine's. deprived of her, he would not easily find 
another mistress with whom he could indulge his passion so fully, and 
so safely. he therefore pressed her with earnest to use the means 
of preservation which she had declared to be in her possession. 

'yes!' reply matilda; 'since you have made me feel that life is 
valuable, i will rescue mine at any rate. no danger shall appall me: 
i will look upon the consequence of my action boldly, nor shudder at 
the horrors which they present. i will think my sacrifice scarcely 
worthy to purchase your possession, and remember that a moment past in 
your arms in this world o'er-pays an age of punishment in the next. 
but before i take this step, ambrosio, give me your solemn oath never 
to enquire by what means i shall preserve myself.' 

he did so in a manner the most binding. 

'i thank you, my beloved. this precaution is necessary, for though you 
know it not, you are under the command of vulgar prejudices: the 
business on which i must be employ this night, might startle you from 
its singularity, and lower me in your opinion. tell me; are you 
possessed of the key of the low door on the western side of the garden?' 

'the door which open into the burying-ground common to us and the 
sisterhood of st. clare? i have not the key, but can easily procure 
it.' 

'you have only this to do. admit me into the burying-ground at 
midnight; watch while i descend into the vault of st. clare, lest some 
pry eye should observe my actions; leave me there alone for an hour, 
and that life is safe which i dedicate to your pleasures. to prevent 
create suspicion, do not visit me during the day. remember the key, 
and that i expect you before twelve. hark! i hear steps approaching! 
leave me; i will pretend to sleep.' 

the friar obeyed, and left the cell. as he open the door, father 
pablo made his appearance. 

'i come,' said the latter, 'to enquire after the health of my young 
patient.' 

'hush!' reply ambrosio, lay his finger upon his lip; 'speak 
softly; i am just come from him. he has fallen into a profound 
slumber, which doubtless will be of service to him. do not disturb him 
at present, for he wish to repose.' 

father pablo obeyed, and hearing the bell ring, accompany the abbot 
to matins. ambrosio felt embarrass as he enter the chapel. guilt 
was new to him, and he fancy that every eye could read the 
transactions of the night upon his countenance. he strove to pray; his 
bosom no long glow with devotion; his thought insensible wander 
to matilda's secret charms. but what he want in purity of heart, he 
supply by exterior sanctity. the better to cloak his transgression, 
he redouble his pretension to the semblance of virtue, and never 
appear more devoted to heaven as since he had broken through his 
engagements. thus did he unconscious add hypocrisy to perjury and 
incontinence; he had fallen into the latter error from yielding to 
seduction almost irresistible; but he was now guilty of a voluntary 
fault by endeavour to conceal those into which another had betray 
him. 

the matins concluded, ambrosio retired to his cell. the pleasure 
which he had just taste for the first time were still impress upon 
his mind. his brain was bewildered, and present a confuse chaos of 
remorse, voluptuousness, inquietude, and fear. he look back with 
regret to that peace of soul, that security of virtue, which till then 
had been his portion. he had indulge in excesses whose very idea but 
four and twenty hour before he had recoil at with horror. he 
shudder at reflect that a trifling indiscretion on his part, or on 
matilda's, would overturn that fabric of reputation which it had cost 
him thirty years to erect, and render him the abhorrence of that people 
of whom he was then the idol. conscience paint to him in glaring 
colours his perjury and weakness; apprehension magnify to him the 
horrors of punishment, and he already fancy himself in the prison of 
the inquisition. to these torment idea succeed matilda's beauty, 
and those delicious lesson which, once learnt, can never be forgotten. 
a single glance thrown upon these reconcile him with himself. he 
considered the pleasure of the former night to have been purchase at 
an easy price by the sacrifice of innocence and honour. their very 
remembrance fill his soul with ecstacy; he cursed his foolish vanity, 
which had induce him to waste in obscurity the bloom of life, ignorant 
of the blessing of love and woman. he determined at all event to 
continue his commerce with matilda, and call every argument to his 
aid which might confirm his resolution. he ask himself, provided his 
irregularity was unknown, in what would his fault consist, and what 
consequence he had to apprehend? by adhere strict to every rule 
of his order save chastity, he doubt not to retain the esteem of men, 
and even the protection of heaven. he trust easily to be forgiven so 
slight and natural a deviation from his vows: but he forgot that 
have pronounced those vows, incontinence, in laymen the most venial 
of errors, became in his person the most heinous of crimes. 

once decided upon his future conduct, his mind became more easy. he 
threw himself upon his bed, and strove by sleep to recruit his 
strength exhaust by his nocturnal excesses. he awoke refreshed, and 
eager for a repetition of his pleasures. obedient to matilda's order, 
he visit not her cell during the day. father pablo mention in the 
refectory that rosario had at length been prevail upon to follow his 
prescription; but that the medicine had not produce the slightest 
effect, and that he believe no mortal skill could rescue him from the 
grave. with this opinion the abbot agreed, and affected to lament the 
untimely fate of a youth, whose talent had appear so promising. 

the night arrived. ambrosio had taken care to procure from the porter 
the key of the low door opening into the cemetery. furnish with this, 
when all was silent in the monastery, he quit his cell, and hasten 
to matilda's. she had left her bed, and was drest before his arrival. 

'i have been expect you with impatience,' said she; 'my life depend 
upon these moments. have you the key?' 

'i have.' 

'away then to the garden. we have no time to lose. follow me!' 

she took a small cover basket from the table. bearing this in one 
hand, and the lamp, which was flaming upon the hearth, in the other, 
she hasten from the cell. ambrosio follow her. both maintain a 
profound silence. she move on with quick but cautious steps, pass 
through the cloisters, and reach the western side of the garden. her 
eyes flash with a fire and wild which impress the monk at once 
with awe and horror. a determined desperate courage reign upon her 
brow. she gave the lamp to ambrosio; then taking from him the key, she 
unlock the low door, and enter the cemetery. it was a vast and 
spacious square plant with yew trees: half of it belong to the 
abbey; the other half was the property of the sisterhood of st. clare, 
and was protect by a roof of stone. the division was marked by an 
iron railing, the wicket of which was generally left unlocked. 

thither matilda bent her course. she open the wicket and sought for 
the door leading to the subterraneous vaults, where repose the 
moulder body of the votary of st. clare. the night was 
perfectly dark; neither moon or stars were visible. lucky there was 
not a breath of wind, and the friar bore his lamp in full security: by 
the assistance of its beams, the door of the sepulchre was soon 
discovered. it was sunk within the hollow of a wall, and almost 
conceal by thick festoon of ivy hanging over it. three steps of 
rough-hewn stone conduct to it, and matilda was on the point of 
descend them when she sudden start back. 

'there are people in the vaults!' she whisper to the monk; 'conceal 
yourself till they are past. 

she took refuge behind a lofty and magnificent tomb, erect in honour 
of the convent's foundress. ambrosio follow her example, careful 
hiding his lamp lest its beam should betray them. but a few moments 
had elapse when the door was pushed open leading to the subterraneous 
caverns. ray of light proceed up the staircase: they enable the 
conceal spectator to observe two female drest in religious habits, 
who seem engaged in earnest conversation. the abbot had no 
difficulty to recognize the prioress of st. clare in the first, and one 
of the elder nun in her companion. 

'every thing is prepared,' said the prioress; 'her fate shall be 
decided tomorrow. all her tear and sigh will be unavailing. no! in 
five and twenty years that i have been superior of this convent, never 
did i witness a transaction more infamous!' 

'you must expect much opposition to your will;' the other reply in a 
mild voice; 'agnes has many friends in the convent, and in particular 
the mother st. ursula will espouse her cause most warmly. in truth, 
she merit to have friends; and i wish i could prevail upon you to 
consider her youth, and her peculiar situation. she seem sensible of 
her fault; the excess of her grief prove her penitence, and i am 
convinced that her tear flow more from contrition than fear of 
punishment. reverend mother, would you be persuade to mitigate the 
severity of your sentence, would you but deign to overlook this first 
transgression, i offer myself as the pledge of her future conduct.' 

'overlook it, say you? mother camilla, you amaze me! what? after 
disgrace me in the presence of madrid's idol, of the very man on whom 
i most wish to impress an idea of the strict of my discipline? 
how despicable must i have appear to the reverend abbot! no, mother, 
no! i never can forgive the insult. i cannot better convince ambrosio 
that i abhor such crimes, than by punishing that of agnes with all the 
rigour of which our severe law admit. cease then your supplications; 
they will all be unavailing. my resolution is taken: tomorrow agnes 
shall be made a terrible example of my justice and resentment.' 

the mother camilla seem not to give up the point, but by this time 
the nun were out of hearing. the prioress unlock the door which 
communicate with st. clare's chapel, and have enter with her 
companion, closed it again after them. 

matilda now asked, who was this agnes with whom the prioress was thus 
incensed, and what connexion she could have with ambrosio. he related 
her adventure; and he added, that since that time his idea have 
undergone a thorough revolution, he now felt much compassion for the 
unfortunate nun. 

'i design,' said he, 'to request an audience of the domina tomorrow, 
and use every means of obtain a mitigate of her sentence.' 

'beware of what you do!' interrupt matilda; 'your sudden change of 
sentiment may naturally create surprize, and may give birth to 
suspicion which it is most our interest to avoid. rather, redouble 
your outward austerity, and thunder out menace against the error of 
others, the better to conceal your own. abandon the nun to her fate. 
your interfere might be dangerous, and her imprudence merit to be 
punished: she is unworthy to enjoy love's pleasures, who has not wit 
enough to conceal them. but in discuss this trifling subject i 
waste moments which are precious. the night flies apace, and much must 
be done before morning. the nun are retired; all is safe. give me 
the lamp, ambrosio. i must descend alone into these caverns: wait 
here, and if any one approaches, warn me by your voice; but as you 
value your existence, presume not to follow me. your life would fall a 
victim to your imprudent curiosity.' 

thus saying she advanced towards the sepulchre, still holding her lamp 
in one hand, and her little basket in the other. she touched the door: 
it turn slow upon its grating hinges, and a narrow winding 
staircase of black marble present itself to her eyes. she descended 
it. ambrosio remain above, watch the faint beam of the lamp as 
they still proceed up the stairs. they disappeared, and he found 
himself in total darkness. 

left to himself he could not reflect without surprize on the sudden 
change in matilda's character and sentiments. but a few days had past 
since she appear the mildest and softest of her sex, devoted to his 
will, and look up to him as to a superior being. now she assume a 
sort of courage and manly in her manners and discourse but 
ill-calculated to please him. she spoke no long to insinuate, but 
command: he found himself unable to cope with her in argument, and was 
unwilling oblige to confess the superior of her judgment. every 
moment convinced him of the astonish powers of her mind: but what 
she gain in the opinion of the man, she lost with interest in the 
affection of the lover. he regret rosario, the fond, the gentle, 
and submissive: he grieve that matilda prefer the virtue of his 
sex to those of her own; and when he thought of her expression 
respecting the devoted nun, he could not help blame them as cruel and 
unfeminine. pity is a sentiment so natural, so appropriate to the 
female character, that it is scarcely a merit for a woman to possess 
it, but to be without it is a grievous crime. ambrosio could not 
easily forgive his mistress for being deficient in this amiable 
quality. however, though he blame her insensibility, he felt the 
truth of her observations; and though he pity sincerely the 
unfortunate agnes, he resolve to drop the idea of interpose in her 
behalf. 

near an hour had elapsed, since matilda descended into the caverns; 
still she return not. ambrosio's curiosity was excited. he drew 
near the staircase. he listened. all was silent, except that at 
interval he caught the sound of matilda's voice, as it wound along the 
subterraneous passages, and was re-echoed by the sepulchre's vaulted 
roofs. she was at too great a distance for him to distinguish her 
words, and ere they reach him they were deaden into a low murmur. 
he long to penetrate into this mystery. he resolve to disobey her 
injunction and follow her into the cavern. he advanced to the 
staircase; he had already descended some steps when his courage fail 
him. he remember matilda's menace if he infringe her orders, and 
his bosom was fill with a secret unaccountable awe. he return up 
the stairs, resume his former station, and wait impatient for the 
conclusion of this adventure. 

sudden he was sensible of a violent shock: an earthquake rock the 
ground. the column which support the roof under which he stood were 
so strong shaken, that every moment menace him with its fall, and at 
the same moment he heard a loud and tremendous burst of thunder. it 
ceased, and his eyes being fixed upon the staircase, he saw a bright 
column of light flash along the cavern beneath. it was seen but for 
an instant. no soon did it disappear, than all was once more quiet 
and obscure. profound dark again surround him, and the silence 
of night was only broken by the whirr bat, as she flit slow by 
him. 

with every instant ambrosio's amaze increased. another hour 
elapsed, after which the same light again appear and was lost again 
as suddenly. it was accompany by a strain of sweet but solemn music, 
which as it stole through the vault below, inspired the monk with 
mingle delight and terror. it had not long been hushed, when he heard 
matilda's steps upon the staircase. she ascend from the cavern; the 
most lively joy animate her beautiful features. 

'did you see any thing?' she asked. 

'twice i saw a column of light flash up the staircase.' 

'nothing else?' 

'nothing.' 

'the morning is on the point of breaking. let us retire to the abbey, 
lest daylight should betray us.' 

with a light step she hasten from the burying-ground. she regain 
her cell, and the curious abbot still accompany her. she closed the 
door, and disembarrass herself of her lamp and basket. 

'i have succeeded!' she cried, throw herself upon his bosom: 
'succeeded beyond my fondest hopes! i shall live, ambrosio, shall live 
for you! the step which i shudder at taking prove to me a source of 
joy inexpressible! oh! that i dare communicate those joy to you! 
oh! that i were permit to share with you my power, and raise you as 
high above the level of your sex, as one bold deed has exalted me above 
mine!' 

'and what prevent you, matilda?' interrupt the friar; 'why is your 
business in the cavern made a secret? do you think me undeserv of 
your confidence? matilda, i must doubt the truth of your affection, 
while you have joy in which i am forbidden to share.' 

'you reproach me with injustice. i grieve sincerely that i am oblige 
to conceal from you my happiness. but i am not to blame: the fault 
lie not in me, but in yourself, my ambrosio! you are still too much 
the monk. your mind is enslave by the prejudice of education; and 
superstition might make you shudder at the idea of that which 
experience has taught me to prize and value. at present you are unfit 
to be trust with a secret of such importance: but the strength of 
your judgment; and the curiosity which i rejoice to see sparkle in 
your eyes, make me hope that you will one day deserve my confidence. 
till that period arrives, restrain your impatience. remember that you 
have given me your solemn oath never to enquire into this night's 
adventures. i insist upon your keeping this oath: for though' she 
add smiling, while she seal his lip with a wanton kiss; 'though i 
forgive your break your vow to heaven, i expect you to keep your 
vow to me.' 

the friar return the embrace which had set his blood on fire. the 
luxurious and unbounded excesses of the former night were renewed, and 
they separate not till the bell rang for matins. 

the same pleasure were frequent repeated. the monk rejoice in the 
feign rosario's unexpected recovery, and none of them suspect his 
real sex. the abbot possessed his mistress in tranquillity, and 
perceive his frailty unsuspected, abandoned himself to his passion 
in full security. shame and remorse no long torment him. frequent 
repetition made him familiar with sin, and his bosom became proof 
against the sting of conscience. in these sentiment he was 
encourage by matilda; but she soon was aware that she had satiate her 
lover by the unbounded freedom of her caresses. her charm becoming 
accustomed to him, they cease to excite the same desire which at 
first they had inspired. the delirium of passion being past, he had 
leisure to observe every trifling defect: where none were to be found, 
satiety made him fancy them. the monk was glut with the full of 
pleasure: a week had scarcely elapse before he was weary of his 
paramour: his warm constitution still made him seek in her arms the 
gratification of his lust: but when the moment of passion was over, he 
quit her with disgust, and his humour, naturally inconstant, made 
him sigh impatient for variety. 

possession, which cloy man, only increase the affection of woman. 
matilda with every succeed day grew more attach to the friar. 
since he had obtain her favours, he was become dear to her than 
ever, and she felt grateful to him for the pleasure in which they had 
equally been sharers. unfortunately as her passion grew ardent, 
ambrosio's grew cold; the very mark of her fond excited his 
disgust, and its excess serve to extinguish the flame which already 
burn but feeble in his bosom. matilda could not but remark that her 
society seem to him daily less agreeable: he was inattentive while 
she spoke: her musical talents, which she possessed in perfection, had 
lost the power of amuse him; or if he deign to praise them, his 
compliments were evidently forced and cold. he no long gaze upon 
her with affection, or applaud her sentiment with a lover's 
partiality. this matilda well perceived, and redouble her effort to 
revive those sentiment which he once had felt. she could not but fail, 
since he considered as importune the pains which she took to please 
him, and was disgust by the very means which she use to recall the 
wanderer. still, however, their illicit commerce continued: but it 
was clear that he was led to her arms, not by love, but the craving of 
brutal appetite. his constitution made a woman necessary to him, and 
matilda was the only one with whom he could indulge his passion 
safely: in spite of her beauty, he gaze upon every other female with 
more desire; but fear that his hypocrisy should be made public, he 
confine his inclination to his own breast. 

it was by no means his nature to be timid: but his education had 
impress his mind with fear so strongly, that apprehension was now 
become part of his character. had his youth been pass in the world, 
he would have shown himself possessed of many brilliant and manly 
qualities. he was naturally enterprizing, firm, and fearless: he had 
a warrior's heart, and he might have shone with splendour at the head 
of an army. there was no want of generous in his nature: the 
wretched never fail to find in him a compassionate auditor: his 
abilities were quick and shining, and his judgment, vast, solid, and 
decisive. with such qualifications he would have been an ornament to 
his country: that he possessed them, he had given proof in his 
earliest infancy, and his parent had beheld his dawn virtue with 
the fondest delight and admiration. unfortunately, while yet a child
