he was deprived of those parents. he fell into the power of a relation 
whose only wish about him was never to hear of him more; for that 
purpose he gave him in charge to his friend, the former superior of the 
capuchins. the abbot, a very monk, use all his endeavour to persuade 
the boy that happiness exist not without the wall of a convent. he 
succeed fully. to deserve admittance into the order of st. francis 
was ambrosio's highest ambition. his instructor careful repressed 
those virtue whose grandeur and disinterested were ill-suited to 
the cloister. instead of universal benevolence, he adopt a selfish 
partiality for his own particular establishment: he was taught to 
consider compassion for the error of other as a crime of the blackest 
dye: the noble frank of his temper was exchange for servile 
humility; and in order to break his natural spirit, the monk terrify 
his young mind by place before him all the horrors with which 
superstition could furnish them: they paint to him the torment of 
the damn in colours the most dark, terrible, and fantastic, and 
threaten him at the slightest fault with eternal perdition. no 
wonder that his imagination constant dwelling upon these fearful 
object should have render his character timid and apprehensive. add 
to this, that his long absence from the great world, and total 
unacquaintance with the common danger of life, made him form of them 
an idea far more dismal than the reality. while the monk were busy 
in root out his virtue and narrow his sentiments, they allow 
every vice which had fallen to his share to arrive at full perfection. 
he was suffer to be proud, vain, ambitious, and disdainful: he was 
jealous of his equals, and despise all merit but his own: he was 
implacable when offended, and cruel in his revenge. still in spite of 
the pains taken to pervert them, his natural good quality would 
occasional break through the gloom cast over them so carefully: 

at such times the contest for superior between his real and acquire 
character was striking and unaccountable to those unacquainted with his 
original disposition. he pronounced the most severe sentence upon 
offenders, which, the moment after, compassion induce him to mitigate: 
he undertook the most daring enterprizes, which the fear of their 
consequence soon oblige him to abandon: his inborn genius dart a 
brilliant light upon subject the most obscure; and almost 
instantaneous his superstition replunge them in dark more 
profound than that from which they had just been rescued. his brother 
monks, regarding him as a superior being, remark not this 
contradiction in their idol's conduct. they were persuade that what 
he did must be right, and supposed him to have good reason for 
change his resolutions. the fact was, that the different sentiment 
with which education and nature had inspired him were combat in his 
bosom: it remain for his passions, which as yet no opportunity had 
call into play, to decide the victory. unfortunately his passion 
were the very worst judges, to whom he could possibly have applied. 
his monastic seclusion had till now been in his favour, since it gave 
him no room for discover his bad qualities. the superior of his 
talent raise him too far above his companion to permit his being 
jealous of them: his exemplary piety, persuasive eloquence, and 
pleasing manners had secure him universal esteem, and consequently he 
had no injury to revenge: his ambition was justified by his 
acknowledge merit, and his pride considered as no more than proper 
confidence. he never saw, much less converse with, the other sex: he 
was ignorant of the pleasure in woman's power to bestow, and if he 
read in the course of his study 

'that men were fond, he smiled, and wonder how!' 

for a time, spare diet, frequent watching, and severe penance cool 
and represt the natural warmth of his constitution: but no soon did 
opportunity present itself, no soon did he catch a glimpse of joy to 
which he was still a stranger, than religion's barrier were too feeble 
to resist the overwhelming torrent of his desires. all impediment 
yield before the force of his temperament, warm, sanguine, and 
voluptuous in the excess. 

as yet his other passion lay dormant; but they only need to be once 
awakened, to display themselves with violence as great and irresistible. 

he continue to be the admiration of madrid. the enthusiasm create by 
his eloquence seem rather to increase than diminish. 

every thursday, which was the only day when he appear in public, the 
capuchin cathedral was crowded with auditors, and his discourse was 
always received with the same approbation. he was name confessor to 
all the chief family in madrid; and no one was count fashionable 
who was injoine penance by any other than ambrosio. in his resolution 
of never stirring out of his convent, he still persisted. this 
circumstance create a still greater opinion of his sanctity and 
self-denial. above all, the women sang forth his praises loudly, less 
influence by devotion than by his noble countenance, majestic air, and 
well-turned, graceful figure. the abbey door was throng with 
carriage from morning to night; and the noblest and fairest dame of 
madrid confessed to the abbot their secret peccadilloes. 

the eyes of the luxurious friar devour their charms: had his 
penitent consult those interpreters, he would have need no other 
means of express his desires. for his misfortune, they were so 
strong persuade of his continence, that the possibility of his 
harbour indecent thought never once enter their imaginations. 
the climate's heat, 'tis well known, operate with no small influence 
upon the constitution of the spain ladies: but the most abandoned 
would have thought it an easy task to inspire with passion the marble 
statue of st. francis than the cold and rigid heart of the immaculate 
ambrosio. 

on his part, the friar was little acquaint with the depravity of the 
world; he suspect not that but few of his penitent would have 
reject his addresses. yet had he been better instruct on this 
head, the danger attend such an attempt would have seal up his 
lip in silence. he knew that it would be difficult for a woman to 
keep a secret so strange and so important as his frailty; and he even 
tremble lest matilda should betray him. anxious to preserve a 
reputation which was infinite dear to him, he saw all the risque of 
commit it to the power of some vain giddy female; and as the 
beauty of madrid affected only his senses without touching his heart, 
he forgot them as soon as they were out of his sight. the danger of 
discovery, the fear of being repulsed, the loss of reputation, all 
these consideration counsel him to stifle his desires: and though 
he now felt for it the most perfect indifference, he was necessitate 
to confine himself to matilda's person. 

one morning, the confluence of penitent was greater than usual. he was 
detain in the confessional chair till a late hour. at length the 
crowd was dispatched, and he prepared to quit the chapel, when two 
female enter and drew near him with humility. they threw up their 
veils, and the youngest entreat him to listen to her for a few 
moments. the melody of her voice, of that voice to which no man ever 
listen without interest, immediately caught ambrosio's attention. he 
stopped. the petitioner seem bowed down with affliction: her cheek 
were pale, her eyes dim with tears, and her hair fell in disorder 
over her face and bosom. still her countenance was so sweet, so 
innocent, so heavenly, as might have charm an heart less susceptible, 
than that which pant in the abbot's breast. with more than usual 
soft of manner he desire her to proceed, and heard her speak as 
follow with an emotion which increase every moment. 

'reverend father, you see an unfortunate, threaten with the loss of 
her dearest, of almost her only friend! my mother, my excellent mother 
lie upon the bed of sickness. a sudden and dreadful malady seize her 
last night; and so rapid has been its progress, that the physician 
despair of her life. human aid fail me; nothing remains for me but to 
implore the mercy of heaven. father, all madrid ring with the report 
of your piety and virtue. deign to remember my mother in your prayers: 
perhaps they may prevail on the almighty to spare her; and should that 
be the case, i engage myself every thursday in the next three month to 
illuminate the shrine of st. francis in his honour.' 

'so!' thought the monk; 'here we have a second vincentio della ronda. 
rosario's adventure began thus,' and he wish secret that this might 
have the same conclusion. 

he accede to the request. the petitioner return him thanks with 
every mark of gratitude, and then continued. 

'i have yet another favour to ask. we are stranger in madrid; my 
mother needs a confessor, and know not to whom she should apply. we 
understand that you never quit the abbey, and alas! my poor mother is 
unable to come hither! if you would have the goodness, reverend 
father, to name a proper person, whose wise and pious consolation may 
soften the agony of my parent's deathbed, you will confer an 
everlasting favour upon heart not ungrateful.' 

with this petition also the monk complied. indeed, what petition would 
he have refused, if urge in such enchanting accents? the suppliant 
was so interesting! her voice was so sweet, so harmonious! her very 
tear became her, and her affliction seem to add new lustre to her 
charms. he promise to send to her a confessor that same evening, and 
beg her to leave her address. the companion present him with a 
card on which it was written, and then withdrew with the fair 
petitioner, who pronounced before her departure a thousand benediction 
on the abbot's goodness. his eyes follow her out of the chapel. it 
was not till she was out of sight that he examine the card, on which 
he read the following words. 

'donna elvira dalfa, strada di san iago, four door from the palace 
d'albornos.' 

the suppliant was no other than antonia, and leonella was her 
companion. the latter had not consent without difficulty to 
accompany her niece to the abbey: ambrosio had inspired her with such 
awe that she tremble at the very sight of him. her fear had 
conquer even her natural loquacity, and while in his presence she 
utter not a single syllable. 

the monk retired to his cell, whither he was pursue by antonia's 
image. he felt a thousand new emotion spring in his bosom, and he 
tremble to examine into the cause which gave them birth. they were 
total different from those inspired by matilda, when she first 
declared her sex and her affection. he felt not the provocation of 
lust; no voluptuous desire riot in his bosom; nor did a burning 
imagination picture to him the charm which modesty had veiled from his 
eyes. on the contrary, what he now felt was a mingle sentiment of 
tenderness, admiration, and respect. a soft and delicious melancholy 
infuse itself into his soul, and he would not have exchange it for 
the most lively transport of joy. society now disgust him: he 
delight in solitude, which permit his indulge the vision of 
fancy: his thought were all gentle, sad, and soothing, and the whole 
wide world present him with no other object than antonia. 

'happy man!' he exclaim in his romantic enthusiasm; 'happy man, who 
is destined to possess the heart of that lovely girl! what delicacy in 
her features! what elegance in her form! how enchanting was the timid 
innocence of her eyes, and how different from the wanton expression, 
the wild luxurious fire which sparkle in matilda's! oh! sweet must 
one kiss be snatch from the rosy lip of the first, than all the full 
and lustful favours bestow so freely by the second. matilda glut me 
with enjoyment even to loathing, forces me to her arms, ape the 
harlot, and glory in her prostitution. disgusting! did she know the 
inexpressible charm of modesty, how irresistible it enthrall the heart 
of man, how firm it chain him to the throne of beauty, she never 
would have thrown it off. what would be too dear a price for this 
lovely girl's affections? what would i refuse to sacrifice, could i be 
release from my vows, and permit to declare my love in the sight of 
earth and heaven? while i strove to inspire her with tenderness, with 
friendship and esteem, how tranquil and undisturbed would the hour 
roll away! gracious god! to see her blue downcast eyes beam upon mine 
with timid fondness! to sit for days, for years listen to that 
gentle voice! to acquire the right of obliging her, and hear the 
artless expression of her gratitude! to watch the emotion of her 
spotless heart! to encourage each dawn virtue! to share in her joy 
when happy, to kiss away her tear when distrest, and to see her fly to 
my arms for comfort and support! yes; if there is perfect bliss on 
earth, 'tis his lot alone, who become that angel's husband.' 

while his fancy coin these ideas, he pace his cell with a disorder 
air. his eyes were fixed upon vacancy: his head recline upon his 
shoulder; a tear roll down his cheek, while he reflect that the 
vision of happiness for him could never be realized. 

'she is lost to me!' he continued; 'by marriage she cannot be mine: 
and to seduce such innocence, to use the confidence repose in me to 
work her ruin.... oh! it would be a crime, black than yet the 
world ever witnessed! fear not, lovely girl! your virtue runs no 
risque from me. not for indy would i make that gentle bosom know the 
torture of remorse.' 

again he pace his chamber hastily. then stopping, his eye fell upon 
the picture of his once-admired madona. he tore it with indignation 
from the wall: he threw it on the ground, and spurn it from him with 
his foot. 

'the prostitute!' 

unfortunate matilda! her paramour forgot that for his sake alone she 
had forfeit her claim to virtue; and his only reason for despise 
her was that she had love him much too well. 

he threw himself into a chair which stood near the table. he saw the 
card with elvira's address. he took it up, and it brought to his 
recollection his promise respecting a confessor. he pass a few 
minutes in doubt: but antonia's empire over him was already too much 
decided to permit his making a long resistance to the idea which struck 
him. he resolve to be the confessor himself. he could leave the 
abbey unobserved without difficulty: by wrapping up his head in his 
cowl he hope to pass through the street without being recognised: by 
taking these precautions, and by recommend secrecy to elvira's 
family, he doubt not to keep madrid in ignorance that he had broken 
his vow never to see the outside of the abbey walls. matilda was the 
only person whose vigilance he dreaded: but by inform her at the 
refectory that during the whole of that day, business would confine him 
to his cell, he thought himself secure from her wakeful jealousy. 
accordingly, at the hour when the spaniard are generally taking their 
siesta, he venture to quit the abbey by a private door, the key of 
which was in his possession. the cowl of his habit was thrown over his 
face: from the heat of the weather the street were almost total 
deserted: the monk met with few people, found the strada di san iago, 
and arrive without accident at donna elvira's door. he rang, was 
admitted, and immediately usher into an upper apartment. 

it was here that he ran the greatest risque of a discovery. had 
leonella been at home, she would have recognize him directly: her 
communicative disposition would never have permit her to rest till 
all madrid was informed that ambrosio had venture out of the abbey, 
and visit her sister. fortune here stood the monk's friend. on 
leonella's return home, she found a letter instruct her that a 
cousin was just dead, who had left what little he possessed between 
herself and elvira. to secure this bequest she was oblige to set out 
for cordova without lose a moment. amidst all her foible her heart 
was truly warm and affectionate, and she was unwill to quit her 
sister in so dangerous a state. but elvira insist upon her taking 
the journey, conscious that in her daughter's forlorn situation no 
increase of fortune, however trifling, ought to be neglected. 
accordingly, leonella left madrid, sincerely grieve at her sister's 
illness, and give some few sigh to the memory of the amiable but 
inconstant don christoval. she was fully persuade that at first she 
had made a terrible breach in his heart: but hearing nothing more of 
him, she supposed that he had quit the pursuit, disgust by the 
low of her origin, and knowing upon other terms than marriage he 
had nothing to hope from such a dragon of virtue as she professed 
herself; or else, that being naturally capricious and changeable, the 
remembrance of her charm had been efface from the conde's heart by 
those of some new beauty. whatever was the cause of her lose him, 
she lament it sorely. she strove in vain, as she assured every body 
who was kind enough to listen to her, to tear his image from her too 
susceptible heart. she affected the airs of a lovesick virgin, and 
carry them all to the most ridiculous excess. she heave lamentable 
sighs, walk with her arms folded, utter long soliloquies, and her 
discourse generally turn upon some forsaken maid who expire of a 
broken heart! her fiery locks were always ornament with a garland of 
willow; every evening she was seen stray upon the bank of a rivulet 
by moonlight; and she declared herself a violent admirer of murmur 
stream and nightingales; 

'of lonely haunts, and twilight groves, 
'places which pale passion loves!' 

such was the state of leonella's mind, when oblige to quit madrid. 
elvira was out of patience at all these follies, and endeavour at 
persuade her to act like a reasonable woman. her advice was thrown 
away: leonella assured her at parting that nothing could make her 
forget the perfidious don christoval. in this point she was 
fortunately mistaken. an honest youth of cordova, journeyman to an 
apothecary, found that her fortune would be sufficient to set him up in 
a genteel shop of his own: in consequence of this reflection he avowed 
himself her admirer. leonella was not inflexible. the ardour of his 
sigh melt her heart, and she soon consent to make him the happiest 
of mankind. she wrote to inform her sister of her marriage; but, for 
reason which will be explain hereafter, elvira never answer her 
letter. 

ambrosio was conduct into the antichamber to that where elvira was 
reposing. the female domestic who had admitted him left him alone 
while she announce his arrival to her mistress. antonia, who had been 
by her mother's bedside, immediately came to him. 

'pardon me, father,' said she, advance towards him; when recognize 
his features, she stop suddenly, and utter a cry of joy. 'is it 
possible!' she continued; 

'do not my eyes deceive me? has the worthy ambrosio broken through his 
resolution, that he may soften the agony of the best of women? what 
pleasure will this visit give my mother! let me not delay for a moment 
the comfort which your piety and wisdom will afford her.' 

thus saying, she open the chamber door, present to her mother her 
distinguished visitor, and have placed an armed-chair by the side of 
the bed, withdrew into another department. 

elvira was highly gratify by this visit: her expectations had been 
raise high by general report, but she found them far exceeded. 
ambrosio, endow by nature with powers of pleasing, exert them to 
the utmost while converse with antonia's mother. with persuasive 
eloquence he calm every fear, and dissipated every scruple: he bad 
her reflect on the infinite mercy of her judge, despoil death of his 
darts and terrors, and taught her to view without shrink the abyss 
of eternity, on whose brink she then stood. elvira was absorb in 
attention and delight: while she listen to his exhortations, 
confidence and comfort stole insensible into her mind. she unbosom 
to him without hesitation her care and apprehensions. the latter 
respecting a future life he had already quieted: and he now remove 
the former, which she felt for the concern of this. she tremble for 
antonia. she had none to whose care she could recommend her, save to 
the marquis de las cisterna and her sister leonella. the protection 
of the one was very uncertain; and as to the other, though fond of her 
niece, leonella was so thoughtless and vain as to make her an improper 
person to have the sole direction of a girl so young and ignorant of 
the world. the friar no soon learnt the cause of her alarm than he 
beg her to make herself easy upon that head. he doubt not being 
able to secure for antonia a safe refuge in the house of one of his 
penitents, the marchioness of villa-franca: this was a lady of 
acknowledge virtue, remarkable for strict principles and extensive 
charity. should accident deprive her of this resource, he engaged to 
procure antonia a reception in some respectable convent: that is to 
say, in quality of boarder; for elvira had declared herself no friend 
to a monastic life, and the monk was either candid or complaisant 
enough to allow that her disapprobation was not unfounded. 

these proof of the interest which he felt for her completely won 
elvira's heart. in thank him she exhaust every expression which 
gratitude could furnish, and protest that now she should resign 
herself with tranquill to the grave. ambrosio rose to take leave: 
he promise to return the next day at the same hour, but request that 
his visit might be kept secret. 

'i am unwilling' said he, 'that my break through a rule impose by 
necessity should be generally known. had i not resolve never to quit 
my convent, except upon circumstances as urgent as that which has 
conduct me to your door, i should be frequent summon upon 
insignificant occasions: that time would be engross by the curious, 
the unoccupied, and the fanciful, which i now pass at the bedside of 
the sick, in comfort the expire penitent, and clearing the passage 
to eternity from thorns.' 

elvira commend equally his prudence and compassion, promising to 
conceal careful the honour of his visits. the monk then gave her his 
benediction, and retired from the chamber. 

in the antiroom he found antonia: he could not refuse himself the 
pleasure of passing a few moments in her society. he bad her take 
comfort, for that her mother seem compose and tranquil, and he hope 
that she might yet do well. he enquire who attend her, and engaged 
to send the physician of his convent to see her, one of the most 
skilful in madrid. he then launch out in elvira's commendation, 
praise her purity and fortitude of mind, and declared that she had 
inspired him with the highest esteem and reverence. antonia's innocent 
heart swell with gratitude: joy dance in her eyes, where a tear 
still sparkled. the hope which he gave her of her mother's recovery, 
the lively interest which he seem to feel for her, and the flatter 
way in which she was mention by him, add to the report of his 
judgment and virtue, and to the impression made upon her by his 
eloquence, confirmed the favourable opinion with which his first 
appearance had inspired antonia. she reply with diffidence, but 
without restraint: she fear not to relate to him all her little 
sorrows, all her little fear and anxieties; and she thank him for 
his goodness with all the genuine warmth which favours kindle in a 
young and innocent heart. such alone know how to estimate benefit at 
their full value. they who are conscious of mankind's perfidy and 
selfishness, ever receive an obligation with apprehension and distrust: 
they suspect that some secret motive must lurk behind it: they express 
their thanks with restraint and caution, and fear to praise a kind 
action to its full extent, aware that some future day a return may be 
required. not so antonia; she thought the world was compose only of 
those who resemble her, and that vice existed, was to her still a 
secret. the monk had been of service to her; he said that he wish 
her well; she was grateful for his kindness, and thought that no terms 
were strong enough to be the vehicle of her thanks. with what delight 
did ambrosio listen to the declaration of her artless gratitude! the 
natural grace of her manners, the unequalled sweet of her voice, 
her modest vivacity, her unstudied elegance, her expressive 
countenance, and intelligent eyes united to inspire him with pleasure 
and admiration, while the solidity and correct of her remark 
received additional beauty from the unaffected simplicity of the 
language in which they were conveyed. 

ambrosio was at length oblige to tear himself from this conversation 
which possessed for him but too many charms. he repeated to antonia 
his wish that his visit should not be made known, which desire she 
promise to observe. he then quit the house, while his enchantress 
hasten to her mother, ignorant of the mischief which her beauty had 
caused. she was eager to know elvira's opinion of the man whom she had 
praise in such enthusiast terms, and was delight to find it 
equally favourable, if not even more so, than her own. 

'even before he spoke,' said elvira, 'i was prejudiced in his favour: 
the fervour of his exhortations, dignity of his manner, and close 
of his reasoning, were very far from induce me to alter my opinion. 
his fine and full-toned voice struck me particularly; but surely, 
antonia, i have heard it before. it seem perfectly familiar to my 
ear. either i must have known the abbot in former times, or his voice 
bear a wonderful resemblance to that of some other, to whom i have 
often listened. 

there were certain tone which touched my very heart, and made me feel 
sensation so singular, that i strive in vain to account for them.' 

'my dearest mother, it produce the same effect upon me: yet certainly 
neither of us ever heard his voice till we came to madrid. i suspect 
that what we attribute to his voice, really proceeds from his pleasant 
manners, which forbid our considering him as a stranger. i know not 
why, but i feel more at my ease while converse with him than i 
usually do with people who are unknown to me. i fear not to repeat 
to him all my childish thoughts; and somehow i felt confident that he 
would hear my folly with indulgence. oh! i was not deceive in him! 
he listen to me with such an air of kindness and attention! he 
answer me with such gentleness, such condescension! he did not call 
me an infant, and treat me with contempt, as our cross old confessor at 
the castle use to do. i verily believe that if i had live in murcia 
a thousand years, i never should have like that fat old father 
dominic!' 

'i confess that father dominic had not the most pleasing manners in the 
world; but he was honest, friendly, and well-meaning.' 

'ah! my dear mother, those quality are so common!' 

'god grant, my child, that experience may not teach you to think them 
rare and precious: i have found them but too much so! but tell me, 
antonia; why is it impossible for me to have seen the abbot before?' 

'because since the moment when he enter the abbey, he has never been 
on the outside of its walls. he told me just now, that from his 
ignorance of the streets, he had some difficulty to find the strada di 
san iago, though so near the abbey.' 

'all this is possible, and still i may have seen him before he enter 
the abbey: in order to come out, it was rather necessary that he 
should first go in.' 

'holy virgin! as you say, that is very true.--oh! but might he not 
have been born in the abbey?' 

elvira smiled. 

'why, not very easily.' 

'stay, stay! now i recollect how it was. he was put into the abbey 
quite a child; the common people say that he fell from heaven, and was 
sent as a present to the capuchin by the virgin.' 

'that was very kind of her. and so he fell from heaven, antonia? 

he must have had a terrible tumble.' 

'many do not credit this, and i fancy, my dear mother, that i must 
number you among the unbelievers. indeed, as our landlady told my 
aunt, the general idea is that his parents, being poor and unable to 
maintain him, left him just born at the abbey door. the late superior 
from pure charity had him educated in the convent, and he prove to be 
a model of virtue, and piety, and learning, and i know not what else 
besides: in consequence, he was first received as a brother of the 
order, and not long ago was chosen abbot. however, whether this 
account or the other is the true one, at least all agree that when the 
monk took him under their care, he could not speak: therefore, you 
could not have heard his voice before he enter the monastery, because 
at that time he had no voice at all.' 

'upon my word, antonia, you argue very closely! your conclusion are 
infallible! i did not suspect you of being so able a logician.' 

'ah! you are mock me! but so much the better. it delight me to see 
you in spirits: besides you seem tranquil and easy, and i hope that 
you will have no more convulsions. oh! i was sure the abbot's visit 
would do you good!' 

'it has indeed done me good, my child. he has quiet my mind upon 
some points which agitate me, and i already feel the effects of his 
attention. my eyes grow heavy, and i think i can sleep a little. draw 
the curtains, my antonia: but if i should not wake before midnight, do 
not sit up with me, i charge you.' 

antonia promise to obey her, and have received her blessing drew the 
curtains of the bed. she then seat herself in silence at her 
embroidery frame, and beguile the hour with building castle in the 
air. her spirits were enliven by the evident change for the better 
in elvira, and her fancy present her with vision bright and 
pleasing. in these dream ambrosio made no despicable figure. she 
thought of him with joy and gratitude; but for every idea which fell to 
the friar's share, at least two were unconscious bestow upon 
lorenzo. thus pass the time, till the bell in the neighbouring 
steeple of the capuchin cathedral announce the hour of midnight: 
antonia remember her mother's injunctions, and obey them, though 
with reluctance. she undrew the curtains with caution. elvira was 
enjoy a profound and quiet slumber; her cheek glow with health's 
return colours: a smile declared that her dream were pleasant, and 
as antonia bent over her, she fancy that she heard her name 
pronounced. she kiss her mother's forehead softly, and retired to 
her chamber. there she knelt before a statue of st. rosolia, her 
patroness; she recommend herself to the protection of heaven, and as 
had been her custom from infancy, conclude her devotions by chaunt 
the following stanzas. 

midnight hymn 

now all is hushed; the solemn chime 
no long swell the nightly gale: 
thy awful presence, hour sublime, 
with spotless heart once more i hail. 

'tis now the moment still and dread, 
when sorcerer use their baleful power; 
when grave give up their bury dead 
to profit by the sanction hour: 

from guilt and guilty thought secure, 
to duty and devotion true, 
with bosom light and conscience pure, 
repose, thy gentle aid i woo. 

good angels, take my thanks, that still 
the snare of vice i view with scorn; 
thanks, that to-night as free from ill 
i sleep, as when i woke at morn. 

yet may not my unconscious breast 
harbour some guilt to me unknown? 
some wish impure, which unreprest 
you blush to see, and i to own? 

if such there be, in gentle dream 
instruct my feet to shun the snare; 
bid truth upon my error beam, 
and deign to make me still your care. 

chase from my peaceful bed away 
the witching spell, a foe to rest, 
the nightly goblin, wanton fay, 
the ghost in pain, and fiend unblest: 

let not the tempt in mine ear 
pour lesson of unhallowed joy; 
let not the night-mare, wandering near 
my couch, the calm of sleep destroy; 

let not some horrid dream affright 
with strange fantastic form mine eyes; 
but rather bid some vision bright 
display the bliss of yonder skies. 

show me the crystal dome of heaven, 
the world of light where angel lie; 
shew me the lot to mortal given, 
who guiltless live, who guiltless die. 

then show me how a seat to gain 
amidst those blissful realm of 
air; teach me to shun each guilty stain, 
and guide me to the good and fair. 

so every morn and night, my voice 
to heaven the grateful strain shall raise; 
in you as guardian powers rejoice, 
good angels, and exalt your praise: 

so will i strive with zealous fire 
each vice to shun, each fault correct; 
will love the lesson you inspire, 
and prize the virtue you protect. 

then when at length by high command 
my body seek the grave's repose, 
when death draw nigh with friendly hand 
my failing pilgrim eyes to close; 

pleased that my soul has 'scaped the wreck, 
sighless will i my life resign, 
and yield to god my spirit back, 
as pure as when it first was mine. 


have finished her usual devotions, antonia retired to bed. sleep soon 
stole over her senses; and for several hour she enjoy that calm 
repose which innocence alone can know, and for which many a monarch 
with pleasure would exchange his crown. 



chapter iv 

----ah! how dark 
these long-extended realm and rueful wastes; 
where nought but silence reigns, and night, dark night, 
dark as was chaos ere the infant sun 
was roll together, or had try its beam 
athwart the gloom profound! 
the sickly taper 
by glimmer through thy low-browed misty vaults, 
fur round with mouldy damps, and ropy slime, 
let fall a supernumerary horror, 
and only serve to make 
thy night more irksome! 
blair. 

return undiscovered to the abbey, ambrosio's mind was fill with the 
most pleasing images. he was wilful blind to the danger of expose 
himself to antonia's charms: he only remember the pleasure which her 
society had afford him, and rejoice in the prospect of that pleasure 
being repeated. he fail not to profit by elvira's indisposition to 
obtain a sight of her daughter every day. at first he bound his 
wish to inspire antonia with friendship: but no soon was he 
convinced that she felt that sentiment in its fullest extent, than his 
aim became more decided, and his attention assume a warm colour. 
the innocent familiarity with which she treat him, encourage his 
desires: grown use to her modesty, it no long command the same 
respect and awe: he still admire it, but it only made him more 
anxious to deprive her of that quality which form her principal 
charm. warmth of passion, and natural penetration, of which latter 
unfortunately both for himself and antonia he possessed an ample share, 
supply a knowledge of the arts of seduction. he easily distinguished 
the emotion which were favourable to his designs, and seize every 
means with avid of infuse corruption into antonia's bosom. this 
he found no easy matter. extreme simplicity prevent her from 
perceive the aim to which the monk's insinuation tended; but the 
excellent morals which she owe to elvira's care, the solidity and 
correct of her understanding, and a strong sense of what was right 
implant in her heart by nature, made her feel that his precept must 
be faulty. by a few simple words she frequent overthrew the whole 
bulk of his sophistic arguments, and made him conscious how weak they 
were when oppose to virtue and truth. on such occasion he took refuge 
in his eloquence; he overpower her with a torrent of philosophical 
paradoxes, to which, not understanding them, it was impossible for her 
to reply; and thus though he did not convince her that his reasoning 
was just, he at least prevent her from discover it to be false. 
he perceive that her respect for his judgment augment daily, and 
doubt not with time to bring her to the point desired. 

he was not unconscious that his attempt were highly criminal: he saw 
clearly the base of seduce the innocent girl: but his passion 
was too violent to permit his abandon his design. he resolve to 
pursue it, let the consequence be what they might. he depend upon 
finding antonia in some unguarded moment; and seeing no other man 
admitted into her society, nor hearing any mention either by her or 
by elvira, he imagine that her young heart was still unoccupied. while 
he wait for the opportunity of satisfying his unwarrantable lust, 
every day increase his cold for matilda. not a little was this 
occasion by the consciousness of his fault to her. to hide them 
from her he was not sufficient master of himself: yet he dread 
lest, in a transport of jealous rage, she should betray the secret on 
which his character and even his life depended. matilda could not but 
remark his indifference: he was conscious that she remark it, and 
fear her reproaches, shun her studiously. yet when he could not 
avoid her, her mild might have convinced him that he had nothing to 
dread from her resentment. she had resume the character of the gentle 
interesting rosario: she tax him not with ingratitude; but her eyes 
fill with involuntary tears, and the soft melancholy of her 
countenance and voice utter complaint far more touching than words 
could have conveyed. ambrosio was not unmoved by her sorrow; but 
unable to remove its cause, he forbore to show that it affected him. 
as her conduct convinced him that he need not fear her vengeance, he 
continue to neglect her, and avoid her company with care. matilda 
saw that she in vain attempt to regain his affections: yet she 
stifle the impulse of resentment, and continue to treat her 
inconstant lover with her former fond and attention. 

by degree elvira's constitution recover itself. she was no long 
trouble with convulsions, and antonia cease to tremble for her 
mother. ambrosio beheld this reestablishment with displeasure. he saw 
that elvira's knowledge of the world would not be the dupe of his 
sanctify demeanour, and that she would easily perceive his view upon 
her daughter. he resolve therefore, before she quit her chamber, 
to try the extent of his influence over the innocent antonia. 

one evening, when he had found elvira almost perfectly restore to 
health, he quit her early than was his usual custom. not finding 
antonia in the antichamber, he venture to follow her to her own. it 
was only separate from her mother's by a closet, in which flora, the 
waiting-woman, generally slept. antonia sat upon a sopha with her back 
towards the door, and read attentively. she heard not his approach, 
till he had seat himself by her. she started, and welcome him with 
a look of pleasure: then rising, she would have conduct him to the 
sitting-room; but ambrosio taking her hand, oblige her by gentle 
violence to resume her place. she comply without difficulty: she 
knew not that there was more impropriety in converse with him in one 
room than another. she thought herself equally secure of his 
principles and her own, and have replace herself upon the sopha, she 
began to prattle to him with her usual ease and vivacity. 

he examine the book which she had been reading, and had now placed 
upon the table. it was the bible. 

'how!' said the friar to himself; 'antonia read the bible, and is 
still so ignorant?' 

but, upon a further inspection, he found that elvira had made exactly 
the same remark. that prudent mother, while she admire the beauty 
of the sacred writings, was convinced that, unrestricted, no reading 
more improper could be permit a young woman. many of the narrative 
can only tend to excite idea the worst calculate for a female breast: 
every thing is call plainly and roundly by its name; and the annals 
of a brothel would scarcely furnish a greater choice of indecent 
expressions. yet this is the book which young women are recommend to 
study; which is put into the hand of children, able to comprehend 
little more than those passage of which they had better remain 
ignorant; and which but too frequent inculcate the first rudiments 
of vice, and give the first alarm to the still sleep passions. of 
this was elvira so fully convinced, that she would have prefer 
putt into her daughter's hand 'amadis de gaul,' or 'the valiant 
champion, tirante the white;' and would soon have authorise her 
study the lewd exploit of 'don galaor,' or the lascivious joke of 
the 'damsel plazer di mi vida.' she had in consequence made two 
resolution respecting the bible. the first was that antonia should 
not read it till she was of an age to feel its beauties, and profit by 
its morality: the second, that it should be copy out with her own 
hand, and all improper passage either alter or omitted. she had 
adhere to this determination, and such was the bible which antonia was 
reading: it had been lately deliver to her, and she peruse it with 
an avidity, with a delight that was inexpressible. ambrosio perceive 
his mistake, and replace the book upon the table. 

antonia spoke of her mother's health with all the enthusiast joy of a 
youthful heart. 

'i admire your filial affection,' said the abbot; 'it prove the 
excellence and sensibility of your character; it promise a treasure to 
him whom heaven has destined to possess your affections. the breast, 
so capable of fond for a parent, what will it feel for a lover? 
nay, perhaps, what feel it for one even now? tell me, my lovely 
daughter; have you known what it is to love? answer me with sincerity: 
forget my habit, and consider me only as a friend.' 

'what it is to love?' said she, repeating his question; 'oh! yes, 
undoubtedly; i have love many, many people.' 

'that is not what i mean. the love of which i speak can be felt only 
for one. have you never seen the man whom you wish to be your 
husband?' 

'oh! no, indeed!' 

this was an untruth, but she was unconscious of its falsehood: she knew 
not the nature of her sentiment for lorenzo; and never have seen him 
since his first visit to elvira, with every day his image grew less 
feeble impress upon her bosom. besides, she thought of an husband 
with all a virgin's terror, and negative the friar's demand without a 
moment's hesitation. 

'and do you not long to see that man, antonia? do you feel no void in 
your heart which you fain would have fill up? do you heave no sigh 
for the absence of some one dear to you, but who that some one is, you 
know not? perceive you not that what formerly could please, has charm 
for you no longer? that a thousand new wishes, new ideas, new 
sensations, have sprang in your bosom, only to be felt, never to be 
described? or while you fill every other heart with passion, is it 
possible that your own remains insensible and cold? it cannot be! 
that melting eye, that blush cheek, that enchanting voluptuous 
melancholy which at times overspread your features, all these mark 
belye your words. you love, antonia, and in vain would hide it from 
me.' 

'father, you amaze me! what is this love of which you speak? i 
neither know its nature, nor if i felt it, why i should conceal the 
sentiment.' 

'have you seen no man, antonia, whom though never seen before, you 
seem long to have sought? whose form, though a stranger's, was 
familiar to your eyes? the sound of whose voice soothe you, pleased 
you, penetrate to your very soul? in whose presence you rejoiced, for 
whose absence you lamented? with whom your heart seem to expand, and 
in whose bosom with confidence unbounded you repose the care of your 
own? have you not felt all this, antonia?' 

'certainly i have: the first time that i saw you, i felt it.' 

ambrosio started. scarcely dare he credit his hearing. 

'me, antonia?' he cried, his eyes sparkle with delight and 
impatience, while he seize her hand, and pressed it rapturous to his 
lips. 'me, antonia? you felt these sentiment for me?' 

'even with more strength than you have described. the very moment that 
i beheld you, i felt so pleased, so interested! i wait so eager to 
catch the sound of your voice, and when i heard it, it seem so sweet! 
it spoke to me a language till then so unknown! methought, it told me 
a thousand things which i wish to hear! it seem as if i had long 
known you; as if i had a right to your friendship, your advice, and 
your protection. 

i wept when you departed, and long for the time which should restore 
you to my sight.' 

'antonia! my charming antonia!' exclaim the monk, and caught her to 
his bosom; 'can i believe my senses? repeat it to me, my sweet girl! 
tell me again that you love me, that you love me truly and tenderly!' 

'indeed, i do: let my mother be excepted, and the world hold no one 
more dear to me!' 

at this frank avowal ambrosio no long possessed himself; wild with 
desire, he clasp the blush tremble in his arms. he fasten his 
lip greedy upon hers, suck in her pure delicious breath, violate 
with his bold hand the treasure of her bosom, and wound around him her 
soft and yielding limbs. startled, alarmed, and confuse at his action, 
surprize at first deprived her of the power of resistance. at length 
recover herself, she strove to escape from his embrace. 

'father! .... ambrosio!' she cried; 'release me, for god's sake!' 

but the licentious monk heed not her prayers: he persist in his 
design, and proceed to take still greater liberties. antonia prayed, 
wept, and struggled: terrify to the extreme, though at what she knew 
not, she exert all her strength to repulse the friar, and was on the 
point of shriek for assistance when the chamber door was sudden 
thrown open. ambrosio had just sufficient presence of mind to be 
sensible of his danger. reluctantly he quit his prey, and start 
hasty from the couch. antonia utter an exclamation of joy, flew 
towards the door, and found herself clasp in the arms of her mother. 

alarm at some of the abbot's speeches, which antonia had innocent 
repeated, elvira resolve to ascertain the truth of her suspicions. 
she had known enough of mankind not to be impose upon by the monk's 
reputed virtue. she reflect on several circumstances, which though

frequent visits, which as far as she could see, were confine to her

with his conversation in her presence, all these circumstances inspired 
her with doubt respecting the purity of ambrosio's friendship. in 
consequence, she resolved, when he should next be alone with antonia, 
to endeavour at surprize him. her plan had succeeded. 'tis true, 
that when she enter the room, he had already abandoned his prey; but 
the disorder of her daughter's dress, and the shame and confusion 
stamp upon the friar's countenance, suffice to prove that her 
suspicion were but too well-founded. however, she was too prudent to 
make those suspicion known. she judge that to unmask the imposter 
would be no easy matter, the public being so much prejudiced in his 
favour: and have but few friends, she thought it dangerous to make 
herself so powerful an enemy. she affected therefore not to remark his 
agitation, seat herself tranquil upon the sopha, assign some 
trifling reason for have quit her room unexpectedly, and converse

reassure by her behaviour, the monk began to recover himself. he 
strove to answer elvira without appear embarrassed: but he was 
still too great a novice in dissimulation, and he felt that he must 
look confuse and awkward. he soon broke off the conversation, and 
rose to depart. what was his vexation, when on taking leave, elvira 
told him in polite terms, that being now perfectly reestablished, she 
thought it an injustice to deprive other of his company, who might be 
more in need of it! she assured him of her eternal gratitude, for the 
benefit which during her illness she had derive from his society and

the multitude of business which his situation must of necessity impose

though deliver in the mildest language this hint was too plain to be 
mistaken. still, he was prepare to put in a remonstrance when an 
expressive look from elvira stop him short. he dare not press her 
to receive him, for her manner convinced him that he was discovered: 
he submit without reply, took an hasty leave, and retired to the 
abbey, his heart fill with rage and shame, with bitter and 
disappointment. 

antonia's mind felt relieved by his departure; yet she could not help 
lament that she was never to see him more. elvira also felt a 
secret sorrow; she had received too much pleasure from thinking him her 
friend, not to regret the necessity of change her opinion: but her

but she was oblige to treat the subject with caution, lest in remove

to receive them but in company. with this injunction antonia promise 
to comply. 

ambrosio hasten to his cell. he closed the door after him, and threw 
himself upon the bed in despair. the impulse of desire, the sting of 
disappointment, the shame of detection, and the fear of being public 
unmasked, render his bosom a scene of the most horrible confusion. 
he knew not what course to pursue. debar the presence of antonia, 
he had no hope of satisfying that passion which was now become a part 
of his existence. he reflect that his secret was in a woman's power: 
he tremble with apprehension when he beheld the precipice before him, 
and with rage, when he thought that had it not been for elvira, he 
should now have possessed the object of his desires. with the direct 
imprecation he vow vengeance against her; he swore that, cost what 
it would, he still would possess antonia. start from the bed, he 
pace the chamber with disorder steps, howl with impotent fury, 
dashed himself violent against the walls, and indulge all the 
transport of rage and madness. 

he was still under the influence of this storm of passion when he 
heard a gentle knock at the door of his cell. conscious that his voice 
must have been heard, he dare not refuse admittance to the importuner: 
he strove to compose himself, and to hide his agitation. have in 
some degree succeeded, he drew back the bolt: the door opened, and 
matilda appeared. 

at this precise moment there was no one with whose presence he could 
better have dispensed. he had not sufficient command over himself to 
conceal his vexation. he start back, and frowned. 

'i am busy,' said he in a stern and hasty tone; 'leave me!' 

matilda heed him not: she again fasten the door, and then advanced 
towards him with an air gentle and supplicating. 

'forgive me, ambrosio,' said she; 'for your own sake i must not obey 
you. fear no complaint from me; i come not to reproach you with your

long be mine, i request the next best gift, your confidence and

shun me? why such anxiety to fly my presence? you have sorrows, 
but will not permit me to share them; you have disappointments, but 
will not accept my comfort; you have wishes, but forbid my aid your 
pursuits. 'tis of this which i complain, not of your indifference to

her mild had an instantaneous effect upon ambrosio's feelings. 

'generous matilda!' he replied, taking her hand, 'how far do you rise 
superior to the foible of your sex! yes, i accept your offer. i have

not in your power!' 

'it lie in no one's power but mine. ambrosio, your secret is none to 
me; your every step, your every action has been observe by my 
attentive eye. you love.' 

'matilda!' 

'why conceal it from me? fear not the little jealousy which taint the 
generality of women: my soul disdain so despicable a passion. you 
love, ambrosio; antonia dalfa is the object of your flame. i know 
every circumstance respecting your passion: every conversation has

'to success? oh! impossible!' 

'to them who dare nothing is impossible. rely upon me, and you may yet 
be happy. the time is come, ambrosio, when regard for your comfort and 
tranquill compel me to reveal a part of my history, with which you 
are still unacquainted. listen, and do not interrupt me: should my

satisfy your wishes, and restore that peace to your heart which at 
present has abandoned it. i formerly mention that my guardian was a 
man of uncommon knowledge: he took pains to instil that knowledge into 
my infant mind. among the various science which curiosity had induce 
him to explore, he neglect not that which by most is esteem 
impious, and by many chimerical. i speak of those arts which relate to 
the world of spirits. his deep research into cause and effects, his

and unlimited knowledge of the property and virtue of every gem 
which enrich the deep, of every herb which the earth produces, at

earnestly. his curiosity was fully slaked, his ambition ample

nature; his eye read the mandate of futurity, and the infernal spirits 
were submissive to his commands. why shrink you from me? i understand 
that enquiring look. your suspicion are right, though your terror

obedient to my orders, i saw him tremble at my frown, and found that, 
instead of sell my soul to a master, my courage had purchase for

endless perdition; you have barter for momentary power eternal

absurd! but i am not deceived, ambrosio! it is not virtue which make 
you reject my offer: you would accept it, but you dare not. 'tis not 
the crime which hold your hand, but the punishment; 'tis not respect 
for god which restrain you, but the terror of his vengeance! fain 
would you offend him in secret, but you tremble to profess yourself his 
foe. now shame on the coward soul, which wants the courage either to 
be a firm friend or open enemy!' 

'to look upon guilt with horror, matilda, is in itself a merit: in this 
respect i glory to confess myself a coward. though my passion have 
made me deviate from her laws, i still feel in my heart an innate love 
of virtue. but it ill become you to tax me with my perjury: you, who 
first seduce me to violate my vows; you, who first rouze my sleep 
vices, made me feel the weight of religion's chains, and bad me be 
convinced that guilt had pleasures. yet though my principles have 
yield to the force of temperament, i still have sufficient grace to 
shudder at sorcery, and avoid a crime so monstrous, so unpardonable!' 

'unpardonable, say you? where then is your constant boast of the 
almighty's infinite mercy? has he of late set bounds to it? receive 
he no long a sinner with joy? you injure him, ambrosio; you will 
always have time to repent, and he have goodness to forgive. afford 
him a glorious opportunity to exert that goodness: the greater your 
crime, the greater his merit in pardoning. away then with these 
childish scruples: be persuade to your good, and follow me to the 
sepulchre.' 

'oh! cease, matilda! that scoff tone, that bold and impious 
language, is horrible in every mouth, but most so in a woman's. let us 
drop a conversation which excite no other sentiment than horror and 
disgust. i will not follow you to the sepulchre, or accept the 
service of your infernal agents. antonia shall be mine, but mine by 
human means.' 

'then yours she will never be! you are banish her presence; her 
mother has open her eyes to your designs, and she is now upon her 
guard against them. nay more, she love another. a youth of 
distinguished merit possess her heart, and unless you interfere, a 
few days will make her his bride. this intelligence was brought me by 
my invisible servants, to whom i had recourse on first perceive your 
indifference. they watch your every action, related to me all that 
past at elvira's, and inspired me with the idea of favour your 
designs. their report have been my only comfort. though you shun 
my presence, all your proceedings were known to me: nay, i was 
constant with you in some degree, thanks to this precious gift!' 

with these words she drew from beneath her habit a mirror of polish 
steel, the border of which were marked with various strange and 
unknown characters. 

'amidst all my sorrows, amidst all my regrets for your coldness, i was 
sustain from despair by the virtue of this talisman. on pronounce 
certain words, the person appear in it on whom the observer's thought 
are bent: thus though _i_ was exile from your sight, you, ambrosio, 
were ever present to mine.' 

the friar's curiosity was excited strongly. 

'what you relate is incredible! matilda, are you not amuse yourself 
with my credulity?' 

'be your own eyes the judge.' 

she put the mirror into his hand. curiosity induce him to take it, 
and love, to wish that antonia might appear. matilda pronounced the 
magic words. immediately, a thick smoke rose from the character 
trace upon the borders, and spread itself over the surface. it 
disperse again gradually; a confuse mixture of colours and image 
present themselves to the friar's eyes, which at length arrange 
themselves in their proper places, he beheld in miniature antonia's 
lovely form. 

the scene was a small closet belong to her apartment. she was 
undress to bathe herself. the long tresses of her hair were already 
bound up. the amorous monk had full opportunity to observe the 
voluptuous contour and admirable symmetry of her person. she threw 
off her last garment, and advance to the bath prepared for her, she 
put her foot into the water. it struck cold, and she drew it back 
again. though unconscious of being observed, an inbred sense of 
modesty induce her to veil her charms; and she stood hesitate upon 
the brink, in the attitude of the venus de medicis. at this moment a 
tame linnet flew towards her, nestle its head between her breasts, and 
nibble them in wanton play. the smile antonia strove in vain to 
shake off the bird, and at length raise her hand to drive it from its 
delightful harbour. ambrosio could bear no more: his desire were 
work up to phrenzy. 

'i yield!' he cried, dashing the mirror upon the ground: 'matilda, i 
follow you! do with me what you will!' 

she wait not to hear his consent repeated. it was already midnight. 
she flew to her cell, and soon return with her little basket and the 
key of the cemetery, which had remain in her possession since her 
first visit to the vaults. she gave the monk no time for reflection. 

'come!' she said, and took his hand; 'follow me, and witness the 
effects of your resolve!' 

this said, she drew him hasty along. they pass into the 
burying-ground unobserved, open the door of the sepulchre, and found 
themselves at the head of the subterraneous staircase. as yet the 
beam of the full moon had guide their steps, but that resource now 
fail them. matilda had neglect to provide herself with a lamp. 
still holding ambrosio's hand she descended the marble steps; but the 
profound obscurity with which they were overspread oblige them to walk 
slow and cautiously. 

'you tremble!' said matilda to her companion; 'fear not; the destined 
spot is near.' 

they reach the foot of the staircase, and continue to proceed, 
feeling their way along the walls. on turning a corner suddenly, they 
descry faint gleam of light which seem burning at a distance. 
thither they bent their steps: the ray proceed from a small 
sepulchral lamp which flame unceasing before the statue of st. 
clare. it tinge with dim and cheerless beam the massy column which 
support the roof, but was too feeble to dissipate the thick gloom in 
which the vault above were buried. 

matilda took the lamp. 

'wait for me!' said she to the friar; 'in a few moments i am here 
again.' 

with these words she hasten into one of the passage which branch 
in various directions from this spot, and form a sort of labyrinth. 
ambrosio was now left alone: dark the most profound surround him, 
and encourage the doubt which began to revive in his bosom. he had 
been hurried away by the delirium of the moment: the shame of 
betray his terrors, while in matilda's presence, had induce him to 
repress them; but now that he was abandoned to himself, they resume 
their former ascendancy. he tremble at the scene which he was soon to 
witness. he knew not how far the delusion of magic might operate upon 
his mind, and possibly might force him to some deed whose commission 
would make the breach between himself and heaven irreparable. in this 
fearful dilemma, he would have implore god's assistance, but was 
conscious that he had forfeit all claim to such protection. gladly 
would he have return to the abbey; but as he had past through 
innumerable cavern and winding passages, the attempt of regain the 
stairs was hopeless. his fate was determined: no possibility of 
escape present itself: he therefore combat his apprehensions, and 
call every argument to his succour, which might enable him to support 
the try scene with fortitude. he reflect that antonia would be the 
reward of his daring: he inflamed his imagination by enumerate her 
charms. he persuade himself that (as matilda had observed), he always 
should have time sufficient for repentance, and that as he employ her 
assistance, not that of the daemons, the crime of sorcery could not be 
laid to his charge. he had read much respecting witchcraft: he 
understood that unless a formal act was sign renounce his claim to 
salvation, satan would have no power over him. he was fully determined 
not to execute any such act, whatever threat might be used, or 
advantage held out to him. 

such were his meditation while wait for matilda. they were 
interrupt by a low murmur which seem at no great distance from him. 
he was startled. he listened. some minutes past in silence, after 
which the murmur was repeated. it appear to be the groan of one 
in pain. in any other situation, this circumstance would only have 
excited his attention and curiosity: 

in the present, his predominant sensation was that of terror. his 
imagination total engross by the idea of sorcery and spirits, he 
fancy that some unquiet ghost was wandering near him; or else that 
matilda had fallen a victim to her presumption, and was perishing under 
the cruel fang of the daemons. the noise seem not to approach, but 
continue to be heard at intervals. sometimes it became more audible, 
doubtless as the suffering of the person who utter the groan became 
more acute and insupportable. ambrosio now and then thought that he 
could distinguish accents; and once in particular he was almost 
convinced that he heard a faint voice exclaim, 

'god! oh! god! no hope! no succour!' 

yet deep groan follow these words. they die away gradually, and 
universal silence again prevailed. 

'what can this mean?' thought the bewilder monk. 

at that moment an idea which flash into his mind, almost petrify 
him with horror. he started, and shudder at himself. 

'should it be possible!' he groan involuntarily; 'should it but be 
possible, oh! what a monster am i!' 

he wish to resolve his doubts, and to repair his fault, if it were 
not too late already: but these generous and compassionate sentiment 
were soon put to flight by the return of matilda. he forgot the 
groan sufferer, and remember nothing but the danger and 
embarrassment of his own situation. the light of the return lamp 
gilded the walls, and in a few moments after matilda stood beside him. 
she had quit her religious habit: she was now cloathe in a long 
sable robe, on which was trace in gold embroidery a variety of unknown 
characters: it was fasten by a girdle of precious stones, in which 
was fixed a poignard. her neck and arms were uncovered. in her hand 
she bore a golden wand. her hair was loose and flow wildly upon her 
shoulders; her eyes sparkle with terrific expression; and her whole 
demeanour was calculate to inspire the behold with awe and 
admiration. 

'follow me!' she said to the monk in a low and solemn voice; 'all is 
ready!' 

his limb trembled, while he obey her. she led him through various 
narrow passages; and on every side as they past along, the beam of the 
lamp display none but the most revolting objects; skulls, bones, 
graves, and image whose eyes seem to glare on them with horror and 
surprize. at length they reach a spacious cavern, whose lofty roof 
the eye sought in vain to discover. a profound obscurity hover 
through the void. damp vapours struck cold to the friar's heart; and 
he listen sadly to the blast while it howl along the lonely vaults. 
here matilda stopped. she turn to ambrosio. his cheek and lip 
were pale with apprehension. by a glance of mingle scorn and anger 
she reprove his pusillanimity, but she spoke not. she placed the lamp 
upon the ground, near the basket. she motion that ambrosio should be 
silent, and began the mysterious rites. she drew a circle round him, 
another round herself, and then taking a small phial from the basket, 
pour a few drops upon the ground before her. she bent over the 
place, mutter some indistinct sentences, and immediately a pale 
sulphurous flame arose from the ground. it increase by degrees, and 
at length spread its wave over the whole surface, the circle alone 
excepted in which stood matilda and the monk. it then ascend the 
huge column of unhewn stone, glide along the roof, and form the 
cavern into an immense chamber total cover with blue tremble 
fire. it emit no heat: on the contrary, the extreme chill of 
the place seem to augment with every moment. matilda continue her 
incantations: at interval she took various articles from the basket, 
the nature and name of most of which were unknown to the friar: but 
among the few which he distinguished, he particularly observe three 
human fingers, and an agnu dei which she broke in pieces. she threw 
them all into the flame which burn before her, and they were 
instantly consumed. 

the monk beheld her with anxious curiosity. sudden she utter a 
loud and piercing shriek. she appear to be seize with an access of 
delirium; she tore her hair, beat her bosom, use the most frantic 
gestures, and drawing the poignard from her girdle plunge it into her 
left arm. the blood gush out plentifully, and as she stood on the 
brink of the circle, she took care that it should fall on the outside. 
the flame retired from the spot on which the blood was pouring. a 
volume of dark cloud rose slow from the ensanguined earth, and 
ascend gradually, till it reach the vault of the cavern. at the 
same time a clap of thunder was heard: the echo peal fearful along 
the subterraneous passages, and the ground shook beneath the feet of 
the enchantress. 

it was now that ambrosio repent of his rashness. the solemn 
singular of the charm had prepared him for something strange and 
horrible. he wait with fear for the spirit's appearance, whose 
coming was announce by thunder and earthquakes. he look wildly 
round him, expect that some dreadful apparition would meet his eyes, 
the sight of which would drive him mad. a cold shiver seize his 
body, and he sank upon one knee, unable to support himself. 

'he comes!' exclaim matilda in a joyful accent. 

ambrosio started, and expect the daemon with terror. what was his 
surprize, when the thunder cease to roll, a full strain of melodious 
music sound in the air. at the same time the cloud dispersed, and he 
beheld a figure more beautiful than fancy's pencil ever drew. it was a 
youth seemingly scarce eighteen, the perfection of whose form and face 
was unrivalled. he was perfectly naked: a bright star sparkle upon 
his forehead; two crimson wings extend themselves from his shoulders; 
and his silken locks were confine by a band of many-coloured fires, 
which play round his head, form themselves into a variety of 
figures, and shone with a brilliance far surpassing that of precious 
stones. circlet of diamond were fasten round his arms and ankles, 
and in his right hand he bore a silver branch, imitate myrtle. his 
form shone with dazzle glory: he was surround by cloud of 
rose-coloured light, and at the moment that he appeared, a refreshing 
air breathe perfume through the cavern. enchant at a vision so 
contrary to his expectations, ambrosio gaze upon the spirit with 
delight and wonder: yet however beautiful the figure, he could not but 
remark a wild in the daemon's eyes, and a mysterious melancholy 
impress upon his features, betray the fallen angel, and inspire 
the spectator with secret awe. 

the music ceased. matilda address herself to the spirit: she spoke 
in a language unintelligible to the monk, and was answer in the same. 
she seem to insist upon something which the daemon was unwill to 
grant. he frequent dart upon ambrosio angry glances, and at such 
times the friar's heart sank within him. matilda appear to grow 
incensed. she spoke in a loud and commanding tone, and her gesture 
declared that she was threaten him with her vengeance. her menace 
had the desire effect: the spirit sank upon his knee, and with a 
submissive air present to her the branch of myrtle. no soon had 
she received it, than the music was again heard; a thick cloud spread 
itself over the apparition; the blue flame disappeared, and total 
obscurity reign through the cave. the abbot move not from his 
place: his faculty were all bound up in pleasure, anxiety, and 
surprize. at length the dark dispersing, he perceive matilda 
standing near him in her religious habit, with the myrtle in her hand. 
no trace of the incantation, and the vault were only illuminate by 
the faint ray of the sepulchral lamp. 

'i have succeeded,' said matilda, 'though with more difficulty than i 
expected. lucifer, whom i summon to my assistance, was at first 
unwill to obey my commands: to enforce his compliance i was 
constrained to have recourse to my strongest charms. they have 
produce the desire effect, but i have engaged never more to invoke 
his agency in your favour. beware then, how you employ an opportunity 
which never will return. my magic arts will now be of no use to you: 
in future you can only hope for supernatural aid by invoke the 
daemon yourself, and accept the conditions of their service. this 
you will never do: you want strength of mind to force them to 
obedience, and unless you pay their establish price, they will not be 
your voluntary servants. in this one instance they consent to obey 
you: i offer you the means of enjoy your mistress, and be careful 
not to lose the opportunity. receive this constellate myrtle: while 
you bear this in your hand, every door will fly open to you. it will 
procure you access tomorrow night to antonia's chamber: then breathe 
upon it thrice, pronounce her name, and place it upon her pillow. a 
death-like slumber will immediately seize upon her, and deprive her of 
the power of resist your attempts. sleep will hold her till break 
of morning. in this state you may satisfy your desire without danger 
of being discovered; since when daylight shall dispel the effects of 
the enchantment, antonia will perceive her dishonour, but be ignorant 
of the ravisher. be happy then, my ambrosio, and let this service 
convince you that my friendship is disinterested and pure. the night 
must be near expiring: let us return to the abbey, lest our absence 
should create surprize.' 

the abbot received the talisman with silent gratitude. his idea were 
too much bewilder by the adventure of the night to permit his 
express his thanks audibly, or indeed as yet to feel the whole value 
of her present. matilda took up her lamp and basket, and guide her 
companion from the mysterious cavern. she restore the lamp to its 
former place, and continue her route in darkness, till she reach the 
foot of the staircase. the first beam of the rising sun dart down 
it facilitate the ascent. matilda and the abbot hasten out of the 
sepulchre, closed the door after them, and soon regain the abbey's 
western cloister. no one met them, and they retired unobserved to 
their respective cells. 

the confusion of ambrosio's mind now began to appease. he rejoice in 
the fortunate issue of his adventure, and reflect upon the virtue 
of the myrtle, look upon antonia as already in his power. 
imagination retrace to him those secret charm betray to him by the 
enchant mirror, and he wait with impatience for the approach of 
midnight.
