all the research of the marquis de las cisterna prove vain: agnes 
was lost to him for ever. despair produce so violent an effect upon 
his constitution, that the consequence was a long and severe illness. 
this prevent him from visiting elvira as he had intended; and she 
being ignorant of the cause of his neglect, it gave her no trifling 
uneasiness. his sister's death had prevent lorenzo from 
communicate to his uncle his designs respecting antonia: the 
injunction of her mother forbad his present himself to her without 
the duke's consent; and as she heard no more of him or his proposals, 
elvira conjecture that he had either met with a better match, or had 
been command to give up all thought of her daughter. every day made 
her more uneasy respecting antonia's fate: while she retain the 
abbot's protection, she bore with fortitude the disappointment of her 
hope with regard to lorenzo and the marquis. that resource now fail 
her. she was convinced that ambrosio had meditate her daughter's 
ruin: and when she reflect that her death would leave antonia 
friendless and unprotected in a world so base, so perfidious and 
depraved, her heart swell with the bitter of apprehension. at 
such times she would sit for hour gaze upon the lovely girl; and 
seeming to listen to her innocent prattle, while in reality her 
thought dwelt upon the sorrow into which a moment would suffice to 
plunge her. then she would clasp her in her arms suddenly, lean her 
head upon her daughter's bosom, and bedew it with her tears. 

an event was in preparation which, had she known it, would have 
relieved her from her inquietude. lorenzo now wait only for a 
favourable opportunity to inform the duke of his intended marriage: 
however, a circumstance which occur at this period, oblige him to 
delay his explanation for a few days longer. 

don raymond's malady seem to gain ground. lorenzo was constant at 
his bedside, and treat him with a tender truly fraternal. both 
the cause and effects of the disorder were highly afflict to the 
brother of agnes: yet theodore's grief was scarcely less sincere. that 
amiable boy quit not his master for a moment, and put every means in 
practice to console and alleviate his sufferings. the marquis had 
conceive so rooted an affection for his deceased mistress, that it was 
evident to all that he never could survive her loss: nothing could 
have prevent him from sink under his grief but the persuasion of 
her being still alive, and in need of his assistance. though convinced 
of its falsehood, his attendant encourage him in a belief which 
form his only comfort. he was assured daily that fresh perquisite 
were making respecting the fate of agnes: story were invent 
recount the various attempt made to get admittance into the 
convent; and circumstances were related which, though they did not 
promise her absolute recovery, at least were sufficient to keep his 
hope alive. the marquis constant fell into the most terrible excess 
of passion when informed of the failure of these supposed attempts. 
still he would not credit that the succeed one would have the same 
fate, but flatter himself that the next would prove more fortunate. 

theodore was the only one who exert himself to realize his master's 
chimoeras. he was eternal busy in plan scheme for enter 
the convent, or at least of obtain from the nun some intelligence 
of agnes. to execute these scheme was the only inducement which could 
prevail on him to quit don raymond. he became a very proteus, change 
his shape every day; but all his metamorphose were to very little 
purpose: he regularly return to the palace de las cisterna without 
any intelligence to confirm his master's hopes. one day he took it 
into his head to disguise himself as a beggar. he put a patch over his 
left eye, took his guitar in hand, and post himself at the gate of 
the convent. 

'if agnes is really confine in the convent,' thought he, 'and hear my 
voice, she will recollect it, and possibly may find means to let me 
know that she is here.' 

with this idea he mingle with a crowd of beggar who assemble daily 
at the gate of st. clare to receive soup, which the nun were 
accustomed to distribute at twelve o'clock. all were provided with 
jug or bowls to carry it away; but as theodore had no utensil of this 
kind, he beg leave to eat his portion at the convent door. this was 
grant without difficulty: his sweet voice, and in spite of his 
patch eye, his engaging countenance, won the heart of the good old 
porteress, who, aid by a lay-sister, was busy in serving to each 
his mess. theodore was bad to stay till the other should depart, and 
promise that his request should then be granted. the youth desire no 
better, since it was not to eat soup that he present himself at the 
convent. he thank the porteress for her permission, retired from the 
door, and seating himself upon a large stone, amuse himself in tune 
his guitar while the beggar were served. 

as soon as the crowd was gone, theodore was beckon to the gate, and 
desire to come in. he obey with infinite readiness, but affected 
great respect at passing the hallow threshold, and to be much daunt 
by the presence of the reverend ladies. his feign timid flatter 
the vanity of the nuns, who endeavour to reassure him. the porteress 
took him into her awn little parlour: in the meanwhile, the lay-sister 
went to the kitchen, and soon return with a double portion of soup, 
of better quality than what was given to the beggars. his hostess 
add some fruits and confection from her own private store, and both 
encourage the youth to dine heartily. to all these attention he 
reply with much seeming gratitude, and abundance of blessing upon 
his benefactresses. while he ate, the nun admire the delicacy of his 
features, the beauty of his hair, and the sweet and grace which 
accompany all his actions. they lament to each other in whispers, 
that so charming a youth should be expose to the seduction of the 
world, and agreed, that he would be a worthy pillar of the catholic 
church. they conclude their conference by resolve that heaven would 
be render a real service if they entreat the prioress to intercede 
with ambrosio for the beggar's admission into the order of capuchins. 

this being determined, the porteress, who was a person of great 
influence in the convent, post away in all haste to the domina's 
cell. here she made so flaming a narrative of theodore's merit that 
the old lady grew curious to see him. accordingly, the porteress was 
commission to convey him to the parlour grate. in the interim, the 
supposed beggar was sift the lay-sister with respect to the fate of 
agnes: her evidence only corroborate the domina's assertions. she 
said that agnes had been taken ill on return from confession, had 
never quit her bed from that moment, and that she had herself been 
present at the funeral. she even attested have seen her dead body, 
and assist with her own hand in adjust it upon the bier. this 
account discourage theodore: yet as he had pushed the adventure so 
far, he resolve to witness its conclusion. 

the porteress now returned, and ordered him to follow her. he obeyed, 
and was conduct into the parlour, where the lady prioress was already 
post at the grate. the nun surround her, who all flock with 
eager to a scene which promise some diversion. theodore salute 
them with profound respect, and his presence had the power to smooth 
for a moment even the stern brow of the superior. she ask several 
question respecting his parents, his religion, and what had reduce 
him to a state of beggary. to these demand his answer were perfectly 
satisfactory and perfectly false. he was then ask his opinion of a 
monastic life: he reply in terms of high estimation and respect for 
it. upon this, the prioress told him that his obtain an entrance 
into a religious order was not impossible; that her recommendation 
would not permit his poverty to be an obstacle, and that if she found 
him deserving it, he might depend in future upon her protection. 
theodore assured her that to merit her favour would be his highest 
ambition; and have ordered him to return next day, when she would 
talk with him further, the domina quit the parlour. 

the nuns, whom respect for the superior had till then kept silent, now 
crowded all together to the grate, and assail the youth with a 
multitude of questions. he had already examine each with attention: 
alas! agnes was not amongst them. the nun heap question upon 
question so thick that it was scarcely possible for him to reply. 
one ask where he was born, since his accent declared him to be a 
foreigner: another want to know, why he wore a patch upon his left 
eye: sister helena enquire whether he had not a sister like him, 
because she should like such a companion; and sister rachael was fully 
persuade that the brother would be the pleasant companion of the 
two. theodore amuse himself with retail to the credulous nun for 
truth all the strange story which his imagination could invent. he 
related to them his supposed adventures, and penetrate every auditor 
with astonishment, while he talk of giants, savages, ship-wrecks, and 
island inhabit 

'by anthropophagi, and men whose head 
do grow beneath their shoulders,' 

with many other circumstances to the full as remarkable. he said, that 
he was born in terra incognita, was educated at an hottentot 
university, and had past two years among the america of silesia. 

'for what regards the loss of my eye' said he, 'it was a just 
punishment upon me for disrespect to the virgin, when i made my second 
pilgrimage to loretto. i stood near the altar in the miraculous 
chapel: the monk were proceeding to array the statue in her best 
apparel. the pilgrim were ordered to close their eyes during this 
ceremony: but though by nature extremely religious, curiosity was too 
powerful. at the moment ..... i shall penetrate you with horror, 
reverend ladies, when i reveal my crime! .... at the moment that 
the monk were change her shift, i venture to open my left eye, and 
gave a little peep towards the statue. that look was my last! the 
glory which surround the virgin was too great to be supported. i 
hasty shut my sacrilegious eye, and never have been able to unclose 
it since!' 

at the relation of this miracle the nun all cross themselves, and 
promise to intercede with the blessed virgin for the recovery of his 
sight. they express their wonder at the extent of his travels, and 
at the strange adventure which he had met with at so early an age. 
they now remark his guitar, and enquire whether he was an adept in 
music. he reply with modesty that it was not for him to decide upon 
his talents, but request permission to appeal to them as judges. 
this was grant without difficulty. 

'but at least,' said the old porteress, 'take care not to sing any 
thing profane.' 

'you may depend upon my discretion,' reply theodore: 'you shall hear 
how dangerous it is for young women to abandon themselves to their 
passions, illustrate by the adventure of a damsel who fell sudden in 
love with an unknown knight.' 

'but is the adventure true?' enquire the porteress. 

'every word of it. it happen in denmark, and the heroine was thought 
so beautiful that she was known by no other name but that of "the 
lovely maid".' 

'in denmark, say you?' mumble an old nun; 'are not the people all 
black in denmark?' 

'by no means, reverend lady; they are of a delicate pea-green with 
flame-coloured hair and whiskers.' 

'mother of god! pea-green?' exclaim sister helena; 'oh! 'tis 
impossible!' 

'impossible?' said the porteress with a look of contempt and 
exultation: 'not at all: when i was a young woman, i remember seeing 
several of them myself.' 

theodore now put his instrument in proper order. he had read the story 
of a king of england whose prison was discover by a minstrel; and he 
hope that the same scheme would enable him to discover agnes, should 
she be in the convent. he chose a ballad which she had taught him 
herself in the castle of lindenberg: she might possibly catch the 
sound, and he hope to hear her reply to some of the stanzas. his 
guitar was now in tune, and he prepared to strike it. 

'but before i begin,' said he 'it is necessary to inform you, ladies, 
that this same denmark is terribly infest by sorcerers, witches, and 
evil spirits. every element possess its appropriate daemons. the 
wood are haunt by a malignant power, call "the erl- or 
oak-king:" he it is who blight the trees, spoils the harvest, and 
command the imp and goblins: he appear in the form of an old man of 
majestic figure, with a golden crown and long white beard: his 
principal amusement is to entice young children from their parents, and 
as soon as he get them into his cave, he tear them into a thousand 
pieces--the river are govern by another fiend, call "the 
water-king:" his province is to agitate the deep, occasion 
ship-wrecks, and drag the drown sailor beneath the waves: he wear 
the appearance of a warrior, and employ himself in lure young 
virgin into his snare: what he do with them, when he catch them 
in the water, reverend ladies, i leave for you to imagine--"the 
fire-king" seem to be a man all form of flames: he raise the 
meteor and wandering lights which beguile traveller into pond and 
marshes, and he direct the lightning where it may do most 
mischief--the last of these elementary daemon is call "the 
cloud-king;" his figure is that of a beautiful youth, and he is 
distinguished by two large sable wings: though his outside is so 
enchanting, he is not a bit better disposed than the others: he is 
continual employ in raise storms, tear up forest by the 
roots, and blow castle and convent about the ear of their 
inhabitants. the first has a daughter, who is queen of the elves and 
fairies; the second has a mother, who is a powerful enchantress: 
neither of these ladies are worth more than the gentlemen: i do not 
remember to have heard any family assign to the two other daemons, 
but at present i have no business with any of them except the fiend of 
the waters. he is the hero of my ballad; but i thought it necessary 
before i began, to give you some account of his proceedings--' 

theodore then play a short symphony; after which, stretch his 
voice to its utmost extent to facilitate its reach the ear of agnes, 
he sang the following stanzas.

the youth cease to sing. the nun were delight with the sweet

'hah!' said the porteress; 'here come the mother st. ursula with a

'agnes!' she whisper in a voice scarcely intelligible. theodore,

and she look if possible more stern than ever. 

'mother st. ursula, i would speak with you in private.'

st. ursula obey her; soon after, the refectory bell ring a second

have recognise your page, i venture to send these few lines. procure 
an order from the cardinal-duke for seize my person, and that of the 
domina; but let it not be execute till friday at midnight. it is the 
festival of st. clare: there will be a procession of nun by

intention be known: should a syllable be dropt to excite the domina's

the memory of agnes, and wish to punish her assassins. i have that to 
tell, will freeze your blood with horror. st. ursula. 

no soon had the marquis read the note than he fell back upon his 
pillow deprived of sense or motion. the hope fail him which till now 
had support his existence; and these lines convinced him but too 
positively that agnes was indeed no more. lorenzo felt this 
circumstance less forcibly, since it had always been his idea that his 
sister had perish by unfair means. when he found by the mother st. 
ursula's letter how true were his suspicions, the confirmation excited 
no other sentiment in his bosom than a wish to punish the murder as 
they deserved. it was no easy task to recall the marquis to himself. 
as soon as he recover his speech, he broke out into execrate 
against the assassin of his beloved, and vow to take upon them a

could support itself no longer, and he relapse into insensibility. 
his melancholy situation sincerely affected lorenzo, who would 
willing have remain in the apartment of his friend; but other care

seize the prioress of st. clare. for this purpose, have committed

his disappointment was excessive, when he found that affair of state 
had oblige the cardinal to set out for a distant province. 

it want but five to friday: yet by travel day and night, he 
hope to return in time for the pilgrimage of st. clare. in this he

medina hasten back to madrid, which he reach on the friday a few

but while lorenzo was anxious to unmask one religious hypocrite, he was

antonia's ruin. the moment destined to be so fatal to her arrived. 
she had taken leave of her mother for the night.

such ungrounded sadness, and warn her how dangerous it was to 
encourage such ideas. 

to all her remonstrance she received no other answer than, 

'mother! dear mother! oh! would to god, it were morning!' 

elvira, whose inquietude respecting her daughter was a great obstacle

she was still in this state of insensibility when she was disturbed by

the music ceased: the performer dispersed, and silence prevail 
through the street. antonia quit the window with regret: she as 
usual recommend herself to the protection of st. rosolia, said her 
accustomed prayers, and retired to bed. sleep was not long absent, and 
his presence relieved her from her terror and inquietude. 

it was almost two o'clock before the lustful monk venture to bend his 
steps towards antonia's dwelling. it has been already mention that 
the abbey was at no great distance from the strada di san iago. he 
reach the house unobserved. here he stopped, and hesitate for a 
moment. he reflect on the enormity of the crime, the consequence of 
a discovery, and the probability, after what had passed, of elvira's 
suspect him to be her daughter's ravisher: on the other hand it was 
suggest that she could do no more than suspect; that no proof of his 
guilt could be produced; that it would seem impossible for the rape to 
have been committed without antonia's knowing when, where, or by whom; 
and finally, he believe that his fame was too firm establish to be 
shaken by the unsupported accusation of two unknown women. this 
latter argument was perfectly false: he knew not how uncertain is the 
air of popular applause, and that a moment suffice to make him today 
the detest of the world, who yesterday was its idol. the result 
of the monk's deliberation was that he should proceed in his 
enterprize. he ascend the steps leading to the house. no soon did 
he touch the door with the silver myrtle, than it flew open, and 
present him with a free passage. he entered, and the door closed 
after him of its own accord. 

guide by the moonbeams, he proceed up the staircase with slow and 
cautious steps. he look round him every moment with apprehension and 
anxiety. he saw a spy in every shadow, and heard a voice in every 
murmur of the night breeze. consciousness of the guilty business on 
which he was employ appall his heart, and render it more timid 
than a woman's. yet still he proceeded. he reach the door of 
antonia's chamber. he stopped, and listened. all was hush within. 
the total silence persuade him that his intended victim was retired to 
rest, and he venture to lift up the latch. the door was fastened, and 
resist his efforts: but no soon was it touched by the talisman, 
than the bolt flew back. the ravish stept on, and found himself in 
the chamber, where slept the innocent girl, unconscious how dangerous a 
visitor was drawing near her couch. the door closed after him, and the 
bolt shot again into its fastening. 

ambrosio advanced with precaution. he took care that not a board 
should creak under his foot, and held in his breath as he approach 
the bed. his first attention was to perform the magic ceremony, as 
matilda had charged him: he breathe thrice upon the silver myrtle, 
pronounced over it antonia's name, and laid it upon her pillow. the 
effects which it had already produce permit not his doubt its 
success in prolong the slumber of his devoted mistress. no soon 
was the enchantment perform than he considered her to be absolutely 
in his power, and his eyes flame with lust and impatience. he now 
venture to cast a glance upon the sleep beauty. a single lamp, 
burning before the statue of st. rosolia, shed a faint light through 
the room, and permit him to examine all the charm of the lovely 
object before him. the heat of the weather had oblige her to throw 
off part of the bed-cloathes: those which still cover her, 
ambrosio's insolent hand hasten to remove. she lay with her cheek 
recline upon one ivory arm; the other rest on the side of the bed 
with graceful indolence. a few tresses of her hair had escape from 
beneath the muslin which confine the rest, and fell careless over 
her bosom, as it heave with slow and regular suspiration. the warm 
air had spread her cheek with higher colour than usual. a smile 
inexpressible sweet play round her ripe and coral lips, from which 
every now and then escape a gentle sigh or an half-pronounced 
sentence. an air of enchanting innocence and candour pervade her 
whole form; and there was a sort of modesty in her very naked which 
add fresh sting to the desire of the lustful monk. 

he remain for some moments devour those charm with his eyes which 
soon were to be subject to his ill-regulated passions. her mouth 
half-opened seem to solicit a kiss: he bent over her; he join his 
lip to hers, and drew in the fragrance of her breath with rapture. 
this momentary pleasure increase his longing for still greater. his 
desire were raise to that frantic height by which brute are 
agitated. he resolve not to delay for one instant long the 
accomplishment of his wishes, and hasty proceed to tear off those 
garment which impede the gratification of his lust. 

'gracious god!' exclaim a voice behind him; 'am i not deceived? 

is not this an illusion?' 

terror, confusion, and disappointment accompany these words, as they 
struck ambrosio's hearing. he started, and turn towards it. elvira 
stood at the door of the chamber, and regard the monk with looks of 
surprize and detestation. 

a frightful dream had represent to her antonia on the verge of a 
precipice. she saw her tremble on the brink: every moment seem to

antonia's chamber just in time to rescue her from the grasp of the 
ravisher.

the lady was the first to recover herself. 

'it is no dream!' she cried; 'it is really ambrosio, who stand before

informed of your incontinence. i will unmask you, villain, and 
convince the church what a viper she cherish in her bosom.' 

pale and confuse the baffle culprit stood tremble before her. 

he would fain have extenuate his offence, but could find no apology

'this slumber cannot be natural!' cry the amaze elvira, whose

it; but tremble, hypocrite; all your villainy shall soon be unravelled! 
help! help!' she exclaim aloud; 'within there! flora! flora!' 

'hear me for one moment, lady!' cry the monk, restore to himself by

that your daughter's honour is still unviolated. forgive my

regain the abbey undisturbed. grant me this request in mercy! i 
promise not only that antonia shall be secure from me in future, but 
that the rest of my life shall prove .....' 

elvira interrupt him abruptly. 

'antonia secure from you? _i_ will secure her! you shall betray no 
long the confidence of parents! your iniquity shall be unveil to 
the public eye: all madrid shall shudder at your perfidy, your 
hypocrisy and incontinence. what ho! there! flora! flora, i say!' 

while she spoke thus, the remembrance of agnes struck upon his mind. 
thus had she sue to him for mercy, and thus had he refuse her prayer! 
it was now his turn to suffer, and he could not but acknowledge that 
his punishment was just. in the meanwhile elvira continue to call 
flora to her assistance; but her voice was so choake with passion that

his intention: he trust that could he reach the abbey unobserved by 
any other than elvira, her single testimony would not suffice to ruin a

gather up such garment as he had already thrown off, and hasten 
towards the door. elvira was aware of his design; she follow him, 
and ere he could draw back the bolt, seize him by the arm, and 
detain him. 

'attempt not to fly!' said she; 'you quit not this room without 
witness of your guilt.' 

ambrosio struggle in vain to disengage himself. elvira quit not 
her hold, but redouble her cries for succour. the friar's danger grew 
more urgent. he expect every moment to hear people assemble at her 
voice; and work up to madness by the approach of ruin, he adopt a 
resolution equally desperate and savage. turning round suddenly, with 
one hand he grasp elvira's throat so as to prevent her continue her

scarcely had power to strive at force herself from his grasp: while

covering with it elvira's face, and pressing his knee upon her stomach

succeed but too well. her natural strength increase by the excess

vain. the monk continue to kneel upon her breast, witness without

were on the point of separating. those agony at length were over. 
she cease to struggle for life. the monk took off the pillow, and 
gaze upon her. her face was cover with a frightful blackness: 

her limb move no more; the blood was chill in her veins; her heart 
had forgotten to beat, and her hand were stiff and frozen. 

ambrosio beheld before him that once noble and majestic form, now 
become a corse, cold, senseless and disgusting. 

this horrible act was no soon perpetrated, than the friar beheld the 
enormity of his crime. a cold dew flow over his limbs; his eyes 
closed; he stagger to a chair, and sank into it almost as lifeless as 
the unfortunate who lay extend at his feet. from this state he was 
rouze by the necessity of flight, and the danger of being found in 
antonia's apartment. he had no desire to profit by the execution of 
his crime. antonia now appear to him an object of disgust. a deadly 
cold had usurp the place of that warmth which glow in his bosom: 
no idea offer themselves to his mind but those of death and guilt, 
of present shame and future punishment. agitate by remorse and fear 
he prepared for flight: yet his terror did not so compleat master 
his recollection, as to prevent his taking the precaution necessary 
for his safety. he replace the pillow upon the bed, gather up his

was oppose by legion of phantoms; whereever he turned, the disfigure 
corse seem to lie in his passage, and it was long before he succeed 
in reach the door. the enchant myrtle produce its former effect. 
the door opened, and he hasten down the staircase. he enter the 
abbey unobserved, and have shut himself into his cell, he abandoned 
his soul to the torture of unavailing remorse, and terror of 
impending detection.

ambrosio shudder at himself, when he reflect on his rapid advances 
in iniquity. the enormous crime which he had just committed fill him 
with real horror. the murder elvira was continual before his eyes, 
and his guilt was already punish by the agony of his conscience. 
time, however, considerably weaken these impressions: one day pass 
away, another follow it, and still not the least suspicion was thrown 
upon him. impunity reconcile him to his guilt: he began to resume 
his spirits; and as his fear of detection die away, he paid less 
attention to the reproach of remorse. matilda exert herself to 
quiet his alarms. at the first intelligence of elvira's death, she 
seem greatly affected, and join the monk in deplore the unhappy 
catastrophe of his adventure: but when she found his agitation to be 
somewhat calmed, and himself better disposed to listen to her 
arguments, she proceed to mention his offence in mild terms, and 
convince him that he was not so highly culpable as he appear to 
consider himself. she represent that he had only avail himself of 
the rights which nature allow to every one, those of 
self-preservation: that either elvira or himself must have perished, 
and that her inflexible and resolution to ruin him had deservedly 
marked her out for the victim. she next stated, that as he had before 
render himself suspect to elvira, it was a fortunate event for him 
that her lip were closed by death; since without this last adventure, 
her suspicion if made public might have produce very disagreeable 
consequences. he had therefore free himself from an enemy, to whom 
the error of his conduct were sufficient known to make her 
dangerous, and who was the greatest obstacle to his designs upon 
antonia. those designs she encourage him not to abandon. she assured 
him that, no long protect by her mother's watchful eye, the 
daughter would fall an easy conquest; and by praise and enumerate 
antonia's charms, she strove to rekindle the desire of the monk. in 
this endeavour she succeed but too well. 

as if the crime into which his passion had seduce him had only 
increase its violence, he long more eager than ever to enjoy 
antonia. the same success in conceal his present guilt, he trust 
would attend his future. he was deaf to the murmur of conscience, and 
resolve to satisfy his desire at any price. he wait only for an 
opportunity of repeating his former enterprize; but to procure that 
opportunity by the same means was now impracticable. in the first 
transport of despair he had dashed the enchant myrtle into a 
thousand pieces: matilda told him plainly that he must expect no 
further assistance from the infernal powers unless he was willing to 
subscribe to their establish conditions. this ambrosio was 
determined not to do: he persuade himself that however great might be 
his iniquity, so long as he preserve his claim to salvation, he need 
not despair of pardon. he therefore resolute refuse to enter into 
any bond or compact with the fiends; and matilda finding him obstinate 
upon this point, forbore to press him further. she exert her 
invention to discover some means of putt antonia into the abbot's 
power: nor was it long before that means present itself. 

while her ruin was thus meditating, the unhappy girl herself suffer 
severe from the loss of her mother. every morning on waking, it was 
her first care to hasten to elvira's chamber. on that which follow 
ambrosio's fatal visit, she woke later than was her usual custom: of 
this she was convinced by the abbey chimes. she start from her bed, 
threw on a few loose garment hastily, and was speeding to enquire how 
her mother had pass the night, when her foot struck against something 
which lay in her passage. she look down. what was her horror at 
recognize elvira's livid corse! she utter a loud shriek, and threw 
herself upon the floor. she clasp the inanimate form to her bosom, 
felt that it was dead-cold, and with a movement of disgust, of which 
she was not the mistress, let it fall again from her arms. the cry had 
alarm flora, who hasten to her assistance. the sight which she 
beheld penetrate her with horror; but her alarm was more audible than 
antonia's. she made the house ring with her lamentations, while her 
mistress, almost suffocate with grief, could only mark her distress by 
sob and groans. flora's shriek soon reach the ear of the hostess, 
whose terror and surprize were excessive on learning the cause of this 
disturbance. a physician was immediately sent for: but on the first 
moment of behold the corse, he declared that elvira's recovery was 
beyond the power of art. he proceed therefore to give his assistance 
to antonia, who by this time was truly in need of it. she was convey 
to bed, while the landlady busy herself in give orders for elvira's 
burial. dame jacintha was a plain good kind of woman, charitable, 
generous, and devout: but her intellect were weak, and she was a 
miserable slave to fear and superstition. she shudder at the idea of 
passing the night in the same house with a dead body: she was persuade 
that elvira's ghost would appear to her, and no less certain that such 
a visit would kill her with fright. from this persuasion, she resolve 
to pass the night at a neighbour's, and insist that the funeral 
should take place the next day. st. clare's cemetery being the nearest, 
it was determined that elvira should be bury there. dame jacintha 
engaged to defray every expence attend the burial. she knew not in 
what circumstances antonia was left, but from the sparing manner in 
which the family had lived, she conclude them to be indifferent. 

consequently, she entertain very little hope of ever being 
recompensed; but this consideration prevent her not from taking care 
that the interment was perform with decency, and from showing the 
unfortunate antonia all possible respect. 

nobody die of mere grief; of this antonia was an instance. aid by 
her youth and healthy constitution, she shook off the malady which her 
mother's death had occasioned; but it was not so easy to remove the 
disease of her mind. her eyes were constant fill with tears: every 
trifle affected her, and she evidently nourish in her bosom a 
profound and rooted melancholy. the slightest mention of elvira, the 
most trivial circumstance recall that beloved parent to her memory, 
was sufficient to throw her into serious agitation. how much would her 
grief have been increased, had she known the agony which terminate 
her mother's existence! but of this no one entertain the least 
suspicion. elvira was subject to strong convulsions: it was supposed 
that, aware of their approach, she had drag herself to her 
daughter's chamber in hope of assistance; that a sudden access of her 
fit had seize her, too violent to be resist by her already 
enfeeble state of health; and that she had expire ere she had time to 
reach the medicine which generally relieved her, and which stood upon a 
shelf in antonia's room. this idea was firm credit by the few 
people, who interested themselves about elvira: her death was esteem 
a natural event, and soon forgotten by all save by her, who had but too 
much reason to deplore her loss. 

in truth antonia's situation was sufficient embarrass and 
unpleasant. she was alone in the midst of a dissipated and expensive 
city; she was ill provided with money, and worse with friends. her 
aunt leonella was still at cordova, and she knew not her direction. of 
the marquis de las cisterna she heard no news: as to lorenzo, she had 
long given up the idea of possess any interest in his bosom. she 
knew not to whom she could address herself in her present dilemma. she 
wish to consult ambrosio; but she remember her mother's injunction 
to shun him as much as possible, and the last conversation which elvira 
had held with her upon the subject had given her sufficient lights 
respecting his designs to put her upon her guard against him in future. 
still all her mother's warning could not make her change her good 
opinion of the friar. she continue to feel that his friendship and 
society were requisite to her happiness: she look upon his failing 
with a partial eye, and could not persuade herself that he really had 
intended her ruin. however, elvira had positively command her to 
drop his acquaintance, and she had too much respect for her orders to 
disobey them. 

at length she resolve to address herself for advice and protection to 
the marquis de las cisternas, as being her nearest relation. she wrote 
to him, brief state her desolate situation; she besought him to 
compassionate his brother's child, to continue to her elvira's pension, 
and to authorise her retiring to his old castle in murcia, which till 
now had been her retreat. have seal her letter, she gave it to the 
trusty flora, who immediately set out to execute her commission. but 
antonia was born under an unlucky star. had she made her application 
to the marquis but one day sooner, received as his niece and placed at 
the head of his family, she would have escape all the misfortune with 
which she was now threatened. raymond had always intended to execute 
this plan: but first, his hope of making the proposal to elvira 
through the lip of agnes, and afterwards, his disappointment at lose 
his intended bride, as well as the severe illness which for some time 
had confine him to his bed, made him defer from day to day the give 
an asylum in his house to his brother's widow. he had commission 
lorenzo to supply her liberally with money: but elvira, unwill to 
receive obligation from that nobleman, had assured him that she need 
no immediate pecuniary assistance. consequently, the marquis did not 
imagine that a trifling delay on his part could create any 
embarrassment; and the distress and agitation of his mind might well 
excuse his negligence. 

had he been informed that elvira's death had left her daughter 
friendless and unprotected, he would doubtless have taken such 
measures, as would have ensure her from every danger: but antonia was 
not destined to be so fortunate. the day on which she sent her letter 
to the palace de las cisterna was that following lorenzo's departure 
from madrid. the marquis was in the first paroxysm of despair at the 
conviction that agnes was indeed no more: he was delirious, and his 
life being in danger, no one was suffer to approach him. flora was 
informed that he was incapable of attend to letters, and that 
probably a few hour would decide his fate. with this unsatisfactory 
answer she was oblige to return to her mistress, who now found herself 
plunge into greater difficulty than ever. 

flora and dame jacintha exert themselves to console her. the latter 
beg her to make herself easy, for that as long as she chose to stay 
with her, she would treat her like her own child. antonia, finding that 
the good woman had taken a real affection for her, was somewhat 
comfort by thinking that she had at least one friend in the world. a 
letter was now brought to her, direct to elvira. she recognize 
leonella's writing, and opening it with joy, found a detailed account 
of her aunt's adventure at cordova. she informed her sister that she 
had recover her legacy, had lost her heart, and had received in 
exchange that of the most amiable of apothecaries, past, present, and 
to come. she add that she should be at madrid on the tuesday night, 
and meant to have the pleasure of present her caro sposo in form. 
though her nuptials were far from pleasing antonia, leonella's speedy 
return gave her niece much delight. she rejoice in thinking that she 
should once more be under a relation's care. she could not but judge 
it to be highly improper, for a young woman to be living among absolute 
strangers, with no one to regulate her conduct, or protect her from the 
insult to which, in her defenceless situation, she was exposed. she 
therefore look forward with impatience to the tuesday night. 

it arrived. antonia listen anxious to the carriages, as they 
roll along the street. none of them stopped, and it grew late 
without leonella's appearing. still, antonia resolve to sit up till 
her aunt's arrival, and in spite of all her remonstrances, dame 
jacintha and flora insist upon do the same. the hour pass on 
slow and tediously. lorenzo's departure from madrid had put a stop to 
the nightly serenades: she hope in vain to hear the usual sound of 
guitar beneath her window. she took up her own, and struck a few 
chords: but music that evening had lost its charm for her, and she 
soon replace the instrument in its case. she seat herself at her 
embroidery frame, but nothing went right: the silk were missing, the 
thread snap every moment, and the needle were so expert at fall 
that they seem to be animated. at length a flake of wax fell from 
the taper which stood near her upon a favourite wreath of violets: this 
compleat discompose her; she threw down her needle, and quit the 
frame. it was decree that for that night nothing should have the 
power of amuse her. she was the prey of ennui, and employ herself 
in making fruitless wish for the arrival of her aunt. 

as she walk with a listless air up and down the chamber, the door 
caught her eye conduct to that which had been her mother's. she 
remember that elvira's little library was arrange there, and thought 
that she might possibly find in it some book to amuse her till leonella 
should arrive. accordingly she took her taper from the table, pass 
through the little closet, and enter the adjoin apartment. as she 
look around her, the sight of this room brought to her recollection a 
thousand painful ideas. it was the first time of her enter it since 
her mother's death. the total silence prevailing through the chamber, 
the bed despoil of its furniture, the cheerless hearth where stood an 
extinguish lamp, and a few die plant in the window which, since 
elvira's loss, had been neglected, inspired antonia with a melancholy 
awe. the gloom of night gave strength to this sensation. she placed 
her light upon the table, and sank into a large chair, in which she had 
seen her mother seat a thousand and a thousand times. she was never 
to see her seat there again! tear unbidden stream down her cheek, 
and she abandoned herself to the sad which grew deep with every 
moment. 

ashamed of her weakness, she at length rose from her seat: she 
proceed to seek for what had brought her to this melancholy scene. 
the small collection of books was arrange upon several shelves in 
order. antonia examine them without finding any thing likely to 
interest her, till she put her hand upon a volume of old spain 
ballads. she read a few stanza of one of them: they excited her 
curiosity. she took down the book, and seat herself to peruse it 
with more ease. she trim the taper, which now drew towards its end, 
and then read the following ballad. 

alonzo the brave, and fair imogine 

a warrior so bold, and a virgin so bright 
conversed, as they sat on the green: 
they gaze on each other with tender delight; 
alonzo the brave was the name of the knight, 
the maid's was the fair imogine. 

'and oh!' said the youth, 'since to-morrow i go 
to fight in a far distant land, 
your tear for my absence soon leave to flow, 
some other will court you, and you will bestow 
on a wealthy suitor your hand.' 

'oh! hush these suspicions,' fair imogine said, 
'offensive to love and to me! 
for if ye be living, or if ye be dead, 
i swear by the virgin, that none in your stead 
shall husband of imogine be. 

'if e'er i by lust or by wealth led aside 
forget my alonzo the brave, 
god grant, that to punish my falsehood and pride 
your ghost at the marriage may sit by my side, 
may tax me with perjury, claim me as bride, 
and bear me away to the grave!' 

to palestine hasten the hero so bold; 
his love, she lament him sore: 
but scarce had a twelve-month elapsed, when behold, 
a baron all cover with jewel and gold 
arrive at fair imogine's door. 

his treasure, his presents, his spacious domain 
soon made her untrue to her vows: 
he dazzle her eyes; he bewilder her brain; 
he caught her affection so light and so vain, 
and carry her home as his spouse. 

and now had the marriage been blest by the priest; 
the revelry now was begun: 
the tables, they groan with the weight of the feast; 
nor yet had the laughter and merriment ceased, 
when the bell of the castle told,--'one!' 

then first with amaze fair imogine found 
that a stranger was placed by her side: his air was terrific; 
he utter no sound; he spoke not, he move not, 
he look not around, 
but earnest gaze on the bride. 

his vizor was closed, and gigantic his height; 
his armour was sable to view: 
all pleasure and laughter were hush at his sight; 
the dogs as they eye him drew back in affright, 
the lights in the chamber burn blue! 

his presence all bosom appear to dismay; 
the guest sat in silence and fear. 
at length spoke the bride, while she trembled; 
'i pray, sir knight, that your helmet aside you would lay, 
and deign to partake of our chear.' 

the lady is silent: the stranger complies. 
his vizor lie slow unclosed: 
oh! god! what a sight met fair imogine's eyes! 
what words can express her dismay and surprize, 
when a skeleton's head was exposed. 

all present then utter a terrify shout; 
all turn with disgust from the scene. 
the worms, they crept in, and the worms, they crept out, 
and sport his eyes and his temple about, 
while the spectre address imogine. 

'behold me, thou false one! behold me!' he cried; 
'remember alonzo the brave! 
god grants, that to punish thy falsehood and pride 
my ghost at thy marriage should sit by thy side, 
should tax thee with perjury, claim thee as bride 
and bear thee away to the grave!' 

thus saying, his arms round the lady he wound, 
while loud she shriek in dismay; 
then sank with his prey through the wide-yawning ground: 
nor ever again was fair imogine found, 
or the spectre who bore her away. 

not long live the baron; and none since that time 
to inhabit the castle presume: 
for chronicle tell, that by order sublime 
there imogine suffer the pain of her crime, 
and mourn her deplorable doom. 

at midnight four times in each year do her spright 
when mortal in slumber are bound, 
array in her bridal apparel of white, 
appear in the hall with the skeleton-knight, 
and shriek, as he whirl her around. 

while they drink out of skull newly torn from the grave, 
dance round them the spectre are seen: 
their liquor is blood, and this horrible stave 
they howl.--'to the health of alonzo the brave, 
and his consort, the false imogine!' 


the peruse of this story was ill-calculated to dispel antonia's 
melancholy. she had naturally a strong inclination to the marvellous; 
and her nurse, who believe firm in apparitions, had related to her 
when an infant so many horrible adventure of this kind, that all 
elvira's attempt had fail to eradicate their impression from her 
daughter's mind. antonia still nourish a superstitious prejudice in 
her bosom: she was often susceptible of terror which, when she 
discover their natural and insignificant cause, made her blush at her 
own weakness. with such a turn of mind, the adventure which she had 
just been reading suffice to give her apprehension the alarm. the 
hour and the scene combine to authorize them. it was the dead of 
night: she was alone, and in the chamber once occupy by her deceased 
mother. the weather was comfortless and stormy: the wind howl 
around the house, the door rattle in their frames, and the heavy rain 
patter against the windows. no other sound was heard. the taper, 
now burnt down to the socket, sometimes flare upwards shot a gleam of 
light through the room, then sink again seem upon the point of 
expiring. antonia's heart throb with agitation: her eyes wander 
fearful over the object around her, as the tremble flame 
illuminate them at intervals. she attempt to rise from her seat; 
but her limb tremble so violent that she was unable to proceed. 
she then call flora, who was in a room at no great distance: but 
agitation choake her voice, and her cries die away in hollow murmurs. 

she pass some minutes in this situation, after which her terror 
began to diminish. she strove to recover herself, and acquire strength 
enough to quit the room: sudden she fancied, that she heard a low 
sigh drawn near her. this idea brought back her former weakness. she 
had already raise herself from her seat, and was on the point of 
taking the lamp from the table. the imaginary noise stop her: she 
drew back her hand, and support herself upon the back of a chair. 
she listen anxiously, but nothing more was heard. 

'gracious god!' she said to herself; 'what could be that sound? was i 
deceived, or did i really hear it?' 

her reflection were interrupt by a noise at the door scarcely 
audible: it seem as if somebody was whispering. antonia's alarm 
increased: yet the bolt she knew to be fastened, and this idea in some 
degree reassure her. presently the latch was lift up softly, and 
the door move with caution backwards and forwards. excess of terror 
now supply antonia with that strength, of which she had till then 
been deprived. she start from her place and made towards the closet 
door, whence she might soon have reach the chamber where she expect 
to find flora and dame jacintha. scarcely had she reach the middle 
of the room when the latch was lift up a second time. an involuntary 
movement oblige her to turn her head. slow and gradual the door 
turn upon its hinges, and standing upon the threshold she beheld a 
tall thin figure, wrap in a white shroud which cover it from head 
to foot. 

this vision arrest her feet: she remain as if petrify in the 
middle of the apartment. the stranger with measured and solemn steps 
drew near the table. the die taper dart a blue and melancholy 
flame as the figure advanced towards it. over the table was fixed a 
small clock; the hand of it was upon the stroke of three. the figure 
stop opposite to the clock: it raise its right arm, and pointed to 
the hour, at the same time look earnest upon antonia, who wait 
for the conclusion of this scene, motionless and silent. 

the figure remain in this posture for some moments. the clock struck. 
when the sound had ceased, the stranger advanced yet a few steps near 
antonia. 

'yet three days,' said a voice faint, hollow, and sepulchral; 'yet 
three days, and we meet again!' 

antonia shudder at the words. 

'we meet again?' she pronounced at length with difficulty: 'where shall 
we meet? whom shall i meet?' 

the figure pointed to the ground with one hand, and with the other 
raise the linen which cover its face. 

'almighty god! my mother!' 

antonia shrieked, and fell lifeless upon the floor. 

dame jacintha who was at work in a neighbouring chamber, was alarm by 
the cry: flora was just gone down stairs to fetch fresh oil for the 
lamp, by which they had been sitting. jacintha therefore hasten 
alone to antonia's assistance, and great was her amaze to find her 
extend upon the floor. she raise her in her arms, convey her to 
her apartment, and placed her upon the bed still senseless. she then 
proceed to bathe her temples, chafe her hands, and use all possible 
means of bring her to herself. with some difficulty she succeeded. 
antonia open her eyes, and look round her wildly. 

'where is she?' she cry in a tremble voice; 'is she gone? am i 
safe? speak to me! comfort me! oh! speak to me for god's sake!' 

'safe from whom, my child?' reply the astonish jacintha; 'what 
alarm you? of whom are you afraid?' 

'in three days! she told me that we should meet in three days! i heard 
her say it! i saw her, jacintha, i saw her but this moment!' 

she threw herself upon jacintha's bosom. 

'you saw her? saw whom?' 

'my mother's ghost!' 

'christ jesus!' cry jacintha, and start from the bed, let fall 
antonia upon the pillow, and flee in consternation out of the room. 

as she hasten down stairs, she met flora ascend them. 

'go to your mistress, flora,' said she; 'here are rare doings! oh! i am 
the most unfortunate woman alive! my house is fill with ghost and 
dead bodies, and the lord know what besides; yet i am sure, nobody 
likes such company less than i do. but go your way to donna antonia, 
flora, and let me go mine.' 

thus saying, she continue her course to the street door, which she 
opened, and without allow herself time to throw on her veil, she 
made the best of her way to the capuchin abbey. in the meanwhile, 
flora hasten to her lady's chamber, equally surprize and alarm at 
jacintha's consternation. she found antonia lie upon the bed 
insensible. she use the same means for her recovery that jacintha had 
already employed; but finding that her mistress only recover from one 
fit to fall into another, she sent in all haste for a physician. while 
expect his arrival, she undrest antonia, and convey her to bed. 

heedless of the storm, terrify almost out of her senses, jacintha ran 
through the streets, and stop not till she reach the gate of the 
abbey. she rang loud at the bell, and as soon as the porter 
appeared, she desire permission to speak to the superior. ambrosio 
was then confer with matilda upon the means of procure access to 
antonia. the cause of elvira's death remain unknown, he was 
convinced that crime were not so swift follow by punishment, as 
his instructor the monk had taught him, and as till then he had 
himself believed. this persuasion made him resolve upon antonia's 
ruin, for the enjoyment of whose person danger and difficulty only 
seem to have increase his passion. the monk had already made one 
attempt to gain admission to her presence; but flora had refuse him in 
such a manner as to convince him that all future endeavour must be 
vain. elvira had confide her suspicion to that trusty servant: she 
had desire her never to leave ambrosio alone with her daughter, and if 
possible to prevent their meeting altogether. flora promise to obey 
her, and had execute her orders to the very letter. ambrosio's visit 
had been reject that morning, though antonia was ignorant of it. he 
saw that to obtain a sight of his mistress by open means was out of the 
question; and both himself and matilda had consume the night, in 
endeavour to invent some plan, whose event might be more successful. 
such was their employment, when a lay-brother enter the abbot's cell, 
and informed him that a woman calling herself jacintha zuniga request 
audience for a few minutes. 

ambrosio was by no means disposed to grant the petition of his visitor. 
he refuse it positively, and bad the lay-brother tell the stranger to 
return the next day. matilda interrupt him. 

'see this woman,' said she in a low voice; 'i have my reasons.' 

the abbot obey her, and signify that he would go to the parlour 
immediately. with this answer the lay-brother withdrew. as soon as 
they were alone ambrosio enquire why matilda wish him to see this 
jacintha. 

'she is antonia's hostess,' reply matilda; 'she may possibly be of 
use to you: but let us examine her, and learn what bring her hither.' 

they proceed together to the parlour, where jacintha was already 
wait for the abbot. she had conceive a great opinion of his piety 
and virtue; and supposing him to have much influence over the devil, 
thought that it must be an easy matter for him to lay elvira's ghost in 
the red sea. fill with this persuasion she had hasten to the 
abbey. as soon as she saw the monk enter the parlour, she drop upon 
her knees, and began her story as follows. 

'oh! reverend father! such an accident! such an adventure! i know 
not what course to take, and unless you can help me, i shall certainly 
go distracted. well, to be sure, never was woman so unfortunate, as 
myself! all in my power to keep clear of such abomination have i done, 
and yet that all is too little. what signify my telling my bead 
four times a day, and observing every fast prescribed by the calendar? 
what signify my have made three pilgrimage to st. james of 
compostella, and purchase as many pardon from the pope as would buy 
off cain's punishment? nothing prosper with me! all go wrong, and 
god only knows, whether any thing will ever go right again! why now, 
be your holiness the judge. my lodger die in convulsions; out of pure 
kindness i bury her at my own expence; (not that she is any relation of 
mine, or that i shall be benefit a single pistole by her death: i 
got nothing by it, and therefore you know, reverend father, that her 
living or die was just the same to me. but that is nothing to the 
purpose; to return to what i was saying,) i took care of her funeral, 
had every thing perform decent and properly, and put myself to 
expence enough, god knows! and how do you think the lady repay me for 
my kindness? why truly by refuse to sleep quiet in her comfortable 
deal coffin, as a peaceable well-disposed spirit ought to do, and 
coming to plague me, who never wish to set eyes on her again. 
forsooth, it well become her to go racket about my house at 
midnight, pop into her daughter's room through the keyhole, and 
frighten the poor child out of her wits! though she be a ghost, she 
might be more civil than to bolt into a person's house, who likes her 
company so little. but as for me, reverend father, the plain state of 
the case is this: if she walk into my house, i must walk out of it, 
for i cannot abide such visitors, not i! thus you see, your sanctity, 
that without your assistance i am ruin and undone for ever. i shall 
be oblige to quit my house; nobody will take it, when 'tis known that 
she haunt it, and then i shall find myself in a fine situation! 
miserable woman that i am! what shall i do! what will become of me!' 

here she wept bitterly, wrung her hands, and beg to know the abbot's 
opinion of her case. 

'in truth, good woman,' reply he, 'it will be difficult for me to 
relieve you without knowing what is the matter with you. you have 
forgotten to tell me what has happened, and what it is you want.' 

'let me die' cry jacintha, 'but your sanctity is in the right! this 
then is the fact state briefly. a lodger of mine is lately dead, a 
very good sort of woman that i must needs say for her as far as my 
knowledge of her went, though that was not a great way: 

she kept me too much at a distance; for indeed she was given to be upon 
the high ropes, and whenever i venture to speak to her, she had a look 
with her which always made me feel a little queerish, god forgive me 
for saying so. however, though she was more stately than needful, and 
affected to look down upon me (though if i am well informed, i come of 
as good parent as she could do for her ears, for her father was a 
shoe-maker at cordova, and mine was an hatter at madrid, aye, and a 
very creditable hatter too, let me tell you,) yet for all her pride, 
she was a quiet well-behaved body, and i never wish to have a better 
lodger. this make me wonder the more at her not sleep quiet in 
her grave: but there is no trust to people in this world! for my 
part, i never saw her do amiss, except on the friday before her death. 
to be sure, i was then much scandalize by seeing her eat the wing of a 
chicken! "how, madona flora!" quoth i; (flora, may it please your 
reverence, is the name of the wait maid)--"how, madona flora!" 
quoth i; "does your mistress eat flesh upon fridays? well! well! 
see the event, and then remember that dame jacintha warn you of it!" 
these were my very words, but alas! i might as well have held my 
tongue! nobody minded me; and flora, who is somewhat pert and 
snappish, (more is the pity, say i) told me that there was no more harm 
in eat a chicken than the egg from which it came. nay, she even 
declared that if her lady add a slice of bacon, she would not be an 
inch near damnation, god protect us! a poor ignorant sinful soul! i 
protest to your holiness, i tremble to hear her utter such 
blasphemies, and expect every moment to see the ground open and 
swallow her up, chicken and all! for you must know, worshipful father, 
that while she talk thus, she held the plate in her hand, on which 
lay the identical roast fowl. and a fine bird it was, that i must say 
for it! done to a turn, for i superintend the cooking of it myself: 
it was a little gallician of my own raising, may it please your 
holiness, and the flesh was as white as an egg-shell, as indeed donna 
elvira told me herself. "dame jacintha," said she, very 
good-humouredly, though to say the truth, she was always very polite to 
me .....' 

here ambrosio's patience fail him. eager to know jacintha's business 
in which antonia seem to be concerned, he was almost distracted while 
listen to the rambling of this prose old woman. he interrupt 
her, and protest that if she did not immediately tell her story and 
have done with it, he should quit the parlour, and leave her to get out 
of her difficulty by herself. this threat had the desire effect. 
jacintha related her business in as few words as she could manage; but 
her account was still so prolix that ambrosio had need of his patience 
to bear him to the conclusion. 

'and so, your reverence,' said she, after relate elvira's death and 
burial, with all their circumstances; 'and so, your reverence, upon 
hearing the shriek, i put away my work, and away post i to donna 
antonia's chamber. finding nobody there, i past on to the next; but i 
must own, i was a little timorous at go in, for this was the very 
room where donna elvira use to sleep. however, in i went, and sure 
enough, there lay the young lady at full length upon the floor, as cold 
as a stone, and as white as a sheet. i was surprize at this, as your 
holiness may well suppose; but oh me! how i shook when i saw a great 
tall figure at my elbow whose head touched the ceiling! the face was 
donna elvira's, i must confess; but out of its mouth came cloud of 
fire, its arms were loaded with heavy chain which it rattle 
piteously, and every hair on its head was a serpent as big as my arm! 
at this i was frightened enough, and began to say my ave-maria: but 
the ghost interrupt me utter three loud groans, and roar out in 
a terrible voice, "oh! that chicken's wing! my poor soul suffer for 
it!" as soon as she had said this, the ground opened, the spectre 
sank down, i heard a clap of thunder, and the room was fill with a 
smell of brimstone. when i recover from my fright, and had brought 
donna antonia to herself, who told me that she had cry out upon 
seeing her mother's ghost, (and well might she cry, poor soul! had i 
been in her place, i should have cry ten times louder) it directly 
came into my head, that if any one had power to quiet this spectre, it 
must be your reverence. so hither i came in all diligence, to beg that 
you will sprinkle my house with holy water, and lay the apparition in 
the red sea.' 

ambrosio stare at this strange story, which he could not credit. 

'did donna antonia also see the ghost?' said he. 

'as plain as i see you, reverend father!' 

ambrosio pause for a moment. here was an opportunity offer him of 
gain access to antonia, but he hesitate to employ it. the 
reputation which he enjoy in madrid was still dear to him; and since 
he had lost the reality of virtue, it appear as if its semblance was 
become more valuable. he was conscious that public to break through 
the rule never to quit the abbey precincts, would derogate much from 
his supposed austerity. in visiting elvira, he had always taken care to 
keep his features conceal from the domestics. except by the lady, 
her daughter, and the faithful flora, he was known in the family by no 
other name than that of father jerome. should he comply with 
jacintha's request, and accompany her to her house, he knew that the 
violate of his rule could not be kept a secret. however, his 
eager to see antonia obtain the victory: he even hoped, that the 
singular of this adventure would justify him in the eyes of madrid: 
but whatever might be the consequences, he resolve to profit by the 
opportunity which chance had present to him. an expressive look from 
matilda confirmed him in this resolution. 

'good woman,' said he to jacintha, 'what you tell me is so 
extraordinary that i can scarcely credit your assertions. however, i 
will comply with your request. tomorrow after matins you may expect me 
at your house: i will then examine into what i can do for you, and if 
it is in my power, will free you from this unwelcome visitor. now then 
go home, and peace be with you!' 

'home?' exclaim jacintha; 'i go home? not i by my troth! except 
under your protection, i set no foot of mine within the threshold. god 
help me, the ghost may meet me upon the stairs, and whisk me away with 
her to the devil! oh! that i had accept young melchior basco's 
offer! then i should have had somebody to protect me; but now i am a 
lone woman, and meet with nothing but cross and misfortunes! thank 
heaven, it is not yet too late to repent! there is simon gonzalez will 
have me any day of the week, and if i live till daybreak, i will marry 
him out of hand: an husband i will have, that is determined, for now 
this ghost is once in my house, i shall be frightened out of my wit to 
sleep alone. but for god's sake, reverend father, come with me now. i 
shall have no rest till the house is purified, or the poor young lady 
either. the dear girl! she is in a piteous taking: i left her in 
strong convulsions, and i doubt, she will not easily recover her 
fright.' 

the friar started, and interrupt her hastily. 

'in convulsions, say you? antonia in convulsions? lead on, good 
woman! i follow you this moment!' 

jacintha insist upon his stopping to furnish himself with the vessel 
of holy water: with this request he complied. thinking herself safe 
under his protection should a legion of ghost attack her, the old 
woman return the monk a profusion of thanks, and they departed 
together for the strada di san iago. 

so strong an impression had the spectre made upon antonia, that for the 
first two or three hour the physician declared her life to be in 
danger. the fit at length becoming less frequent induce him to alter 
his opinion. he said that to keep her quiet was all that was 
necessary; and he ordered a medicine to be prepared which would 
tranquillize her nerves, and procure her that repose which at present 
she much wanted. the sight of ambrosio, who now appear with jacintha 
at her bedside, contribute essentially to compose her ruffle spirits. 
elvira had not sufficient explain herself upon the nature of his 
designs, to make a girl so ignorant of the world as her daughter aware 
how dangerous was his acquaintance. at this moment, when penetrate 
with horror at the scene which had just past, and dread to 
contemplate the ghost's prediction, her mind had need of all the 
succour of friendship and religion, antonia regard the abbot with an 
eye doubly partial. that strong prepossession in his favour still 
exist which she had felt for him at first sight: she fancied, yet 
knew not wherefore, that his presence was a safeguard to her from every 
danger, insult, or misfortune. 

she thank him grateful for his visit, and related to him the 
adventure, which had alarm her so seriously. 

the abbot strove to reassure her, and convince her that the whole had 
been a deception of her overheate fancy. the solitude in which she 
had pass the evening, the gloom of night, the book which she had been 
reading, and the room in which she sat, were all calculate to place 
before her such a vision. he treat the idea of ghost with ridicule, 
and produce strong argument to prove the fallacy of such a system. 
his conversation tranquillize and comfort her, but did not convince 
her. she could not believe that the spectre had been a mere creature 
of her imagination; every circumstance was impress upon her mind too 
forcibly, to permit her flatter herself with such an idea. she 
persist in assert that she had really seen her mother's ghost, had 
heard the period of her dissolution announce and declared that she 
never should quit her bed alive. ambrosio advise her against 
encourage these sentiments, and then quit her chamber, have 
promise to repeat his visit on the morrow. antonia received this 
assurance with every mark of joy: but the monk easily perceive that 
he was not equally acceptable to her attendant. flora obey elvira's 
injunction with the most scrupulous observance. she examine every 
circumstance with an anxious eye likely in the least to prejudice her 
young mistress, to whom she had been attach for many years. she was 
a native of cuba, had follow elvira to spain, and love the young 
antonia with a mother's affection. flora quit not the room for a 
moment while the abbot remain there: she watch his every word, his 
every look, his every action. he saw that her suspicious eye was 
always fixed upon him, and conscious that his designs would not bear 
inspection so minute, he felt frequent confuse and disconcerted. he 
was aware that she doubt the purity of his intentions; that she would 
never leave him alone with antonia, and his mistress defend by the 
presence of this vigilant observer, he despair of finding the means 
to gratify his passion. 

as he quit the house, jacintha met him, and beg that some masses 
might be sung for the repose of elvira's soul, which she doubt not 
was suffering in purgatory. he promise not to forget her request; but 
he perfectly gain the old woman's heart by engaging to watch during 
the whole of the approach night in the haunt chamber. jacintha 
could find no terms sufficient strong to express her gratitude, and 
the monk departed loaded with her benedictions. 

it was broad day when he return to the abbey. his first care was to 
communicate what had past to his confident. he felt too sincere a 
passion for antonia to have heard unmoved the prediction of her speedy 
death, and he shudder at the idea of lose an object so dear to him. 
upon this head matilda reassure him. she confirmed the argument 
which himself had already used: she declared antonia to have been 
deceive by the wandering of her brain, by the spleen which opprest her 
at the moment, and by the natural turn of her mind to superstition, and 
the marvellous. as to jacintha's account, the absurd refute 
itself; the abbot hesitate not to believe that she had fabricate the 
whole story, either confuse by terror, or hope to make him comply 
more readily with her request. have overrule the monk's 
apprehensions, matilda continue thus. 

'the prediction and the ghost are equally false; but it must be your 
care, ambrosio, to verify the first. antonia within three days must 
indeed be dead to the world; but she must live for you. 

her present illness, and this fancy which she has taken into her head, 
will colour a plan which i have long meditated, but which was 
impracticable without your procure access to antonia. she shall be 
yours, not for a single night, but for ever. all the vigilance of her 
duenna shall not avail her: you shall riot unrestrained in the charm 
of your mistress. this very day must the scheme be put in execution, 
for you have no time to lose. the nephew of the duke of medina celi 
prepare to demand antonia for his bride: in a few days she will be 
remove to the palace of her relation, the marquis de las cisternas, 
and there she will be secure from your attempts. thus during your 
absence have i been informed by my spies, who are ever employ in 
bring me intelligence for your service. now then listen to me. 
there is a juice extract from certain herbs, known but to few, which 
bring on the person who drink it the exact image of death. let this 
be administer to antonia: you may easily find means to pour a few 
drops into her medicine. the effect will be throw her into strong 
convulsion for an hour: after which her blood will gradual cease to 
flow, and heart to beat; a mortal pale will spread itself over her 
features, and she will appear a corse to every eye. she has no friends 
about her: you may charge yourself unsuspected with the 
superintend of her funeral, and cause her to be bury in the 
vault of st. clare. their solitude and easy access render these 
cavern favourable to your designs. give antonia the soporific draught 
this evening: eight and forty hour after she has drank it, life will 
revive to her bosom. she will then be absolutely in your power: she 
will find all resistance unavailing, and necessity will compel her to 
receive you in her arms.' 

'antonia will be in my power!' exclaim the monk; 'matilda, you 
transport me! at length then, happiness will be mine, and that 
happiness will be matilda's gift, will be the gift of friendship! 

i shall clasp antonia in my arms, far from every pry eye, from every 
torment intruder! i shall sigh out my soul upon her bosom; shall 
teach her young heart the first rudiments of pleasure, and revel 
uncontrouled in the endless variety of her charms! and shall this 
delight indeed by mine? shall i give the reins to my desires, and 
gratify every wild tumultuous wish? oh! matilda, how can i express to 
you my gratitude?' 

'by profit by my counsels. ambrosio, i live but to serve you: 

your interest and happiness are equally mine. be your person antonia's, 
but to your friendship and your heart i still assert my claim. 
contribute to yours form now my only pleasure. should my exertion 
procure the gratification of your wishes, i shall consider my trouble 
to be ample repaid. but let us lose no time. the liquor of which i 
spoke is only to be found in st. clare's laboratory. hasten then to 
the prioress; request of her admission to the laboratory, and it will 
not be denied. there is a closet at the lower end of the great room, 
fill with liquid of different colours and qualities. the bottle in 
question stand by itself upon the third shelf on the left. it 
contain a greenish liquor: fill a small phial with it when you are 
unobserved, and antonia is your own.' 

the monk hesitate not to adopt this infamous plan. his desires, but 
too violent before, had acquire fresh vigour from the sight of 
antonia. as he sat by her bedside, accident had discover to him some 
of those charm which till then had been conceal from him: he found 
them even more perfect, than his ardent imagination had picture them. 
sometimes her white and polish arm was display in arrange the 
pillow: sometimes a sudden movement discover part of her swelling 
bosom: but whereever the new-found charm present itself, there 
rest the friar's glote eyes. scarcely could he master himself 
sufficient to conceal his desire from antonia and her vigilant 
duenna. inflamed by the remembrance of these beauties, he enter into 
matilda's scheme without hesitation. 

no soon were matins over than he bent his course towards the convent 
of st. clare: his arrival threw the whole sisterhood into the utmost 
amazement. the prioress was sensible of the honour done her convent by 
his pay it his first visit, and strove to express her gratitude by 
every possible attention. he was parade through the garden, shown all 
the relique of saint and martyrs, and treat with as much respect 
and distinction as had he been the pope himself. on his part, ambrosio 
received the domina's civility very graciously, and strove to remove 
her surprize at his have broken through his resolution. he stated, 
that among his penitents, illness prevent many from quit their 
houses. these were exactly the people who most need his advice and 
the comfort of religion: many representations had been made to him 
upon this account, and though highly repugnant to his own wishes, he 
had found it absolutely necessary for the service of heaven to change 
his determination, and quit his beloved retirement. the prioress 
applaud his zeal in his profession and his charity towards mankind: 
she declared that madrid was happy in possess a man so perfect and 
irreproachable. in such discourse, the friar at length reach the 
laboratory. he found the closet: the bottle stood in the place which 
matilda had described, and the monk seize an opportunity to fill his 
phial unobserved with the soporific liquor. then have partaken of a 
collation in the refectory, he retired from the convent pleased with 
the success of his visit, and leave the nun delight by the honour 
confer upon them. 

he wait till evening before he took the road to antonia's dwelling. 
jacintha welcome him with transport, and besought him not to forget 
his promise to pass the night in the haunt chamber: that promise he 
now repeated. he found antonia tolerably well, but still harp upon 
the ghost's prediction. flora move not from her lady's bed, and by 
symptom yet strong than on the former night testify her dislike to 
the abbot's presence. still ambrosio affected not to observe them. 
the physician arrived, while he was converse with antonia. it was 
dark already; lights were call for, and flora was compel to 
descend for them herself. however, as she left a third person in the 
room, and expect to be absent but a few minutes, she believe that 
she risque nothing in quit her post. no soon had she left the 
room, than ambrosio move towards the table, on which stood antonia's 
medicine: it was placed in a recess of the window. the physician 
seat in an armed-chair, and employ in questioning his patient, paid 
no attention to the proceedings of the monk. ambrosio seize the 
opportunity: he drew out the fatal phial, and let a few drops fall 
into the medicine. he then hasty left the table, and return to the 
seat which he had quitted. when flora made her appearance with lights, 
every thing seem to be exactly as she had left it. 

the physician declared that antonia might quit her chamber the next day 
with perfect safety. he recommend her following the same 
prescription which, on the night before, had procure her a refreshing 
sleep: flora reply that the draught stood ready upon the table: he 
advise the patient to take it without delay, and then retired. flora 
pour the medicine into a cup and present it to her mistress. at 
that moment ambrosio's courage fail him. might not matilda have 
deceive him? might not jealousy have persuade her to destroy her 
rival, and substitute poison in the room of an opiate? this idea 
appear so reasonable that he was on the point of prevent her from 
swallow the medicine. his resolution was adopt too late: the cup 
was already emptied, and antonia restore it into flora's hands. no 
remedy was now to be found: ambrosio could only expect the moment 
impatiently, destined to decide upon antonia's life or death, upon his 
own happiness or despair. 

dread to create suspicion by his stay, or betray himself by his 
mind's agitation, he took leave of his victim, and withdrew from the 
room. antonia part from him with less cordiality than on the former 
night. flora had represent to her mistress that to admit his visit 
was to disobey her mother's orders: she describe to her his emotion 
on enter the room, and the fire which sparkle in his eyes while he 
gaze upon her. this had escape antonia's observation, but not her 
attendant's; who explain the monk's designs and their probable 
consequence in terms much clear than elvira's, though not quite so 
delicate, had succeed in alarm her young lady, and persuade her 
to treat him more distantly than she had done hitherto. the idea of 
obey her mother's will at once determined antonia. though she 
grieve at lose his society, she conquer herself sufficient to 
receive the monk with some degree of reserve and coldness. she thank 
him with respect and gratitude for his former visits, but did not 
invite his repeating them in future. it now was not the friar's 
interest to solicit admission to her presence, and he took leave of her 
as if not designing to return. fully persuade that the acquaintance 
which she dread was now at an end, flora was so much work upon by 
his easy compliance that she began to doubt the justice of her 
suspicions. as she light him down stairs, she thank him for have 
endeavour to root out from antonia's mind her superstitious terror 
of the spectre's prediction: she added, that as he seem interested 
in donna antonia's welfare, should any change take place in her 
situation, she would be careful to let him know it. the monk in 
reply took pains to raise his voice, hope that jacintha would hear 
it. in this he succeeded; as he reach the foot of the stairs with 
his conductress, the landlady fail not to make her appearance. 

'why surely you are not go away, reverend father?' cry she; 'did 
you not promise to pass the night in the haunt chamber? christ jesus! 
i shall be left alone with the ghost, and a fine pickle i shall be in 
by morning! do all i could, say all i could, that obstinate old brute,
