simon gonzalez, refuse to marry me today; and before tomorrow comes, i 
suppose, i shall be torn to pieces, by the ghosts, and goblins, and 
devils, and what not! for god's sake, your holiness, do not leave me in 
such a woeful condition! on my bended knee i beseech you to keep your 
promise: watch this night in the haunt chamber; lay the apparition 
in the red sea, and jacintha remember you in her prayer to the last 
day of her existence!' 

this request ambrosio expect and desired; yet he affected to raise 
objections, and to seem unwill to keep his word. he told jacintha 
that the ghost exist nowhere but in her own brain, and that her 
insist upon his stay all night in the house was ridiculous and 
useless. jacintha was obstinate: she was not to be convinced, and 
pressed him so urgent not to leave her a prey to the devil, that at 
length he grant her request. all this show of resistance impose not 
upon flora, who was naturally of a suspicious temper. she suspect 
the monk to be acting a part very contrary to his own inclinations, and 
that he wish for no better than to remain where he was. she even 
went so far as to believe that jacintha was in his interest; and the 
poor old woman was immediately set down, as no better than a procuress. 
while she applaud herself for have penetrate into this plot 
against her lady's honour, she resolve in secret to render it 
fruitless. 

'so then,' said she to the abbot with a look half-satirical and half 
indignant; 'so then you mean to stay here tonight? do so, in god's 
name! nobody will prevent you. sit up to watch for the ghost's 
arrival: i shall sit up too, and the lord grant that i may see nothing 
worse than a ghost! i quit not donna antonia's bedside during this 
blessed night: let me see any one dare to enter the room, and be he 
mortal or immortal, be he ghost, devil, or man, i warrant his repent 
that ever he cross the threshold!' 

this hint was sufficient strong, and ambrosio understood its meaning. 
but instead of showing that he perceive her suspicions; he reply 
mildly that he approve the duenna's precautions, and advise her to 
persevere in her intention. this, she assured him faithfully that he 
might depend upon her doing. jacintha then conduct him into the 
chamber where the ghost had appeared, and flora return to her lady's. 

jacintha open the door of the haunt room with a tremble hand: 
she venture to peep in; but the wealth of india would not have tempt 
her to cross the threshold. she gave the taper to the monk, wish him 
well through the adventure, and hasten to be gone. ambrosio entered. 
he bolt the door, placed the light upon the table, and seat himself 
in the chair which on the former night had sustain antonia. in spite 
of matilda's assurance that the spectre was a mere creation of fancy, 
his mind was impress with a certain mysterious horror. he in vain 
endeavour to shake it off. the silence of the night, the story of 
the apparition, the chamber wainscot with dark oak pannells, the 
recollection which it brought with it of the murder elvira, and his 
incertitude respecting the nature of the drops given by him to antonia, 
made him feel uneasy at his present situation. but he thought much 
less of the spectre, than of the poison. should he have destroy the 
only object which render life dear to him; should the ghost's 
prediction prove true; should antonia in three days be no more, and he 
the wretched cause of her death ...... the supposition was too 
horrible to dwell upon. he drove away these dreadful images, and as 
often they present themselves again before him. matilda had assured 
him that the effects of the opiate would be speedy. he listen with 
fear, yet with eagerness, expect to hear some disturbance in the 
adjoin chamber. all was still silent. he conclude that the drops 
had not begun to operate. great was the stake, for which he now 
played: a moment would suffice to decide upon his misery or happiness. 
matilda had taught him the means of ascertain that life was not 
extinct for ever: upon this assay depend all his hopes. with every 
instant his impatience redoubled; his terror grew more lively, his 
anxiety more awake. unable to bear this state of incertitude, he 
endeavour to divert it by substitute the thought of other to his 
own. the books, as was before mentioned, were range upon shelves near 
the table: this stood exactly opposite to the bed, which was placed in 
an alcove near the closet door. ambrosio took down a volume, and 
seat himself by the table: but his attention wander from the page 
before him. antonia's image and that of the murder elvira persist 
to force themselves before his imagination. still he continue to 
read, though his eyes ran over the character without his mind being 
conscious of their import. such was his occupation, when he fancy 
that he heard a footstep. he turn his head, but nobody was to be 
seen. 

he resume his book; but in a few minutes after the same sound was 
repeated, and follow by a rustling noise close behind him. he now 
start from his seat, and look round him, perceive the closet door 
standing half-unclosed. on his first enter the room he had try to 
open it, but found it bolt on the inside. 

'how is this?' said he to himself; 'how come this door unfastened?' 

he advanced towards it: he pushed it open, and look into the closet: 
no one was there. while he stood irresolute, he thought that he 
distinguished a groan in the adjacent chamber: it was antonia's, 
and he supposed that the drops began to take effect: but upon 
listen more attentively, he found the noise to be cause by 
jacintha, who had fallen asleep by the lady's bedside, and was snore 
most lustily. ambrosio drew back, and return to the other room, 
muse upon the sudden opening of the closet door, for which he strove 
in vain to account. 

he pace the chamber up and down in silence. at length he stopped, and 
the bed attract his attention. the curtain of the recess was but 
half-drawn. he sigh involuntarily. 

'that bed,' said he in a low voice, 'that bed was elvira's! there has 
she past many a quiet night, for she was good and innocent. how sound 
must have been her sleep! and yet now she sleep sounder! do she 
indeed sleep? oh! god grant that she may! what if she rose from her 
grave at this sad and silent hour? what if she broke the bonds of the 
tomb, and glide angry before my blasted eyes? oh! i never could 
support the sight! again to see her form distort by die agonies, 
her blood-swollen veins, her livid countenance, her eyes burst from 
their socket with pain! to hear her speak of future punishment, 
menace me with heaven's vengeance, tax me with the crime i have 
committed, with those i am go to commit ..... great god! what is 
that?' 

as he utter these words, his eyes which were fixed upon the bed, saw 
the curtain shaken gently backwards and forwards. the apparition was 
recall to his mind, and he almost fancy that he beheld elvira's 
visionary form recline upon the bed. a few moments consideration 
suffice to reassure him. 

'it was only the wind,' said he, recover himself. 

again he pace the chamber; but an involuntary movement of awe and 
inquietude constant led his eye towards the alcove. he drew near it 
with irresolution. he pause before he ascend the few steps which led 
to it. he put out his hand thrice to remove the curtain, and as often 
drew it back. 

'absurd terrors!' he cry at length, ashamed of his own weakness---- 

hasty he mount the steps; when a figure drest in white start from 
the alcove, and gliding by him, made with precipitation towards the 
closet. madness and despair now supply the monk with that courage, 
of which he had till then been destitute. he flew down the steps, 
pursue the apparition, and attempt to grasp it. 

'ghost, or devil, i hold you!' he exclaimed, and seize the spectre by 
the arm. 

'oh! christ jesus!' cry a shrill voice; 'holy father, how you gripe 
me! i protest that i meant no harm!' 

this address, as well as the arm which he held, convinced the abbot 
that the supposed ghost was substantial flesh and blood. he drew the 
intruder towards the table, and holding up the light, discover the 
features of ...... madona flora! 

incense at have been betray by this trifling cause into fear so 
ridiculous, he ask her sternly, what business had brought her to that 
chamber. flora, ashamed at being found out, and terrify at the 
severity of ambrosio's looks, fell upon her knees, and promise to make 
a full confession. 

'i protest, reverend father,' said she, 'that i am quite grieve at 
have disturbed you: nothing was further from my intention. i meant 
to get out of the room as quiet as i got in; and had you been 
ignorant that i watch you, you know, it would have been the same 
thing as if i had not watch you at all. to be sure, i did very wrong 
in being a spy upon you, that i cannot deny; but lord! your reverence, 
how can a poor weak woman resist curiosity? mine was so strong to know 
what you were doing, that i could not but try to get a little peep, 
without any body knowing any thing about it. so with that i left old 
dame jacintha sitting by my lady's bed, and i venture to steal into 
the closet. being unwill to interrupt you, i contented myself at 
first with putt my eye to the keyhole; but as i could see nothing by 
this means, i undrew the bolt, and while your back was turn to the 
alcove, i whipt me in soft and silently. here i lay snug behind the 
curtain, till your reverence found me out, and seize me ere i had time 
to regain the closet door. this is the whole truth, i assure you, holy 
father, and i beg your pardon a thousand times for my impertinence.' 

during this speech the abbot had time to recollect himself: he was 
satisfy with reading the penitent spy a lecture upon the danger of 
curiosity, and the mean of the action in which she had been just 
discovered. flora declared herself fully persuade that she had done 
wrong; she promise never to be guilty of the same fault again, and was 
retiring very humble and contrite to antonia's chamber, when the closet 
door was sudden thrown open, and in rush jacintha pale and out of 
breath. 

'oh! father! father!' she cry in a voice almost choake with 
terror; 'what shall i do! what shall i do! here is a fine piece of 
work! nothing but misfortunes! nothing but dead people, and die 
people! oh! i shall go distracted! i shall go distracted!' 

'speak! speak!' cry flora and the monk at the same time; 'what has 
happened? what is the matter?' 

'oh! i shall have another corse in my house! some witch has certainly 
cast a spell upon it, upon me, and upon all about me! poor donna 
antonia! there she lie in just such convulsions, as kill her 
mother! the ghost told her true! i am sure, the ghost has told her 
true!' 

flora ran, or rather flew to her lady's chamber: ambrosio follow her, 
his bosom tremble with hope and apprehension. they found antonia as 
jacintha had described, torn by rack convulsion from which they in 
vain endeavour to relieve her. the monk dispatch jacintha to the 
abbey in all haste, and commission her to bring father pablo back 
with her, without lose a moment. 

'i will go for him,' reply jacintha, 'and tell him to come hither; 
but as to bring him myself, i shall do no such thing. i am sure that 
the house is bewitched, and burn me if ever i set foot in it again.' 

with this resolution she set out for the monastery, and deliver to 
father pablo the abbot's orders. she then betook herself to the house 
of old simon gonzalez, whom she resolve never to quit, till she had 
made him her husband, and his dwelling her own. 

father pablo had no soon beheld antonia, than he pronounced her 
incurable. the convulsion continue for an hour: during that time 
her agony were much mild than those which her groan create in the 
abbot's heart. her every pang seem a dagger in his bosom, and he 
cursed himself a thousand times for have adopt so barbarous a 
project. the hour being expired, by degree the fit became less 
frequent, and antonia less agitated. she felt that her dissolution was 
approaching, and that nothing could save her. 

'worthy ambrosio,' she said in a feeble voice, while she pressed his 
hand to her lips; 'i am now at liberty to express, how grateful is my 
heart for your attention and kindness. i am upon the bed of death; yet 
an hour, and i shall be no more. i may therefore acknowledge without 
restraint, that to relinquish your society was very painful to me: but 
such was the will of a parent, and i dare not disobey. i die without 
repugnance: there are few, who will lament my leave them; there are 
few, whom i lament to leave. among those few, i lament for none more 
than for yourself; but we shall meet again, ambrosio! we shall one day 
meet in heaven: there shall our friendship be renewed, and my mother 
shall view it with pleasure!' 

she paused. the abbot shudder when she mention elvira: antonia 
impute his emotion to pity and concern for her. 

'you are grieve for me, father,' she continued; 'ah! sigh not for my 
loss. i have no crime to repent, at least none of which i am 
conscious, and i restore my soul without fear to him from whom i 
received it. i have but few request to make: yet let me hope that 
what few i have shall be granted. let a solemn mass be said for my 
soul's repose, and another for that of my beloved mother. not that i 
doubt her rest in her grave: i am now convinced that my reason 
wandered, and the falsehood of the ghost's prediction is sufficient to 
prove my error. but every one has some failing: my mother may have 
had hers, though i knew them not: i therefore wish a mass to be 
celebrated for her repose, and the expence may be defray by the 
little wealth of which i am possessed. whatever may then remain, i 
bequeath to my aunt leonella. when i am dead, let the marquis de las 
cisterna know that his brother's unhappy family can no long 
importune him. but disappointment make me unjust: they tell me that 
he is ill, and perhaps had it been in his power, he wish to have 
protect me. tell him then, father, only that i am dead, and that if 
he had any fault to me, i forgave him from my heart. this done, i have 
nothing more to ask for, than your prayers: promise to remember my 
requests, and i shall resign my life without a pang or sorrow.' 

ambrosio engaged to comply with her desires, and proceed to give her 
absolution. every moment announce the approach of antonia's fate: 
her sight failed; her heart beat sluggishly; her finger stiffened, and 
grew cold, and at two in the morning she expire without a groan. as 
soon as the breath had forsaken her body, father pablo retired, 
sincerely affected at the melancholy scene. on her part, flora gave 
way to the most unbridled sorrow. 

far different concern employ ambrosio: he sought for the pulse 
whose throbbing, so matilda had assured him, would prove antonia's 
death but temporal. he found it; he pressed it; it palpitate beneath 
his hand, and his heart was fill with ecstacy. however, he careful 
conceal his satisfaction at the success of his plan. he assume a 
melancholy air, and address himself to flora, warn her against 
abandon herself to fruitless sorrow. her tear were too sincere to 
permit her listen to his counsels, and she continue to weep 
unceasingly. 

the friar withdrew, first promising to give orders himself about the 
funeral, which, out of consideration for jacintha as he pretended, 
should take place with all expedition. plunge in grief for the loss 
of her beloved mistress, flora scarcely attend to what he said. 
ambrosio hasten to command the burial. he obtain permission from 
the prioress, that the corse should be deposit in st. clare's 
sepulchre: and on the friday morning, every proper and needful ceremony 
being performed, antonia's body was committed to the tomb. 

on the same day leonella arrive at madrid, intend to present her 
young husband to elvira. various circumstances had oblige her to 
defer her journey from tuesday to friday, and she had no opportunity of 
making this alteration in her plan known to her sister. as her heart 
was truly affectionate, and as she had ever entertain a sincere 
regard for elvira and her daughter, her surprize at hearing of their 
sudden and melancholy fate was fully equal by her sorrow and 
disappointment. ambrosio sent to inform her of antonia's bequest: at 
her solication, he promised, as soon as elvira's trifling debt were 
discharged, to transmit to her the remainder. this being settled, no 
other business detain leonella in madrid, and she return to cordova 
with all diligence. 



chapter iii 

oh! could i worship aught beneath the sky 
that earth hath seen or fancy could devise, 
thine altar, sacred liberty, should stand, 
built by no mercenary vulgar hand, 
with fragrant turf, and flower as wild and fair, 
as ever dress a bank, or scent summer air. 
cowper. 

his whole attention bent upon bring to justice the assassin of his 
sister, lorenzo little thought how severe his interest was suffering 
in another quarter. as was before mentioned, he return not to madrid 
till the evening of that day on which antonia was buried. signify to 
the grand inquisitor the order of the cardinal-duke (a ceremony not to 
be neglected, when a member of the church was to be arrest publicly) 
communicate his design to his uncle and don ramirez, and assemble a 
troop of attendant sufficient to prevent opposition, furnish him 
with full occupation during the few hour preceding midnight. 
consequently, he had no opportunity to enquire about his mistress, and 
was perfectly ignorant both of her death and her mother's. 

the marquis was by no means out of danger: his delirium was gone, but 
had left him so much exhaust that the physician decline pronounce 
upon the consequence likely to ensue. as for raymond himself, he 
wish for nothing more earnest than to join agnes in the grave. 
existence was hateful to him: he saw nothing in the world deserving 
his attention; and he hope to hear that agnes was revenged, and 
himself given over in the same moment. 

follow by raymond's ardent prayer for success, lorenzo was at the 
gate of st. clare a full hour before the time appoint by the mother 
st. ursula. he was accompany by his uncle, by don ramirez de mello, 
and a party of chosen archers. though in considerable numbers their 
appearance create no surprize: a great crowd was already assemble 
before the convent doors, in order to witness the procession. it was 
naturally supposed that lorenzo and his attendant were conduct 
thither by the same design. the duke of medina being recognised, the 
people drew back, and made way for his party to advance. lorenzo 
placed himself opposite to the great gate, through which the pilgrim 
were to pass. convinced that the prioress could not escape him, he 
wait patient for her appearance, which she was expect to make 
exactly at midnight. 

the nun were employ in religious duty establish in honour of st. 
clare, and to which no prophane was ever admitted. the chapel window 
were illuminated. as they stood on the outside, the auditor heard the 
full swell of the organ, accompany by a chorus of female voices, rise 
upon the still of the night. this die away, and was succeed by a 
single strain of harmony: it was the voice of her who was destined to 
sustain in the procession the character of st. clare. for this office 
the most beautiful virgin of madrid was always selected, and she upon 
whom the choice fell esteem it as the highest of honours. while 
listen to the music, whose melody distance only seem to render 
sweeter, the audience was wrap up in profound attention. universal 
silence prevail through the crowd, and every heart was fill with 
reverence for religion. every heart but lorenzo's. conscious that 
among those who chaunte the praises of their god so sweetly, there 
were some who cloak with devotion the foulest sins, their hymn 
inspired him with detest at their hypocrisy. he had long observe 
with disapprobation and contempt the superstition which govern 
madrid's inhabitants. his good sense had pointed out to him the 
artifice of the monks, and the gross absurd of their miracles, 
wonders, and supposititious reliques. he blush to see his countrymen 
the dupe of deception so ridiculous, and only wish for an 
opportunity to free them from their monkish fetters. that opportunity, 
so long desire in vain, was at length present to him. he resolve 
not to let it slip, but to set before the people in glaring colours how 
enormous were the abuse but too frequent practised in monasteries, 
and how unjust public esteem was bestow indiscriminate upon all 
who wore a religious habit. he long for the moment destined to 
unmask the hypocrites, and convince his countrymen that a sanctify 
exterior do not always hide a virtuous heart. 

the service lasted, till midnight was announce by the convent bell. 
that sound being heard, the music ceased: the voice die away softly, 
and soon after the lights disappear from the chapel windows. 
lorenzo's heart beat high, when he found the execution of his plan to 
be at hand. from the natural superstition of the people he had 
prepared himself for some resistance. but he trust that the mother 
st. ursula would bring good reason to justify his proceeding. he had 
force with him to repel the first impulse of the populace, till his 
argument should be heard: his only fear was lest the domina, 
suspect his design, should have spirited away the nun on whose 
deposition every thing depended. unless the mother st. ursula should 
be present, he could only accuse the prioress upon suspicion; and this 
reflection gave him some little apprehension for the success of his 
enterprize. the tranquill which seem to reign through the convent 
in some degree re-assured him: still he expect the moment eagerly, 
when the presence of his ally should deprive him of the power of 
doubting. 

the abbey of capuchin was only separate from the convent by the 
garden and cemetery. the monk had been invite to assist at the 
pilgrimage. they now arrived, march two by two with light torch 
in their hands, and chaunt hymn in honour of st. clare. father 
pablo was at their head, the abbot have excuse himself from 
attending. the people made way for the holy train, and the friar 
placed themselves in ranks on either side of the great gates. a few 
minutes suffice to arrange the order of the procession. this being 
settled, the convent door were thrown open, and again the female 
chorus sound in full melody. first appear a band of choristers: 
as soon as they had passed, the monk fell in two by two, and follow 
with steps slow and measured. next came the novices; they bore no 
tapers, as did the professed, but move on with eyes bent downwards, 
and seem to be occupy by telling their beads. to them succeed a 
young and lovely girl, who represent st. lucia: she held a golden 
bason in which were two eyes: her own were cover by a velvet 
bandage, and she was conduct by another nun habit as an angel. she 
was follow by st. catherine, a palm-branch in one hand, a flaming 
sword in the other: she was robe in white, and her brow was 
ornament with a sparkle diadem. after her appear st. genevieve, 
surround by a number of imps, who putt themselves into grotesque 
attitudes, drawing her by the robe, and sporting round her with antic 
gestures, endeavour to distract her attention from the book, on which 
her eyes were constant fixed. these merry devil greatly entertain 
the spectators, who testify their pleasure by repeated burst of 
laughter. the prioress had been careful to select a nun whose 
disposition was naturally solemn and saturnine. she had every reason 
to be satisfy with her choice: the drollery of the imp were 
entire thrown away, and st. genevieve move on without discompose a 
muscle. 

each of these saint was separate from the other by a band of 
choristers, exalt her praise in their hymns, but declare her to be 
very much inferior to st. clare, the convent's avowed patroness. these 
have passed, a long train of nun appeared, bearing like the 
chorister each a burning taper. next came the relique of st. clare, 
inclose in vase equally precious for their material and workmanship: 
but they attract not lorenzo's attention. the nun who bore the heart 
occupy him entirely. according to theodore's description, he doubt 
not her being the mother st. ursula. she seem to look round with 
anxiety. as he stood foremost in the rank by which the procession 
past, her eye caught lorenzo's. a flush of joy overspread her till 
then pallid cheek. she turn to her companion eagerly. 

'we are safe!' he heard her whisper; ''tis her brother!' 

his heart being now at ease, lorenzo gaze with tranquill upon the 
remainder of the show. now appear its most brilliant ornament. it 
was a machine fashion like a throne, rich with jewel and dazzle 
with light. it roll onwards upon conceal wheels, and was guide by 
several lovely children, dress as seraphs. the summit was cover 
with silver clouds, upon which recline the most beautiful form that 
eyes ever witnessed. it was a damsel represent st. clare: her dress 
was of inestimable price, and round her head a wreath of diamond 
form an artificial glory: but all these ornament yield to the 
lustre of her charms. as she advanced, a murmur of delight ran through 
the crowd. even lorenzo confessed secretly, that he never beheld more 
perfect beauty, and had not his heart been antonia's, it must have 
fallen a sacrifice to this enchanting girl. as it was, he considered 
her only as a fine statue: she obtain from him no tribute save cold 
admiration, and when she had pass him, he thought of her no more. 

'who is she?' ask a by-stander in lorenzo's hearing. 

'one whose beauty you must often have heard celebrated. her name is 
virginia de villa-franca: she is a pensioner of st. clare's convent, a 
relation of the prioress, and has been select with justice as the 
ornament of the procession.' 

the throne move onwards. it was follow by the prioress herself: 
she march at the head of the remain nun with a devout and 
sanctify air, and closed the procession. she move on slowly: her 
eyes were raise to heaven: her countenance calm and tranquil seem 
abstracted from all sublunary things, and no feature betray her 
secret pride at display the pomp and opulence of her convent. she 
pass along, accompany by the prayer and benediction of the 
populace: but how great was the general confusion and surprize, when 
don ramirez start forward, challenge her as his prisoner. 

for a moment amaze held the domina silent and immoveable: but no 
soon did she recover herself, than she exclaim against sacrilege 
and impiety, and call the people to rescue a daughter of the church. 
they were eager prepare to obey her; when don ramirez, protect by 
the archer from their rage, command them to forbear, and threaten 
them with the severest vengeance of the inquisition. at that dread 
word every arm fell, every sword shrunk back into its scabbard. the 
prioress herself turn pale, and trembled. the general silence 
convinced her that she had nothing to hope but from innocence, and she 
besought don ramirez in a faultere voice, to inform her of what crime 
she was accused. 

'that you shall know in time,' reply he; 'but first i must secure the 
mother st. ursula.' 

'the mother st. ursula?' repeated the domina faintly. 

at this moment casting her eyes round, she saw near her lorenzo and the 
duke, who had follow don ramirez. 

'ah! great god!' she cried, clasp her hand together with a frantic 
air; 'i am betrayed!' 

'betrayed?' reply st. ursula, who now arrive conduct by some of 
the archers, and follow by the nun her companion in the procession: 
'not betrayed, but discovered. in me recognise your accuser: you know 
not how well i am instruct in your guilt!--segnor!' she continued, 
turning to don ramirez; 'i commit myself to your custody. i charge the 
prioress of st. clare with murder, and stake my life for the justice of 
my accusation.' 

a general cry of surprize was utter by the whole audience, and an 
explanation was demand loudly. the tremble nuns, terrify at the 
noise and universal confusion, had dispersed, and flee different ways. 
some regain the convent; other sought refuge in the dwelling of 
their relations; and many, only sensible of their present danger, and 
anxious to escape from the tumult, ran through the streets, and 
wandered, they knew not whither. the lovely virginia was one of the 
first to fly: and in order that she might be better seen and heard, 
the people desire that st. ursula should harangue them from the vacant 
throne. the nun complied; she ascend the glittering machine, and 
then address the surrounding multitude as follows. 

'however strange and unseemly may appear my conduct, when considered to 
be adopt by a female and a nun, necessity will justify it most fully. 
a secret, an horrible secret weigh heavy upon my soul: no rest can be 
mine till i have reveal it to the world, and satisfy that innocent 
blood which call from the grave for vengeance. much have i dare to 
gain this opportunity of lighten my conscience. had i fail in my 
attempt to reveal the crime, had the domina but suspect that the 
mystery was none to me, my ruin was inevitable. angel who watch 
unceasing over those who deserve their favour, have enable me to 
escape detection: i am now at liberty to relate a tale, whose 
circumstances will freeze every honest soul with horror. mine is the 
task to rend the veil from hypocrisy, and show misguided parent to 
what danger the woman is exposed, who falls under the sway of a 
monastic tyrant. 

'among the votary of st. clare, none was more lovely, none more 
gentle, than agnes de medina. i knew her well; she entrust to me 
every secret of her heart; i was her friend and confident, and i love 
her with sincere affection. nor was i singular in my attachment. her 
piety unfeigned, her willing to oblige, and her angel 
disposition, render her the darling of all that was estimable in the 
convent. the prioress herself, proud, scrupulous and forbidding, could 
not refuse agnes that tribute of approbation which she bestow upon no 
one else. every one has some fault: alas! agnes had her weakness! 
she violate the law of our order, and incur the inveterate hate of 
the unforgive domina. st. clare's rule are severe: but grown 
antiquated and neglected, many of late years have either been 
forgotten, or change by universal consent into mild punishments. 
the penance, adjudge to the crime of agnes, was most cruel, most 
inhuman! the law had been long exploded: alas! it still existed, and 
the revengeful prioress now determined to revive it. 

this law decree that the offender should be plunge into a private 
dungeon, expressly constitute to hide from the world for ever the 
victim of cruelty and tyrannical superstition. in this dreadful abode 
she was to lead a perpetual solitude, deprived of all society, and 
believe to be dead by those whom affection might have prompt to 
attempt her rescue. thus was she to languish out the remainder of her 
days, with no other food than bread and water, and no other comfort 
than the free indulgence of her tears.' 

the indignation create by this account was so violent, as for some 
moments to interrupt st. ursula's narrative. when the disturbance 
ceased, and silence again prevail through the assembly, she continue 
her discourse, while at every word the domina's countenance betray 
her increase terrors. 

'a council of the twelve elder nun was called: i was of the number. 
the prioress in exaggerate colours describe the offence of agnes, and 
scruple not to propose the revival of this almost forgotten law. to 
the shame of our sex be it spoken, that either so absolute was the 
domina's will in the convent, or so much had disappointment, solitude, 
and self-denial harden their heart and sowere their temper that 
this barbarous proposal was assent to by nine voice out of the 
twelve. i was not one of the nine. frequent opportunity had 
convinced me of the virtue of agnes, and i love and pity her most 
sincerely. the mother bertha and cornelia join my party: we made 
the strongest opposition possible, and the superior found herself 
compel to change her intention. in spite of the majority in her 
favour, she fear to break with us openly. she knew that support by 
the medina family, our forces would be too strong for her to cope with: 
and she also knew that after being once imprison and supposed dead, 
should agnes be discovered, her ruin would be inevitable. she 
therefore gave up her design, though which much reluctance. she 
demand some days to reflect upon a mode of punishment which might be 
agreeable to the whole community; and she promised, that as soon as her 
resolution was fixed, the same council should be again summoned. two 
days pass away: on the evening of the third it was announce that on 
the next day agnes should be examined; and that according to her 
behaviour on that occasion, her punishment should be either 
strengthen or mitigated. 

'on the night preceding this examination, i stole to the cell of agnes 
at an hour when i supposed the other nun to be bury in sleep. i 
comfort her to the best of my power: i bad her take courage, told 
her to rely upon the support of her friends, and taught her certain 
signs, by which i might instruct her to answer the domina's question 
by an assent or negative. conscious that her enemy would strive to 
confuse, embarrass, and daunt her, i fear her being ensnare into 
some confession prejudicial to her interests. being anxious to keep my 
visit secret, i stay with agnes but a short time. i bad her not let 
her spirits be cast down; i mingle my tear with those which stream 
down her cheek, embrace her fondly, and was on the point of retiring, 
when i heard the sound of steps approach the cell. i start back. 
a curtain which veiled a large crucifix offer me a retreat, and i 
hasten to place myself behind it. the door opened. the prioress 
entered, follow by four other nuns. they advanced towards the bed of 
agnes. the superior reproach her with her error in the bitterest 
terms: she told her that she was a disgrace to the convent, that she 
was resolve to deliver the world and herself from such a monster, and 
command her to drink the contents of a goblet now present to her by 
one of the nuns. aware of the fatal property of the liquor, and 
tremble to find herself upon the brink of eternity, the unhappy girl 
strove to excite the domina's pity by the most affecting prayers. 

she sue for life in terms which might have melt the heart of a 
fiend: she promise to submit patient to any punishment, to shame, 
imprisonment, and torture, might she but be permit to live! oh! 
might she but live another month, or week, or day! her merciless enemy 
listen to her complaint unmoved: she told her that at first she 
meant to have spare her life, and that if she had alter her 
intention, she had to thank the opposition of her friends. she 
continue to insist upon her swallow the poison: she bad her 
recommend herself to the almighty's mercy, not to hers, and assured her 
that in an hour she would be number with the dead. perceive that 
it was vain to implore this unfeeling woman, she attempt to spring 
from her bed, and call for assistance: she hoped, if she could not 
escape the fate announce to her, at least to have witness of the 
violence committed. the prioress guess her design. she seize her 
forcibly by the arm, and pushed her back upon her pillow. at the same 
time drawing a dagger, and place it at the breast of the unfortunate 
agnes, she protest that if she utter a single cry, or hesitate a 
single moment to drink the poison, she would pierce her heart that 
instant. already half-dead with fear, she could make no further 
resistance. the nun approach with the fatal goblet. the domina 
oblige her to take it, and swallow the contents. she drank, and the 
horrid deed was accomplished. the nun then seat themselves round 
the bed. they answer her groan with reproaches; they interrupt 
with sarcasm the prayer in which she recommend her parting soul to 
mercy: they threaten her with heaven's vengeance and eternal 
perdition: they bad her despair of pardon, and strowe with yet sharper 
thorn death's painful pillow. such were the suffering of this young 
unfortunate, till release by fate from the malice of her tormentors. 
she expire in horror of the past, in fear for the future; and her 
agony were such as must have ample gratify the hate and vengeance 
of her enemies. as soon as her victim cease to breathe, the domina 
retired, and was follow by her accomplices. 

'it was now that i venture from my concealment. i dare not to assist 
my unhappy friend, aware that without preserve her, i should only 
have brought on myself the same destruction. shock and terrify 
beyond expression at this horrid scene, scarcely had i sufficient 
strength to regain my cell. as i reach the door of that of agnes, i 
venture to look towards the bed, on which lay her lifeless body, once 
so lovely and so sweet! i breathe a prayer for her departed spirit, 
and vow to revenge her death by the shame and punishment of her 
assassins. with danger and difficulty have i kept my oath. i unwari 
drop some words at the funeral of agnes, while thrown off my guard 
by excessive grief, which alarm the guilty conscience of the 
prioress. my every action was observed; my every step was traced. i 
was constant surround by the superior's spies. it was long before 
i could find the means of convey to the unhappy girl's relations an 
intimate of my secret. it was given out that agnes had expire 
suddenly: this account was credit not only by her friends in madrid, 
but even by those within the convent. the poison had left no mark 
upon her body: no one suspect the true cause of her death, and it 
remain unknown to all, save the assassin and myself. 

'i have no more to say: for what i have already said, i will answer 
with my life. i repeat that the prioress is a murderess; that she has 
driven from the world, perhaps from heaven, an unfortunate whose 
offence was light and venial; that she has abuse the power intrust 
to her hands, and has been a tyrant, a barbarian, and an hypocrite. i 
also accuse the four nuns, violante, camilla, alix, and mariana, as 
being her accomplices, and equally criminal.' 

here st. ursula end her narrative. it create horror and surprize 
throughout: but when she related the inhuman murder of agnes, the 
indignation of the mob was so audible testified, that it was scarcely 
possible to hear the conclusion. this confusion increase with every 
moment: at length a multitude of voice exclaim that the prioress 
should be given up to their fury. to this don ramirez refuse to 
consent positively. even lorenzo bad the people remember that she had 
undergone no trial, and advise them to leave her punishment to the 
inquisition. all representations were fruitless: the disturbance grew 
still more violent, and the populace more exasperated. in vain did 
ramirez attempt to convey his prisoner out of the throng. wherever he 
turned, a band of riot barred his passage, and demand her being 
deliver over to them more loud than before. ramirez ordered his 
attendant to cut their way through the multitude: oppress by 
numbers, it was impossible for them to draw their swords. he 
threaten the mob with the vengeance of the inquisition: but in this 
moment of popular phrenzy even this dreadful name had lost its effect. 
though regret for his sister made him look upon the prioress with 
abhorrence, lorenzo could not help pity a woman in a situation so 
terrible: but in spite of all his exertions, and those of the duke, of 
don ramirez, and the archers, the people continue to press onwards. 
they forced a passage through the guard who protect their destined 
victim, drag her from her shelter, and proceed to take upon her a 
most summary and cruel vengeance. wild with terror, and scarcely 
knowing what she said, the wretched woman shriek for a moment's 
mercy: she protest that she was innocent of the death of agnes, and 
could clear herself from the suspicion beyond the power of doubt. the 
riot heed nothing but the gratification of their barbarous 
vengeance. they refuse to listen to her: they show her every sort 
of insult, loaded her with mud and filth, and call her by the most 
opprobrious appellations. they tore her one from another, and each new 
tormentor was more savage than the former. they stifle with howl and 
execrate her shrill cries for mercy; and drag her through the 
streets, spurn her, trample her, and treat her with every 
species of cruelty which hate or vindictive fury could invent. at 
length a flint, aim by some well-directing hand, struck her full upon 
the temple. she sank upon the ground bath in blood, and in a few 
minutes terminate her miserable existence. yet though she no long 
felt their insults, the riot still exercise their impotent rage 
upon her lifeless body. they beat it, trod upon it, and ill-used it, 
till it became no more than a mass of flesh, unsightly, shapeless, and 
disgusting. 

unable to prevent this shocking event, lorenzo and his friends had 
beheld it with the utmost horror: but they were rouze from their 
compel inactivity, on hearing that the mob was attack the convent 
of st. clare. the incense populace, confound the innocent with the 
guilty, had resolve to sacrifice all the nun of that order to their 
rage, and not to leave one stone of the building upon another. alarm 
at this intelligence, they hasten to the convent, resolve to defend 
it if possible, or at least to rescue the inhabitant from the fury of 
the rioters. most of the nun had fled, but a few still remain in 
their habitation. their situation was truly dangerous. however, as 
they had taken the precaution of fastening the inner gates, with this 
assistance lorenzo hope to repel the mob, till don ramirez should 
return to him with a more sufficient force. 

have been conduct by the former disturbance to the distance of some 
street from the convent, he did not immediately reach it: when he 
arrived, the throng surrounding it was so excessive as to prevent his 
approach the gates. in the interim, the populace besiege the 
building with persevering rage: they batter the walls, threw light 
torch in at the windows, and swore that by break of day not a nun of 
st. clare's order should be left alive. lorenzo had just succeed in 
piercing his way through the crowd, when one of the gate was forced 
open. the riot pour into the interior part of the building, where 
they exercise their vengeance upon every thing which found itself in 
their passage. they broke the furniture into pieces, tore down the 
pictures, destroy the reliques, and in their hatred of her servant 
forgot all respect to the saint. some employ themselves in searching 
out the nuns, other in pull down parts of the convent, and other 
again in setting fire to the pictures and valuable furniture which it 
contained. these latter produce the most decisive desolation: indeed 
the consequence of their action were more sudden than themselves had 
expect or wished. the flame rising from the burning piles caught 
part of the building, which being old and dry, the conflagration spread 
with rapid from room to room. the wall were soon shaken by the 
devour element: the column gave way: the roof came tumble down 
upon the rioters, and crush many of them beneath their weight. 
nothing was to be heard but shriek and groans; the convent was wrap 
in flames, and the whole present a scene of devastate and horror. 

lorenzo was shock at have been the cause, however innocent, of this 
frightful disturbance: he endeavour to repair his fault by 
protect the helpless inhabitant of the convent. he enter it with 
the mob, and exert himself to repress the prevailing fury, till the 
sudden and alarm progress of the flame compel him to provide for 
his own safety. the people now hurried out, as eager as they had 
before throng in; but their numbers clog up the doorway, and the 
fire gain upon them rapidly, many of them perish ere they had time 
to effect their escape. lorenzo's good fortune direct him to a small 
door in a farther aisle of the chapel. the bolt was already undrawn: 
he open the door, and found himself at the foot of st. clare's 
sepulchre. 

here he stop to breathe. the duke and some of his attendant had 
follow him, and thus were in security for the present. they now 
consulted, what steps they should take to escape from this scene of 
disturbance: but their deliberation were considerably interrupt by 
the sight of volumes of fire rising from amidst the convent's massy 
walls, by the noise of some heavy arch tumble down in ruins, or by 
the mingle shriek of the nun and rioters, either suffocate in the 
press, perishing in the flames, or crush beneath the weight of the 
fall mansion. 

lorenzo enquired, whither the wicket led? he was answered, to the 
garden of the capuchins, and it was resolve to explore an outlet upon 
that side. accordingly the duke raise the latch, and pass into the 
adjoin cemetery. the attendant follow without ceremony. 
lorenzo, being the last, was also on the point of quit the 
colonnade, when he saw the door of the sepulchre open softly. 
someone look out, but on perceive stranger utter a loud shriek, 
start back again, and flew down the marble stairs. 

'what can this mean?' cry lorenzo; 'here is some mystery concealed. 
follow me without delay!' 

thus saying, he hasten into the sepulchre, and pursue the person who 
continue to fly before him. the duke knew not the cause of his 
exclamation, but supposing that he had good reason for it, he follow 
him without hesitation. the other did the same, and the whole party 
soon arrive at the foot of the stairs. 

the upper door have been left open, the neighbouring flame dart 
from above a sufficient light to enable lorenzo's catching a glance of 
the fugitive running through the long passage and distant vaults: but 
when a sudden turn deprived him of this assistance, total dark 
succeeded, and he could only trace the object of his enquiry by the 
faint echo of retiring feet. the pursuer were now compel to 
proceed with caution: as well as they could judge, the fugitive also 
seem to slacken pace, for they heard the steps follow each other at 
long intervals. they at length were bewilder by the labyrinth of 
passages, and disperse in various directions. carry away by his 
eager to clear up this mystery, and to penetrate into which he was 
impel by a movement secret and unaccountable, lorenzo heed not 
this circumstance till he found himself in total solitude. the noise of 
footstep had ceased. all was silent around, and no clue offer 
itself to guide him to the flying person. he stop to reflect on the 
means most likely to aid his pursuit. he was persuade that no common 
cause would have induce the fugitive to seek that dreary place at an 
hour so unusual: the cry which he had heard, seem utter in a voice 
of terror, and he was convinced that some mystery was attach to this 
event. after some minutes past in hesitation he continue to proceed, 
feeling his way along the wall of the passage. he had already past 
some time in this slow progress, when he descry a spark of light 
glimmer at a distance. guide by this observation, and have drawn 
his sword, he bent his steps towards the place, whence the beam seem 
to be emitted. 

it proceed from the lamp which flame before st. clare's statue. 
before it stood several females, their white garment stream in the 
blast, as it howl along the vaulted dungeons. curious to know what 
had brought them together in this melancholy spot, lorenzo drew near 
with precaution. the stranger seem earnest engaged in 
conversation. they heard not lorenzo's steps, and he approach 
unobserved, till he could hear their voice distinctly. 

'i protest,' continue she who was speak when he arrived, and to 
whom the rest were listen with great attention; 'i protest, that i 
saw them with my own eyes. i flew down the steps; they pursue me, and 
i escape fall into their hand with difficulty. had it not been 
for the lamp, i should never have found you.' 

'and what could bring them hither?' said another in a tremble voice; 
'do you think that they were look for us?' 

'god grant that my fear may be false,' rejoin the first; 'but i 
doubt they are murderers! if they discover us, we are lost! as for me, 
my fate is certain: my affinity to the prioress will be a sufficient 
crime to condemn me; and though till now these vault have afford me 
a retreat.......' 

here look up, her eye fell upon lorenzo, who had continue to 
approach softly. 

'the murderers!' she cried-- 

she start away from the statue's pedestal on which she had been 
seated, and attempt to escape by flight. her companion at the same 
moment utter a terrify scream, while lorenzo arrest the fugitive 
by the arm. frightened and desperate she sank upon her knee before 
him. 

'spare me!' she exclaimed; 'for christ's sake, spare me! i am 
innocent, indeed, i am!' 

while she spoke, her voice was almost choake with fear. the beam of 
the lamp dart full upon her face which was unveiled, lorenzo 
recognize the beautiful virginia de villa-franca. he hasten to 
raise her from the ground, and besought her to take courage. he 
promise to protect her from the rioters, assured her that her retreat 
was still a secret, and that she might depend upon his readiness to 
defend her to the last drop of his blood. during this conversation, 
the nun had thrown themselves into various attitudes: one knelt, and 
address herself to heaven; another hid her face in the lap of her 
neighbour; some listen motionless with fear to the discourse of the 
supposed assassin; while other embrace the statue of st. clare, and 
implore her protection with frantic cries. on perceive their 
mistake, they crowded round lorenzo and heap benediction on him by 
dozens. he found that, on hearing the threat of the mob, and 
terrify by the cruelty which from the convent tower they had seen 
inflict on the superior, many of the pensioner and nun had taken 
refuge in the sepulchre. among the former was to be reckon the 
lovely virginia. nearly related to the prioress, she had more reason 
than the rest to dread the rioters, and now besought lorenzo earnest 
not to abandon her to their rage. her companions, most of whom were 
women of noble family, made the same request, which he readily granted. 
he promise not to quit them, till he had seen each of them safe in the 
arms of her relations: but he advise their defer to quit the 
sepulchre for some time longer, when the popular fury should be 
somewhat calmed, and the arrival of military force have disperse the 
multitude. 

'would to god!' cry virginia, 'that i were already safe in my 
mother's embraces! how say you, segnor; will it be long, ere we may 
leave this place? every moment that i pass here, i pass in torture!' 

'i hope, not long,' said he; 'but till you can proceed with security, 
this sepulchre will prove an impenetrable asylum. here you run no 
risque of a discovery, and i would advise your remain quiet for the 
next two or three hours.' 

'two or three hours?' exclaim sister helena; 'if i stay another hour 
in these vaults, i shall expire with fear! not the wealth of world 
should bribe me to undergo again what i have suffer since my coming 
hither. blessed virgin! to be in this melancholy place in the middle 
of night, surround by the moulder body of my deceased 
companions, and expect every moment to be torn in pieces by their 
ghost who wander about me, and complain, and groan, and wail in 
accent that make my blood run cold, ..... christ jesus! it is 
enough to drive me to madness!' 

'excuse me,' reply lorenzo, 'if i am surprize that while menace by 
real woe you are capable of yielding to imaginary dangers. these 
terror are puerile and groundless: combat them, holy sister; i have 
promise to guard you from the rioters, but against the attack of 
superstition you must depend for protection upon yourself. the idea of 
ghost is ridiculous in the extreme; and if you continue to be sway 
by ideal terror ...' 

'ideal?' exclaim the nun with one voice; 'why we heard it ourselves, 
segnor! every one of us heard it! it was frequent repeated, and it 
sound every time more melancholy and deep. you will never persuade me 
that we could all have been deceived. not we, indeed; no, no; had the 
noise been merely create by fancy ....' 

'hark! hark!' interrupt virginia in a voice of terror; 'god preserve 
us! there it is again!' 

the nun clasp their hand together, and sank upon their knees. 

lorenzo look round him eagerly, and was on the point of yielding to 
the fear which already had possessed the women. universal silence 
prevailed. he examine the vault, but nothing was to be seen. he now 
prepared to address the nuns, and ridicule their childish 
apprehensions, when his attention was arrest by a deep and long-drawn 
groan. 

'what was that?' he cried, and started. 

'there, segnor!' said helena; 'now you must be convinced! you have 
heard the noise yourself! now judge, whether our terror are 
imaginary. since we have been here, that groan has been repeated 
almost every five minutes. doubtless, it proceeds from some soul in 
pain, who wish to be pray out of purgatory: but none of us here 
dare ask it the question. as for me, were i to see an apparition, the 
fright, i am very certain, would kill me out of hand.' 

as she said this, a second groan was heard yet more distinctly. the 
nun cross themselves, and hasten to repeat their prayer against 
evil spirits. lorenzo listen attentively. he even thought that he 
could distinguish sounds, as of one speak in complaint; but distance 
render them inarticulate. the noise seem to come from the midst of 
the small vault in which he and the nun then were, and which a 
multitude of passage branch out in various directions, form into 
a sort of star. lorenzo's curiosity which was ever awake, made him 
anxious to solve this mystery. he desire that silence might be kept. 
the nun obey him. all was hushed, till the general still was 
again disturbed by the groaning, which was repeated several times 
successively. he perceive it to be most audible, when upon following 
the sound he was conduct close to the shrine of st. clare: 

'the noise come from hence,' said he; 'whose is this statue?' 

helena, to whom he address the question, pause for a moment. 
sudden she clap her hand together. 

'aye!' cry she, 'it must be so. i have discover the meaning of 
these groans.' 

the nun crowded round her, and besought her eager to explain 
herself. she grave reply that for time immemorial the statue had 
been famous for perform miracles: from this she infer that the 
saint was concerned at the conflagration of a convent which she 
protected, and express her grief by audible lamentations. not have 
equal faith in the miraculous saint, lorenzo did not think this 
solution of the mystery quite so satisfactory, as the nuns, who 
subscribe to it without hesitation. in one point, 'tis true, that he 
agree with helena. 

he suspect that the groan proceed from the statue: the more he 
listened, the more was he confirmed in this idea. he drew near to 
the image, designing to inspect it more closely: but perceive his 
intention, the nun besought him for god's sake to desist, since if he 
touched the statue, his death was inevitable. 

'and in what consist the danger?' said he. 

'mother of god! in what?' reply helena, ever eager to relate a 
miraculous adventure; 'if you had only heard the hundredth part of 
those marvellous story about this statue which the domina use to 
recount! she assured us often and often, that if we only dare to lay 
a finger upon it, we might expect the most fatal consequences. among 
other things she told us that a robber have enter these vault by 
night, he observe yonder ruby, whose value is inestimable. do you see 
it, segnor? it sparkle upon the third finger of the hand, in which 
she hold a crown of thorns. this jewel naturally excited the 
villain's cupidity. he resolve to make himself master of it. for 
this purpose he ascend the pedestal: he support himself by 
grasping the saint's right arm, and extend his own towards the ring. 
what was his surprize, when he saw the statue's hand raise in a 
posture of menace, and heard her lip pronounce his eternal perdition! 
penetrate with awe and consternation, he desist from his attempt, 
and prepared to quit the sepulchre. in this he also failed. flight 
was deny him. he found it impossible to disengage the hand, which 
rest upon the right arm of the statue. in vain did he struggle: he 
remain fixed to the image, till the insupportable and fiery anguish 
which dart itself through his veins, compel his shriek for 
assistance. 

the sepulchre was now fill with spectators. the villain confessed 
his sacrilege, and was only release by the separation of his hand from 
his body. it has remain ever since fasten to the image. the 
robber turn hermit, and led ever after an exemplary life: but yet 
the saint's decree was performed, and tradition say that he continue 
to haunt this sepulchre, and implore st. clare's pardon with groan and 
lamentations. now i think of it, those which we have just heard, may 
very possibly have been utter by the ghost of this sinner: but of 
this i will not be positive. all that i can say is, that since that 
time no one has ever dare to touch the statue: then do not be 
foolhardy, good segnor! for the love of heaven, give up your design, 
nor expose yourself unnecessary to certain destruction.' 

not being convinced that his destruction would be so certain as helena 
seem to think it, lorenzo persist in his resolution. the nun 
besought him to desist in piteous terms, and even pointed out the 
robber's hand, which in effect was still visible upon the arm of the 
statue. this proof, as they imagined, must convince him. it was very 
far from do so; and they were greatly scandalize when he declared 
his suspicion that the dry and shrivel finger had been placed 
there by order of the prioress. in spite of their prayer and threat 
he approach the statue. he sprang over the iron rails which defend 
it, and the saint underwent a thorough examination. the image at first 
appear to be of stone, but prove on further inspection to be form 
of no more solid material than coloured wood. he shook it, and 
attempt to move it; but it appear to be of a piece with the base 
which it stood upon. he examine it over and over: still no clue 
guide him to the solution of this mystery, for which the nun were 
become equally solicitous, when they saw that he touched the statue 
with impunity. he paused, and listened: the groan were repeated at 
intervals, and he was convinced of being in the spot nearest to them. 
he muse upon this singular event, and ran over the statue with 
enquiring eyes. sudden they rest upon the shrivel hand. it 
struck him, that so particular an injunction was not given without 
cause, not to touch the arm of the image. he again ascend the 
pedestal; he examine the object of his attention, and discover a 
small knob of iron conceal between the saint's shoulder and what was 
supposed to have been the hand of the robber. this observation 
delight him. he applied his finger to the knob, and pressed it down 
forcibly. immediately a rumbling noise was heard within the statue, as 
if a chain tight stretch was flying back. startle at the sound the 
timid nun start away, prepared to hasten from the vault at the first 
appearance of danger. all remain quiet and still, they again 
gather round lorenzo, and beheld his proceedings with anxious 
curiosity. 

finding that nothing follow this discovery, he descended. as he took 
his hand from the saint, she tremble beneath his touch. this create 
new terror in the spectators, who believe the statue to be animated. 
lorenzo's idea upon the subject were widely different. he easily 
comprehend that the noise which he had heard, was occasion by his 
have loosen a chain which attach the image to its pedestal. he 
once more attempt to move it, and succeed without much exertion. 
he placed it upon the ground, and then perceive the pedestal to be 
hollow, and cover at the opening with an heavy iron grate. 

this excited such general curiosity that the sister forgot both their 
real and imaginary dangers. lorenzo proceed to raise the grate, in 
which the nun assist him to the utmost of their strength. the 
attempt was accomplished with little difficulty. a deep abyss now 
present itself before them, whose thick obscurity the eye strove in 
vain to pierce. the ray of the lamp were too feeble to be of much 
assistance. nothing was discernible, save a flight of rough unshapen 
steps which sank into the yawn gulph and were soon lost in darkness. 
the groan were heard no more; but all believe them to have ascend 
from this cavern. as he bent over it, lorenzo fancy that he 
distinguished something bright twinkling through the gloom. he gaze 
attentive upon the spot where it show itself, and was convinced 
that he saw a small spark of light, now visible, now disappearing. he 
communicate this circumstance to the nuns: they also perceive the 
spark; but when he declared his intention to descend into the cave, 
they united to oppose his resolution. all their remonstrance could not 
prevail on him to alter it. none of them had courage enough to 
accompany him; neither could he think of deprive them of the lamp. 
alone therefore, and in darkness, he prepared to pursue his design, 
while the nun were contented to offer up prayer for his success and 
safety. 

the steps were so narrow and uneven, that to descend them was like 
walking down the side of a precipice. the obscurity by which he was 
surround render his footing insecure. he was oblige to proceed 
with great caution, lest he should miss the steps and fall into the 
gulph below him. this he was several times on the point of doing. 
however, he arrive soon upon solid ground than he had expected: he 
now found that the thick dark and impenetrable mists which reign 
through the cavern had deceive him into the belief of its being much 
more profound than it prove upon inspection. he reach the foot of 
the stairs unhurt: he now stopped, and look round for the spark 
which had before caught his attention. he sought it in vain: all was 
dark and gloomy. he listen for the groans; but his ear caught no 
sound, except the distant murmur of the nun above, as in low voice 
they repeated their ave-marias. he stood irresolute to which side he 
should address his steps. at all event he determined to proceed: he 
did so, but slowly, fear lest instead of approaching, he should be 
retiring from the object of his search. the groan seem to announce 
one in pain, or at least in sorrow, and he hope to have the power of 
relieve the mourner's calamities. a plaintive tone, sound at no 
great distance, at length reach his hearing; he bent his course 
joyful towards it. it became more audible as he advanced; and he 
soon beheld again the spark of light, which a low project wall had 
hitherto conceal from him. 

it proceed from a small lamp which was placed upon an heap of stones, 
and whose faint and melancholy ray serve rather to point out, than 
dispell the horrors of a narrow gloomy dungeon form in one side of 
the cavern; it also show several other recess of similar 
construction, but whose depth was bury in obscurity. cold play 
the light upon the damp walls, whose dew-stained surface gave back a 
feeble reflection. a thick and pestilent fog cloud the height of 
the vaulted dungeon. as lorenzo advanced, he felt a piercing chill 
spread itself through his veins. the frequent groan still engaged him 
to move forwards. he turn towards them, and by the lamp's glimmer 
beam beheld in a corner of this loathsome abode, a creature stretch 
upon a bed of straw, so wretched, so emaciated, so pale, that he 
doubt to think her woman. she was half-naked: her long dishevelled 
hair fell in disorder over her face, and almost entire conceal it. 
one waste arm hung listless upon a tattered rug which cover her 
convulse and shiver limbs: the other was wrap round a small 
bundle, and held it close to her bosom. a large rosary lay near her: 
opposite to her was a crucifix, on which she bent her sunk eyes 
fixedly, and by her side stood a basket and a small earthen pitcher. 

lorenzo stopped: he was petrify with horror. he gaze upon the 
miserable object with disgust and pity. he tremble at the spectacle; 
he grew sick at heart: his strength fail him, and his limb were 
unable to support his weight. he was oblige to lean against the low 
wall which was near him, unable to go forward, or to address the 
sufferer. she cast her eyes towards the staircase: the wall conceal 
lorenzo, and she observe him not. 

'no one comes!' she at length murmured. 

as she spoke, her voice was hollow, and rattle in her throat: she 
sigh bitterly. 

'no one comes!' she repeated; 'no! they have forgotten me! they will 
come no more!' 

she pause for a moment: then continue mournfully. 

'two days! two long, long days, and yet no food! and yet no hope, no 
comfort! foolish woman! how can i wish to lengthen a life so 
wretched! yet such a death! o! god! to perish by such a death! to 
linger out such age in torture! till now, i knew not what it was to 
hunger! hark! no. no one comes! they will come no more!' 

she was silent. she shivered, and drew the rug over her naked 
shoulders. 

'i am very cold! i am still unused to the damp of this dungeon! 

'tis strange: but no matter. cold shall i soon be, and yet not feel 
it--i shall be cold, cold as thou art!' 

she look at the bundle which lay upon her breast. she bent over it, 
and kiss it: then drew back hastily, and shudder with disgust. 

'it was once so sweet! it would have been so lovely, so like him! i 
have lost it for ever! how a few days have change it! i should not 
know it again myself! yet it is dear to me! god! how dear! i will 
forget what it is: i will only remember what it was, and love it as 
well, as when it was so sweet! so lovely! so like him! i thought that 
i had wept away all my tears, but here is one still lingering.' 

she wipe her eyes with a tress of her hair. she put out her hand for 
the pitcher, and reach it with difficulty. she cast into it a look 
of hopeless enquiry. she sighed, and replace it upon the ground. 

'quite a void! not a drop! not one drop left to cool my scorched-up 
burning palate! now would i give treasure for a draught of water! 
and they are god's servants, who make me suffer thus! they think 
themselves holy, while they torture me like fiends! they are cruel and 
unfeeling; and 'tis they who bid me repent; and 'tis they, who threaten 
me with eternal perdition! saviour, saviour! you think not so!' 

she again fixed her eyes upon the crucifix, took her rosary, and while 
she told her beads, the quick motion of her lip declared her to be 
pray with fervency. 

while he listen to her melancholy accents, lorenzo's sensibility 
became yet more violent affected. the first sight of such misery had 
given a sensible shock to his feelings: but that being past, he now 
advanced towards the captive. she heard his steps, and utter a cry 
of joy, drop the rosary. 

'hark! hark! hark!' she cried: 'some one comes!' 

she strove to raise herself, but her strength was unequal to the 
attempt: she fell back, and as she sank again upon the bed of straw, 
lorenzo heard the rattling of heavy chains. he still approached, while 
the prisoner thus continued. 

'is it you, camilla? you are come then at last? oh! it was time! i 
thought that you had forsaken me; that i was doom to perish of 
hunger. give me to drink, camilla, for pity's sake! i am faint with 
long fasting, and grown so weak that i cannot raise myself from the 
ground. good camilla, give me to drink, lest i expire before you!' 

fear that surprize in her enfeeble state might be fatal, lorenzo 
was at a loss how to address her. 

'it is not camilla,' said he at length, speak in a slow and gentle 
voice. 

'who is it then?' reply the sufferer: 'alix, perhaps, or violante. 
my eyes are grown so dim and feeble that i cannot distinguish your 
features. but whichever it is, if your breast is sensible of the least 
compassion, if you are not more cruel than wolf and tigers, take pity 
on my sufferings. you know that i am die for want of sustenance. 
this is the third day, since these lip have received nourishment. do 
you bring me food? or come you only to announce my death, and learn 
how long i have yet to exist in agony?' 

'you mistake my business,' reply lorenzo; 'i am no emissary of the 
cruel prioress. i pity your sorrows, and come hither to relieve them.' 

'to relieve them?' repeated the captive; 'said you, to relieve them?' 

at the same time start from the ground, and support herself upon 
her hands, she gaze upon the stranger earnestly. 

'great god! it is no illusion! a man! speak! who are you? what 
bring you hither? come you to save me, to restore me to liberty, to 
life and light? oh! speak, speak quickly, lest i encourage an hope 
whose disappointment will destroy me.' 

'be calm!' reply lorenzo in a voice soothe and compassionate; 'the 
domina of whose cruelty you complain, has already paid the forfeit of 
her offences: you have nothing more to fear from her. 

a few minutes will restore you to liberty, and the embrace of your 
friends from whom you have been secluded. you may rely upon my 
protection. give me your hand, and be not fearful. let me conduct you 
where you may receive those attention which your feeble state 
requires.' 

'oh! yes! yes! yes!' cry the prisoner with an exult shriek; 
'there is a god then, and a just one! joy! joy! i shall once more 
breath the fresh air, and view the light of the glorious sunbeams! i 
will go with you! stranger, i will go with you! oh! heaven will bless 
you for pity an unfortunate! but this too must go with me,' she 
add point to the small bundle which she still clasp to her 
bosom; 'i cannot part with this. i will bear it away: it shall 
convince the world how dreadful are the abode so false term 
religious. good stranger, lend me your hand to rise: i am faint with 
want, and sorrow, and sickness, and my forces have quite forsaken me! 
so, that is well!' 

as lorenzo stoop to raise her, the beam of the lamp struck full upon 
his face. 

'almighty god!' she exclaimed; 'is it possible! that look! those 
features! oh! yes, it is, it is .....' 

she extend her arms to throw them round him; but her enfeeble frame 
was unable to sustain the emotion which agitate her bosom. she 
fainted, and again sank upon the bed of straw. 

lorenzo was surprize at her last exclamation. he thought that he had 
before heard such accent as her hollow voice had just formed, but 
where he could not remember. he saw that in her dangerous situation 
immediate physical aid was absolutely necessary, and he hasten to 
convey her from the dungeon. he was at first prevent from do so 
by a strong chain fasten round the prisoner's body, and fix her to 
the neighbouring wall. however, his natural strength being aid by 
anxiety to relieve the unfortunate, he soon forced out the staple to 
which one end of the chain was attached. then taking the captive in his 
arms, he bent his course towards the staircase. the ray of the lamp 
above, as well as the murmur of female voices, guide his steps. he 
gain the stairs, and in a few minutes after arrive at the iron-grate. 

the nun during his absence had been terribly torment by curiosity 
and apprehension: they were equally surprize and delight on seeing 
him sudden emerge from the cave. every heart was fill with 
compassion for the miserable creature whom he bore in his arms. while 
the nuns, and virginia in particular, employ themselves in strive 
to recall her to her senses, lorenzo related in few words the manner of 
his finding her. he then observe to them that by this time the tumult 
must have been quelled, and that he could now conduct them to their 
friends without danger. all were eager to quit the sepulchre: still 
to prevent all possibility of ill-usage, they besought lorenzo to 
venture out first alone, and examine whether the coast was clear. with 
this request he complied. helena offer to conduct him to the 
staircase, and they were on the point of departing, when a strong light 
flash from several passage upon the adjacent walls. at the same 
time steps were heard of people approach hastily, and whose number 
seem to be considerable. the nun were greatly alarm at this 
circumstance: they supposed their retreat to be discovered, and the 
riot to be advance in pursuit of them. hasty quit the 
prisoner who remain insensible, they crowded round lorenzo, and

all this while, ambrosio was unconscious of the dreadful scene which 
were passing so near. the execution of his designs upon antonia 
employ his every thought. hitherto, he was satisfy with the 
success of his plans. antonia had drank the opiate, was bury in the 
vault of st. clare, and absolutely in his disposal. matilda, who was 
well acquaint with the nature and effects of the soporific medicine, 
had compute that it would not cease to operate till one in the 
morning. for that hour he wait with impatience. the festival of st. 
clare present him with a favourable opportunity of consummate his 
crime. he was certain that the friar and nun would be engaged in the 
procession, and that he had no cause to dread an interruption: from 
appear himself at the head of his monks, he had desire to be 
excused. he doubt not, that being beyond the reach of help, cut off 
from all the world, and total in his power, antonia would comply with 
his desires. the affection which she had ever exprest for him, 
warrant this persuasion: but he resolve that should she prove 
obstinate, no consideration whatever should prevent him from enjoy

employ force: if he felt any repugnance, it arose not from a 
principle of shame or compassion, but from his feeling for antonia the 
most sincere and ardent affection, and wish to owe her favours to no 
one but herself. 

the monk quit the abbey at midnight. matilda was among the

behind to watch his motions, or disturb his pleasures, he now hasten 
to the western aisles. his heart beating with hope not unmingled with 
anxiety, he cross the garden, unlock the door which admitted him 
into the cemetery, and in a few minutes he stood before the vaults. 
here he paused. 

he look round him with suspicion, conscious that his business was 
unfit for any other eye. as he stood in hesitation, he heard the 
melancholy shriek of the screech-owl: the wind rattle loud against 
the window of the adjacent convent, and as the current swept by him, 
bore with it the faint note of the chaunt of choristers. he open

closed it again after him. guide by his lamp, he thread the long 
passages, in whose winding matilda had instruct him, and reach the 
private vault which contained his sleep mistress. 

its entrance was by no means easy to discover: but this was no

it too careful to be deceived. he found the door, which was

that horrid act glance upon his mind, it was cloud with a gloomy

antonia's honour. 

'for your sake, fatal beauty!' murmur the monk, while gaze on his

myself to eternal tortures. now you are in my power: the produce of my 
guilt will at least be mine. hope not that your prayer breathe in 
tone of unequalled melody, your bright eyes fill with tears, and 
your hand lift in supplication, as when seek in penitence the 
virgin's pardon; hope not that your moving innocence, your beauteous 
grief, or all your suppliant arts shall ransom you from my embraces. 
before the break of day, mine you must, and mine you shall be!'

'i am no prostitute, ambrosio;' had she told him, when in the full

while your heart was mine, i glory in your embraces: those happy

sudden deprived of pleasures, the use of which had made them an
