for assistance. in vain did the monk strive to threaten her to silence. 

'help!' she continue to exclaim; 'help! help! for god's sake!' 

quicken by her cries, the sound of footstep was heard approaching. 
the abbot expect every moment to see the inquisitor arrive. antonia 
still resisted, and he now enforce her silence by means the most 
horrible and inhuman. he still grasp matilda's dagger: without 
allow himself a moment's reflection, he raise it, and plunge it 
twice in the bosom of antonia! she shrieked, and sank upon the ground. 
the monk endeavour to bear her away with him, but she still embrace 
the pillar firmly. at that instant the light of approach torch 
flash upon the walls. dread a discovery, ambrosio was compel 
to abandon his victim, and hasty flee back to the vault, where he had 
left matilda. 

he flee not unobserved. don ramirez happening to arrive the first, 
perceive a female bleeding upon the ground, and a man flying from the 
spot, whose confusion betray him for the murderer. he instantly 
pursue the fugitive with some part of the archers, while the other 
remain with lorenzo to protect the wound stranger. they raise her, 
and support her in their arms. she had faint from excess of pain, 
but soon gave sign of return life. she open her eyes, and on 
lift up her head, the quantity of fair hair fell back which till 
then had obscure her features. 

'god almighty! it is antonia!' 

such was lorenzo's exclamation, while he snatch her from the 
attendant's arms, and clasp her in his own. 

though aim by an uncertain hand, the poignard had answer but too 
well the purpose of its employer. the wound were mortal, and antonia 
was conscious that she never could recover. yet the few moments which 
remain for her were moments of happiness. the concern exprest upon 
lorenzo's countenance, the frantic fond of his complaints, and his 
earnest enquiry respecting her wounds, convinced her beyond a doubt 
that his affection were her own. she would not be remove from the 
vaults, fear lest motion should only hasten her death; and she was 
unwill to lose those moments which she past in receiving proof of 
lorenzo's love, and assure him of her own. she told him that had she 
still been undefiled she might have lament the loss of life; but that 
deprived of honour and brand with shame, death was to her a blessing: 
she could not have been his wife, and that hope being deny her, she 
resigned herself to the grave without one sigh of regret. she bad him 
take courage, conjure him not to abandon himself to fruitless sorrow, 
and declared that she mourn to leave nothing in the whole world but 
him. while every sweet accent increase rather than lighten 
lorenzo's grief, she continue to converse with him till the moment of 
dissolution. her voice grew faint and scarcely audible; a thick cloud 
spread itself over her eyes; her heart beat slow and irregular, and 
every instant seem to announce that her fate was near at hand. 

she lay, her head recline upon lorenzo's bosom, and her lip still 
murmur to him words of comfort. she was interrupt by the convent 
bell, as toll at a distance, it struck the hour. sudden antonia's 
eyes sparkle with celestial brightness: her frame seem to have 
received new strength and animation. she start from her lover's arms. 

'three o'clock!' she cried; 'mother, i come!' 

she clasp her hands, and sank lifeless upon the ground. lorenzo in 
agony threw himself beside her: he tore his hair, beat his breast, and 
refuse to be separate from the corse. at length his force being 
exhausted, he suffer himself to be led from the vault, and was 
convey to the palace de medina scarcely more alive than the 
unfortunate antonia. 

in the meanwhile, though close pursued, ambrosio succeed in 
regain the vault. the door was already fasten when don ramirez 
arrived, and much time elapsed, ere the fugitive's retreat was 
discovered. but nothing can resist perseverance. though so artful 
concealed, the door could not escape the vigilance of the archers. 
they forced it open, and enter the vault to the infinite dismay of 
ambrosio and his companion. the monk's confusion, his attempt to hide 
himself, his rapid flight, and the blood sprinkle upon his cloaths, 
left no room to doubt his being antonia's murderer. but when he was 
recognize for the immaculate ambrosio, 'the man of holiness,' the idol 
of madrid, the faculty of the spectator were chain up in surprize, 
and scarcely could they persuade themselves that what they saw was no 
vision. the abbot strove not to vindicate himself, but preserve a 
sullen silence. he was secure and bound. the same precaution was 
taken with matilda: her cowl being removed, the delicacy of her 
features and profusion of her golden hair betray her sex, and this 
incident create fresh amazement. the dagger was also found in the 
tomb, where the monk had thrown it; and the dungeon have undergone a 
thorough search, the two culprit were convey to the prison of the 
inquisition. 

don ramirez took care that the populace should remain ignorant both of 
the crime and profession of the captives. he fear a repetition of 
the riot which had follow the apprehend the prioress of st. 
clare. he contented himself with state to the capuchin the guilt of 
their superior. to avoid the shame of a public accusation, and 
dread the popular fury from which they had already save their abbey 
with much difficulty, the monk readily permit the inquisitor to 
search their mansion without noise. no fresh discovery were made. 
the effects found in the abbot's and matilda's cell were seized, and 
carry to the inquisition to be produce in evidence. every thing 
else remain in its former position, and order and tranquill once 
more prevail through madrid. 

st. clare's convent was completely ruin by the united ravages of the 
mob and conflagration. nothing remain of it but the principal walls, 
whose thickness and solidity had preserve them from the flames. the 
nun who had belong to it were oblige in consequence to disperse 
themselves into other societies: but the prejudice against them ran 
high, and the superior were very unwill to admit them. however, 
most of them being related to family the most distinguished for their 
riches, birth and power, the several convent were compel to receive 
them, though they did it with a very ill grace. this prejudice was 
extremely false and unjustifiable: after a close investigation, it was 
prove that all in the convent were persuade of the death of agnes, 
except the four nun whom st. ursula had pointed out. these had fallen 
victim to the popular fury; as had also several who were perfectly 
innocent and unconscious of the whole affair. blind by resentment, 
the mob had sacrifice every nun who fell into their hands: they who 
escape were entire indebted to the duke de medina's prudence and 
moderation. of this they were conscious, and felt for that nobleman a 
proper sense of gratitude. 

virginia was not the most sparing of her thanks: she wish equally to 
make a proper return for his attentions, and to obtain the good graces 
of lorenzo's uncle. in this she easily succeeded. 

the duke beheld her beauty with wonder and admiration; and while his 
eyes were enchant with her form, the sweet of her manners and her 
tender concern for the suffering nun prepossessed his heart in her 
favour. this virginia had discernment enough to perceive, and she 
redouble her attention to the invalid. when he part from her at the 
door of her father's palace, the duke entreat permission to enquire 
occasional after her health. his request was readily granted: 
virginia assured him that the marquis de villa-franca would be proud of 
an opportunity to thank him in person for the protection afford to 
her. they now separated, he enchant with her beauty and gentleness, 
and she much pleased with him and more with his nephew. 

on enter the palace, virginia's first care was to summon the family 
physician, and take care of her unknown charge. her mother hasten to 
share with her the charitable office. alarm by the riots, and 
tremble for his daughter's safety, who was his only child, the 
marquis had flown to st. clare's convent, and was still employ in 
seek her. messenger were now dispatch on all side to inform him 
that he would find her safe at his hotel, and desire him to hasten 
thither immediately. his absence gave virginia liberty to bestow her 
whole attention upon her patient; and though much disorder herself by 
the adventure of the night, no persuasion could induce her to quit the 
bedside of the sufferer. her constitution being much enfeeble by want 
and sorrow, it was some time before the stranger was restore to her 
senses. she found great difficulty in swallow the medicine 
prescribed to her: but this obstacle being removed, she easily 
conquer her disease which proceed from nothing but weakness. the 
attention which was paid her, the wholesome food to which she had been 
long a stranger, and her joy at being restore to liberty, to society, 
and, as she dare to hope, to love, all this combine to her speedy 
re-establishment. 

from the first moment of knowing her, her melancholy situation, her 
suffering almost unparalleled had engaged the affection of her 
amiable hostess: virginia felt for her the most lively interest; but 
how was she delighted, when her guest being sufficient recover to 
relate her history, she recognize in the captive nun the sister of 
lorenzo! 

this victim of monastic cruelty was indeed no other than the 
unfortunate agnes. during her abode in the convent, she had been well 
known to virginia: but her emaciate form, her features alter by 
affliction, her death universally credited, and her overgrown and 
matt hair which hung over her face and bosom in disorder at first had 
prevent her being recollected. the prioress had put every artifice 
in practice to induce virginia to take the veil; for the heiress of 
villa-franca would have been no despicable acquisition. her seeming 
kindness and unremitt attention so far succeed that her young 
relation began to think seriously upon compliance. better instruct 
in the disgust and ennui of a monastic life, agnes had penetrate the 
designs of the domina: she tremble for the innocent girl, and 
endeavour to make her sensible of her error. she paint in their 
true colours the numerous inconvenient attach to a convent, the 
continue restraint, the low jealousies, the petty intrigues, the 
servile court and gross flattery expect by the superior. she then 
bad virginia reflect on the brilliant prospect which present itself 
before her: the idol of her parents, the admiration of madrid, endow 
by nature and education with every perfection of person and mind, she 
might look forward to an establishment the most fortunate. her riches 
furnish her with the means of exercise in their fullest extent, 
charity and benevolence, those virtue so dear to her; and her stay in 
the world would enable her discover object worthy her protection, 
which could not be done in the seclusion of a convent. 

her persuasion induce virginia to lay aside all thought of the veil: 
but another argument, not use by agnes, had more weight with her than 
all the other put together. she had seen lorenzo, when he visit his 
sister at the grate. his person pleased her, and her conversation 
with agnes generally use to terminate in some question about her 
brother. she, who dote upon lorenzo, wish for no better than an 
opportunity to trumpet out his praise. she spoke of him in terms of 
rapture; and to convince her auditor how just were his sentiments, how 
cultivated his mind, and elegant his expressions, she show her at 
different times the letters which she received from him. she soon 
perceive that from these communications the heart of her young friend 
had imbibe impressions, which she was far from intend to give, but 
was truly happy to discover. she could not have wish her brother a 
more desirable union: heiress of villa-franca, virtuous, affectionate, 
beautiful, and accomplished, virginia seem calculate to make him 
happy. she sound her brother upon the subject, though without 
mention name or circumstances. he assured her in his answer that 
his heart and hand were total disengaged, and she thought that upon 
these grounds she might proceed without danger. she in consequence 
endeavour to strengthen the dawn passion of her friend. lorenzo 
was made the constant topic of her discourse; and the avid with 
which her auditor listened, the sigh which frequent escape from her 
bosom, and the eager with which upon any digression she brought 
back the conversation to the subject whence it had wandered, suffice 
to convince agnes that her brother's address would be far from 
disagreeable. she at length venture to mention her wish to the 
duke: though a stranger to the lady herself, he knew enough of her 
situation to think her worthy his nephew's hand. it was agree between 
him and his niece, that she should insinuate the idea to lorenzo, and 
she only wait his return to madrid to propose her friend to him as 
his bride. the unfortunate event which took place in the interim, 
prevent her from execute her design. virginia wept her loss 
sincerely, both as a companion, and as the only person to whom she 
could speak of lorenzo. her passion continue to prey upon her heart 
in secret, and she had almost determined to confess her sentiment to 
her mother, when accident once more threw their object in her way. the 
sight of him so near her, his politeness, his compassion, his 
intrepidity, had combine to give new ardour to her affection. when 
she now found her friend and advocate restore to her, she look upon 
her as a gift from heaven; she venture to cherish the hope of being 
united to lorenzo, and resolve to use with him his sister's influence. 

supposing that before her death agnes might possibly have made the 
proposal, the duke had placed all his nephew's hint of marriage to 
virginia's account: consequently, he gave them the most favourable 
reception. on return to his hotel, the relation given him of 
antonia's death, and lorenzo's behaviour on the occasion, made evident 
his mistake. he lament the circumstances; but the unhappy girl being 
effectually out of the way, he trust that his designs would yet be 
executed. 'tis true that lorenzo's situation just then ill-suited him 
for a bridegroom. his hope disappointed at the moment when he 
expect to realize them, and the dreadful and sudden death of his 
mistress had affected him very severely. the duke found him upon the 
bed of sickness. his attendant express serious apprehension for 
his life; but the uncle entertain not the same fears. he was of 
opinion, and not unwisely, that 'men have died, and worm have eat 
them; but not for love!' he therefore flatter himself that however 
deep might be the impression made upon his nephew's heart, time and 
virginia would be able to efface it. he now hasten to the afflict 
youth, and endeavour to console him: he sympathise in his distress, 
but encourage him to resist the encroachment of despair. he allow 
that he could not but feel shock at an event so terrible, nor could 
he blame his sensibility; but he besought him not to torment himself 
with vain regrets, and rather to struggle with affliction, and preserve 
his life, if not for his own sake, at least for the sake of those who 
were fondly attach to him. while he labour thus to make lorenzo 
forget antonia's loss, the duke paid his court assiduous to virginia, 
and seize every opportunity to advance his nephew's interest in her 
heart. 

it may easily be expect that agnes was not long without enquiring 
after don raymond. she was shock to hear the wretched situation to 
which grief had reduce him; yet she could not help exult secretly, 
when she reflected, that his illness prove the sincerity of his love. 
the duke undertook the office himself, of announce to the invalid the 
happiness which await him. though he omit no precaution to 
prepare him for such an event, at this sudden change from despair to 
happiness raymond's transport were so violent, as nearly to have 
prove fatal to him. these once passed, the tranquill of his mind, 
the assurance of felicity, and above all the presence of agnes, (who 
was no soon reestablishe by the care of virginia and the 
marchioness, than she hasten to attend her lover) soon enable him to 
overcome the effects of his late dreadful malady. the calm of his soul 
communicate itself to his body, and he recover with such rapid as 
to create universal surprize. 

no so lorenzo. antonia's death accompany with such terrible 
circumstances weigh upon his mind heavily. he was worn down to a 
shadow. nothing could give him pleasure. he was persuade with 
difficulty to swallow nourishment sufficient for the support of life, 
and a consumption was apprehended. the society of agnes form his 
only comfort. though accident had never permit their being much 
together, he entertain for her a sincere friendship and attachment. 
perceive how necessary she was to him, she seldom quit his 
chamber. she listen to his complaint with unweary attention, and 
soothe him by the gentle of her manners, and by sympathise with 
his distress. she still inhabit the palace de villa-franca, the 
possessor of which treat her with marked affection. the duke had 
intimate to the marquis his wish respecting virginia. the match was 
unexceptionable: lorenzo was heir to his uncle's immense property, and 
was distinguished in madrid for his agreeable person, extensive 
knowledge, and propriety of conduct: add to this, that the marchioness 
had discover how strong was her daughter's prepossession in his 
favour. 

in consequence the duke's proposal was accept without hesitation: 
every precaution was taken to induce lorenzo's seeing the lady with 
those sentiment which she so well merit to excite. in her visit to 
her brother agnes was frequent accompany by the marchioness; and as 
soon as he was able to move into his antichamber, virginia under her 
mother's protection was sometimes permit to express her wish for 
his recovery. this she did with such delicacy, the manner in which she 
mention antonia was so tender and soothing, and when she lament her 
rival's melancholy fate, her bright eyes shone so beautiful through her 
tears, that lorenzo could not behold, or listen to her without emotion. 
his relations, as well as the lady, perceive that with every day her 
society seem to give him fresh pleasure, and that he spoke of her in 
terms of strong admiration. however, they prudent kept their 
observations to themselves. no word was drop which might lead him 
to suspect their designs. they continue their former conduct and 
attention, and left time to ripen into a warm sentiment the 
friendship which he already felt for virginia. 

in the mean while, her visit became more frequent; and latterly there 
was scarce a day, of which she did not pass some part by the side of 
lorenzo's couch. he gradual regain his strength, but the progress 
of his recovery was slow and doubtful. one evening he seem to be in 
better spirits than usual: agnes and her lover, the duke, virginia, 
and her parent were sitting round him. he now for the first time 
entreat his sister to inform him how she had escape the effects of 
the poison which st. ursula had seen her swallow. fearful of recall 
those scene to his mind in which antonia had perished, she had 
hitherto conceal from him the history of her sufferings. as he now 
start the subject himself, and thinking that perhaps the narrative of 
her sorrow might draw him from the contemplation of those on which he 
dwelt too constantly, she immediately comply with his request. the 
rest of the company had already heard her story; but the interest which 
all present felt for its heroine made them anxious to hear it repeated. 
the whole society second lorenzo's entreaties, agnes obeyed. she 
first recount the discovery which had taken place in the abbey 
chapel, the domina's resentment, and the midnight scene of which st. 
ursula had been a conceal witness. though the nun had already 
describe this latter event, agnes now related it more circumstantial 
and at large: after which she proceed in her narrative as follows. 

conclusion of the history of agnes de medina 

my supposed death was attend with the greatest agonies. those 
moments which i believe my last, were embitter by the domina's 
assurance that i could not escape perdition; and as my eyes closed, i 
heard her rage exhale itself in curse on my offence. the horror of 
this situation, of a death-bed from which hope was banished, of a sleep 
from which i was only to wake to find myself the prey of flame and 
furies, was more dreadful than i can describe. when animation revive 
in me, my soul was still impress with these terrible ideas: i look 
round with fear, expect to behold the minister of divine vengeance. 
for the first hour, my senses were so bewildered, and my brain so 
dizzy, that i strove in vain to arrange the strange image which 
float in wild confusion before me. if i endeavour to raise myself 
from the ground, the wandering of my head deceive me. every thing 
around me seem to rock, and i sank once more upon the earth. my weak 
and dazzle eyes were unable to bear a near approach to a gleam of 
light which i saw tremble above me. i was compel to close them 
again, and remain motionless in the same posture. 

a full hour elapsed, before i was sufficient myself to examine the 
surrounding objects. when i did examine them, what terror fill my 
bosom i found myself extend upon a sort of wicker couch: it had six 
handle to it, which doubtless had serve the nun to convey me to my 
grave. i was cover with a linen cloth: 

several fade flower were strown over me: on one side lay a small 
wooden crucifix; on the other, a rosary of large beads. four low narrow 
wall confine me. the top was also covered, and in it was practised a 
small grate door: through this was admitted the little air which 
circulate in this miserable place. a faint glimmer of light which 
stream through the bars, permit me to distinguish the surrounding 
horrors. i was opprest by a noisome suffocate smell; and perceive 
that the grate door was unfastened, i thought that i might possibly 
effect my escape. as i raise myself with this design, my hand rest 
upon something soft: i grasp it, and advanced it towards the light. 
almighty god! what was my disgust, my consternation! in spite of its 
putridity, and the worm which prey upon it, i perceive a corrupt 
human head, and recognise the features of a nun who had die some 
month before! 

i threw it from me, and sank almost lifeless upon my bier. 

when my strength returned, this circumstance, and the consciousness of 
being surround by the loathsome and moulder body of my 
companions, increase my desire to escape from my fearful prison. i 
again move towards the light. the grate door was within my reach: i 
lift it without difficulty; probably it had been left unclosed to 
facilitate my quit the dungeon. aid myself by the irregularity 
of the wall some of whose stone project beyond the rest, i 
contrived to ascend them, and drag myself out of my prison. i now 
found myself in a vault tolerably spacious. several tombs, similar in 
appearance to that whence i had just escaped, were range along the 
side in order, and seem to be considerably sunk within the earth. a 
sepulchral lamp was suspend from the roof by an iron chain, and shed 
a gloomy light through the dungeon. emblem of death were seen on 
every side: skulls, shoulder-blades, thigh-bones, and other leavings 
of mortality were scattered upon the dewy ground. each tomb was 
ornament with a large crucifix, and in one corner stood a wooden 
statue of st. clare. to these object i at first paid no attention: a 
door, the only outlet from the vault, had attract my eyes. i 
hasten towards it, have wrap my winding-sheet close round me. 
i pushed against the door, and to my inexpressible terror found that it 
was fasten on the outside. 

i guess immediately that the prioress, mistake the nature of the 
liquor which she had compel me to drink, instead of poison had 
administer a strong opiate. from this i conclude that being to all 
appearance dead i had received the rite of burial; and that deprived 
of the power of making my existence known, it would be my fate to 
expire of hunger. this idea penetrate me with horror, not merely for 
my own sake, but that of the innocent creature, who still live within 
my bosom. i again endeavour to open the door, but it resist all my 
efforts. i stretch my voice to the extent of its compass, and 
shriek for aid: i was remote from the hearing of every one: no 
friendly voice reply to mine. a profound and melancholy silence 
prevail through the vault, and i despair of liberty. my long 
abstinence from food now began to torment me. the torture which 
hunger inflict on me, were the most painful and insupportable: yet 
they seem to increase with every hour which past over my head. 
sometimes i threw myself upon the ground, and roll upon it wild and 
desperate: sometimes start up, i return to the door, again strove 
to force it open, and repeated my fruitless cries for succour. often 
was i on the point of striking my temple against the sharp corner of 
some monument, dashing out my brains, and thus terminate my woe at 
once; but still the remembrance of my baby vanquish my resolution: i 
tremble at a deed which equally endanger my child's existence and my 
own. then would i vent my anguish in loud exclamation and passionate 
complaints; and then again my strength failing me, silent and hopeless 
i would sit me down upon the base of st. clare's statue, fold my arms, 
and abandon myself to sullen despair. thus pass several wretched 
hours. death advanced towards me with rapid strides, and i expect 
that every succeed moment would be that of my dissolution. sudden 
a neighbouring tomb caught my eye: a basket stood upon it, which till 
then i had not observed. i start from my seat: i made towards it as 
swift as my exhaust frame would permit. how eager did i seize 
the basket, on finding it to contain a loaf of coarse bread and a small 
bottle of water. 

i threw myself with avid upon these humble aliments. they had to all 
appearance been placed in the vault for several days; the bread was 
hard, and the water tainted; yet never did i taste food to me so 
delicious. when the craving of appetite were satisfied, i busy 
myself with conjecture upon this new circumstance: i debate whether 
the basket had been placed there with a view to my necessity. hope 
answer my doubt in the affirmative. yet who could guess me to be in 
need of such assistance? if my existence was known, why was i detain 
in this gloomy vault? if i was kept a prisoner, what meant the 
ceremony of commit me to the tomb? or if i was doom to perish 
with hunger, to whose pity was i indebted for provisions placed within 
my reach? a friend would not have kept my dreadful punishment a 
secret; neither did it seem probable that an enemy would have taken 
pains to supply me with the means of existence. upon the whole i was 
inclined to think that the domina's designs upon my life had been 
discover by some one of my partizan in the convent, who had found 
means to substitute an opiate for poison: that she had furnish me 
with food to support me, till she could effect my delivery: and that 
she was then employ in give intelligence to my relations of my 
danger, and point out a way to release me from captivity. yet why 
then was the quality of my provisions so coarse? how could my friend 
have enter the vault without the domina's knowledge? and if she had 
entered, why was the door fasten so carefully? these reflection 
stagger me: yet still this idea was the most favourable to my hopes, 
and i dwelt upon it in preference. 

my meditation were interrupt by the sound of distant footsteps. 
they approached, but slowly. ray of light now dart through the 
crevice of the door. uncertain whether the person who advanced came 
to relieve me, or were conduct by some other motive to the vault, i 
fail not to attract their notice by loud cries for help. still the 
sound drew near: the light grew stronger: at length with 
inexpressible pleasure i heard the key turning in the lock. persuade 
that my deliverance was at hand, i flew towards the door with a shriek 
of joy. it opened: but all my hope of escape die away, when the 
prioress appear follow by the same four nuns, who had been 
witness of my supposed death. they bore torch in their hands, and 
gaze upon me in fearful silence. 

i start back in terror. the domina descended into the vault, as did 
also her companions. she bent upon me a stern resentful eye, but 
express no surprize at finding me still living. she took the seat 
which i had just quitted: the door was again closed, and the nun 
range themselves behind their superior, while the glare of their 
torches, dim by the vapours and damp of the vault, gilded with 
cold beam the surrounding monuments. for some moments all preserve a 
dead and solemn silence. i stood at some distance from the prioress. 
at length she beckon me to advance. tremble at the severity of her 
aspect my strength scarce suffice me to obey her. i drew near, but my 
limb were unable to support their burthen. i sank upon my knees; i 
clasp my hands, and lift them up to her for mercy, but had no power 
to articulate a syllable. 

she gaze upon me with angry eyes. 

'do i see a penitent, or a criminal?' she said at length; 'are those 
hand raise in contrition for your crimes, or in fear of meeting their 
punishment? do those tear acknowledge the justice of your doom, or 
only solicit mitigate of your sufferings? i fear me, 'tis the 
latter!' 

she paused, but kept her eye still fixt upon mine. 

'take courage;' she continued: 'i wish not for your death, but your 
repentance. the draught which i administered, was no poison, but an 
opiate. my intention in deceive you was to make you feel the agony 
of a guilty conscience, had death overtaken you sudden while your 
crime were still unrepented. you have suffer those agonies: i have 
brought you to be familiar with the sharp of death, and i trust 
that your momentary anguish will prove to you an eternal benefit. it 
is not my design to destroy your immortal soul; or bid you seek the 
grave, burthen with the weight of sin unexpiated. no, daughter, far 
from it: i will purify you with wholesome chastisement, and furnish 
you with full leisure for contrition and remorse. hear then my 
sentence; the ill-judged zeal of your friends delay its execution, 
but cannot now prevent it. all madrid believe you to be no more; your 
relations are thorough persuade of your death, and the nun your 
partizan have assist at your funeral. your existence can never be 
suspected; i have taken such precautions, as must render it an 
impenetrable mystery. then abandon all thought of a world from which 
you are eternal separated, and employ the few hour which are allow 
you, in prepare for the next.' 

this exordium led me to expect something terrible. i trembled, and 
would have spoken to deprecate her wrath: but a motion of the domina 
command me to be silent. she proceeded. 

'though of late years unjust neglected, and now oppose by many of 
our misguided sisters, (whom heaven convert!) it is my intention to 
revive the law of our order in their full force. that against 
incontinence is severe, but no more than so monstrous an offence 
demands: submit to it, daughter, without resistance; you will find the 
benefit of patience and resignation in a better life than this. listen 
then to the sentence of st. clare. beneath these vault there exist 
prisons, intended to receive such criminal as yourself: artful is 
their entrance concealed, and she who enter them, must resign all 
hope of liberty. thither must you now be conveyed. food shall be 
supply you, but not sufficient for the indulgence of appetite: you 
shall have just enough to keep together body and soul, and its quality 
shall be the simplest and coarsest. weep, daughter, weep, and moisten 
your bread with your tears: god know that you have ample cause for 
sorrow! chain down in one of these secret dungeons, shut out from 
the world and light for ever, with no comfort but religion, no society 
but repentance, thus must you groan away the remainder of your days. 
such are st. clare's orders; submit to them without repining. follow 
me!' 

thunderstruck at this barbarous decree, my little remain strength 
abandoned me. i answer only by fall at her feet, and bathing them 
with tears. the domina, unmoved by my affliction, rose from her seat 
with a stately air. she repeated her command in an absolute tone: 
but my excessive faint made me unable to obey her. mariana and 
alix raise me from the ground, and carry me forward in their arms. 
the prioress move on, leaning upon violante, and camilla precede her 
with a torch. thus pass our sad procession along the passages, in 
silence only broken by my sigh and groans. we stop before the 
principal shrine of st. clare. the statue was remove from its 
pedestal, though how i knew not. the nun afterwards raise an iron 
grate till then conceal by the image, and let it fall on the other 
side with a loud crash. the awful sound, repeated by the vault above, 
and cavern below me, rouze me from the despondent apathy in which i 
had been plunged. i look before me: an abyss present itself to my 
affrighte eyes, and a steep and narrow staircase, whither my 
conductor were leading me. i shrieked, and start back. i implore 
compassion, rent the air with my cries, and summon both heaven and 
earth to my assistance. in vain! i was hurried down the staircase, and 
forced into one of the cell which line the cavern's sides. 

my blood ran cold, as i gaze upon this melancholy abode. the cold 
vapours hover in the air, the wall green with damp, the bed of 
straw so forlorn and comfortless, the chain destined to bind me for 
ever to my prison, and the reptile of every description which as the 
torch advanced towards them, i descry hurry to their retreats, 
struck my heart with terror almost too exquisite for nature to bear. 
driven by despair to madness, i burst sudden from the nun who held 
me: i threw myself upon my knee before the prioress, and besought her 
mercy in the most passionate and frantic terms. 

'if not on me,' said i, 'look at least with pity on that innocent 
being, whose life is attach to mine! great is my crime, but let not 
my child suffer for it! my baby has committed no fault: oh! spare me 
for the sake of my unborn offspring, whom ere it taste life your 
severity doom to destruction!' 

the prioress drew back haughtily: she forced her habit from my grasp, 
as if my touch had been contagious. 

'what?' she exclaim with an exasperate air; 'what? dare you plead 
for the produce of your shame? shall a creature be permit to live, 
conceive in guilt so monstrous? abandoned woman, speak for him no 
more! better that the wretch should perish than live: begotten in 
perjury, incontinence, and pollution, it cannot fail to prove a prodigy 
of vice. hear me, thou guilty! expect no mercy from me either for 
yourself, or brat. rather pray that death may seize you before you 
produce it; or if it must see the light, that its eyes may immediately 
be closed again for ever! no aid shall be given you in your labour; 
bring your offspring into the world yourself, feed it yourself, nurse 
it yourself, bury it yourself: god grant that the latter may happen 
soon, lest you receive comfort from the fruit of your iniquity!' 

this inhuman speech, the threat which it contained, the dreadful 
suffering foretold to me by the domina, and her prayer for my 
infant's death, on whom though unborn i already doated, were more than 
my exhaust frame could support. utter a deep groan, i fell 
senseless at the feet of my unrelenting enemy. i know not how long i 
remain in this situation; but i imagine that some time must have 
elapse before my recovery, since it suffice the prioress and her nun 
to quit the cavern. when my senses returned, i found myself in silence 
and solitude. i heard not even the retiring footstep of my 
persecutors. all was hushed, and all was dreadful! i had been thrown 
upon the bed of straw: the heavy chain which i had already eye with 
terror, was wound around my waist, and fasten me to the wall. a lamp 
glimmer with dull, melancholy ray through my dungeon, permit my 
distinguish all its horrors: it was separate from the cavern by a 
low and irregular wall of stone: a large chasm was left open in it 
which form the entrance, for door there was none. a leaden crucifix 
was in front of my straw couch. a tattered rug lay near me, as did 
also a chaplet of beads; and not far from me stood a pitcher of water, 
and a wicker basket contain a small loaf, and a bottle of oil to 
supply my lamp. 

with a despondent eye did i examine this scene of suffering: when i 
reflect that i was doom to pass in it the remainder of my days, my 
heart was rent with bitter anguish. i had once been taught to look 
forward to a lot so different! at one time my prospects had appear 
so bright, so flattering! now all was lost to me. friends, comfort, 
society, happiness, in one moment i was deprived of all! dead to the 
world, dead to pleasure, i live to nothing but the sense of misery. 
how fair did that world seem to me, from which i was for ever excluded! 
how many love object did it contain, whom i never should behold 
again! as i threw a look of terror round my prison, as i shrunk from 
the cutting wind which howl through my subterraneous dwelling, the 
change seem so striking, so abrupt, that i doubt its reality. 

that the duke de medina's niece, that the destined bride of the marquis 
de las cisternas, one bred up in affluence, related to the noblest 
family in spain, and rich in a multitude of affectionate friends, 
that she should in one moment become a captive, separate from the 
world for ever, weigh down with chains, and reduce to support life 
with the coarsest aliments, appear a change so sudden and incredible, 
that i believe myself the sport of some frightful vision. its 
continuance convinced me of my mistake with but too much certainty. 
every morning my hope were disappointed. at length i abandoned all 
idea of escaping: i resigned myself to my fate, and only expect 
liberty when she came the companion of death. 

my mental anguish, and the dreadful scene in which i had been an 
actress, advanced the period of my labour. in solitude and misery, 
abandoned by all, unassisted by art, uncomforted by friendship, with 
pang which if witness would have touched the hardest heart, was i 
deliver of my wretched burthen. it came alive into the world; but i 
knew not how to treat it, or by what means to preserve its existence. 
i could only bathe it with tears, warm it in my bosom, and offer up 
prayer for its safety. i was soon deprived of this mournful 
employment: the want of proper attendance, my ignorance how to nurse 
it, the bitter cold of the dungeon, and the unwholesome air which 
inflated its lungs, terminate my sweet babe's short and painful 
existence. it expire in a few hour after its birth, and i witness 
its death with agony which beggar all description. 

but my grief was unavailing. my infant was no more; nor could all my 
sigh impart to its little tender frame the breath of a moment. i rent 
my winding-sheet, and wrap in it my lovely child. i placed it on my 
bosom, its soft arm fold round my neck, and its pale cold cheek 
rest upon mine. thus did its lifeless limb repose, while i cover 
it with kisses, talk to it, wept, and moan over it without 
remission, day or night. camilla enter my prison regularly once every 
twenty-four hours, to bring me food. in spite of her flinty nature, 
she could not behold this spectacle unmoved. she fear that grief so 
excessive would at length turn my brain, and in truth i was not always 
in my proper senses. from a principle of compassion she urge me to 
permit the corse to be buried: but to this i never would consent. i 
vow not to part with it while i had life: its presence was my only 
comfort, and no persuasion could induce me to give it up. it soon 
became a mass of putridity, and to every eye was a loathsome and 
disgust object; to every eye but a mother's. in vain did human 
feelings bid me recoil from this emblem of mortality with repugnance: 
i withstood, and vanquish that repugnance. i persist in holding my 
infant to my bosom, in lament it, loving it, adore it! hour after 
hour have i pass upon my sorry couch, contemplate what had once 
been my child: i endeavour to retrace its features through the livid 
corruption, with which they were overspread: during my confinement this 
sad occupation was my only delight; and at that time world should not 
have bribe me to give it up. even when release from my prison, i 
brought away my child in my arms. the representations of my two kind 
friends,"--(here she took the hand of the marchioness and virginia, 
and pressed them alternate to her lips)--"at length persuade me to 
resign my unhappy infant to the grave. yet i part from it with 
reluctance: however, reason at length prevailed; i suffer it to be 
taken from me, and it now repose in consecrate ground. 

i before mention that regularly once a day camilla brought me food. 
she sought not to embitter my sorrow with reproach: she bad me, 'tis 
true, resign all hope of liberty and worldly happiness; but she 
encourage me to bear with patience my temporary distress, and advise 
me to draw comfort from religion. 

my situation evidently affected her more than she venture to express: 
but she believe that to extenuate my fault would make me less anxious 
to repent it. often while her lip paint the enormity of my guilt in 
glaring colours, her eyes betrayed, how sensible she was to my 
sufferings. in fact i am certain that none of my tormentors, (for the 
three other nun enter my prison occasionally) were so much actuate 
by the spirit of oppressive cruelty as by the idea that to afflict my 
body was the only way to preserve my soul. nay, even this persuasion 
might not have had such weight with them, and they might have thought 
my punishment too severe, had not their good disposition been represt 
by blind obedience to their superior. her resentment exist in full 
force. my project of elope have been discover by the abbot of 
the capuchins, she supposed herself lower in his opinion by my 
disgrace, and in consequence her hate was inveterate. she told the 
nun to whose custody i was committed that my fault was of the most 
heinous nature, that no suffering could equal the offence, and that 
nothing could save me from eternal perdition but punishing my guilt 
with the utmost severity. the superior's word is an oracle to but too 
many of a convent's inhabitants. the nun believe whatever the 
prioress chose to assert: though contradict by reason and charity, 
they hesitate not to admit the truth of her arguments. they follow 
her injunction to the very letter, and were fully persuade that to 
treat me with lenity, or to show the least pity for my woes, would be a 
direct means to destroy my chance for salvation. 

camilla, being most employ about me, was particularly charged by the 
prioress to treat me with harshness. in compliance with these orders, 
she frequent strove to convince me, how just was my punishment, and 
how enormous was my crime: she bad me think myself too happy in saving 
my soul by mortify my body, and even threaten me sometimes with 
eternal perdition. yet as i before observed, she always conclude by 
words of encouragement and comfort; and though utter by camilla's 
lips, i easily recognise the domina's expressions. once, and once 
only, the prioress visit me in my dungeon. she then treat me with 
the most unrelenting cruelty: she loaded me with reproaches, taunt 
me with my frailty, and when i implore her mercy, told me to ask it of 
heaven, since i deserve none on earth. she even gaze upon my 
lifeless infant without emotion; and when she left me, i heard her 
charge camilla to increase the hardship of my captivity. unfeeling 
woman! but let me check my resentment: she has expiate her error by 
her sad and unexpected death. peace be with her; and may her crime be 
forgiven in heaven, as i forgive her my suffering on earth! 

thus did i drag on a miserable existence. far from grow familiar 
with my prison, i beheld it every moment with new horror. the cold 
seem more piercing and bitter, the air more thick and pestilential. 
my frame became weak, feverish, and emaciated. i was unable to rise 
from the bed of straw, and exercise my limb in the narrow limits, to 
which the length of my chain permit me to move. though exhausted, 
faint, and weary, i tremble to profit by the approach of sleep: my 
slumber were constant interrupt by some obnoxious insect crawl 
over me. 

sometimes i felt the bloated toad, hideous and pamper with the 
poisonous vapours of the dungeon, drag his loathsome length along 
my bosom: sometimes the quick cold lizard rouze me leave his slimy 
track upon my face, and entangle itself in the tresses of my wild and 
matt hair: often have i at waking found my finger ring with the 
long worm which bred in the corrupt flesh of my infant. at such 
times i shriek with terror and disgust, and while i shook off the 
reptile, tremble with all a woman's weakness. 

such was my situation, when camilla was sudden taken ill. a 
dangerous fever, supposed to be infectious, confine her to her bed. 
every one except the lay-sister appoint to nurse her, avoid her 
with caution, and fear to catch the disease. she was perfectly 
delirious, and by no means capable of attend to me. the domina and 
the nun admitted to the mystery, had latterly given me over entire 
to camilla's care: in consequence, they busy themselves no more 
about me; and occupy by prepare for the approach festival, it is 
more than probable that i never once enter into their thoughts. of 
the reason of camilla's negligence, i have been informed since my 
release by the mother st. ursula; at that time i was very far from 
suspect its cause. on the contrary, i wait for my gaoler's 
appearance at first with impatience, and afterwards with despair. one 
day pass away; another follow it; the third arrived. still no 
camilla! still no food! i knew the lapse of time by the waste of my 
lamp, to supply which fortunately a week's supply of oil had been left 
me. i supposed, either that the nun had forgotten me, or that the 
domina had ordered them to let me perish. the latter idea seem the 
most probable; yet so natural is the love of life, that i tremble to 
find it true. though embitter by every species of misery, my 
existence was still dear to me, and i dread to lose it. every 
succeed minute prove to me that i must abandon all hope of relief. 
i was become an absolute skeleton: my eyes already fail me, and my 
limb were beginning to stiffen. i could only express my anguish, and 
the pang of that hunger which gnaw my heart-strings, by frequent 
groans, whose melancholy sound the vaulted roof of the dungeon 
re-echoed. i resigned myself to my fate: i already expect the 
moment of dissolution, when my guardian angel, when my beloved brother 
arrive in time to save me. my sight grown dim and feeble at first 
refuse to recognize him; and when i did distinguish his features, the 
sudden burst of rapture was too much for me to bear. i was overpower 
by the swell of joy at once more behold a friend, and that a friend 
so dear to me. nature could not support my emotions, and took her 
refuge in insensibility. 

you already know, what are my obligation to the family of 
villa-franca: but what you cannot know is the extent of my gratitude, 
boundless as the excellence of my benefactors. lorenzo! raymond! 
name so dear to me! teach me to bear with fortitude this sudden 
transition from misery to bliss. so lately a captive, opprest with 
chains, perishing with hunger, suffering every inconvenience of cold 
and want, hidden from the light, exclude from society, hopeless, 
neglected, and as i feared, forgotten; now restore to life and 
liberty, enjoy all the comfort of affluence and ease, surround by 
those who are most love by me, and on the point of becoming his bride 
who has long been wedded to my heart, my happiness is so exquisite, so 
perfect, that scarcely can my brain sustain the weight. one only wish 
remains ungratified: it is to see my brother in his former health, and 
to know that antonia's memory is bury in her grave. 

grant this prayer, i have nothing more to desire. i trust, that my 
past suffering have purchase from heaven the pardon of my momentary 
weakness. that i have offended, offend greatly and grievously, i am 
fully conscious; but let not my husband, because he once conquer my 
virtue, doubt the propriety of my future conduct. i have been frail 
and full of error: but i yield not to the warmth of constitution; 
raymond, affection for you betray me. i was too confident of my 
strength; but i depend no less on your honour than my own. i had 
vow never to see you more: had it not been for the consequence of 
that unguarded moment, my resolution had been kept. fate will it 
otherwise, and i cannot but rejoice at its decree. still my conduct 
has been highly blameable, and while i attempt to justify myself, i 
blush at recollect my imprudence. let me then dismiss the 
ungrateful subject; first assure you, raymond, that you shall have no 
cause to repent our union, and that the more culpable have been the 
error of your mistress, the more exemplary shall be the conduct of 
your wife. 

here agnes ceased, and the marquis reply to her address in terms 
equally sincere and affectionate. lorenzo express his satisfaction at 
the prospect of being so close connected with a man for whom he had 
ever entertain the highest esteem. the pope's bull had fully and 
effectually release agnes from her religious engagements: the 
marriage was therefore celebrated as soon as the needful preparation 
had been made, for the marquis wish to have the ceremony perform 
with all possible splendour and publicity. this being over, and the 
bride have received the compliments of madrid, she departed with don 
raymond for his castle in andalusia: lorenzo accompany them, as did 
also the marchioness de villa-franca and her lovely daughter. it is 
needless to say that theodore was of the party, and would be impossible 
to describe his joy at his master's marriage. previous to his 
departure, the marquis, to atone in some measure for his past neglect, 
made some enquiry relative to elvira. finding that she as well as her 
daughter had received many service from leonella and jacintha, he 
show his respect to the memory of his sister-in-law by making the two 
women handsome presents. lorenzo follow his example--leonella was 
highly flatter by the attention of noblemen so distinguished, and 
jacintha blessed the hour on which her house was bewitched. 

on her side, agnes fail not to reward her convent friends. the worthy 
mother st. ursula, to whom she owe her liberty, was name at her 
request superintendent of 'the ladies of charity:' this was one of the 
best and most opulent society throughout spain. bertha and cornelia 
not choose to quit their friend, were appoint to principal charge 
in the same establishment. as to the nun who had aid the domina in 
persecute agnes, camilla being confine by illness to her bed, had 
perish in the flame which consume st. clare's convent. mariana, 
alix, and violante, as well as two more, had fallen victim to the 
popular rage. the three other who in council had support the 
domina's sentence, were severe reprimanded, and banish to religious 
house in obscure and distant provinces: here they languish away a 
few years, ashamed of their former weakness, and shun by their 
companion with aversion and contempt. 

nor was the fidelity of flora permit to go unrewarded. her wish 
being consulted, she declared herself impatient to revisit her native 
land. in consequence, a passage was procure for her to cuba, where 
she arrive in safety, loaded with the presents of raymond and lorenzo. 

the debt of gratitude discharged, agnes was at liberty to pursue her 
favourite plan. lodge in the same house, lorenzo and virginia were 
eternal together. the more he saw of her, the more was he convinced 
of her merit. on her part, she laid herself out to please, and not to 
succeed was for her impossible. 

lorenzo witness with admiration her beautiful person, elegant 
manners, innumerable talents, and sweet disposition: he was also much 
flatter by her prejudice in his favour, which she had not sufficient 
art to conceal. however, his sentiment partook not of that ardent 
character which had marked his affection for antonia. the image of 
that lovely and unfortunate girl still live in his heart, and baffle 
all virginia's effort to displace it. still when the duke propose to 
him the match, which he wish to earnest to take place, his nephew 
did not reject the offer. the urgent supplicate of his friends, and 
the lady's merit conquer his repugnance to enter into new 
engagements. he propose himself to the marquis de villa-franca, and 
was accept with joy and gratitude. virginia became his wife, nor did 
she ever give him cause to repent his choice. his esteem increase for 
her daily. her unremitt endeavour to please him could not but 
succeed. his affection assume strong and warm colours. antonia's 
image was gradual efface from his bosom; and virginia became sole 
mistress of that heart, which she well deserve to possess without a 
partner. 

the remain years of raymond and agnes, of lorenzo and virginia, were 
happy as can be those allot to mortals, born to be the prey of 
grief, and sport of disappointment. the exquisite sorrow with which 
they had been afflicted, made them think lightly of every succeed 
woe. they had felt the sharpest darts in misfortune's quiver; those 
which remain appear blunt in comparison. have weather fate's 
heaviest storms, they look calm upon its terrors: or if ever they 
felt affliction's casual gales, they seem to them gentle as zephyr 
which breathe over summer-seas.
