----he was a fell despightful fiend: 
hell hold none worse in baleful bower below: 
by pride, and wit, and rage, and rancor keened; 
of man alike, if good or bad the foe. 
thomson. 

on the day following antonia's death, all madrid was a scene of 
consternation and amazement. an archer who had witness the adventure 
in the sepulchre had indiscreet related the circumstances of the 
murder: he had also name the perpetrator. the confusion was without 
example which this intelligence raise among the devotees. most of 
them disbelieve it, and went themselves to the abbey to ascertain the 
fact. anxious to avoid the shame to which their superior's ill-conduct 
expose the whole brotherhood, the monk assured the visitor that 
ambrosio was prevent from receiving them as usual by nothing but 
illness. this attempt was unsuccessful: the same excuse being repeated 
day after day, the archer's story gradual obtain confidence. his 
partizan abandoned him: no one entertain a doubt of his guilt; and 
they who before had been the warmest in his praise were now the most 
vociferous in his condemnation. 

while his innocence or guilt was debate in madrid with the utmost 
acrimony, ambrosio was a prey to the pang of conscious villainy, and 
the terror of punishment impending over him. when he look back to 
the eminence on which he had lately stood, universally honour and 
respected, at peace with the world and with himself, scarcely could he 
believe that he was indeed the culprit whose crime and whose fate he 
tremble to envisage. but a few week had elapsed, since he was pure 
and virtuous, court by the wisest and noblest in madrid, and regard 
by the people with a reverence that approach idolatry: he now saw 
himself stain with the most loathe and monstrous sins, the object of 
universal execration, a prisoner of the holy office, and probably 
doom to perish in torture the most severe. he could not hope to 
deceive his judges: the proof of his guilt were too strong. his 
being in the sepulchre at so late an hour, his confusion at the 
discovery, the dagger which in his first alarm he own had been 
conceal by him, and the blood which had spirt upon his habit from 
antonia's wound, sufficient marked him out for the assassin. he 
wait with agony for the day of examination: he had no resource to 
comfort him in his distress. religion could not inspire him with 
fortitude: if he read the books of morality which were put into his 
hands, he saw in them nothing but the enormity of his offences; if he 
attempt to pray, he recollect that he deserve not heaven's 
protection, and believe his crime so monstrous as to baffle even 
god's infinite goodness. for every other sinner he thought there might 
be hope, but for him there could be none. shudder at the past, 
anguished by the present, and dread the future, thus pass he the 
few days preceding that which was marked for his trial. 

that day arrived. at nine in the morning his prison door was unlocked, 
and his gaoler entering, command him to follow him. he obey with 
trembling. he was conduct into a spacious hall, hung with black 
cloth. at the table sat three grave, stern-looking men, also habit in 
black: one was the grand inquisitor, whom the importance of this cause 
had induce to examine into it himself. at a small table at a little 
distance sat the secretary, provided with all necessary implement for 
writing. ambrosio was beckon to advance, and take his station at the 
lower end of the table. as his eye glance downwards, he perceive 
various iron instrument lie scattered upon the floor. their form 
were unknown to him, but apprehension immediately guess them to be 
engine of torture. he turn pale, and with difficulty prevent 
himself from sink upon the ground. 

profound silence prevailed, except when the inquisitor whisper a few 
words among themselves mysteriously. near an hour past away, and with 
every second of it ambrosio's fear grew more poignant. at length a 
small door, opposite to that by which he had enter the hall, grate 
heavy upon its hinges. an officer appeared, and was immediately 
follow by the beautiful matilda. her hair hung about her face wildly; 
her cheek were pale, and her eyes sunk and hollow. she threw a 
melancholy look upon ambrosio: he reply by one of aversion and 
reproach. she was placed opposite to him. a bell then sound thrice. 
it was the signal for opening the court, and the inquisitor enter 
upon their office. 

in these trial neither the accusation is mentioned, or the name of the 
accuser. the prisoner are only asked, whether they will confess: if 
they reply that have no crime they can make no confession, they are 
put to the torture without delay. this is repeated at intervals, 
either till the suspect avow themselves culpable, or the perseverance 
of the examinant is worn out and exhausted: but without a direct 
acknowledgment of their guilt, the inquisition never pronounce the 
final doom of its prisoners. 

in general much time is suffer to elapse without their being 
questioned: but ambrosio's trial had been hastened, on account of a 
solemn auto da fe which would take place in a few days, and in which 
the inquisitor meant this distinguished culprit to perform a part, and 
give a striking testimony of their vigilance. 

the abbot was not merely accused of rape and murder: the crime of 
sorcery was laid to his charge, as well as to matilda's. she had been 
seize as an accomplice in antonia's assassination. on searching her 
cell, various suspicious books and instrument were found which 
justified the accusation brought against her. to criminate the monk, 
the constellate mirror was produced, which matilda had accidental 
left in his chamber. the strange figures engrave upon it caught the 
attention of don ramirez, while searching the abbot's cell: in 
consequence, he carry it away with him. it was shown to the grand 
inquisitor, who have considered it for some time, took off a small 
golden cross which hung at his girdle, and laid it upon the mirror. 
instantly a loud noise was heard, resemble a clap of thunder, and the 
steel shiver into a thousand pieces. this circumstance confirmed the 
suspicion of the monk's have dealt in magic: it was even supposed 
that his former influence over the mind of the people was entire to 
be ascribe to witchcraft. 

determined to make him confess not only the crime which he had 
committed, but those also of which he was innocent, the inquisitor 
began their examination. though dread the tortures, as he dread 
death still more which would consign him to eternal torments, the abbot 
assert his purity in a voice bold and resolute. matilda follow his 
example, but spoke with fear and trembling. have in vain exhort 
him to confess, the inquisitor ordered the monk to be put to the 
question. the decree was immediately executed. ambrosio suffer the 
most excruciating pang that ever were invent by human cruelty: yet 
so dreadful is death when guilt accompany it, that he had sufficient 
fortitude to persist in his disavowal. his agony were redouble in 
consequence: nor was he release till faint from excess of pain, 
insensibility rescue him from the hand of his tormentors. 

matilda was next ordered to the torture: but terrify by the sight of 
the friar's sufferings, her courage total desert her. she sank 
upon her knees, acknowledge her corresponding with infernal spirits, 
and that she had witness the monk's assassinate of antonia: but as 
to the crime of sorcery, she declared herself the sole criminal, and 
ambrosio perfectly innocent. the latter assertion met with no credit. 
the abbot had recover his senses in time to hear the confession of 
his accomplice: but he was too much enfeeble by what he had already 
undergone to be capable at that time of sustain new torments. 

he was command back to his cell, but first informed that as soon as 
he had gain strength sufficient, he must prepare himself for a second 
examination. the inquisitor hope that he would then be less harden 
and obstinate. to matilda it was announce that she must expiate her 
crime in fire on the approach auto da fe. all her tear and 
entreaty could procure no mitigate of her doom, and she was drag 
by force from the hall of trial. 

return to his dungeon, the suffering of ambrosio's body were far 
more supportable than those of his mind. his dislocate limbs, the 
nail torn from his hand and feet, and his finger mash and broken 
by the pressure of screws, were far surpass in anguish by the 
agitation of his soul and vehemence of his terrors. he saw that, 
guilty or innocent, his judge were bent upon condemn him: the 
remembrance of what his denial had already cost him terrify him at 
the idea of being again applied to the question, and almost engaged him 
to confess his crimes. then again the consequence of his confession 
flash before him, and render him once more irresolute. his death 
would be inevitable, and that a death the most dreadful: he had 
listen to matilda's doom, and doubt not that a similar was reserved 
for him. he shudder at the approach auto da fe, at the idea of 
perishing in flames, and only escape from indurable torment to pass 
into other more subtile and ever-lasting! with affright did he bend 
his mind's eye on the space beyond the grave; nor could hide from 
himself how just he ought to dread heaven's vengeance. in this 
labyrinth of terrors, fain would he have taken his refuge in the gloom 
of atheism: fain would he have deny the soul's immortality; have 
persuade himself that when his eyes once closed, they would never more 
open, and that the same moment would annihilate his soul and body. 
even this resource was refuse to him. to permit his being blind to 
the fallacy of this belief, his knowledge was too extensive, his 
understanding too solid and just. he could not help feeling the 
existence of a god. those truths, once his comfort, now present 
themselves before him in the clearest light; but they only serve to 
drive him to distraction. they destroy his ill-grounded hope of 
escape punishment; and dispel by the irresistible bright of 
truth and convinction, philosophy's deceitful vapours fade away like a 
dream. 

in anguish almost too great for mortal frame to bear, he expect the 
time when he was again to be examined. he busy himself in plan 
ineffectual scheme for escape both present and future punishment. 
of the first there was no possibility; of the second despair made him 
neglect the only means. while reason forced him to acknowledge a god's 
existence, conscience made him doubt the infinity of his goodness. he 
disbelieve that a sinner like him could find mercy. he had not been 
deceive into error: ignorance could furnish him with no excuse. he 
had seen vice in her true colours; before he committed his crimes, he 
had compute every scruple of their weight; and yet he had committed 
them. 

'pardon?' he would cry in an access of phrenzy 'oh! there can be none 
for me!' 

persuade of this, instead of humble himself in penitence, of 
deplore his guilt, and employ his few remain hour in 
deprecate heaven's wrath, he abandoned himself to the transport of 
desperate rage; he sorrow for the punishment of his crimes, not their 
commission; and exhale his bosom's anguish in idle sighs, in vain 
lamentations, in blasphemy and despair. as the few beam of day which 
pierce through the bar of his prison window gradual disappeared, 
and their place was supply by the pale and glimmer lamp, he felt 
his terror redouble, and his idea become more gloomy, more solemn, 
more despondent. he dread the approach of sleep: no soon did his 
eyes close, weary with tear and watching, than the dreadful vision 
seem to be realise on which his mind had dwelt during the day. he 
found himself in sulphurous realm and burning caverns, surround by 
fiend appoint his tormentors, and who drove him through a variety of 
tortures, each of which was more dreadful than the former. amidst 
these dismal scene wander the ghost of elvira and her daughter. 
they reproach him with their deaths, recount his crime to the 
daemons, and urge them to inflict torment of cruelty yet more 
refined. such were the pictures which float before his eyes in 
sleep: they vanish not till his repose was disturbed by excess of 
agony. then would he start from the ground on which he had stretch 
himself, his brow running down with cold sweat, his eyes wild and 
phrenzied; and he only exchange the terrible certainty for surmize 
scarcely more supportable. he pace his dungeon with disorder steps; 
he gaze with terror upon the surrounding darkness, and often did he 
cry, 

'oh! fearful is night to the guilty!' 

the day of his second examination was at hand. he had been compel 
to swallow cordials, whose virtue were calculate to restore his 
bodily strength, and enable him to support the question longer. on the 
night preceding this dread day, his fear for the morrow permit 
him not to sleep. his terror were so violent, as nearly to annihilate 
his mental powers. he sat like one stupefy near the table on which 
his lamp was burning dimly. despair chain up his faculty in 
idiotism, and he remain for some hours, unable to speak or move, or 
indeed to think. 

'look up, ambrosio!' said a voice in accent well-known to him-- 

the monk started, and raise his melancholy eyes. matilda stood before 
him. she had quit her religious habit. she now wore a female 
dress, at once elegant and splendid: a profusion of diamond blaze 
upon her robes, and her hair was confine by a coronet of roses. in 
her right hand she held a small book: a lively expression of pleasure 
beam upon her countenance; but still it was mingle with a wild 
imperious majesty which inspired the monk with awe, and represt in some 
measure his transport at seeing her. 

'you here, matilda?' he at length exclaimed; 'how have you gain 
entrance? where are your chains? what means this magnificence, and 
the joy which sparkle in your eyes? have our judge relented? is 
there a chance of my escaping? answer me for pity, and tell me, what i 
have to hope, or fear.' 

'ambrosio!' she reply with an air of commanding dignity; 'i have 
baffle the inquisition's fury. i am free: a few moments will place 
kingdom between these dungeon and me. yet i purchase my liberty at a 
dear, at a dreadful price! dare you pay the same, ambrosio? dare you 
spring without fear over the bounds which separate men from 
angels?--you are silent.--you look upon me with eyes of suspicion and 
alarm--i read your thought and confess their justice. yes, ambrosio; 
i have sacrifice all for life and liberty. i am no long a candidate 
for heaven! i have renounce god's service, and am enlist beneath 
the banner of his foes. the deed is past recall: yet were it in my 
power to go back, i would not. oh! my friend, to expire in such 
torments! to die amidst curse and execrations! to bear the insult of 
an exasperate mob! to be expose to all the mortification of shame 
and infamy! who can reflect without horror on such a doom? let me 
then exult in my exchange. i have sold distant and uncertain happiness 
for present and secure: i have preserve a life which otherwise i had 
lost in torture; and i have obtain the power of procure every bliss 
which can make that life delicious! the infernal spirits obey me as 
their sovereign: by their aid shall my days be past in every 
refinement of luxury and voluptuousness. i will enjoy unrestrained the 
gratification of my senses: every passion shall be indulged, even to 
satiety; then will i bid my servant invent new pleasures, to revive 
and stimulate my glut appetites! i go impatient to exercise my 
newly-gained dominion. i pant to be at liberty. nothing should hold me 
one moment long in this abhor abode, but the hope of persuade 
you to follow my example. ambrosio, i still love you: our mutual 
guilt and danger have render you dear to me than ever, and i would 
fain save you from impending destruction. summon then your resolution 
to your aid; and renounce for immediate and certain benefit the hope 
of a salvation, difficult to obtain, and perhaps altogether erroneous. 
shake off the prejudice of vulgar souls; abandon a god who has 
abandoned you, and raise yourself to the level of superior beings!' 

she pause for the monk's reply: he shuddered, while he gave it. 

'matilda!' he said after a long silence in a low and unsteady voice; 
'what price gave you for liberty?' 

she answer him firm and dauntless. 

'ambrosio, it was my soul!' 

'wretched woman, what have you done? pass but a few years, and how 
dreadful will be your sufferings!' 

'weak man, pass but this night, and how dreadful will be your own! do 
you remember what you have already endured? tomorrow you must bear 
torment doubly exquisite. do you remember the horrors of a fiery 
punishment? in two days you must be led a victim to the stake! what 
then will become of you? still dare you hope for pardon? still are 
you beguile with vision of salvation? think upon your crimes! think 
upon your lust, your perjury, inhumanity, and hypocrisy! think upon 
the innocent blood which cries to the throne of god for vengeance, and 
then hope for mercy! then dream of heaven, and sigh for world of 
light, and realm of peace and pleasure! absurd! open your eyes, 
ambrosio, and be prudent. hell is your lot; you are doom to eternal 
perdition; nought lie beyond your grave but a gulph of devour 
flames. and will you then speed towards that hell? will you clasp that 
perdition in your arms, ere 'tis needful? will you plunge into those 
flame while you still have the power to shun them? 'tis a madman's 
action. no, no, ambrosio: let us for awhile fly from divine 
vengeance. be advise by me; purchase by one moment's courage the 
bliss of years; enjoy the present, and forget that a future lag 
behind.' 

'matilda, your counsel are dangerous: i dare not, i will not follow 
them. i must not give up my claim to salvation. monstrous are my 
crimes; but god is merciful, and i will not despair of pardon.' 

'is such your resolution? i have no more to say. i speed to joy and 
liberty, and abandon you to death and eternal torments.' 

'yet stay one moment, matilda! you command the infernal daemons: 

you can force open these prison doors; you can release me from these 
chain which weigh me down. save me, i conjure you, and bear me from 
these fearful abodes!' 

'you ask the only boon beyond my power to bestow. i am forbidden to 
assist a churchman and a partizan of god: renounce those titles, and 
command me.' 

'i will not sell my soul to perdition.' 

'persist in your obstinacy, till you find yourself at the stake: then 
will you repent your error, and sigh for escape when the moment is gone 
by. i quit you. yet ere the hour of death arrive should wisdom 
enlighten you, listen to the means of repair your present fault. i 
leave with you this book. read the four first lines of the seventh 
page backwards: the spirit whom you have already once beheld will 
immediately appear to you. if you are wise, we shall meet again: if 
not, farewell for ever!' 

she let the book fall upon the ground. a cloud of blue fire wrap 
itself round her: she wave her hand to ambrosio, and disappeared. 
the momentary glare which the flame pour through the dungeon, on 
dissipate suddenly, seem to have increase its natural gloom. the 
solitary lamp scarcely gave light sufficient to guide the monk to a 
chair. he threw himself into his seat, fold his arms, and leaning 
his head upon the table, sank into reflection perplex and 
unconnected. 

he was still in this attitude when the opening of the prison door 
rouze him from his stupor. he was summon to appear before the grand 
inquisitor. he rose, and follow his gaoler with painful steps. he 
was led into the same hall, placed before the same examiners, and was 
again interrogate whether he would confess. he reply as before, that 
have no crimes, he could acknowledge none: but when the executioner 
prepared to put him to the question, when he saw the engine of 
torture, and remember the pang which they had already inflicted, his 
resolution fail him entirely. forget the consequences, and only 
anxious to escape the terror of the present moment, he made an ample 
confession. he disclose every circumstance of his guilt, and own 
not merely the crime with which he was charged, but those of which he 
had never been suspected. being interrogate as to matilda's flight 
which had create much confusion, he confessed that she had sold 
herself to satan, and that she was indebted to sorcery for her escape. 
he still assured his judge that for his own part he had never enter 
into any compact with the infernal spirits; but the threat of being 
torture made him declare himself to be a sorcerer, and heretic, and 
whatever other title the inquisitor chose to fix upon him. in 
consequence of this avowal, his sentence was immediately pronounced. 
he was ordered to prepare himself to perish in the auto da fe, which 
was to be solemnize at twelve o'clock that night. this hour was 
chosen from the idea that the horror of the flame being heighten by 
the gloom of midnight, the execution would have a greater effect upon 
the mind of the people. 

ambrosio rather dead than alive was left alone in his dungeon. the 
moment in which this terrible decree was pronounced had nearly prove 
that of his dissolution. he look forward to the morrow with despair, 
and his terror increase with the approach of midnight. sometimes he 
was bury in gloomy silence: at other he rave with delirious 
passion, wrung his hands, and cursed the hour when he first beheld the 
light. in one of these moments his eye rest upon matilda's 
mysterious gift. his transport of rage were instantly suspended. he 
look earnest at the book; he took it up, but immediately threw it 
from him with horror. he walk rapid up and down his dungeon: then 
stopped, and again fixed his eyes on the spot where the book had 
fallen. he reflect that here at least was a resource from the fate 
which he dreaded. he stooped, and took it up a second time. 

he remain for some time tremble and irresolute: he long to try 
the charm, yet fear its consequences. the recollection of his 
sentence at length fixed his indecision. he open the volume; but his 
agitation was so great that he at first sought in vain for the page 
mention by matilda. ashamed of himself, he call all his courage to 
his aid. he turn to the seventh leaf. he began to read it aloud; 
but his eyes frequent wander from the book, while he anxious cast 
them round in search of the spirit, whom he wished, yet dread to 
behold. still he persist in his design; and with a voice unassured 
and frequent interruptions, he contrived to finish the four first lines 
of the page. 

they were in a language, whose import was total unknown to him. 

scarce had he pronounced the last word when the effects of the charm 
were evident. a loud burst of thunder was heard; the prison shook to 
its very foundations; a blaze of lightning flash through the cell; 
and in the next moment, borne upon sulphurous whirl-winds, lucifer 
stood before him a second time. but he came not as when at matilda's 
summons he borrow the seraph's form to deceive ambrosio. he appear 
in all that ugly which since his fall from heaven had been his 
portion: his blasted limb still bore mark of the almighty's thunder: 
a swarthy dark spread itself over his gigantic form: his hand and 
feet were armed with long talons: fury glare in his eyes, which might 
have struck the bravest heart with terror: over his huge shoulder 
wave two enormous sable wings; and his hair was supply by living 
snakes, which twin themselves round his brow with frightful 
hissings. in one hand he held a roll of parchment, and in the other an 
iron pen. still the lightning flash around him, and the thunder with 
repeated bursts, seem to announce the dissolution of nature. 

terrify at an apparition so different from what he had expected, 
ambrosio remain gaze upon the fiend, deprived of the power of 
utterance. the thunder had cease to roll: universal silence reign 
through the dungeon. 

'for what am i summon hither?' said the daemon, in a voice which 
sulphurous fog had damp to hoarseness-- 

at the sound nature seem to tremble: a violent earthquake rock the 
ground, accompany by a fresh burst of thunder, loud and more 
appalling than the first. 

ambrosio was long unable to answer the daemon's demand. 

'i am condemn to die;' he said with a faint voice, his blood running 
cold, while he gaze upon his dreadful visitor. 'save me! bear me 
from hence!' 

'shall the reward of my service be paid me? dare you embrace my 
cause? will you be mine, body and soul? are you prepared to renounce 
him who made you, and him who die for you? answer but "yes" and 
lucifer is your slave.' 

'will no less price content you? can nothing satisfy you but my 
eternal ruin? spirit, you ask too much. yet convey me from this 
dungeon: be my servant for one hour, and i will be yours for a 
thousand years. will not this offer suffice?' 

'it will not. i must have your soul; must have it mine, and mine for 
ever.' 

'insatiate daemon, i will not doom myself to endless torments. i will 
not give up my hope of being one day pardoned.' 

'you will not? on what chimaera rest then your hopes? short-sighted 
mortal! miserable wretch! are you not guilty? are you not infamous in 
the eyes of men and angels. can such enormous sin be forgiven? hope 
you to escape my power? your fate is already pronounced. the eternal 
has abandoned you; mine you are marked in the book of destiny, and mine 
you must and shall be!' 

'fiend, 'tis false! infinite is the almighty's mercy, and the penitent 
shall meet his forgiveness. my crime are monstrous, but i will not 
despair of pardon: haply, when they have received due chastisement....' 

'chastisement? was purgatory meant for guilt like yours? hope you 
that your offence shall be bought off by prayer of superstitious 
dotard and drone monks? ambrosio, be wise! mine you must be: you 
are doom to flames, but may shun them for the present. sign this 
parchment: i will bear you from hence, and you may pass your remain 
years in bliss and liberty. enjoy your existence: indulge in every 
pleasure to which appetite may lead you: but from the moment that it 
quits your body, remember that your soul belong to me, and that i will 
not be defraud of my right.' 

the monk was silent; but his looks declared that the tempter's words 
were not thrown away. he reflect on the conditions propose with 
horror: on the other hand, he believe himself doom to perdition and 
that, by refuse the daemon's succour, he only hasten torture which 
he never could escape. the fiend saw that his resolution was shaken: 
he renew his instances, and endeavour to fix the abbot's 
indecision. he describe the agony of death in the most terrific 
colours; and he work so powerful upon ambrosio's despair and fear 
that he prevail upon him to receive the parchment. he then struck 
the iron pen which he held into a vein of the monk's left hand. it 
pierce deep, and was instantly fill with blood; yet ambrosio felt no 
pain from the wound. the pen was put into his hand: it trembled. the 
wretch placed the parchment on the table before him, and prepared to 
sign it. sudden he held his hand: he start away hastily, and 
threw the pen upon the table. 

'what am i doing?' he cried--then turning to the fiend with a desperate 
air, 'leave me! begone! i will not sign the parchment.' 

'fool!' exclaim the disappointed daemon, dart looks so furious as 
penetrate the friar's soul with horror; 'thus am i trifle with? go 
then! rave in agony, expire in tortures, and then learn the extent of 
the eternal's mercy! but beware how you make me again your mock! call 
me no more till resolve to accept my offers! summon me a second time 
to dismiss me thus idly, and these talon shall rend you into a 
thousand pieces! speak yet again; will you sign the parchment?' 

'i will not! leave me! away!' 

instantly the thunder was heard to roll horribly: once more the earth 
tremble with violence: the dungeon resound with loud shrieks, and 
the daemon flee with blasphemy and curses. 

at first, the monk rejoice at have resist the seducer's arts, and 
obtain a triumph over mankind's enemy: but as the hour of punishment 
drew near, his former terror revive in his heart. their momentary 
repose seem to have given them fresh vigour. the near that the 
time approached, the more did he dread appear before the throne of 
god. he shudder to think how soon he must be plunge into eternity; 
how soon meet the eyes of his creator, whom he had so grievous 
offended. the bell announce midnight: it was the signal for being 
led to the stake! as he listen to the first stroke, the blood cease 
to circulate in the abbot's veins: he heard death and torture murmur 
in each succeed sound. he expect to see the archer enter his 
prison; and as the bell forbore to toll, he seize the magic volume in 
a fit of despair. he open it, turn hasty to the seventh page, 
and as if fear to allow himself a moment's thought ran over the 
fatal lines with rapidity. accompany by his former terrors, lucifer 
again stood before the trembler. 

'you have summon me,' said the fiend; 'are you determined to be wise? 
will you accept my conditions? you know them already. renounce your 
claim to salvation, make over to me your soul, and i bear you from this 
dungeon instantly. yet is it time. resolve, or it will be too late. 
will you sign the parchment?' 

'i must!--fate urge me! i accept your conditions.' 

'sign the parchment!' reply the daemon in an exult tone. 

the contract and the bloody pen still lay upon the table. ambrosio drew 
near it. he prepared to sign his name. a moment's reflection made him 
hesitate. 

'hark!' cry the tempter; 'they come! be quick! sign the parchment, 
and i bear you from hence this moment.' 

in effect, the archer were heard approaching, appoint to lead 
ambrosio to the stake. the sound encourage the monk in his resolution. 

'what is the import of this writing?' said he. 

'it make your soul over to me for ever, and without reserve.' 

'what am i to receive in exchange?' 

'my protection, and release from this dungeon. sign it, and this 
instant i bear you away.' 

ambrosio took up the pen; he set it to the parchment. again his courage 
fail him: he felt a pang of terror at his heart, and once more threw 
the pen upon the table. 

'weak and puerile!' cry the exasperate fiend: 'away with this folly! 
sign the writing this instant, or i sacrifice you to my rage!' 

at this moment the bolt of the outward door was drawn back. the 
prisoner heard the rattling of chains; the heavy bar fell; the archer 
were on the point of entering. work up to phrenzy by the urgent 
danger, shrink from the approach of death, terrify by the daemon's 
threats, and seeing no other means to escape destruction, the wretched 
monk complied. he sign the fatal contract, and gave it hasty into 
the evil spirit's hands, whose eyes, as he received the gift, glare 
with malicious rapture. 

'take it!' said the god-abandoned; 'now then save me! snatch me from 
hence!' 

'hold! do you freely and absolutely renounce your creator and his son?' 

'i do! i do!' 

'do you make over your soul to me for ever?' 

'for ever!' 

'without reserve or subterfuge? without future appeal to the divine 
mercy?' 

the last chain fell from the door of the prison: the key was heard 
turning in the lock: already the iron door grate heavy upon its 
rusty hinges. 

'i am yours for ever and irrevocably!' cry the monk wild with terror: 
'i abandon all claim to salvation! i own no power but yours! hark! 
hark! they come! oh! save me! bear me away!' 

'i have triumphed! you are mine past reprieve, and i fulfil my 
promise.' 

while he spoke, the door unclosed. instantly the daemon grasp one of 
ambrosio's arms, spread his broad pinions, and sprang with him into the 
air. the roof open as they soar upwards, and closed again when 
they had quit the dungeon. 

in the meanwhile, the gaoler was thrown into the utmost surprize by the 
disappearance of his prisoner. though neither he nor the archer were 
in time to witness the monk's escape, a sulphurous smell prevailing 
through the prison sufficient informed them by whose aid he had been 
liberated. they hasten to make their report to the grand inquisitor. 
the story, how a sorcerer had been carry away by the devil, was soon 
noise about madrid; and for some days the whole city was employ in 
discuss the subject. gradual it cease to be the topic of 
conversation: other adventure arose whose novelty engaged universal 
attention; and ambrosio was soon forgotten as totally, as if he never 
had existed. while this was passing, the monk support by his 
infernal guide, traverse the air with the rapid of an arrow, and a 
few moments placed him upon a precipice's brink, the steepest in sierra 
morena. 

though rescue from the inquisition, ambrosio as yet was insensible of 
the blessing of liberty. the damning contract weigh heavy upon his 
mind; and the scene in which he had been a principal actor had left 
behind them such impression as render his heart the seat of anarchy 
and confusion. the object now before his eyes, and which the full 
moon sailing through cloud permit him to examine, were 
ill-calculated to inspire that calm, of which he stood so much in need. 
the disorder of his imagination was increase by the wild of the 
surrounding scenery; by the gloomy cavern and steep rocks, rising 
above each other, and divide the passing clouds; solitary cluster of 
tree scattered here and there, among whose thick-twined branch the 
wind of night sigh hoarse and mournfully; the shrill cry of 
mountain eagles, who had built their nest among these lonely desarts; 
the stunning roar of torrents, as swell by late rains they rush 
violent down tremendous precipices; and the dark waters of a silent 
sluggish stream which faint reflect the moonbeams, and bath the 
rock's base on which ambrosio stood. the abbot cast round him a look 
of terror. his infernal conductor was still by his side, and eye him 
with a look of mingle malice, exultation, and contempt. 

'whither have you brought me?' said the monk at length in an hollow 
tremble voice: 'why am i placed in this melancholy scene? bear me 
from it quickly! carry me to matilda!' 

the fiend reply not, but continue to gaze upon him in silence. 

ambrosio could not sustain his glance; he turn away his eyes, while 
thus spoke the daemon: 

'i have him then in my power! this model of piety! this being without 
reproach! this mortal who placed his puny virtue on a level with 
those of angels. he is mine! irrevocably, eternal mine! companion 
of my sufferings! denizen of hell! how grateful will be my present!' 

he paused; then address himself to the monk---- 

'carry you to matilda?' he continued, repeating ambrosio's words: 

'wretch! you shall soon be with her! you well deserve a place near her, 
for hell boast no miscreant more guilty than yourself. 

hark, ambrosio, while i unveil your crimes! you have shed the blood of 
two innocents; antonia and elvira perish by your hand. that antonia 
whom you violated, was your sister! that elvira whom you murdered, gave 
you birth! tremble, abandoned hypocrite! inhuman parricide! incestuous 
ravisher! tremble at the extent of your offences! and you it was who 
thought yourself proof against temptation, absolve from human 
frailties, and free from error and vice! is pride then a virtue? is 
inhumanity no fault? know, vain man! that i long have marked you for 
my prey: i watch the movements of your heart; i saw that you were 
virtuous from vanity, not principle, and i seize the fit moment of 
seduction. i observe your blind idolatry of the madona's picture. i 
bad a subordinate but crafty spirit assume a similar form, and you 
eager yield to the blandishments of matilda. your pride was 
gratify by her flattery; your lust only need an opportunity to 
break forth; you ran into the snare blindly, and scruple not to commit 
a crime which you blame in another with unfeeling severity. it was i 
who threw matilda in your way; it was i who gave you entrance to 
antonia's chamber; it was i who cause the dagger to be given you which 
pierce your sister's bosom; and it was i who warn elvira in dream 
of your designs upon her daughter, and thus, by prevent your 
profit by her sleep, compel you to add rape as well as incest to 
the catalogue of your crimes. hear, hear, ambrosio! had you resist 
me one minute longer, you had save your body and soul. the guard whom 
you heard at your prison door came to signify your pardon. but i had 
already triumphed: my plot had already succeeded. scarcely could i 
propose crime so quick as you perform them. you are mine, and 
heaven itself cannot rescue you from my power. hope not that your 
penitence will make void our contract. here is your bond sign with 
your blood; you have given up your claim to mercy, and nothing can 
restore to you the rights which you have foolish resigned. believe 
you that your secret thought escape me? no, no, i read them all! 
you trust that you should still have time for repentance. i saw your 
artifice, knew its falsity, and rejoice in deceive the deceiver! 
you are mine beyond reprieve: i burn to possess my right, and alive 
you quit not these mountains.' 

during the daemon's speech, ambrosio had been stupefy by terror and 
surprize. this last declaration rouze him. 

'not quit these mountain alive?' he exclaimed: 'perfidious, what mean 
you? have you forgotten our contract?' 

the fiend answer by a malicious laugh: 

'our contract? have i not perform my part? what more did i promise 
than to save you from your prison? have i not done so? are you not 
safe from the inquisition--safe from all but from me? fool that you 
were to confide yourself to a devil! why did you not stipulate for 
life, and power, and pleasure? then all would have been granted: now, 
your reflection come too late. miscreant, prepare for death; you have 
not many hour to live!' 

on hearing this sentence, dreadful were the feelings of the devoted 
wretch! he sank upon his knees, and raise his hand towards heaven. 
the fiend read his intention and prevent it-- 

'what?' he cried, dart at him a look of fury: 'dare you still 
implore the eternal's mercy? would you feign penitence, and again act 
an hypocrite's part? villain, resign your hope of pardon. thus i 
secure my prey!' 

as he said this, dart his talon into the monk's shaven crown, he 
sprang with him from the rock. the cave and mountain rang with 
ambrosio's shrieks. the daemon continue to soar aloft, till reach a 
dreadful height, he release the sufferer. headlong fell the monk 
through the airy waste; the sharp point of a rock received him; and he 
roll from precipice to precipice, till bruise and mangle he rest 
on the river's banks. life still exist in his miserable frame: he 
attempt in vain to raise himself; his broken and dislocate limb 
refuse to perform their office, nor was he able to quit the spot where 
he had first fallen. the sun now rose above the horizon; its scorching 
beam dart full upon the head of the expire sinner. myriad of 
insect were call forth by the warmth; they drank the blood which 
trickle from ambrosio's wounds; he had no power to drive them from 
him, and they fasten upon his sores, dart their sting into his 
body, cover him with their multitudes, and inflict on him torture 
the most exquisite and insupportable. the eagle of the rock tore his 
flesh piecemeal, and dug out his eyeball with their crooked beaks. a 
burning thirst torment him; he heard the river's murmur as it roll 
beside him, but strove in vain to drag himself towards the sound. 
blind, maimed, helpless, and despairing, vent his rage in blasphemy 
and curses, execrate his existence, yet dread the arrival of death 
destined to yield him up to greater torments, six miserable days did 
the villain languish. on the seventh a violent storm arose: the wind 
in fury rent up rocks and forests: the sky was now black with clouds, 
now sheet with fire: the rain fell in torrents; it swell the 
stream; the wave overflow their banks; they reach the spot where 
ambrosio lay, and when they abate carry with them into the river the 
corse of the despairing monk.
